1. Introduction
1.1 Background and Problem Context
•	What are the principal power-quality challenges currently affecting medium-voltage distribution substations, particularly 33/11 kV substations?
Key power-quality challenges in 33/11 kV and similar MV substations
Medium voltage (MV) distribution substations, including 33/11 kV injection and 11 kV feeders, are mainly challenged by voltage magnitude problems, harmonics, unbalance, and losses, increasingly driven by power electronic and distributed generation (DG) connections.
Major power quality issues at MV/33–11 kV level
1. Voltage magnitude disturbances
•	Sags, swells, flicker, and short interruptions are reported as the dominant PQ events in MV and LV networks, especially where large motors or faults occur (Matter et al., 2023; Razmi et al., 2023).
•	Case studies of 33 kV and 35 kV feeders show overvoltage, reverse power flow, and unacceptable voltage profiles when PV or embedded generators are integrated without control (, 2023; F. et al., 2021).
•	Voltage non compliance at MV busbars and end of line nodes is common, especially with weak grids and long feeders (, 2023; Zangpo et al., 2025; Li et al., 2025).
2. Harmonics and waveform distortion
•	Nonlinear and power electronic loads (VFDs, UPS, PV inverters, IT loads) introduce current and voltage harmonics, elevating THD and risking standard violations (Alawasa & Al-Badi, 2024; Razmi et al., 2023; F. et al., 2021; Abood & Abdul-Wahhab, 2021).
•	Studies on 33/11 kV networks and their 11 kV feeders show harmonic related overheating, increased losses, and transformer/feeder overloading (Alawasa & Al-Badi, 2024; F. et al., 2021; Abood & Abdul-Wahhab, 2021).
3. Unbalance and phase issues
•	Voltage unbalance at MV is repeatedly highlighted as one of the most common PQ problems, particularly with unbalanced loading and single phase connections (Matter et al., 2023; Razmi et al., 2023).
•	Unbalance degrades motor performance and accelerates heating more severely than some other PQ issues (Matter et al., 2023).
4. Technical losses and reactive power/voltage control
•	MV feeders and 33/11 kV substations suffer from high technical losses, aggravated by old conductors, overloaded transformers, and long 11 kV feeders (Naruyinda et al., 2023; Zangpo et al., 2025; Kryltcov et al., 2021).
•	Reactive power shortage and poor voltage at end nodes drive low voltage quality and instability, requiring optimized capacitor placement and reactive power planning (Zangpo et al., 2025; Li et al., 2025).
Emerging system level concerns
•	Rising DG and RES penetration increases voltage fluctuation, harmonics, and transients, making PQ a central design and operation constraint in MV distribution (Razmi et al., 2023; Dehaghani et al., 2025; , 2023).
•	Many 33/11 kV substations in practice also face low reliability and frequent outages, linking PQ with interruption indices (SAIFI, SAIDI) and outage cost impact on consumers (Itua et al., 2025; Ogum et al., 2023).
Conclusion
For modern 33/11 kV and comparable MV substations, the principal PQ challenges are voltage sags/swells and profile violations, harmonic distortion from nonlinear and converter based resources, voltage unbalance, and elevated losses tied to reactive power and ageing asset issues, all exacerbated by growing DG and sensitive loads.

•	How have increasing nonlinear loads, distributed energy resources, and urban load growth changed operating conditions in medium-voltage distribution networks?
Nonlinear loads, DERs, and urban growth have made MV networks more stressed, bidirectional, and power electronics dominated, with tighter voltage and thermal margins.
Key changes in operating conditions
1. Heavier, more uneven loading and tighter voltage margins
•	Urban load growth deepens peak loads, causing larger voltage drops and higher losses, especially at remote buses; in a 33 bus test system, raising load to 150% dropped minimum voltage by ~26% and sharply increased active/reactive losses (Anggara et al., 2025; Alao & Popoola, 2024).
•	MV networks now face insufficient reactive power and low end of line voltages, requiring explicit reactive power optimization and capacitor planning (Li et al., 2025).
•	Urban MV feeders operate closer to thermal limits, with many branches overloaded once new resources are added (Dimovski et al., 2023).
2. From unidirectional to bidirectional, DER constrained operation
•	High PV and DER penetrations shift MV networks from passive, top down flows to locally variable, bidirectional power flows, altering protection, voltage control, and congestion patterns (Zapf et al., 2021; Razmi et al., 2023; Maghami et al., 2023).
•	DER injections can both improve or degrade voltages: PV can mitigate low voltage at peak load but cause overvoltages and branch overloading beyond certain penetration levels, especially in dense urban feeders (Dimovski et al., 2023; Maghami et al., 2023; Nam et al., 2025).
•	Energy communities and clustered DERs create localized hotspots of line and transformer loading, forcing earlier network reinforcement or more active control (Dimovski et al., 2023).
3. Power electronics dominance and power quality impacts
•	Nonlinear loads and inverter based DERs increase harmonic currents and voltage distortion, though multiple devices can sometimes partially cancel harmonics (Michalec et al., 2021; Adesina et al., 2025; Ahsan et al., 2021).
•	Inverter interfaced DERs reduce effective inertia and change dynamic behavior, contributing to voltage fluctuations and stability challenges under disturbances (Howlader, 2024; Razmi et al., 2023).
4. Asset aging and reliability
•	New DER driven loading patterns cause short but high cable utilizations, accelerating thermal aging of legacy MV cables (e.g., PILC) and shortening lifetime despite modest average loading (Zapf et al., 2021).
Summary Table: Dominant Trends
Driver	Main network effect	Typical consequences	Citations
Urban load growth	Higher peaks, longer heavily loaded feeders	Voltage drop, higher losses, low end voltages	(Anggara et al., 2025; Li et al., 2025; Alao & Popoola, 2024)
DER penetration	Bidirectional, variable flows	Over/undervoltage, branch overload, new constraints	(Dimovski et al., 2023; Razmi et al., 2023; Maghami et al., 2023; Nam et al., 2025)
Nonlinear/inverter loads	Distorted currents/voltages	Harmonics, altered dynamics, PQ issues	(Michalec et al., 2021; Adesina et al., 2025; Razmi et al., 2023; Ahsan et al., 2021)
New operating patterns	Short high load events	Accelerated cable aging, reliability risk	(Zapf et al., 2021)
Figure 1: How modern drivers reshape MV network operating conditions.
Conclusion
Modern MV distribution networks, especially in urban areas, now operate closer to thermal and voltage limits, with bidirectional, time variable flows dominated by power electronic interfaces. This raises losses, congestion, harmonic distortion, and asset aging risks, and forces a shift from passive planning to active, optimization based operation and reinforcement.

•	Why is harmonic resonance now considered a strategic operational and planning issue in modern distribution substations?
Harmonic resonance is now a system level constraint in planning and operating modern distribution substations because power electronic harmonics and changing network impedance can amplify distortion, losses, and failure risk in unpredictable ways.
Why it has become a strategic issue
1. Growth of harmonics + capacitors = high resonance risk
•	Widespread non linear loads and inverter based DERs inject broad harmonic spectra into MV/LV networks (Razmi et al., 2023; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Kaur & Bath, 2025).
•	Shunt capacitor banks used for power factor/Volt VAR control create LC circuits with feeder inductance, making parallel/series resonance much more likely and often over a band of harmonic orders (Peerzada et al., 2024; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ardi et al., 2024).
•	When injected harmonics are near the network’s resonant frequency, simulations and measurements show large amplification of voltage/current THD, eddy current losses, and equipment heating at feeders and substation transformers (Peerzada et al., 2024; Adesina et al., 2025; Pereira et al., 2021).
2. Consequences now affect reliability, asset life, and compliance
•	Resonance amplified harmonics cause overheating, insulation stress, capacitor and transformer overloading, nuisance tripping, and even feeder shutdowns (Peerzada et al., 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ko & Tuomainen, 2021; Klyuev, 2025).
•	Studies on substations and traction/metro systems report equipment damage, overvoltages, and instability when resonance bands overlap with converter generated harmonics (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ko & Tuomainen, 2021; Guo et al., 2025; Xie & Zhong, 2025).
•	Meeting IEEE 519 and grid code limits is no longer a simple local check; resonance can push otherwise compliant devices to system level non compliance (Peerzada et al., 2024; Adesina et al., 2025; Pereira et al., 2021; Kaur & Bath, 2025).
3. Highly variable, configuration dependent phenomenon
•	Resonant frequencies shift with load level, DER output, capacitor switching, cable/line mix, and network reconfiguration, so risk changes across time and operating states (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Shakeri et al., 2024).
•	New methods optimize monitor placement and resonance indices to track “worst case” resonance across scenarios, reflecting its status as a planning level problem rather than a one off study (Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024).
4. Need for deliberate design and active mitigation
•	Planning now routinely includes filter design, detuned reactors, DSTATCOM/DVR solutions, and impedance shaping strategies specifically to avoid or damp resonance (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ko & Tuomainen, 2021; Abbas et al., 2021; Ardi et al., 2024; Guo et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Because harmonic resonance can emerge from normal capacitor use and evolving DER/non linear load patterns, and can swiftly escalate into widespread PQ violations and equipment failures, it is treated as a strategic operational and planning constraint in modern distribution substations.

1.2 Harmonic Resonance Risk in 33/11 kV Substations
•	What mechanisms make 33/11 kV substations vulnerable to harmonic resonance under varying loading and network configurations?
33/11 kV substations are vulnerable because their inductive–capacitive structure, weak short circuit strength, and switching/configuration changes shift resonant frequencies into bands where modern harmonics are injected.
Core physical mechanisms
1. LC resonance between network inductance and capacitances
•	The 33/11 kV transformer, upstream network inductance, shunt capacitor banks, cable/feeder capacitance, and grading/stray capacitances together form multiple parallel/series resonant LC circuits at harmonic frequencies (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Kovernikova, 2023; Peerzada et al., 2024).
•	Shunt capacitors for power factor or Volt/VAR control are the main driver: adding or switching stages changes the impedance–frequency curve, creating sharp resonance peaks at low order harmonics (5th–11th) typical of inverters and rectifiers (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Park et al., 2021; Kovernikova, 2023; Peerzada et al., 2024).
2. Weak short circuit strength at MV bus
•	Short circuit power drops strongly from HV to MV levels, so the same harmonic current produces larger voltage distortion at 11 kV (Lowczowski et al., 2024).
•	At 150–250 MVA short circuit levels typical of MV substations, resonance can amplify otherwise modest harmonic injections into THD levels exceeding limits (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Peerzada et al., 2024).
3. Sensitivity to loading, topology, and control actions
•	Load level and feeder switching alter effective inductance and capacitance, shifting resonant frequencies; harmonic peaks move in and out of alignment with dominant 3rd, 5th, 7th, 11th orders from aggregated nonlinear loads (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Zheng et al., 2025; Kovernikova, 2023; Mathenge et al., 2025; Kang & Li, 2023).
•	Tap changer positions and reactive power/Volt–VAR control (including DER inverters and BESS VAR support) reshape system impedance, again moving resonance bands (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Kamaludeen et al., 2025).
•	Network reconfiguration and capacitor bank stage switching create different resonance modes over time, so vulnerable configurations occur intermittently rather than in a single “worst case” state (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025; Kovernikova, 2023; Peerzada et al., 2024).
4. Multiple dispersed harmonic sources
•	Modern substations see many uncoordinated converters (industrial drives, PV, EV chargers, SAPFs, etc.), whose spectra often peak near low order harmonics and may overlap with resonant bands, causing large circulating currents and overvoltages locally at 11 kV (Razmi et al., 2023; Sharma et al., 2022; Peerzada et al., 2024).
Summary of vulnerability drivers
Mechanism	Effect on 33/11 kV substation	Citations
LC interaction (Tx + caps + cables)	Creates multiple resonant frequencies	(Lowczowski et al., 2024; Park et al., 2021; Kovernikova, 2023; Peerzada et al., 2024)
Low MV short circuit power	Larger voltage THD for given harmonic current	(Lowczowski et al., 2024; Peerzada et al., 2024)
Switching / reconfiguration	Time varying, configuration dependent resonance	(Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025; Kovernikova, 2023; Mathenge et al., 2025; Kang & Li, 2023)
Many nonlinear/DER sources	Align with resonant bands, amplifying distortion	(Zheng et al., 2025; Razmi et al., 2023; Sharma et al., 2022; Peerzada et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Key mechanisms driving resonance vulnerability at 33/11 kV.
Conclusion
In 33/11 kV substations, the combination of LC resonance paths, relatively weak MV bus strength, time varying impedance from taps and capacitor/feeder switching, and dense power electronic harmonic sources makes harmonic resonance highly likely under certain loading and configuration states.

•	What are the reported technical consequences of harmonic resonance in medium-voltage substations for voltage quality, equipment stress, and network reliability?
Harmonic resonance in MV substations can severely degrade voltage quality, intensify equipment stress (heating, insulation aging, failures), and trigger protection trips that reduce network reliability.
Impacts on Voltage Quality
•	Resonance amplifies specific harmonic or supraharmonic components, producing large voltage distortion and overvoltages at substation buses and along feeders (Peerzada et al., 2024; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023).
•	In MV networks with converters, resonance has been linked to kV level supraharmonic overvoltages at substations, far above background distortion (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Letha et al., 2021).
•	Parallel resonance in traction and distribution systems causes line voltage amplification and higher distortion injected back into the upstream grid, threatening compliance with IEEE 519 and similar limits (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Peerzada et al., 2024).
Representative effects
Aspect	Reported consequence	Citations
THD / distortion	Sharp increase in THD at resonant orders	(Lowczowski et al., 2024; Peerzada et al., 2024; Nikolaev et al., 2021)
Overvoltage	Instantaneous and repeating overvoltages at MV	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Letha et al., 2021)
Propagation	Distorted waveforms spread over many feeders	(Letha et al., 2021; Peerzada et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Main voltage-quality impacts of resonance in MV grids.
Equipment Stress and Aging
•	Resonant amplification increases losses and heating in transformers, cables, and terminations; high frequency components significantly raise eddy current losses and hotspot temperatures (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Peerzada et al., 2024; Ali et al., 2024).
•	In MV cable terminations, localized SH resonance causes hot spots, accelerated dielectric aging, partial discharges, and catastrophic failures (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Letha et al., 2021).
•	Inside transformers and medium frequency transformers, resonance can create internal overvoltages, high electric fields, PD inception, and insulation breakdown (Cremasco et al., 2021; Peerzada et al., 2024).
Network Reliability and Protection Performance
•	Overvoltages from resonance in traction/MV systems can trigger overvoltage protections, cause surge arrester failure, and lead to feeder or substation trips and large area outages (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023).
•	Poor power quality from resonant harmonics is associated with reduced reliability indices for transformers and cable lines and shortened asset lifetime (Papaika et al., 2021; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023).
•	Time varying resonance (with load, topology, Volt/VAR actions) makes unexpected PQ excursions and nuisance tripping more likely, complicating secure operation (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Letha et al., 2021; Nikolaev et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Across MV substations, harmonic resonance is linked to severe voltage distortion and overvoltages, substantial thermal and dielectric stress of equipment, and increased risk of protection trips and asset failure, directly undermining long term network reliability.

•	How have previous studies characterised resonance risk in radial or urban 33/11 kV distribution substations?
Resonance risk in 33/11 kV type radial and urban substations is generally characterised via impedance/mode analysis plus severity indices, with strong emphasis on cable length, transformer design, capacitor banks and changing operating states.
How studies characterise “risk”
1. Impedance / modal analysis of the substation–feeder model
•	Several works build a detailed distribution network model (often IEEE 33 bus with a 33/11 kV substation) and scan driving point impedance vs. frequency to identify parallel/series resonant peaks and their alignment with dominant harmonics (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Kang & Li, 2023; Mathenge et al., 2025).
•	Modal (eigenvalue) analysis is used to locate resonant modes and participating buses/elements, and to show how resonance points move when feeders or large loads are added/removed (Kang & Li, 2023).
•	In shale gas and similar radial industrial networks, risk is described by the number and location of resonant frequency points in 0–1 kHz, especially near 5th, 7th, 11th, 13th harmonics of converters (Kang & Li, 2023).
2. Severity  or susceptibility type indices
•	A harmonic resonance severity index is proposed to rank buses and configurations and decide where monitoring is needed; it aggregates mode shapes and impedance magnitudes into a scalar “risk” measure over plausible load/topology states (Ansari et al., 2025).
•	A complementary observability index ranks PQ monitor placements that still capture all “worst case” resonance conditions in radial networks (Ansari et al., 2025).
•	Ferroresonance work defines transformer susceptibility metrics using THDv, THDi, peak overvoltage/current and energy content of oscillations across winding types and cable lengths; these are then used to compare configurations (e.g., Δ vs Yg, vector group, 1.5–2 km 11 kV cables) for distribution systems (Yusuf et al., 2024; Eduful et al., 2025).
3. Scenario based operating state studies
•	Risk is evaluated across multiple operating scenarios: different Volt/VAR control tap/capacitor settings, network expansions, or new converter based plants, showing that small changes can “activate” resonance bands (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Kang & Li, 2023).
•	Radial network studies around IEEE 33 bus 33/11 kV substations couple harmonic scans with load scenario optimization (e.g., capacitor allocation, FRIES operation) and report changes in worst case THD and overvoltages as a proxy for resonance risk (Jin et al., 2025; Mathenge et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Across radial and urban style 33/11 kV distribution contexts, resonance risk is characterised by frequency domain impedance/mode analysis, scalar severity or susceptibility indices, and multi scenario studies that track how network reconfiguration, cable length, capacitor banks, and transformer design move and intensify resonant conditions.

1.3 Practical Monitoring Limitations in Data-Constrained Utilities
•	What practical, financial, and technical barriers limit continuous harmonic monitoring in distribution utilities, especially in developing-country contexts?
Continuous harmonic monitoring is constrained mainly by cost of meters and communications, limited expertise and data systems, and weak utility finances and planning capacity, which are especially acute in developing-country utilities.
Practical & Organisational Barriers
•	Sparse PQ instrumentation: Traditional harmonic estimation methods assume many monitors and good network models, conditions rarely met in distribution utilities; meter scarcity and limited feeder instrumentation restrict observability (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Ahmadi-Gorjayi & Mohsenian-Rad, 2023; Zhu et al., 2025).
•	Staffing and skills: Advanced methods (HSE, AI/ML based estimation, multisource data fusion) need engineers trained in signal processing, optimization, and IT integration, which is often lacking in smaller or resource constrained utilities (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Zhu et al., 2025; Ahmadi-Gorjayi & Mohsenian-Rad, 2023).
•	Operational integration: Utilities struggle to embed PQ monitoring outputs in day to day dispatch, maintenance, or planning workflows; power management and FRIES studies explicitly omit telecoms and control system costs as “future work,” signalling implementation hurdles (Jin et al., 2025).
Financial & Infrastructure Barriers
•	High capital cost of meters and communications: Continuous, high resolution PQ monitoring requires many PQ meters or H PMUs plus reliable telecoms; optimal monitor placement work explicitly aims to minimize the number of PQMs because of cost (Shakeri et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2025).
•	Weak, unreliable ICT and power infrastructure: Limited or unstable communications and frequent outages, widely reported as barriers in other technical e monitoring contexts in low income settings, hinder continuous data transfer and central processing (Endalamaw et al., 2024; Aryee et al., 2024).
•	Constrained utility budgets and competing priorities: PQ projects compete with loss reduction, capacity reinforcement, and basic reliability; even sophisticated optimization studies treat costs of ICT/automation infrastructure as external constraints rather than funded items (Jin et al., 2025).
Characterisation / Data Related Barriers
Area	Limitation	Citations
Network models	Impedance/admittance data often incomplete or outdated, undermining model based HSE	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Ahmadi-Gorjayi & Mohsenian-Rad, 2023)
Data quality	Limited sampling rates, noise, and gaps reduce accuracy of harmonic indices	(Macii et al., 2025; Dango et al., 2025; Mirshekali et al., 2025)
Scenario coverage	Changing PV/load states create uncertainty that is difficult to cover with few monitors	(Shakeri et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2025; Jin et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Key categories of barriers to harmonic monitoring.
Conclusion
Continuous harmonic monitoring in distribution grids is limited less by theory than by meter and ICT costs, lack of skilled staff and robust data systems, and tight, unstable utility finances—challenges that are particularly severe for developing country utilities.

•	What types of operational data are routinely available in substations where detailed harmonic measurements are absent?
In substations without dedicated harmonic meters, utilities usually still have rich SCADA and asset monitoring data (voltages, currents, status, temperatures, alarms, noise, DGA, etc.) that can be repurposed for harmonic risk assessment.
Core electrical & SCADA data
Most substations run a SCADA system that continuously records:
•	Bus/feeder quantities: RMS phase phase/phase neutral voltages, line/feeder currents, power factor, active/reactive power, and frequency, typically at 1–10 s resolution (Dang et al., 2024).
•	Switchgear and protection status: breaker/switch open–close status, operation counts, trip/ alarm events, relay status and settings (Dang et al., 2024).
•	Capacitor/reactor bank status: step on/off status, sometimes kvar output and switching times (Dang et al., 2024).
These variables are stored as raw time series tables plus processed indices and alarms in the substation database or utility SCADA historian (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024).
Asset condition & environmental data
Smart/modern substations add multi sensor condition monitoring streams:
•	Transformers: winding/oil temperatures, load current, voltages, dissolved gas analysis (H₂, CH₄, C₂H₂, CO), bushing capacitance/tan δ, FRA curves, tap changer position, partial discharge indications (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024).
•	Switchgear/cables/capacitors: cabinet/internal temperatures, humidity, partial discharge sensors, leakage current for arresters, capacitor and auxiliary temperatures (Dang et al., 2024).
•	Substation environment: ambient temperature and humidity, SF₆ pressure, inspection notes from routine walkthroughs (Dang et al., 2024).
•	Acoustic noise: wide band sound pressure level and spectra of transformer/reactor/corona noise in some “intelligent noise monitoring” deployments (Chen et al., 2024).
Metering, smart grid and derived data
Even when no dedicated PQ analyzers are installed:
•	Revenue meters / smart meters: interval kWh, kvarh, sometimes per phase power and voltage at 15 min–hourly resolution; sub metering data from advanced smart meters can support indirect THD estimation at LV busbars (Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021).
•	Event and alarm logs: fault records, over/undervoltage, overcurrent, breaker failure alarms and communication health logs (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024).
•	Aggregated indices: calculated equipment health scores, normalized trends, and classification outputs from data mining or ML systems (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024).
Summary Table
Data type	Typical sources	Citations
RMS V, I, P, Q, f	SCADA, CT/VT, IEDs	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024)
Status/alarms	Breakers, capacitors, relays, SCADA logs	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024)
Thermal & DGA	Transformer monitors, sensors	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024)
PD, leakage, FRA	Online condition monitoring systems	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Dang et al., 2024)
Noise spectra	Substation noise monitoring systems	(Chen et al., 2024)
Smart meter aggregates	AMI/smart meters, data concentrators	(Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Main routinely available substation data streams.
Conclusion
Even in the absence of detailed harmonic recorders, substations typically provide extensive SCADA, asset health, metering, and environmental data that can be leveraged to infer harmonic behavior or prioritize where dedicated PQ monitoring should be added.
References
De Souza Duz, F., Zacarias, T., Ribeiro, R., Steiner, F., Assunção, F., Bonaldi, E., & Borges-Da-Silva, L. (2025). Smart Monitoring of Power Transformers in Substation 4.0: Multi-Sensor Integration and Machine Learning Approach. Sensors (Basel, Switzerland), 25. https://doi.org/10.3390/s25175469
Rodríguez-Pajarón, P., Bayo, A., & Milanović, J. (2021). Forecasting voltage harmonic distortion in residential distribution networks using smart meter data. International Journal of Electrical Power & Energy Systems. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijepes.2021.107653

Chen, W., Liu, Y., Gao, Y., Hu, J., Liao, Z., & Zhao, J. (2024). Intelligent Substation Noise Monitoring System: Design, Implementation and Evaluation. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en17133083
•	How do monitoring limitations affect harmonic risk detection, compliance assessment, and preventive maintenance decisions?
Limited harmonic monitoring leads to missed risks, uncertain compliance judgments, and more reactive, less targeted maintenance.
Harmonic risk detection
•	Spatial blind spots: Monitoring only at a single point (typically the PCC) cannot identify which individual units or feeders are responsible for poor power quality; defective wind turbines or converters can remain undetected when only the PCC is monitored (Shao et al., 2023).
•	Inability to localize root causes: PQ issues like harmonics can originate from multiple locations (onshore/offshore, different buses). Without multi point, time synchronized measurement, system wide diagnosis and source localization are unreliable (Shao et al., 2023; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Shakeri et al., 2024).
•	Scenario under coverage: Harmonic resonance and distortion depend on load levels, renewable output, capacitor status, and network configuration. With few PQ monitors, only a subset of operating scenarios is effectively “seen,” so some worst case resonance conditions remain unobserved (Shakeri et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025).
Monitoring limits and compliance assessment
Limitation	Effect on compliance with harmonic/PQ limits	Citations
Single point PCC monitoring	Farm/network may appear compliant while individual units violate limits	(Shao et al., 2023; Taghvaie et al., 2023)
Lack of high fidelity data (no synchronized waveforms)	Reduces accuracy of THD/TDD indices and makes event classification uncertain	(Shao et al., 2023; Ali et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)
Incomplete spatial coverage of resonance conditions	Some buses can exceed compatibility levels without any recorded evidence	(Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)
Figure 1: Key ways limited monitoring biases compliance judgments.
Preventive and predictive maintenance decisions
•	Shift toward corrective/reactive actions: Limited condition and PQ monitoring forces utilities to rely more on time based or breakdown maintenance instead of condition based or predictive strategies, which is less economical and can miss incipient failure modes (Moleda et al., 2023; Alvarez‐Alvarado et al., 2022).
•	Poor visibility of harmonic induced stress: Overheating and accelerated aging from harmonics and supraharmonics in transformers, cables, and capacitors are hard to attribute without adequate distortion monitoring, so maintenance triggers may come only after visible faults or thermal alarms (Kobir et al., 2025; Ali et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
•	Sub optimal targeting of sensors and inspections: Where monitoring exists, optimization studies show that sensor placement strongly affects which “critical points” are visible; insufficient or poorly located monitors weaken the ability to prioritize assets and plan maintenance that protects system reliability (Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024; Alvarez‐Alvarado et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Inadequate harmonic monitoring mainly causes under detection of harmful operating scenarios, ambiguous compliance evaluations, and delayed or mis targeted maintenance, undermining both power quality enforcement and asset health management.

1.4 Research Gap and Motivation for Proxy-Based Screening
•	What gaps exist in the literature between direct harmonic measurement methods and the need for operational harmonic-risk screening in substations?
There is a clear gap between sophisticated measurement centric harmonic methods and practical, screening oriented tools that work with sparse, noisy operational data in substations.
1. Measurement focus vs. operational screening needs
Most work assumes dedicated, accurate harmonic measurements (PQ analyzers, synchronized waveform units, calibrated transducers) as the starting point for analysis and control (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Dewayalage et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2022). These methods target:
•	precise THD / interharmonic indices under IEC/IEEE procedures (Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Dewayalage et al., 2024)- accurate MV/HV and supra harmonic measurements using specialized VTs/CTs, Rogowski coils, or composite sensors (Crotti et al., 2021; Dewayalage et al., 2024; Li et al., 2021)- detailed modeling of aggregated-load harmonics from multi-day waveform campaigns (Zheng et al., 2025; Michalec et al., 2021)By contrast, substations routinely have only limited PQ recorders plus SCADA, revenue meters and asset monitors, but the literature rarely develops screening algorithms explicitly designed for such low fidelity data.
2. Underdeveloped links from harmonics to “risk”
Reviews emphasize detection, estimation, and suppression techniques, but usually stop at waveform/THD accuracy, not at operational risk indicators (overheating margin, transformer loss increment, resonance probability, arrester aging) that operators could screen in real time (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2025; Daniel et al., 2024; Shao et al., 2023).
A few works connect harmonics to transformer losses or resonance using single point measurements, but still assume high quality harmonic data rather than ordinary substation signals (Plienis et al., 2023; Shao et al., 2023).
Measurement–operations Gap
Aspect	Well covered in literature	Poorly covered / gap	Citations
Direct harmonic metering & standards	Algorithms, devices, IEC/IEEE methods	—	(Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Crotti et al., 2021; Dewayalage et al., 2024; Li et al., 2021)
Harmonic detection/estimation	Fourier, wavelet, ML, GPR, etc.	Use with coarse SCADA-only data	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2025; Assaffat et al., 2025; Daniel et al., 2024)
Spatial monitoring strategy	Need for multi-point, synchronized data	Minimal work on sparse-monitor placement for risk screening	(Shao et al., 2023; Xu et al., 2022)
From harmonics to asset risk	Local case studies, loss models	Simple, online risk scores for routine operations	(Zheng et al., 2025; Shao et al., 2023; Plienis et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Key gaps between measurement research and risk screening.
3. Data driven / AI methods not tailored to substation reality
AI/ML reviews and substation harmonic modeling papers typically rely on dedicated waveform campaigns or high-sample-rate data (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2025; Assaffat et al., 2025). They seldom address:
•	how to train and deploy models using sparse, irregular operational archives
•	how to fuse non-PQ data (temperatures, alarms, switching states) into harmonic risk screening.
Conclusion
Research excels at how to measure and estimate harmonics accurately, but pays little attention to how operators with limited, imperfect substation data can routinely screen for harmonic-related risk and prioritize interventions. Bridging this gap will require methods explicitly designed for sparse measurements, multi-source operational data, and risk-oriented outputs.

•	How have proxy-based or indirect risk indicators been used in power systems and related engineering domains when direct measurements are scarce?
Proxy and surrogate indicators are widely used to screen risk when direct measurements are sparse, typically by leveraging observable consequences, historical events, or modeled surrogates of the true hazard.
Types of proxy / indirect risk indicators
1. System level performance and outage metrics
•	Interdependent infrastructure models use lost load, service outage counts, recovery time, and network connectivity as proxies for underlying hazard and component failure risk where detailed condition data are unavailable (Brunner et al., 2023; Mühlhofer et al., 2023).
•	In multi network energy–transport models, change in objective function (e.g., unmet demand) after virtually “reinforcing” a component is used as a reinforcement utility indicator of that component’s vulnerability (Wang et al., 2025).
2. Event and exposure–based proxies
•	Natural hazard risk frameworks link simple exposure thresholds (e.g., any inundation → asset failed) and generic fragility curves to estimate cascading indirect risks in power, water, and transport systems when component level data are missing (Brunner et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2024; Mühlhofer et al., 2023).
•	For UHV transmission towers, landslide displacement and tower tilt are measured and mapped via vulnerability curves as proxies for failure probability and indirect network wide risk (Huang et al., 2024).
3. Historical incidents and asset records
•	Water networks use historic pipe break density and GIS attributes as proxies for “systemic vulnerability hot spots” to optimize limited pressure sensor placement without a detailed hydraulic model (Chen et al., 2024).
•	Traffic safety uses near miss conflicts (time to collision, post encroachment time) as surrogate safety measures instead of rare crash data to infer location risk (Arun et al., 2021).
4. Cross domain and cyber indicators
•	Critical Risk Indicators for grids include space weather indices (Kp, Dst, AE), hydrological and climate variables as proxies for elevated grid failure risk where direct asset stress data are lacking (Judy et al., 2021).
•	Cyber physical and ICS security rely on indicators of compromise, threat model risk scores, and ATT&CK based metrics as operational proxies for latent cyber risk (Zografopoulos et al., 2021; Amro et al., 2022; Asiri et al., 2023; Kalinin et al., 2021).
5. Surrogate / reduced order models
•	Surrogate (meta)models and Gaussian process emulators approximate risk measures or safety indicators from a small set of expensive simulations, effectively acting as functional proxies for full physics based models (Jakeman et al., 2021; Marrel & Iooss, 2024; Stanković et al., 2023; Asensio et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2022).
Example domains and proxies
Domain / system	Typical proxies when data scarce	Citations
Power & lifelines	Lost load, outage area, connectivity, reinforcement utility	(Brunner et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2025; Mühlhofer et al., 2023)
Water networks	Historic break density, pipe attributes	(Chen et al., 2024)
Transport safety	Conflicts / near miss metrics	(Arun et al., 2021)
Space weather–grid	Kp, Dst, AE, solar cycle proxies	(Judy et al., 2021)
Cyber physical	IOCs, scenario risk scores	(Zografopoulos et al., 2021; Amro et al., 2022; Asiri et al., 2023; Kalinin et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Illustrative uses of proxy indicators across domains.
Conclusion
Across power and related infrastructures, proxy indicators commonly translate sparse physical data into observable performance, exposure, or modeled surrogates to screen risk and prioritize monitoring, reinforcement, and operational response when direct measurements are not available.

•	Why is a physics-guided proxy screening framework justified for harmonic resonance risk assessment in 33/11 kV substations?
A physics-guided proxy screening framework is justified because resonance risk is fundamentally governed by grid physics, while direct harmonic measurements at all 33/11 kV buses are infeasible.
Why direct, measurement-only approaches are insufficient
•	Sparse metering and cost constraints: Distribution resonance studies show that monitoring “all harmonic resonance conditions” requires optimized PQ monitor placement; full coverage is impractical and expensive, even when using advanced allocation methods (Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024).
•	Strong dependence on topology and operating state: Resonance observability changes with load level, capacitor status, renewable generation, and configuration changes (Shakeri et al., 2024). A method that only triggers on local THD without embedding network physics will miss or mis-rank risks.
•	Single-point measurement limitations: Transformer loss and resonance-risk algorithms based on one measurement location must infer conditions elsewhere using a network model; this is already a form of physics-guided proxying rather than pure direct measurement (Plienis et al., 2023).
Evidence that physics-based proxies are meaningful for resonance
•	Impedance- and path-based indicators: Real-time railway resonance monitoring uses inferred impedance peaks (from voltage/current spectra) and rule-based criteria to flag resonant conditions, not just raw THD (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021). A Dijkstra-based algorithm identifies resonance risk where branch resistances are lower than the grid connection, again a physics-derived proxy for high harmonic currents and losses (Plienis et al., 2023).
•	Mode and scan analysis as surrogate “risk maps”: Resonance mode analysis and frequency scans construct impedance-based indices and binary observability matrices to decide where resonances can appear and where they can be seen with limited monitors (Ansari et al., 2025; Cartiel et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024). These are explicit physics-informed proxies for risk rather than direct fault/overvoltage observations.
•	Frequency-dependent grounding and SH behavior: Studies of grounding systems and supraharmonics rely on frequency-dependent impedance reasoning to conclude when high-frequency components can be neglected for touch-voltage risk or when SH resonances are likely and harmful (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Küchler et al., 2021).
How this supports a proxy framework at 33/11 kV
Need in 33/11 kV substations	Physics-guided proxy concept	Citations
Can’t meter every bus/harmonic	Use resonance modes, impedance paths, topology to define risk scores per bus/feeder	(Ansari et al., 2025; Cartiel et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024)
Operating-state variability	Scenario-based frequency scans / mode analysis to pre-compute risk regions	(Plienis et al., 2023; Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024)
Real-time screening on existing data	Single-point or few-point measurements mapped to risk via physical network model	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Plienis et al., 2023; Mack et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Physical reasoning supports impedance-based proxy risk indices.
Conclusion
Given limited PQ meters and the strong role of network impedance, topology, and operating conditions in shaping resonance, using physics-guided proxies (impedance peaks, path resistances, mode-based indices) is a justified and already emerging practice to screen harmonic resonance risk in substations when direct, fully resolved measurements are unavailable.

1.5 Aim, Objectives, and Research Questions
•	What core research aim best captures the development of physics-guided proxy indicators for harmonic resonance risk screening?
The core aim is to create impedance  and topology based surrogate metrics that reliably flag buses and feeders with elevated harmonic resonance risk using limited measurements.
Core Research Aim (Framed)
To develop and validate physics guided proxy indicators that map sparse harmonic and power quality measurements, together with network impedance/topology models, into quantitative risk scores for harmonic resonance, enabling efficient screening and monitoring of substations without full system metering.
How this connects to existing work
1. From raw distortion to physics guided indicators
•	Railway resonance detection goes beyond THD, deriving voltage/current spectra and pantograph impedance peaks as rule based criteria for resonance, explicitly linking measured signals to underlying network impedance behavior (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021).
•	Transformer loss evaluation adds a Dijkstra based path resistance index to highlight branches whose lower resistance than the grid connection implies high harmonic currents and resonance risk (Plienis et al., 2023).
2. Using network models where measurements are scarce
•	Optimal PQ monitor placement for harmonic resonance relies solely on frequency scan impedance and resonance condition matrices, not on dense measurements, demonstrating that resonance risk can be inferred from physics models and limited sensors (Shakeri et al., 2024).
•	Reviews of distortion metrics emphasise that harmonic indices are indicators of waveform pollution and must be tailored to modern converter rich systems and extended frequency ranges (Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021).
3. General surrogate risk philosophy
•	Surrogate safety frameworks in traffic treat near miss conflicts as proxy indicators for rare crashes, formalising indicator–outcome relationships rather than waiting for failures (Arun et al., 2021).
•	Similar logic justifies proxy resonance indices that anticipate overvoltages or overheating rather than requiring observed damage.
Conclusion
A concise statement is: design and validate physics grounded surrogate metrics that convert limited harmonic data plus network models into actionable resonance risk rankings for 33/11 kV substations, enabling proactive, system wide screening under measurement scarcity.

•	What specific and measurable objectives are commonly used in studies combining operational data analytics with power-quality risk assessment?
Common specific and measurable objectives
Studies that blend operational data analytics with power quality (PQ) or operational risk assessment typically define objectives in terms of quantifiable prediction, classification, or composite indices:
1. Event and risk prediction performance
•	Predict future risky operating states (e.g., overloads, load shedding) with target accuracy/precision/recall/F1 for hours ahead risk assessment using GNNs or other ML models (Zhang et al., 2025; Zhu et al., 2025; Huang et al., 2025).
•	Predict failures/maintenance needs for microgrid components or circuit breakers with quantified reduction in outage probability or failure probability (%PoF) (Yuan et al., 2024; Kumpalavalee et al., 2025).
2. Power quality level and composite index evaluation
•	Compute node level or system level PQ grades/indices (e.g., combined power quality index, CPQI; grid wide PQ grade) and validate against thresholds or benchmark methods (Elsotohy et al., 2025; Xiang et al., 2024).
•	Quantify voltage quality or PQ decline risk at load nodes via probabilistic risk indices and expected economic loss metrics (Hovorov et al., 2025).
•	Zonal PQ risk levels (low/medium/high) based on data driven indices such as a Renewable Variability Index with explicit numeric thresholds (e.g., RVI < 0.25, 0.25–0.40, >0.40) (Oncioiu et al., 2025).
3. Classification of risk levels or PQ events
•	Classify operations or nodes into discrete risk classes (e.g., risk levels I–III, high/medium/low) with target accuracy, recall, F1 for text  or sensor driven models (Zhu et al., 2025; Huang et al., 2025).
•	Detect and classify PQ disturbances (sags, harmonics, etc.) with specified detection/classification rates using deep learning or advanced analytics (Shao et al., 2023; Oliveira & Bollen, 2023).
4. Decision and resource allocation outcomes
•	Improve monitoring efficiency (e.g., reduced data volume or number of meters while maintaining detection capability) (Oncioiu et al., 2025; Shao et al., 2023).
•	Optimize resource allocation based on quantified risk scores (e.g., substation risk level Ri and resulting budget distribution) (Xiao et al., 2023; Maia et al., 2021).
Example summary table
Objective type	Typical measurable targets	Citations
Prediction	Accuracy, recall, F1, error vs. SCUC or expert baseline	(Zhang et al., 2025; Yuan et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2025; Huang et al., 2025)
PQ indices	Numeric PQ grade/index per node/zone, threshold compliance	(Hovorov et al., 2025; Elsotohy et al., 2025; Xiang et al., 2024)
Classification	Correct risk/event class rates (accuracy, F1)	(Zhu et al., 2025; Shao et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2025; Oliveira & Bollen, 2023)
Decision impact	% data reduction, % risk or failure reduction, cost saved	(Yuan et al., 2024; Oncioiu et al., 2025; Xiao et al., 2023; Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Key measurable goals in data-driven PQ risk studies.
Conclusion
Across this literature, objectives are made specific and measurable by targeting explicit prediction metrics, numeric PQ/risk indices, discrete risk classes, and quantified improvements in monitoring efficiency, reliability, or economic loss.

•	What research questions logically connect proxy indicator design, risk classification, and utility application in this topic?
Proxy indicators, risk classes, and utility actions are linked by questions about validity, thresholds, and operational value.
Connecting Research Questions Across the Chain
1. From physics to proxy indicators
•	Design/validity: 
o	How well do impedance  or topology based proxy metrics (e.g., Dijkstra path resistance index, Renewable Variability Index) correlate with actual PQ problems such as harmonic distortion, voltage sags, or transformer overheating? (Plienis et al., 2023; Hovorov et al., 2025; Oncioiu et al., 2025)- Under what load, topology, and renewable penetration conditions do these proxies remain robust or fail? (Plienis et al., 2023; Ansari et al., 2025; Oncioiu et al., 2025)### 2. From indicators to risk classes
•	Thresholds and calibration: 
o	What indicator thresholds best separate low/medium/high risk zones, balancing missed event vs. false alarm rates? (Hovorov et al., 2025; Liu et al., 2023; Oncioiu et al., 2025)- Can composite indices (combining technical and economic terms like VoLL or expected loss) improve discrimination between risk classes compared with purely technical indices? (Gaha et al., 2021; Hovorov et al., 2025; Liu et al., 2023)- Classification performance:
o	How accurately can nodes/zones be assigned to discrete PQ or system risk classes using proxy indicators plus operational data (accuracy, recall, F1)? (Hovorov et al., 2025; Liu et al., 2023; Tang et al., 2024; Caicedo et al., 2023; Oncioiu et al., 2025)- How stable are these classes over time and changing system conditions (e.g., new RES, network reconfiguration)? (Hovorov et al., 2025; Tang et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025; Oncioiu et al., 2025)### 3. From risk classes to utility application
•	Operational decision value: 
o	To what extent do risk based classes improve monitoring placement (e.g., PQM siting, data rate reductions) while still capturing severe resonance/voltage events? (Plienis et al., 2023; Ansari et al., 2025; Caicedo et al., 2023; Oncioiu et al., 2025)- How do risk informed maintenance, reinforcement, or control strategies (e.g., PFCU set points, asset maintenance plans) change expected outages, losses, or VoLL compared with existing practice? (Plienis et al., 2023; Gaha et al., 2021; Hovorov et al., 2025; Liu et al., 2023; Tang et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025; Oncioiu et al., 2025)## Conclusion
Together, these questions ask whether physics guided proxies are valid, how to turn them into reliable risk classes, and whether those classes materially improve utilities’ monitoring, control, and asset management decisions.

Tang, M., Li, R., Zhang, R., & Yang, S. (2024). Research on New Electric Power System Risk Assessment Based on Cloud Model. Sustainability. https://doi.org/10.3390/su16052014
Ansari, H., Esmaeili, S., Shakeri, S., & Koochi, M. (2025). Harmonic Resonance Monitoring in Distribution Networks Considering Load Variation and Possible Network Configurations. IET Generation, Transmission & Distribution. https://doi.org/10.1049/gtd2.70042
Caicedo, J., Agudelo-Martinez, D., Rivas-Trujillo, E., & Meyer, J. (2023). A systematic review of real-time detection and classification of power quality disturbances. Protection and Control of Modern Power Systems, 8, 1-37. https://doi.org/10.1186/s41601-023-00277-y
Oncioiu, I., Man, M., Pirvu, C., & Hojda, M. (2025). A Data-Driven Zonal Monitoring Framework Based on Renewable Variability for Power Quality Management in Smart Grids. Sustainability. https://doi.org/10.3390/su17177737
1.6 Contributions and Paper Organization
•	What constitutes a clear methodological contribution in Q1 journal papers on power-quality analytics and risk screening?
Clear methodological contributions in Q1 power quality analytics involve novel, well validated methods, not just new case studies.
1. What Q1 papers typically present as a methodological contribution
Across top journals, clear methodological contributions usually include:
•	New modelling or algorithmic frameworks
o	End to end deep CNN architectures that replace the standard “decompose–hand craft features–classify” pipeline for PQ disturbance classification, with explicitly designed multi scale units and closed loop detection–classification flows (Wang & Chen, 2019; Sindi et al., 2021).
o	Hybrid 1D+2D CNN architectures that fuse waveform and image features in a principled way, with ablation against single branch baselines (Sindi et al., 2021).
o	VMD–LSTM pipelines for PQ index prediction coupled with KDE–fuzzy risk models that jointly handle time series forecasting and risk fuzziness (Shen et al., 2024).
o	Knowledge graph–augmented ERNIE CNN for operational risk text analytics, where the fusion of graph semantics and CNN is the core method, not the dataset (Huang et al., 2025).
•	New composite indices or decision frameworks
o	Unified/Global Power Quality Indices (UPQI, GPQI) built via analytic hierarchy process; mathematically defined weighting, normalization, and aggregation schemes that combine multiple PQ phenomena and allow bus  or system level benchmarking (Bajaj et al., 2020; Chen et al., 2024).
o	New risk metrics combining hazard, vulnerability, and consequence, or weighted load reduction for weather/hurricane driven risk (Dehghanian et al., 2019; Li et al., 2025).
o	Multi criteria risk warning frameworks where membership functions, KDE thresholds, and risk levels are systematically derived and justified (Shen et al., 2024; Shao et al., 2023; Bajaj et al., 2020).
2. How Q1 papers make the methodological novelty clear
Typical signals of a strong, explicit methodological contribution are:
Element in paper	What it looks like in Q1 PQ/risk work	Citations
Formal problem set up	Clear mathematical statement of inputs/outputs, loss, or index definition	(Wang & Chen, 2019; Shen et al., 2024; Dehghanian et al., 2019; Bajaj et al., 2020; Chen et al., 2024)
Algorithmic detail	Architecture diagrams, step wise procedures, or flowcharts enabling replication	(Wang & Chen, 2019; Shen et al., 2024; Huang et al., 2025; Bajaj et al., 2020; Chen et al., 2024; Livera et al., 2020)
Comparative evaluation	Benchmarked against state of the art with multiple metrics (accuracy, F1, runtime, robustness, explainability)	(Wang & Chen, 2019; Shen et al., 2024; Shao et al., 2023; Dehghanian et al., 2019; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Sindi et al., 2021)
Sensitivity/uncertainty analysis	Stress tests over noise, class imbalance, scenario variations, or parameter weights	(Shen et al., 2024; Dehghanian et al., 2019; Bajaj et al., 2020; Livera et al., 2020; Li et al., 2025)
Demonstrated utility	Link from method outputs to screening, ranking, or operational decisions (e.g., risk levels, PQ grades)	(Shen et al., 2024; Shao et al., 2023; Dehghanian et al., 2019; Bajaj et al., 2020; Chen et al., 2024; Li et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Common markers of methodological novelty in Q1 PQ analytics.
Conclusion
In Q1 power quality analytics and risk screening papers, a clear methodological contribution is a new or substantially improved algorithm/index/framework, rigorously specified, benchmarked, and stress tested, and explicitly linked to better PQ disturbance classification, composite PQ or risk indices, or more informative risk screening decisions.

•	How should the practical, theoretical, and methodological contributions of a proxy-based harmonic-risk paper be articulated?
A proxy based harmonic risk paper should separate and align its practical, theoretical, and methodological contributions around what proxies are, how they’re built/validated, and how they improve decisions.
Theoretical contribution
Focus on what the work adds to understanding of proxies and risk:
•	Formalize the conceptual link between unobservable “true” harmonic risk (e.g., overloading, resonance probability) and the observable proxy indicators, using causal diagrams or structural relations between concepts and proxies (Knox et al., 2022).
•	Specify conditions under which imperfect proxies still permit valid inference about risk ranking or intervention direction, extending ideas like bias signing and contamination of proxies from other factors (Knox et al., 2022).
•	Situate the proxy as a type of reduced order / smart proxy model, clarifying where it sits in existing proxy typologies and what assumptions it relaxes or strengthens (Bahrami et al., 2022).
Methodological contribution
Emphasize what is new in how things are done:
•	Define a general construction and validation pipeline for proxy indicators (data requirements, learning/screening steps, uncertainty quantification for the proxy itself) analogous to smart proxy modelling workflows (Bahrami et al., 2022; Knox et al., 2022).
•	Provide rules or algorithms for calibrating proxy thresholds into risk classes (e.g., indicator based evaluation design in impact frameworks) and for propagating proxy uncertainty into downstream analyses (Reed et al., 2021; Knox et al., 2022).
•	Offer replicable procedures (flowcharts, pseudo code) for integrating the proxy in simulation or screening studies, clearly distinguishing it from prior surrogate or heuristic metrics (Bahrami et al., 2022; Reed et al., 2021; Knox et al., 2022).
Practical contribution
Clarify what changes for users (utilities, regulators):
•	Demonstrate how the proxy reduces computational or data burden (e.g., fewer detailed harmonic studies) while preserving acceptable accuracy, paralleling efficiency gains from proxy/surrogate models in other domains (Bahrami et al., 2022).
•	Show concrete decision improvements: better targeting of monitoring, mitigation, or investment, framed as an indicator based risk evaluation tool within an impact assessment style workflow (Reed et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Articulate: (1) theory—how proxies represent harmonic risk and when that is valid; (2) method—generalizable procedures for building, calibrating, and using such proxies; and (3) practice—measurable efficiency and decision benefits in real planning or screening contexts.
References
Bahrami, P., Moghaddam, F., & James, L. (2022). A Review of Proxy Modeling Highlighting Applications for Reservoir Engineering. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en15145247
Reed, M., Reed, M., Ferré, M., Martin-Ortega, J., Blanche, R., Lawford-Rolfe, R., Dallimer, M., & Holden, J. (2021). Evaluating impact from research: A methodological framework. Research Policy. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.respol.2020.104147
Knox, D., Lucas, C., & Cho, W. (2022). Testing Causal Theories with Learned Proxies. Annual Review of Political Science. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-polisci-051120-111443
•	What paper organization is most common for Q1 engineering articles combining literature, modelling, data analytics, and utility implications?
Most Q1 engineering articles follow a structured IMRaD style core, with added sections linking analytics to real world utility.
Typical Overall Structure
Across Q1 engineering journals (civil, software, transportation, sensing), articles that mix literature, modelling, data analytics, and implications usually adopt variants of the following pattern:
1.	Introduction 
o	Problem importance, gap, and objectives/RQs (Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022).
2.	Background / Literature Review 
o	Stand alone section synthesizing prior work and positioning the study’s niche (methods, domains, gaps) (Dąbrowski et al., 2022; Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022).
3.	Methods / Modelling and Data 
o	Sub sections for: 
	System description / conceptual model
	Mathematical or computational model, assumptions
	Data sources, preprocessing, feature engineering / indicators
	Experimental design or case study set up (Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022).
4.	Results (Data Analytics) 
o	Model performance, sensitivity analyses, comparative baselines, diagnostics (Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022).
5.	Discussion and Utility / Engineering Implications 
o	Interpretation for practice (e.g., design guidelines, screening workflows, monitoring strategies) and links back to literature and theory (Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Thayyib et al., 2023; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022).
6.	Conclusions and Future Work 
o	Summarizes contributions and outlines limitations and research needs (Dąbrowski et al., 2022; Mei & Wang, 2021; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022).
Example Sectioning Pattern
Function	Typical section titles in Q1 engineering papers	Citations
Literature & gaps	“Related Work”, “Background”, “Literature Review”	(Dąbrowski et al., 2022; Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023)
Modelling & data	“Methodology”, “Model and Data”, “System and Experimental Setup”	(Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022)
Analytics	“Results”, sometimes split into “Model Validation”, “Case Studies”	(Mei & Wang, 2021; Hou et al., 2023; Seliya et al., 2021; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022)
Utility	“Discussion”, “Practical Implications”, “Applications and Challenges”	(Mei & Wang, 2021; Thayyib et al., 2023; Rong et al., 2023; Hou et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Common IMRaD-based structure with utility focus.
Conclusion
The dominant organization in Q1 engineering combines an IMRaD core with: a dedicated literature section up front, integrated “Model + Data” methods, results focused on analytics/validation, and a separate discussion that explicitly draws out engineering and utility implications.

2. Literature Review and Theoretical Background
2.1 Harmonic Resonance Mechanisms in Medium-Voltage Distribution Systems
•	What are the dominant physical mechanisms of harmonic resonance in medium-voltage distribution networks, including parallel and series resonance?
Harmonic resonance in MV networks occurs when inverter /load /line induced impedances create near lossless LC paths whose natural frequency coincides with existing harmonic or switching frequencies, yielding large overvoltages (parallel) or overcurrents (series).
Core Physical Mechanisms
1. Formation of LC resonance paths
•	Shunt capacitors (power factor correction, cables, filters) with system inductance (lines, transformers, source) form distributed LC networks whose natural frequencies lie in the harmonic/supraharmonic range (Razmi et al., 2023; Letha et al., 2021).
•	In inverter dominated feeders, L, LC, LCL filters of converters plus grid inductance create additional resonance points, strongly affected by filter design, control loops, and digital delays (Peiris et al., 2025; Li et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2024).
2. Parallel vs series resonance
Resonance type	Physical condition	Typical effect	Citations
Parallel (impedance peak)	Grid/feeder seen from a bus becomes high impedance at a frequency due to L–C in parallel; small harmonic current from converters/loads excites large voltage distortion	Harmonic overvoltage, amplification of background harmonics at MV buses, increased stress on equipment (Lokar et al., 2021; Razmi et al., 2023; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Letha et al., 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024)	
Series (admittance peak)	Network path (line + transformers + filters) presents very low impedance at a frequency; background voltage harmonics or converter switching components drive large currents	Harmonic overcurrent, overheating, interaction with upstream network; often when inverter switching frequency ≈ series resonance of grid side impedance (Fukushima & Okada, 2021; Li et al., 2022; Xie & Zhong, 2025; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021)	
Figure 1: Parallel vs series resonance roles and impacts.
3. Excitation and amplification
•	Non linear loads and grid connected inverters provide harmonic current/voltage sources that excite pre existing resonance modes; matching between emission spectrum and modal frequencies leads to severe amplification (Fukushima & Okada, 2021; Liang et al., 2021; Peiris et al., 2025; Letha et al., 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024).
•	Control interactions (PLL bandwidth, current/voltage loop dynamics, dead time, digital delay) reshape converter active impedance, shifting resonance frequencies and increasing resonance peaks (Li et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2024; Zhao et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2024).
•	In multi bus MV systems, transformer leakage and feeder topology determine whether resonance remains local or propagates across buses; electrical distance and damping govern how strongly remote nodes are involved (Liang et al., 2021; Letha et al., 2021).
Conclusion
In MV distribution networks, harmonic resonance mainly arises from LC combinations of lines/transformers with shunt capacitance and converter filters, reshaped by inverter control dynamics. Parallel resonance manifests as impedance peaks and harmonic overvoltages; series resonance as low impedance paths and harmonic overcurrents, both strongly driven by converter and non linear load emissions.

•	How do network impedance, feeder configuration, capacitor banks, transformers, and nonlinear loads influence resonance conditions?
Resonance conditions are set by how all elements shape the frequency dependent impedance seen by harmonic sources.
Network impedance & feeder configuration
•	The impedance–frequency profile of an MV/LV network has multiple parallel/series resonance points; adding feeders generally lowers impedance peaks but increases the number of resonances and shifts them in frequency (Letha et al., 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
•	Feeder length and cable parameters (R, L, C, skin effect) strongly affect resonance: increasing length introduces more resonance points and shifts them to lower frequencies; transfer impedance along a feeder can first fall, then rise, then fall again with length, changing where amplification appears (Letha et al., 2021).
•	Reconfiguration (tie/sectionalizing switch status, topology changes) alters the admittance matrix and can both create and move resonance conditions across buses (Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
Capacitor banks
•	Shunt capacitors combine with network inductance to form dominant L–C tanks, setting parallel resonance frequencies; when these align with prevalent harmonics, large voltage distortion and capacitor overloading occur (Razmi et al., 2023; Ardi et al., 2024; Ko & Tuomainen, 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
•	Switching capacitors on/off or detuning with series reactors intentionally shifts resonance away from problematic harmonic orders (Ardi et al., 2024; Ko & Tuomainen, 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
Transformers
•	Transformer leakage inductance and tap changer position contribute significantly to system inductance and thus to location and depth of both series and parallel resonances (Lokar et al., 2021; Amini, 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
•	Phase shifting or special connection transformers can alter harmonic transfer paths and reduce amplification at specific buses (Lokar et al., 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
Nonlinear loads (incl. inverters)
•	Nonlinear loads inject harmonic currents that excite existing resonance modes; their aggregate spectrum and location relative to resonant buses control which modes are critical (Razmi et al., 2023; Michalec et al., 2021; Ahsan et al., 2021; Li et al., 2022).
•	Load type and level change effective damping and bus impedance peaks; linear and nonlinear load models can drastically change impedance magnitudes and THD without much shifting harmonic order (Letha et al., 2021; Amini, 2021).
•	Converter dominated feeders: inverter control and filter impedance reshape active impedance, shifting resonance frequencies and increasing peaks, especially in multi inverter networks (Li et al., 2024; Li et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Resonance conditions emerge from the combined impedance of lines/transformers, feeder topology, capacitor placement, and load composition. Any change in these elements can move resonance frequencies, alter amplification magnitude, and switch which buses or feeders become most vulnerable.

•	What theoretical models and equations are most commonly used to explain resonance formation in distribution systems?
Resonance in distribution systems is mainly modeled with frequency domain RLC/impedance formulations, network admittance eigen analysis (modal analysis), and small signal state space/impedance models of converters and grids.
Main Theoretical Frameworks
1. Classical RLC and impedance/admittance equations
•	Networks are reduced to equivalent R L C circuits; resonance is found from the frequency dependent impedance/admittance: 
o	Series resonance: (|Z(j\omega)|) minimum when (X_L(\omega)+X_C(\omega)=0).
o	Parallel resonance: (|Y(j\omega)|) minimum (impedance peak) when (B_L(\omega)+B_C(\omega)=0) (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Li et al., 2022).
•	Frequency scan methods compute bus impedance/admittance over (\omega) to identify peaks/zeros corresponding to resonances (Lokar et al., 2021; Lowczowski et al., 2024).
2. Harmonic resonance mode (modal) analysis
•	Build the network admittance matrix (Y(\omega)) from component models, then solve
[ Y(\omega),v = i ] and its eigen problem
[ Y(\omega),\phi_k = \lambda_k(\omega)\phi_k ] (Lokar et al., 2021; Zhu et al., 2024).
•	Resonance occurs when an eigenvalue (\lambda_k(\omega)) approaches zero (parallel) or becomes very large in the inverse impedance formulation (series) (Lokar et al., 2021).
•	Modal impedances, participation factors, and sensitivity coefficients (\partial \lambda_k/\partial p) quantify which elements and buses form each resonant mode (Lokar et al., 2021; Zhu et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025).
3. Impedance based converter/grid models
•	Power electronic interfaces are linearized around an operating point to obtain dq  or sequence domain impedance matrices (Z_{\text{conv}}(j\omega)), often via harmonic linearization (Xu et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2024; Zhu et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2022).
•	Grid and filters are modeled as (Z_{\text{grid}}(j\omega)); resonance is assessed using series/parallel combinations or minor loop Nyquist criteria on the ratio (Z_{\text{source}}/Z_{\text{load}}) (Xu et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2022).
•	For multi frequency coupling, cross frequency admittance/impedance matrices are used, where off diagonal terms capture harmonic coupling (Wang et al., 2022; Sahin et al., 2022; Yang et al., 2025).
4. State space / swing equation style models (less common for harmonic resonance)
•	Some work uses state space models of converters or swing equation type dynamics; resonance appears when the linearized system eigenvalues approach the imaginary axis at specific excitation frequencies (primary and subharmonic resonance) (Sofroniou & Premnath, 2023).
Conclusion
Across distribution and converter rich systems, resonance formation is most commonly explained through RLC impedance equations, eigen analysis of the network admittance matrix (harmonic modal analysis), and small signal impedance/state space models of inverters and grids, often extended with frequency coupled admittance matrices for modern VSC dominated networks.

2.2 Conventional Harmonic Assessment and Resonance Studies
•	What conventional methods are used for harmonic assessment and resonance analysis in distribution substations, including FFT, impedance scanning, and simulation-based approaches?
Conventional harmonic assessment in distribution substations relies on frequency domain measurement (FFT/DFT), impedance/frequency scanning, and EMT or harmonic power flow simulations.
Measurement based methods (FFT and variants)
•	Classical DFT/FFT: Time domain voltage/current at the substation bus is transformed to the frequency domain to obtain harmonic magnitudes and THD for screening and trend analysis (Zheng et al., 2025; Allawi et al., 2023; Banjanin et al., 2025; Bakar et al., 2024).
•	Enhanced FFT techniques: 
o	Sliding FFT for tracking dips, swells and evolving harmonics in real networks (Banjanin et al., 2025).
o	Self compensating FFT, interpolated DFT, and ZoomFFT to mitigate spectral leakage and fence effects and improve frequency/amplitude estimation of harmonics and interharmonics (Zheng et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Dogan et al., 2025; Xiao et al., 2025).
o	Hybrid signal processing + ML approaches (e.g., EMD+ANN, CWT based methods) extend harmonic/ disturbance classification but still start from FFT style spectral features (Azeem et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2021; Liao et al., 2025; Hamatwi et al., 2023).
Example conventional workflow
Step	Purpose	Typical tools	Citations
Acquire waveforms	Bus voltages/currents	IEDs/PMUs	(Zheng et al., 2025; Banjanin et al., 2025; Dogan et al., 2025)
Spectral analysis	Harmonic orders, THD	DFT/FFT/SFFT	(Zheng et al., 2025; Allawi et al., 2023; Banjanin et al., 2025; Bakar et al., 2024)
Feature extraction	Magnitudes, angles, indices	Windows, IPDFT	(Zheng et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Dogan et al., 2025; Xiao et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Standard FFT centric harmonic assessment workflow in substations
Impedance scanning and resonance analysis
•	Frequency/impedance scan: The substation and connected feeders are represented by R–L–C and transformer/cable models; network impedance/admittance is computed versus frequency to locate resonance peaks and anti resonances, especially around filter or cable capacitance frequencies (Guo et al., 2025; Cartiel et al., 2025; Yang & Hsu, 2023; Shoji & Noda, 2023).
•	Resonance mode analysis: Eigen analysis of the network impedance/admittance matrices (RMA and faster variants) gives modal impedances and participation factors, clarifying which buses/components form each resonant mode and at what frequency (Cartiel et al., 2025).
Simulation based approaches
•	EMT simulations (PSCAD, XTAP, RTDS, PSIM): Used to reproduce harmonic generation and verify resonance conditions derived analytically, including cable induced resonances and multisystem interactions (Guo et al., 2025; Cartiel et al., 2025; Shoji & Noda, 2023; He et al., 2024).
•	Harmonic power flow (e.g., OpenDSS HPF): Computes steady state harmonic voltages/currents given nonlinear sources and filters; frequency scanning within HPF helps detect parallel resonance and assess filter designs (Yang & Hsu, 2023; Bakar et al., 2024).
Conclusion
In distribution substations, conventional harmonic assessment combines FFT based spectral measurement with impedance/frequency scanning and EMT or harmonic power flow simulations to identify distortion levels, locate resonance frequencies, and validate mitigation options.

•	What are the strengths and limitations of EMT, frequency-domain, and field-measurement methods for resonance studies?
Overview
For harmonic resonance studies, EMT, frequency domain, and field measurement methods are complementary. Each has clear strengths and important limitations.
EMT (Electromagnetic Transient) simulations
Strengths
•	High fidelity, nonlinear time domain modeling; captures switching, control interactions, and complex resonance build up accurately (Mukundan, 2025; Subedi et al., 2021; Giraldo et al., 2025).
•	Can be used to generate frequency scans from detailed converter/grid models (white box) and derive impedance for stability analysis (Das et al., 2025; Mukundan, 2025; Zhao & Jiang, 2022; Xie et al., 2023).
•	Suitable for validating equivalent models and mitigation strategies under realistic operating scenarios (Wang et al., 2024; Zhao & Jiang, 2022; Xie et al., 2023).
Limitations
•	Computationally intensive; scalability to large systems requires reduced models, co simulation, or equivalents (Grogan et al., 2025; Filizadeh et al., 2025; Subedi et al., 2021; Fu et al., 2025).
•	Model development is laborious and often limited by missing proprietary control data (Grogan et al., 2025; Filizadeh et al., 2025; Mukundan, 2025).
Frequency domain / impedance based methods
Strengths
•	Linearized models give clear insight into oscillatory modes, resonance frequencies, and damping using eigenvalue or impedance/Nyquist analysis (Wang et al., 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Das et al., 2025; Chou et al., 2021; Zhao & Jiang, 2022).
•	Frequency scanning (analytical or EMT based) is efficient for screening subsynchronous/harmonic resonance conditions in wide frequency bands (Wang et al., 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Das et al., 2025; Xie et al., 2023).
•	Modal analysis decouples overlapping resonances and quantifies parameter sensitivities for targeted mitigation (Lokar et al., 2021; Chou et al., 2021; Zhao & Jiang, 2022).
Limitations
•	Based on small signal linearization around an operating point; may miss large signal, nonlinear, or time varying effects (Wang et al., 2024; Das et al., 2025; Chou et al., 2021).
•	Accuracy depends on quality of equivalent impedance/admittance models and may degrade for very high frequency controller interactions (Wang et al., 2024; Zhao & Jiang, 2022; Xie et al., 2023).
Field measurement / monitoring methods
Strengths
•	Directly observe real system behavior, including unmodeled equipment, parameter drift, and operating variability (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Xu et al., 2024; Artale et al., 2023; Hu et al., 2021).
•	STFT/FFT based spectral analysis of voltages, currents, and derived impedances can track time varying resonances and distinguish infrastructure modes from source emissions (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Xu et al., 2024; Artale et al., 2023).
Limitations
•	Limited to existing configurations and operating conditions; cannot explore “what if” scenarios or design options (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Xu et al., 2024; Hu et al., 2021).
•	Accuracy constrained by sensor bandwidth, placement, noise, and FFT related issues such as spectral leakage and poor resolution for nonstationary signals (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Xu et al., 2024; Artale et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Robust resonance studies typically combine EMT for detailed nonlinear validation, frequency domain tools for systematic mode identification and design, and field measurements for ground truth and online monitoring, leveraging the strengths and mitigating the limits of each.
