•	How have prior studies evaluated harmonic distortion, resonance severity, and mitigation effectiveness in medium-voltage networks?
Prior studies use THD/indices, modal/resonance metrics, and before/after comparisons to quantify harmonic distortion, resonance severity, and mitigation effectiveness in MV networks.
How harmonic distortion is evaluated
•	THD and individual harmonic limits: Most studies use voltage/current THD and individual harmonic distortion (IHD) against IEEE 519 or IEC/EN limits as primary metrics (Shrestha et al., 2025; Klimas et al., 2021; Adesina et al., 2025; Tyover & Ashigwuike, 2025; Obiora et al., 2025; Braide, 2022; Alghamdi et al., 2022).
•	Waveform based assessment: Harmonic spectra from FFT or harmonic load flow/EMT simulations are used to identify dominant orders (e.g., 5th, 7th, 11th, 13th) and quantify distortion at critical buses in MV or coupled LV/MV networks (Shrestha et al., 2025; Alashaary et al., 2025; Braide, 2022; Alghamdi et al., 2022).
•	Statistical/forecasting approaches: Smart meter or monitor data are used to estimate or forecast voltage THD at feeder busbars, often with ANN based models and probabilistic load/harmonic injection models (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021; Adineh et al., 2021).
Common quantitative metrics
Aspect	Typical metric	Citations
Distortion level	THD, TDD, IHD per order	(Shrestha et al., 2025; Aldosary et al., 2025; Klimas et al., 2021; Adesina et al., 2025; Tyover & Ashigwuike, 2025; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Braide, 2022; Alghamdi et al., 2022)
Spatial spread	THD at multiple buses/feeders	(Adesina et al., 2025; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021; Letha et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025)
Equipment impact	Transformer loss increase, overheating	(Adesina et al., 2025; Obiora et al., 2025; Plienis et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Key quantitative indices used for harmonic assessment
How resonance severity is characterized
•	Impedance/frequency scans: Driving point or transfer impedance vs. frequency identifies resonance peaks; height and bandwidth of peaks indicate amplification risk (Shrestha et al., 2025; Alashaary et al., 2025; Lokar et al., 2021; Letha et al., 2021; Yang et al., 2021).
•	Modal / severity indices: Harmonic resonance mode analysis and eigen based resonance severity indices or “harmonic resonance severity index” quantify how strongly each mode can amplify harmonics at each bus, and guide monitor placement (Lokar et al., 2021; Yang et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025).
•	Risk oriented indices: Some methods combine impedance patterns and branch resistances (e.g., via Dijkstra based search) to flag near resonant paths and “hazardous scenarios” with highly distorted currents (Plienis et al., 2023).
How mitigation effectiveness is evaluated
•	Before/after THD comparison: Reduction in THD/IHD at key buses when applying passive filters (STPF, HBF, DR DDTF), APFs, DVRs, or new control/modulation strategies is the main effectiveness metric (Shrestha et al., 2025; Aldosary et al., 2025; Klimas et al., 2021; Tyover & Ashigwuike, 2025; G & M, 2023; Braide, 2022; Alhaider et al., 2024; Alghamdi et al., 2022).
•	System level performance: Studies assess changes in hosting capacity, active power losses, and compliance margin to standards alongside THD reduction (Aldosary et al., 2025; Obiora et al., 2025; Braide, 2022; Alhaider et al., 2024).
•	Simulation + measurement validation: EMT/harmonic flow studies test filter or control designs, sometimes validated against field measurements or real time monitoring algorithms embedded in PLCs or PQ meters (Alashaary et al., 2025; Obiora et al., 2025; Plienis et al., 2023; Letha et al., 2021; Yang et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Across MV networks, prior work consistently combines THD based indices, impedance/modal resonance metrics, and before/after system level indicators (losses, hosting capacity, standards compliance) to quantify both the severity of harmonic/resonant conditions and the effectiveness of mitigation options.

Alghamdi, T., Anayi, F., & Packianather, M. (2022). Modelling and Control Development of a Cascaded NPC-Based MVDC Converter for Harmonic Analysis Studies in Power Distribution Networks. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en15134867
Yang, H., Eggers, M., Just, H., Teske, P., & Dieckerhoff, S. (2021). Linear Time-Periodic Theory-Based Harmonic Resonance Analysis of Converter-Dominated Power System. IEEE Journal of Emerging and Selected Topics in Power Electronics, 9, 7422-7435. https://doi.org/10.1109/jestpe.2020.3041945
Adineh, B., Habibi, M., Akpolat, A., & Blaabjerg, F. (2021). Sensorless Voltage Estimation for Total Harmonic Distortion Calculation Using Artificial Neural Networks in Microgrids. Ansari, H., Esmaeili, S., Shakeri, S., & Koochi, M. (2025). Harmonic Resonance Monitoring in Distribution Networks Considering Load Variation and Possible Network Configurations. IET Generation, Transmission & Distribution. https://doi.org/10.1049/gtd2.70042
2.3 Limitations of Direct Harmonic Monitoring for Operational Screening
•	What are the limitations of relying on direct harmonic measurements for continuous operational screening in substations?
Direct harmonic measurements are essential but, alone, give an incomplete, sometimes misleading basis for continuous resonance screening in substations.
Key technical limitations
1. Limited selectivity and diagnostic capability
•	Simple indices like voltage THD mix many harmonic components with different behaviors; they poorly distinguish genuine infrastructure resonances from source emissions and require post processing/smoothing to be interpretable over time (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021).
•	THD or per order magnitudes do not directly reveal system impedance or modal structure, so incipient resonances may remain hidden until significant amplification occurs (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
2. Instrument transformer and sensor errors
•	At MV/HV, harmonics are measured via VTs/CTs; inductive instrument transformers introduce frequency dependent ratio and phase errors, exacerbated by core nonlinearity and subharmonics, leading to tens of percent errors in some conditions (Crotti et al., 2021; Dewayalage et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
•	Above ~2 kHz, many transducers lack specified accuracy; nonlinearities, stray capacitance, burden dependence, and limited dynamic range compromise both amplitude and phase measurements, especially for supraharmonics (2–150 kHz) (Dewayalage et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
3. Bandwidth, data volume, and non stationarity
•	Capturing higher order/supraharmonics for multiple feeders requires very high sampling rates, synchronized channels, and large storage/communication capacity, which becomes challenging for continuous, multi node monitoring (Yan et al., 2025; Hu et al., 2025; Dinar et al., 2025).
•	Fixed window FFT/IEC 61000 4 30 methods assume quasi stationary signals and suffer from spectral leakage and poor tracking of rapidly time varying components or transient resonant phenomena (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Yan et al., 2025).
4. Operational coverage and modeling gap
•	Measurements only reflect current configurations and operating states; they cannot explore “what if” scenarios, future connections, or protection/controls interactions without complementary modeling.
•	Purely measurement based screening cannot by itself locate resonance paths or predict severity without combining with impedance/modal or probabilistic models (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Zheng et al., 2025; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Summary table
Limitation area	Practical impact in substations	Citations
Selectivity (THD, spectra)	Miss/blur resonance vs emission, needs expert interpretation	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)
Transducer accuracy	Biased harmonic levels, wrong compliance/risk assessment	(Crotti et al., 2021; Dewayalage et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)
Bandwidth & data	Hard to scale to continuous multi node, wide band monitoring	(Hu et al., 2025; Yan et al., 2025; Dinar et al., 2025)
Lack of predictive insight	No forward looking screening or root cause localization	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Zheng et al., 2025; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)
Figure 1: Main constraints of direct harmonic measurements
Conclusion
Direct harmonic measurements are indispensable for ground truth, but for continuous operational screening in substations they are constrained by selectivity, sensor accuracy, bandwidth/data limits, and lack of predictive insight. Best practice is to integrate them with calibrated transducers, advanced signal processing, and impedance/modal or data driven models to obtain reliable, actionable resonance screening.
References

Zheng, J., Song, K., Duan, J., & Wang, Y. (2025). An Adaptive Multi-Task Gaussian Process Regression Approach for Harmonic Modeling of Aggregated Loads in High-Voltage Substations. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18174670

Dewayalage, I., Robinson, D., Elphick, S., & Perera, S. (2024). Measurement of High-Frequency Voltage Harmonics above 2 kHz in High-Voltage Networks. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en17040892

•	How do sampling cost, instrumentation sparsity, data volume, and installation logistics constrain practical harmonic monitoring?
Practical harmonic monitoring is strongly constrained by cost, sparse sensors, massive data streams, and difficult field installation, forcing trade offs between coverage and data quality.
How cost and sparse instrumentation limit coverage
•	Total system cost includes sensors, acquisition hardware, power, communications, civil works, maintenance, and expert analysis time, not just the monitors themselves (Hassani & Dackermann, 2023; Harmel et al., 2023).
•	To stay within budget, utilities must reduce sensor density, so only a subset of substations/feeders is instrumented. Sparse networks risk missing localized problems and reduce the ability to reconstruct system behavior between nodes, similar to structural and water quality monitoring contexts (Jombo & Zhang, 2023; Hassani & Dackermann, 2023; Calderon & Najafi, 2025).
•	Optimal sensor placement and “minimum sensor density” become design problems: enough points to characterize the system, but few enough to be affordable (Jombo & Zhang, 2023; Calderon & Najafi, 2025).
Cost and logistics constraints on deployment
Constraint	Practical effect on harmonic monitoring	Citations
High installed cost (power, cabinets, comms, civil works)	Fewer monitored nodes; avoidance of remote/difficult sites	(Nalakurthi et al., 2024; Hassani & Dackermann, 2023; Harmel et al., 2023)
Limited budget for maintenance & calibration	Drift, downtime, reduced long term data reliability	(Nalakurthi et al., 2024; Hassani & Dackermann, 2023)
Access and safety at MV/HV yards	Restricts where instruments can be physically mounted	(Hassani & Dackermann, 2023; Calderon & Najafi, 2025)
Figure 1: Cost and logistics impacts on sensor deployment
Data volume, sampling, and backhaul constraints
•	High rate, multi channel sampling to capture harmonics/supraharmonics generates very large data volumes; transmission and storage capacities become limiting, especially for wide area, continuous monitoring (Fascista, 2022; Hassani & Dackermann, 2023).
•	Bandwidth limits often force lower sampling rates, fewer channels, or duty cycled recording, which may miss short duration or high frequency events (Fascista, 2022; Hassani & Dackermann, 2023).
•	Irregular or opportunistic sampling (due to sparse sensors or mobile/crowdsensing analogues) reduces reconstruction accuracy unless sophisticated interpolation or field reconstruction methods are used (Fascista, 2022).
Installation logistics and technology choice
•	Sites that require extensive construction or long AC power extensions are often avoided to control initial costs, even if electrically important (Hassani & Dackermann, 2023).
•	Selection among high end instruments vs. lower cost devices involves a trade off between per site accuracy and network spatial density; low cost devices need more calibration and quality control to be trusted (Nalakurthi et al., 2024; Hassani & Dackermann, 2023).
Conclusion
In practice, harmonic monitoring architectures are shaped less by ideal observability and more by installed cost, sensor sparsity, data handling limits, and challenging substation installation logistics, necessitating optimization of sensor placement, sampling strategy, and technology mix.
References
Nalakurthi, N., Abimbola, I., Ahmed, T., Anton, I., Riaz, K., Ibrahim, Q., Banerjee, A., Tiwari, A., & Gharbia, S. (2024). Challenges and Opportunities in Calibrating Low-Cost Environmental Sensors. Sensors (Basel, Switzerland), 24. https://doi.org/10.3390/s24113650
Fascista, A. (2022). Toward Integrated Large-Scale Environmental Monitoring Using WSN/UAV/Crowdsensing: A Review of Applications, Signal Processing, and Future Perspectives. Sensors (Basel, Switzerland), 22. https://doi.org/10.3390/s22051824
Jombo, G., & Zhang, Y. (2023). Acoustic-Based Machine Condition Monitoring—Methods and Challenges. Eng. https://doi.org/10.3390/eng4010004
Hassani, S., & Dackermann, U. (2023). A Systematic Review of Optimization Algorithms for Structural Health Monitoring and Optimal Sensor Placement. Sensors (Basel, Switzerland), 23. https://doi.org/10.3390/s23063293
Harmel, R., Preisendanz, H., King, K., Busch, D., Birgand, F., & Sahoo, D. (2023). A Review of Data Quality and Cost Considerations for Water Quality Monitoring at the Field Scale and in Small Watersheds. Water. https://doi.org/10.3390/w15173110
Calderon, D., & Najafi, M. (2025). Integrated Approach to Optimizing Selection and Placement of Water Pipeline Condition Monitoring Technologies. Eng. https://doi.org/10.3390/eng6050097
•	What literature discusses the mismatch between high-fidelity harmonic monitoring requirements and routine utility operations?
High fidelity harmonic monitoring demands dense, wide band, synchronized measurements and advanced analytics, which often exceed what routine utility power quality programs and standards are set up to provide.
Reviews that highlight the “requirements gap”
1. Need for dense, high quality data vs sparse routine monitoring
•	Traditional harmonic estimation assumes access to system models and many monitors, which is rarely met in practice; AI/ML methods are promoted partly because they reduce this dependence on detailed models and dense monitoring, implicitly acknowledging the gap with typical utility deployments (Taghvaie et al., 2023).
•	Power quality studies on LV networks show that utilities usually rely on class A PQ analyzers at a few points, adequate for compliance but not for continuous, system wide characterization of complex harmonic interactions and cancellation effects among many devices (Michalec et al., 2021).
2. Measurement indices and bandwidth vs operational standards and tools
•	Reviews of THD indices note that IEC standards and most utility PQ programs focus on 0–2 kHz and on steady state harmonics, while modern converters create rich interharmonic and supra harmonic (2–150 kHz) content that existing indices and instruments do not characterize well (Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021).
•	It is emphasized that current standards “cover mainly the low frequency part” and only informatively part of higher frequencies, lacking distortion indices that separate harmonic vs interharmonic components or account for non stationarity, in contrast to what advanced assessments require (Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021).
Illustrative mapping of the mismatch
High fidelity need	Typical routine practice	Citations
Wide band (up to 150 kHz), time frequency analysis	Class A meters, 0–2 kHz, fixed window THD	(Michalec et al., 2021; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021)
Many synchronized monitors and good system models	Few permanent loggers, limited modeling	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Michalec et al., 2021)
Indices separating harmonics/interharmonics, stationary/non stationary	Single THD-type indices per IEC	(Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Gap between ideal and routine harmonic monitoring
Conclusion
Key reviews on harmonic estimation methods and THD metrics explicitly describe how ideal monitoring assumptions (dense, wide band, model rich, time resolved data) go beyond what routine utility power quality programs and current standards typically deliver, motivating AI/ML estimation and new distortion indices as partial workarounds.
References
Taghvaie, A., Warnakulasuriya, T., Kumar, D., Zare, F., Sharma, R., & Vilathgamuwa, M. (2023). A Comprehensive Review of Harmonic Issues and Estimation Techniques in Power System Networks Based on Traditional and Artificial Intelligence/Machine Learning. IEEE Access, 11, 31417-31442. https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2023.3260768
Michalec, Ł., Jasinski, M., Sikorski, T., Leonowicz, Z., Jasiński, Ł., & Suresh, V. (2021). Impact of Harmonic Currents of Nonlinear Loads on Power Quality of a Low Voltage Network–Review and Case Study. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en14123665
Arranz-Gimon, A., Zorita-Lamadrid, Á., Moriñigo-Sotelo, D., & Duque-Pérez, Ó. (2021). A Review of Total Harmonic Distortion Factors for the Measurement of Harmonic and Interharmonic Pollution in Modern Power Systems. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en14206467

2.4 Proxy-Based Power-Quality Assessment: Current State of Knowledge
•	What proxy-based methods have been used in power-quality assessment when direct harmonic or waveform data are unavailable?
Proxy-based power-quality assessment relies on indirect indices, composite PQ scores, and surrogate measurements when detailed harmonics are missing.
Types of proxy-based methods
1. Composite / global power-quality indices
When only standard RMS, THD, unbalance, and power-factor data are available (not full waveforms), many studies build single “global” indices via multi criteria aggregation:
•	Global / combined PQ indices (GPQI, CPQI, Global PQI) use AHP, CRITIC, entropy or grey clustering to weight a small set of indicators (voltage THD, current THD, unbalance, flicker, frequency deviation, voltage deviation, power factor) into one score for each bus or system (Elsotohy et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2024).
•	These indices act as proxies for overall PQ performance, even if detailed spectral content or waveforms are unavailable or not stored long term (Elsotohy et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2024).
2. Simple frequency content indices from reduced data
Recent work proposes simple PQ indices derived from limited metrics that remain robust to acquisition constraints and extended frequency ranges:
•	Betti et al. define new frequency content indices up to 150 kHz that can be computed from compressed or sparsely sampled data, intended as screening tools for PQ issues without full waveform analysis (Betti et al., 2025).
•	For non linear loads, power based indices built from decomposition of active/reactive powers linked to harmonics provide a proxy for harmonic losses using only aggregated power components and THD like terms (De Almeida Coelho et al., 2024).
3. Event  or risk oriented surrogates
•	In offshore wind grids, energy quality (EQ) has been proposed as a higher level proxy that reflects power variability (spectrum, average power, power distortion, standard deviation) even when waveform level PQ metrics are incomplete (Shao et al., 2023).
•	Probabilistic studies use Monte Carlo distributions of standard PQ indices at buses (e.g., voltage unbalance, THD envelopes) as proxies for future PQ risk under uncertain EV and non linear load penetration (Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021).
Summary Table: Main proxy approaches
Proxy type	Typical inputs (no raw waveforms)	Purpose	Citations
Global PQ indices (GPQI, CPQI, etc.)	THD, unbalance, flicker, pf, freq deviation	Overall PQ score per bus/system	(Elsotohy et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2024)
Simple frequency-content indices	Limited spectral/THD info up to 150 kHz	Fast screening of distortion issues	(Betti et al., 2025; De Almeida Coelho et al., 2024)
EQ / probabilistic metrics	Power variability stats, PQ index distributions	PQ risk under RES/EV integration	(Shao et al., 2023; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Main proxy styles when waveforms absent.
Conclusion
Across these works, proxy-based assessment replaces detailed harmonic spectra with weighted composite indices, power-based distortion measures, and variability oriented metrics, enabling practical PQ screening where direct waveform or full harmonic data are not available.
References
Betti, C., Tinarelli, R., Peretto, L., & Mingotti, A. (2025). Experimental Validation of Simple Power Quality Indices for Frequency Content Assessment up to 150 kHz. Sensors (Basel, Switzerland), 25. https://doi.org/10.3390/s25216716
Elsotohy, A., Ali, M., Adail, A., Eisa, A., & Othman, E. (2025). Developing a dynamic combined power quality index for assessing the performance of a nuclear facility. Scientific Reports, 15. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-025-89383-5
Shao, H., Henriques, R., Morais, H., & Tedeschi, E. (2023). Power quality monitoring in electric grid integrating offshore wind energy: A review. Renewable and Sustainable Energy Reviews. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.rser.2023.114094
De Almeida Coelho, R., Araújo, B., Xavier, G., Rodrigues, G., Ferreira, T., & Brito, N. (2024). Novel indices for power quality assessment of non-linear loads. Electric Power Systems Research. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.epsr.2024.110952
Rodríguez-Pajarón, P., Hernández, A., & Milanović, J. (2021). Probabilistic assessment of the impact of electric vehicles and nonlinear loads on power quality in residential networks. International Journal of Electrical Power & Energy Systems. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijepes.2021.106807
Chen, Y., Tang, Z., Weng, X., He, M., Zhang, G., Yuan, D., & Jin, T. (2024). A Novel Approach for Evaluating Power Quality in Distributed Power Distribution Networks Using AHP and S-Transform. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en17020411
•	How have voltage behaviour, loading patterns, operational stress variables, or indirect indicators been linked to power-quality risk in previous studies?
Operational conditions like voltage variation and loading behavior have been repeatedly linked to higher harmonic levels, equipment stress, and PQ risk.
How voltage behaviour has been linked to PQ risk
•	In LV networks, rising or unbalanced phase neutral voltages and increased neutral to ground voltage are interpreted as signs of load imbalance and cumulative harmonic currents, associated with transformer heating, reduced efficiency and higher risk to sensitive loads (Adesina et al., 2025).
•	In renewable rich microgrids and EV charging stations, large voltage deviations (e.g., minimum voltage ≈91% of nominal), high standard deviation, and frequent sags/swells are treated as direct PQ risk indicators, triggering concern for equipment misoperation and non compliance with IEEE 1159 voltage limits (Elazim et al., 2025).
•	Supraharmonic studies derive voltage distortion limits tied to cable termination stress classes (“no risk”, “moderate”, “significant”, “unacceptable”), making distortion itself an operational stress surrogate for insulation failure probability (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
How loading patterns and stress variables have been used as proxies
•	EV charging work links charger configuration and loading level to PQ: partial loading (≈60%) simultaneously reduces 5th/11th harmonics and improves average voltage and variability, so loading pattern becomes a controllable proxy for harmonic and voltage stability risk (Elazim et al., 2025).
•	In tea factories, heavier motor loading is empirically associated with higher amplitude transient overvoltages (fast rise up to 30.04 V/ns), which are then connected to accelerated insulation degradation and long term reliability risk (Pannila & Edirisinghe, 2021).
•	Harmonic studies in solar microgrids connect type and duty of nonlinear loads (UPS, VFDs, rectifiers) to THD levels and additional losses (860 W), effectively using load mix and demand profile as predictors of PQ risk hotspots (Tweve et al., 2025; Kumar, 2022).
•	Real time PV DSTATCOM assessments use sudden load changes, irradiance level, and PCC voltage balance as test stressors; resulting THD and power factor metrics are taken as outcome based indicators of PQ robustness under operational stress (Govil et al., 2025).
Example mapping of indirect indicators to PQ risk
Indicator type	Proxy signal used	Linked PQ risk outcome	Citations
Voltage behaviour	Min/avg voltage, σ, unbalance, N–G rise	Sags/swells, heating, non compliance, trips	(Adesina et al., 2025; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Elazim et al., 2025; Govil et al., 2025; Yılmaz, 2025)
Loading pattern	Load level, charger mix, motor loading	Harmonic magnitude, transient overvoltage	(Elazim et al., 2025; Tweve et al., 2025; Kumar, 2022; Pannila & Edirisinghe, 2021)
Asset stress variables	Cable joint stress curves, insulation RRoV	Failure likelihood, aging acceleration	(Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Pannila & Edirisinghe, 2021)
Figure 1: How indirect variables map to PQ risk.
Conclusion
Across diverse systems, studies translate voltage statistics, loading patterns, and asset stress surrogates into practical predictors of harmonic distortion, transient stress, and insulation or equipment failure risk, providing a foundation for proxy based power quality risk assessment where direct spectral data are limited.

•	What gaps remain in proxy-based power-quality assessment, particularly for harmonic resonance risk in medium-voltage substations?
Proxy-based PQ assessment still lacks validated, physics-aware indicators for harmonic resonance risk in MV substations, especially when only sparse or low bandwidth data are available.
Where current methods fall short
1. From “PQ level” to “resonance risk”
Most proxy work focuses on global PQ indices (THD, composite indices, CPQI) or transformer-loss proxies, not on explicitly predicting resonance conditions in MV substations (Elsotohy et al., 2025; Plienis et al., 2023). THD based indices or loss estimates do not distinguish:
•	benign harmonic distortion vs. near resonant overvoltages or localized current magnification (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Plienis et al., 2023).
•	risk at specific buses or branches vs. system wide averages.
Rail and SH studies characterize resonance behavior via detailed spectra and impedance estimates, not via simple operational proxies that could be computed from SCADA type variables alone (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
2. Need for topology  and configuration aware proxies
Harmonic resonance monitoring and PQM placement methods explicitly use network models, resonance mode analysis and severity indices (Ansari et al., 2025), but assume availability of:
•	network impedance/admittance models and
•	at least some dedicated harmonic monitors.
There is no widely adopted way to map easily available indicators (loading, tap position, PFCU status, fault level, capacitor steps) into a robust “resonance risk index” without detailed frequency domain studies. The Dijkstra based approach that flags branches with resistance lower than the grid connection point is still tied to modeled or calculated branch parameters, not to pure measurement proxies (Plienis et al., 2023).
3. Measurement chain and high frequency gaps
For MV resonance (including 2–9 kHz and supraharmonics), gaps include:
•	Limited accuracy and missing standards for instrument transformers above 2 kHz, undermining any proxy relying on conventional MV measurements (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Dewayalage et al., 2024).
•	Sparse work on validating SH based stress indicators (e.g., cable termination aging) against long term failure statistics in actual MV substations (Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
4. Data driven modeling without explicit resonance labeling
Advanced models (e.g., multi task Gaussian process regression for harmonics at 220 kV buses) focus on forecasting harmonic magnitudes, not on classifying or quantifying resonance risk states, and still rely on waveform derived spectra rather than true proxies (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Overall, there is a clear gap between detailed, measurement heavy resonance analyses and simple, deployable proxy indices that can flag harmonic resonance risk in MV substations using routine operational data, particularly in the 2–9 kHz / supraharmonic range and under changing network configurations.

Dewayalage, I., Robinson, D., Elphick, S., & Perera, S. (2024). Measurement of High-Frequency Voltage Harmonics above 2 kHz in High-Voltage Networks. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en17040892
2.5 Physics-Guided Modelling and Interpretable Risk Analytics in Power Systems
•	What is physics-guided modelling in power systems, and how has it been applied to operational risk assessment or condition monitoring?
Physics-guided modelling combines power-system physics with data-driven models to improve accuracy, generalization, and interpretability for risk and condition monitoring.
What is physics-guided / physics-informed modelling?
Across the literature, physics-guided or physics-informed modelling means embedding power system laws (e.g., power flow equations, swing/thermal PDEs, component physics) into machine learning or reduced models via constraints, architectures, or training strategies (Wu et al., 2024; Bragone et al., 2022; Kruse et al., 2023; Nadal et al., 2024; Bento, 2024).
Typical mechanisms include:
•	Adding PDE/ODE residuals or physical constraints to the loss function (PINNs, PIML) (Wu et al., 2024; Ramirez et al., 2024; Bragone et al., 2022; Kruse et al., 2023).
•	Designing network structure or initialization from AC power flow or system topology (physics guided residual layers, graph models) (Chen & Zhang, 2023; Yamashita et al., 2025; Bento, 2024).
•	Hybrid models where a mechanistic core is corrected by small data driven “kernels” for missing physics or uncertainties (Zhu et al., 2025; Nascimento et al., 2023; Jiang et al., 2025).
Applications to operational risk and condition monitoring
Asset level health and lifetime
•	Power transformers: PINNs use heat diffusion PDEs and boundary conditions to infer top oil and winding temperature fields from limited measurements, enabling spatio temporal insulation ageing assessment and hotspot tracking for transformers in renewable plants (Ramirez et al., 2024; Bragone et al., 2022; Ramirez et al., 2024).
•	Thermal power plant systems: Physics informed VAEs model nonlinear thermal processes with multi level physical constraints, improving prediction under unseen operating conditions for monitoring and optimization (Zhu et al., 2025).
•	Gas turbines: A thermodynamics informed DeepONet surrogate predicts efficiencies and corrected power/heat rate in milliseconds, supporting real time degradation monitoring and predictive maintenance (Jiang et al., 2025).
•	Li ion batteries: Hybrid Bayesian physics informed networks embed electrochemical equations while learning model form and unit to unit variability, improving state of health prognosis under sparse, noisy data (Nascimento et al., 2023).
System level operational risk
•	Dynamic security / stability:
o	Physics informed denoising recovers dynamic PMU data so that dynamic security assessment (DSA) is more reliable under noisy measurements (Li et al., 2025).
o	Physics guided neural networks estimate load margin by reconstructing power flow conditions at the stability boundary, improving stress/risk estimation over black box NNs (Bento, 2024).
o	Physics aware Neural Dynamic Equivalence (NeuDyE) learns dynamic equivalents of external grids using ODE Nets and physics guided constraints, preserving post disturbance behavior for stability studies (Shen et al., 2023).
o	PIML frequency models combine stochastic swing like equations with neural networks driven by techno economic inputs to forecast grid frequency deviations and identify stability relevant parameters (Kruse et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2024).
o	Physics guided or physics informed PF solvers accelerate probabilistic power flow, enabling more tractable risk analyses under uncertainty (Chen & Zhang, 2023).
•	Data limited or topology aware health indices:
o	A graph learning health assessment framework uses physics informed graph convolution/attention (respecting network structure and dynamics) to predict rotor angle and frequency stability indicators from partial PMU coverage, supporting system level health/risk indices in near real time (Yamashita et al., 2025).
Cross domain risk informed predictive maintenance
•	Nuclear power plants: Detailed physics informed models of circulating water pumps/motors simulate fault modes, feeding predictive models coupled with Markov chain risk and economic models to support risk informed predictive maintenance strategies (Agarwal et al., 2021).
•	Accident and QRA modelling: PINNs and multi physics surrogates are used for fast, physics consistent simulation of accident scenarios (e.g., nuclear LOHS events, hydrogen refuelling stations), enabling more detailed quantitative risk assessment with reduced computational cost (Antonello et al., 2023; Suzuki et al., 2021).
Example: roles of physics guidance in risk / monitoring
Application area	Physics guidance does…	Risk / CM benefit	Citations
Transformer ageing	Heat diffusion PDEs, thermal BCs in PINNs	Local hotspot & ageing risk estimation	(Ramirez et al., 2024; Bragone et al., 2022; Ramirez et al., 2024)
System stability indices	Power flow & stability constraints in PGNN, graphs	More robust load margin & health indices	(Yamashita et al., 2025; Bento, 2024)
Frequency risk	Stochastic swing equations + NN for parameters	Short term frequency deviation prediction	(Kruse et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2024)
PdM for rotating assets	Mechanistic pump/turbine models + ML	Fault signature generation and risk based PdM	(Agarwal et al., 2021; Jiang et al., 2025)
Battery prognosis	Electrochemical equations in hybrid RNN/MLP	Better SOH/RUL estimates under sparse data	(Nascimento et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Roles of physics guidance in risk models.
Conclusion
Physics guided modelling in power systems merges first principles with learning to produce faster, more interpretable, and more robust tools for stability margins, dynamic security, asset ageing, and predictive maintenance, especially where pure data driven models struggle with sparsity, noise, or extrapolation.

•	Why are interpretable models such as ordinal regression, constrained models, or generalized additive models preferred in high-stakes engineering applications?
In high stakes engineering, interpretable models are preferred because they enable transparent, checkable, and contestable decisions, while still achieving competitive accuracy.
Why interpretable models are prioritized
1.	Safety, accountability, and regulation
o	In domains like healthcare, autonomous driving, credit, and safety critical infrastructure, there is a moral and often legal duty to understand how a model makes decisions (Rudin et al., 2021; Wang, 2025; Kraus et al., 2023; Watanabe et al., 2021).
o	Intrinsically interpretable models (ordinal regression, constrained models, GAMs, symbolic regression) let experts inspect feature effects directly, supporting transparency, fairness audits, and legal justification of decisions (Rudin et al., 2021; Wang, 2025; Veran et al., 2023; Zhuang et al., 2021; Kraus et al., 2023).
2.	Trust, troubleshooting, and model debugging
o	Black box models can hide serious errors, biases, or data pipeline bugs that are hard to detect even with post hoc explanations (Hassija et al., 2023; Rudin et al., 2021; Wang, 2025).
o	Interpretable parametric models with simple functional forms are easier to troubleshoot and adjust when behavior conflicts with domain knowledge or safety requirements (Rudin et al., 2021; Veran et al., 2023; Kraus et al., 2023).
3.	Alignment with domain knowledge via constraints
o	Constrained GAM type models allow encoding monotonicity, sparsity, and interaction limits that reflect physics or engineering judgement, improving both interpretability and often generalization (Wang, 2025; Kraus et al., 2023; Watanabe et al., 2021; Mariotti et al., 2023).
o	Constrained or generalized additive models used in high stakes settings explicitly allow engineers to enforce “reasonable” behavior (e.g., risk should not decrease with load) and visualize each feature’s contribution (Wang, 2025; Veran et al., 2023; Kraus et al., 2023; Watanabe et al., 2021; Mariotti et al., 2023).
4.	More reliable than post hoc explanations
o	Post hoc tools (e.g., saliency maps, SHAP, LIME) approximate black box logic and can be unstable or misleading, which is dangerous in safety critical scenarios (Hassija et al., 2023; Rudin et al., 2021; Wang, 2025).
o	Interpretable models are self explanatory, so no separate explanation layer (that could contradict the model) is needed (Rudin et al., 2021; Wang, 2025; Zhuang et al., 2021).
Role of specific model classes
•	Ordinal regression / ordered models: Respect the natural order of engineering ratings (e.g., condition classes, risk levels) and yield interpretable predictor effects on the ordered scale (Kook et al., 2021).
•	Constrained additive and neural additive models (GAM, GA2M, CNAM, IGANN): Provide visualizable shape functions and pairwise/structured interactions, plus constraints (monotonicity, sparsity) to balance accuracy with interpretability in regulated or safety critical tasks (Wang, 2025; Kraus et al., 2023; Watanabe et al., 2021; Mariotti et al., 2023).
Conclusion
In high stakes engineering, interpretable models are favored because they let experts see, critique, and align model behavior with physical laws, safety constraints, and regulatory requirements, while avoiding the reliability and accountability problems of opaque black box systems.

•	What evidence exists that physics-guided and interpretable analytics outperform purely black-box approaches in power-system risk screening?
Evidence is emerging that physics-guided and interpretable models can match or outperform black-box ML for power system–like risk tasks, especially in generalization, data efficiency, and robustness.
Power system–specific evidence
Interpretable models vs. black boxes in stability/risk screening
•	A Korean dynamic stability screening study deliberately avoids large NNs and uses a physically motivated feature design plus support vector machine (SVM) classifier. The authors note that NN based black boxes require retraining for each topology and are computationally burdensome, while the simpler, more interpretable SVM with physics guided features achieved effective real time prediction of rotor angle instability from steady state EMS data (Song et al., 2025).
•	A review of interpretable ML in smart grids reports multiple cases where decision tree–based dynamic security assessment and shapelet based voltage stability screening attain high accuracy with explicit safety rules / spatiotemporal features, making them preferable to opaque deep models for grid security tasks (Xu et al., 2022).
Physics guided / gray box models vs. black box
•	In distribution systems with inverter based resources, a gray box model that embeds inverter physics and network reduction inside a neural network outperforms both pure physics-based and pure black-box models in estimation accuracy for dynamic behavior (Zhang et al., 2024).
•	A physics informed deep learning framework for system reliability shows that enforcing physical constraints enables accurate reliability assessment with limited data and far lower computational cost than brute force Monte Carlo, addressing key bottlenecks in risk quantification (Zhou et al., 2021).
•	A physics guided modeling framework for complex engineering systems (drilling and rail) achieves higher test accuracy (0.97) and lower prediction error (0.03) than both traditional physics-only and purely data driven models, while retaining clear physical interpretability of friction/contact parameters—benefits directly relevant to condition and risk monitoring (Zhang et al., 2025).
Broader evidence on interpretable vs. black box performance
•	Large empirical studies on tabular data show no systematic performance advantage for black-box models over advanced interpretable GAM type models; interpretable models (EBM, GAMI Net, etc.) often equal or surpass ensembles and deep nets (Kruschel et al., 2024).
•	Reviews in smart grids and reliability emphasize that physics-informed and interpretable models are typically more robust under topology changes, data drift, and sparse measurements, which are central issues in operational risk screening (Nghiem et al., 2023; Xu et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2021; Zhang et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Direct head to head studies in power system risk screening are still limited, but existing work indicates that physics-guided and interpretable approaches can achieve comparable or better predictive performance than black-box models, with superior robustness, data efficiency, and auditability, making them strong first choice candidates for high stakes power system risk and condition monitoring applications.

2.6 Operational-State Characterization in Substations
•	How are operating states defined and characterized in substation and distribution network studies using routine measurement data?
Operating states are typically defined as clusters or labeled regimes of network or equipment behavior, derived from SCADA / metering time series and linked to topology, loading, and component condition.
Definitions and Levels of “Operating State”
1. Network-level operating state (distribution / substation)
•	In state estimation studies, an operating state is the voltage phasor profile and power flows consistent with network topology and measurements at a given time (snapshot of bus voltages, angles, injections, and flows) (Soltani & Khorsand, 2021; Darmis & Korres, 2023; Madurasinghe & Venayagamoorthy, 2024; Gholami et al., 2021).
•	For active distribution networks, different “operating conditions” are generated by varying topology (radial/meshed), DER presence, and load patterns, and each combination is treated as a distinct state scenario for estimation and coordination algorithms (Gholami et al., 2021).
•	Harmonic and dynamic studies similarly define operating states via harmonic injections, load levels, and network partitions before performing harmonic state estimation or dynamic modeling (Wang et al., 2023; Mitrentsis & Lens, 2021).
Routine-measurement–based characterization
•	Routine SCADA/AMI measurements (P, Q, V at substations/feeders) are assembled into matrices over time; correlation coefficients and clustering are used to group substations with similar load profiles into operating classes, which then represent typical operating states for planning/state estimation with reduced measurements (Dandea et al., 2025; Fotopoulou et al., 2022).
•	Hybrid SCADA/WAMS surveys emphasize that observability analysis and pseudo measurement generation from historical SCADA/AMI data are used to reconstruct states between sparse real time readings, effectively interpolating missing operating states (Cheng et al., 2024; Darmis & Korres, 2023; Wang et al., 2023; Fotopoulou et al., 2022).
Equipment / asset “operating states” in substations
•	For substation equipment, operating state is often a discrete health/condition category (e.g., normal, degraded, faulty), inferred from multivariate SCADA and test data (temperatures, currents, gas levels, switching counts) using mixture models, association rules, and Naive Bayes classification (Dang et al., 2024).
•	In wind turbine and transformer applications, SCADA data are used to define working condition regimes (e.g., shutdown, start up, MPPT, constant speed) via control principle–based phase definitions plus k means clustering or random forests, then used as the context for alarm thresholds and condition assessment (Chen et al., 2021; De Souza Duz et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Across substation and distribution studies, operating states are formalized either as continuous network states (voltage/power profiles under given topology) or as discrete operating regimes/health classes. These are derived and characterized from routine SCADA, smart meter, and test data using correlation analysis, clustering, probabilistic models, and state estimation frameworks.

•	What clustering or state-reduction techniques have been used to identify representative network operating conditions?
Representative operating conditions are usually obtained by clustering time-series (days, scenarios, operating points) and/or reducing system states with network aware model reduction.
Main clustering techniques for representative conditions
Time-series / scenario clustering
•	k means / k medoids on days or scenarios 
o	Widely used to select representative days for capacity expansion planning; both input-based (load, RES time series) and cost-based features are clustered, and medoids/days closest to centroids are kept, sometimes with extra “extreme” days added for stress conditions (Li et al., 2021).
•	Gaussian Mixture Models (GMM) 
o	Applied to smart-meter and transformer demand data to stratify users/transformers into load profile clusters that act as representative operating strata for planning and loss assessment (Mendoza-Vitonera et al., 2025).
•	Density based clustering (DBSCAN) 
o	Used on PV trajectories and dynamic responses to group days/fault cases with similar shapes and extract typical PV scenarios or fault-response patterns while handling noise/outliers (Fu et al., 2025; Li et al., 2025).
•	Affinity Propagation and improved variants 
o	Used for operation mode identification from historical system states; improved affinity propagation on sequences plus temporal convolutional networks classifies typical and future modes (Zhang et al., 2023).
•	Graph based / shared weight clustering 
o	Shared-weight graph clustering over PV days (features: power, energy, variability) yields a small set of representative PV output scenarios for probabilistic power flow and optimization (Fu et al., 2025; Fu et al., 2024).
Network and state-space reduction
•	Topology based aggregation / feeder or LV reduction 
o	Bus/line aggregation based on topology and line types reduces LV networks to a smaller equivalent while preserving load-flow behavior, thereby compressing the space of operating states (Khan & Hayes, 2021).
•	Model order reduction (MOR) of NDAE systems 
o	Structure preserving MOR jointly reduces dynamic and algebraic states, retaining dominant modes while discarding weakly observable/controllable states to represent the essential operating dynamics with far fewer variables (Nadeem & Taha, 2024).
•	State enumeration & Monte Carlo reduction 
o	For reliability, state enumeration is used to identify a reduced set of system states that capture most reliability impact, combined with Monte Carlo for chronology (Ndawula et al., 2024).
•	Feasible operation region (FOR) approximation 
o	Instead of enumerating scenarios, analytical quadratic boundaries characterize the high dimensional region of admissible injections; this defines the full set of feasible operating conditions without explicit clustering (Jiang et al., 2023).
Other uses of clustering for representative modes
•	Operation-condition clustering for ADN equivalents: two step clustering plus Fisher discriminant analysis groups operating points into representative ADN conditions for robust dynamic equivalents (Wang et al., 2021).
•	Topology / coherent-area clustering: spectral and hierarchical clustering of networks or PMU frequency signals identify coherent areas or topology classes that act as representative configurations for protection and stability studies (Pomarico et al., 2024; Liccardo et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Across planning, stability, and reliability studies, representative operating conditions are primarily obtained via k means/k medoids, GMM, DBSCAN/graph clustering, and affinity propagation on time-series or state features, complemented by topology aggregation, state enumeration, and structure preserving MOR to reduce the dimensionality of network states.

•	How does operating-state characterization support planning, screening, forecasting, or power-quality studies in substations?
Operating state characterization enables targeted planning, smarter monitoring, better forecasts, and context aware power quality assessment in substations.
How operating states support different applications
1. Planning and screening
•	Substation clustering into operating classes lets operators install SCADA/RTUs only in a few “pilot” substations per class and infer states for the others, reducing instrumentation cost while retaining acceptable state estimation accuracy for loss minimization, voltage control, and security analysis (Dandea et al., 2025).
•	State estimation itself provides a clean, consistent representation of operating conditions (voltages, flows, injections) that underpins contingency analysis and screening of abnormal conditions in EMS/DSO tools (Cheng et al., 2024; Darmis & Korres, 2023; Dandea et al., 2025).
2. Forecasting and predictive monitoring
•	Historical pairs of measurements + estimated states are used to train ML/DL models that directly forecast future operating states, maintaining observability when real time measurements are missing or under contingencies (Mukherjee et al., 2021; Bhusal et al., 2021).
•	In substations, equipment state labels (normal/degraded/fault) derived via data mining or multi algorithm ensembles enable health prediction and early fault diagnosis for transformers and relay protection, guiding maintenance planning and risk based asset management (Dang et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2024; Zeng & Chu, 2025).
3. Load and energy forecasting at substation level
•	Characterized operating regimes and cleaned state/measurement histories form the feature set for short term and multi horizon load forecasting models at substations, improving accuracy for operational planning and market scheduling (Habbak et al., 2023; Mystakidis et al., 2024; Dewangan et al., 2023; Percuku et al., 2025; Giamarelos et al., 2023; Abumohsen et al., 2023).
4. Power quality and loss/QoS studies
•	Simulated and measured operating conditions are used to reproduce and stress PQ disturbances (sags, notches, harmonics) and to analyze their severity, interactions, and economic impact in a controlled way (Rexhepi & Rexhepi, 2024; Caicedo et al., 2023).
•	Operating state information (flows, switching, RES outputs) contextualizes loss and PQ assessments per component, enabling neural network based loss classification and optimization of substation operation (Rexhepi & Rexhepi, 2024).
Conclusion
By turning raw SCADA/AMI data into structured operating states, utilities gain a common layer that supports cost efficient measurement placement, automated screening, high quality forecasting, and more realistic PQ and loss analyses in substations.

2.7 Knowledge Gap Synthesis and Conceptual Positioning of the Study
•	What unresolved research problems remain at the intersection of harmonic resonance, sparse monitoring, and operational analytics?
Key open problems center on making harmonic-resonance risk observable and actionable when measurements are sparse, heterogeneous, and uncertain.
1. Physics–data fusion under sparse, uncertain monitoring
•	Optimal PQ monitor placement for resonance is usually solved from off line frequency scans and scenario sets, not from noisy field data, and assumes resonance is “obvious” in measurements (Shakeri et al., 2024). How to jointly design sensors and physics guided estimators that reliably infer resonance risk from few, uncertain harmonics remains open.
•	Matrix completion and low rank state estimators struggle to recover realistic AC states under sparse meters and show degenerate solutions (Hu et al., 2025). Extending these methods to frequency domain / harmonic states with observability guarantees is unresolved.
Open directions
•	Physics guided ML that respects network admittance structure and modal properties while learning from limited data (Alhamrouni et al., 2024; Sun & You, 2021; Cunha et al., 2022; Bertozzi et al., 2023).
•	Robust contextual estimators that exploit historical harmonic patterns while handling topology/DER evolution (De La Varga et al., 2025).
2. Event level anomaly analytics for resonance
•	Existing harmonic resonance studies focus on off line modal or sensitivity analysis for planning/mitigation, not real time anomaly detection of evolving resonant modes (Lokar et al., 2021; Pan et al., 2021).
•	Big PQ datasets are only beginning to be used for detecting atypical harmonic patterns, and current methods only partially address missing data, instrument uncertainty, and local/previously unseen patterns (Zyabkina et al., 2025).
Open directions
•	Streaming anomaly detection tailored to high dimensional harmonic spectra with explicit treatment of measurement uncertainty and missing data (Moldovan & Iantovics, 2025; Maged & Zwetsloot, 2024; Zyabkina et al., 2025).
•	Linking detected atypical patterns to specific resonance modes and assets (mode attribution and root cause inference) in near real time (Lokar et al., 2021; Zyabkina et al., 2025; Pan et al., 2021).
3. Closing the loop: from analytics to operational decisions
•	Most work stops at detection or off line mitigation proposals; decision oriented analytics that suggest safe switching, filter tuning, or curtailment actions under uncertainty are rare (Shakeri et al., 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Pan et al., 2021).
•	Integrating harmonic focused analytics with SCADA/IoT condition monitoring frameworks is still at an early stage (Nuruzzaman & Rana, 2025; Li et al., 2024).
Open directions
•	Risk aware operational policies that use probabilistic resonance indicators derived from sparse data.
•	Human in the loop tools that turn complex harmonic anomaly outputs into interpretable guidance for operators (Zyabkina et al., 2025; Nuruzzaman & Rana, 2025; Bertozzi et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Unresolved problems lie in physics consistent state estimation and anomaly detection for harmonics with limited sensors, rigorous handling of uncertainty and missing data, and translating detection into robust, real time operational decisions for resonance risk management.

•	How can the present study be positioned relative to prior work on harmonic analysis, proxy indicators, and interpretable risk modelling?
The study sits at the junction of physics based harmonic analysis, proxy based risk screening, and interpretable, multi indicator risk modelling.
Relative to harmonic analysis
Advanced harmonic methods now capture multi fractal and multi scale features of harmonic signals to better identify weak, low frequency components relevant for power quality and resonance phenomena (Wen, 2025). In fluid machinery, harmonic response analysis coupled with CFD and modal work is used to quantify resonance operating points and equivalent stress as a basis for resonance risk assessment (Zeng et al., 2022). Your work extends this line by using harmonic structure not only for diagnosis but as inputs to higher level risk proxies and screening logic, rather than stopping at waveform characterization or component level risk.
Harmonic analysis vs. present study
Dimension	Prior work	Present study’s niche	Citations
Signal treatment	High resolution or multi fractal harmonic characterization	Uses harmonics specifically to construct physics guided risk indicators	(Zeng et al., 2022; Wen, 2025)
Output	Local resonance or PQ indices	System level screening metrics and decision support	(Zeng et al., 2022; Wen, 2025)
Figure 1: Positioning harmonic analysis within broader risk workflow.
Relative to proxy indicators and risk screening
In environmental and health domains, simple, mechanistically motivated proxies (e.g., ecotoxicity × production volume; area deprivation indices; PSA derived indicators) are used for fast risk screening but need validation and careful interpretation of misclassification (Arvidsson et al., 2022; Brown et al., 2023; Jin et al., 2025). Your physics guided harmonic proxies parallel these designs but add:
•	stronger physical grounding (network/structural resonance, modal content) compared with purely statistical area  or volume based proxies (Arvidsson et al., 2022; Brown et al., 2023)- explicit focus on early, prospective screening under sparse data, similar to prospective nano material screening but in an operational engineering context (Arvidsson et al., 2022).
Relative to interpretable risk modelling
Recent work blends expert judgment, fuzzy sets, ISM, Bayesian networks, and explainable ML to keep risk logic transparent: fuzzy membership risk levels for TBM excavation (Kwon et al., 2025), ISM BN for flood risk (Li et al., 2022), FRAM based quantitative and fuzzy models for sociotechnical risk (Kumar et al., 2025; Kim & Yoon, 2021), and XAI around ML scour models (Wang et al., 2023). Your approach aligns by:
•	treating harmonic proxies as explicit features whose influence on risk is traceable
•	emphasizing distributions, thresholds, and scenario based explanation, closer to fuzzy and FRAM style representations than black box predictors (Kwon et al., 2025; Li et al., 2022; Kumar et al., 2025; Kim & Yoon, 2021; Wang et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Positioned in this landscape, the present study contributes a physics consistent, harmonic based proxy framework that links advanced signal analysis to interpretable, proxy driven risk screening, complementing existing proxy indicators and interpretable risk models that lack explicit treatment of harmonic resonance and sparse operational monitoring.

•	What conceptual gap specifically justifies a physics-guided proxy framework for 33/11 kV distribution substations?
The key gap is the lack of tractable, physics-consistent risk metrics for sparsely monitored, harmonically complex 33/11 kV substations.
1. Full physics models are conceptually rich but operationally unusable
High dimensional safety domain modelling can, in principle, give complete operating boundaries for distribution substations, but it is computationally heavy and aimed at detailed off line analysis rather than routine risk monitoring at many sites (Guan et al., 2025). Harmonic resonance studies likewise require long time windows, many configurations, and detailed impedance models, making them “time consuming” and difficult to apply exhaustively in distribution networks (Lowczowski et al., 2024).
Gap: No scalable way to carry over this rich physics into simple, routinely computable indicators at 33/11 kV buses with limited measurements.
2. Data driven methods ignore substation level harmonic physics
Harmonic estimation and forecasting increasingly use AI to avoid detailed network models, but these approaches are largely black box and focus on signal accuracy, not interpretable, mechanism linked risk measures at specific substations (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2025). Load forecasting at 33/11 kV focuses on active power and market operation, not harmonic or resonance risk (Veeramsetty et al., 2021).
Gap: Existing data driven tools do not encode how admittance, short circuit strength, capacitor banks, or ferro/ferro resonant effects translate into local harmonic vulnerability (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Plienis et al., 2023; Yusuf et al., 2024).
3. Risk models for substations rarely integrate harmonics, resonance, and sparse monitoring
Safety and resilience frameworks for substations (e.g., high dimensional safety regions, seismic resilience) treat operating states and recovery, but not harmonic resonance as a first class risk channel (Guan et al., 2025; Liu & Xie, 2024). Harmonic oriented algorithms that infer resonance risk from single point or LV side measurements exist, but are tailored cases, not generalized proxy frameworks for medium voltage substations (Plienis et al., 2023; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021).
Gap: A conceptual bridge is missing between (i) detailed harmonic/resonance physics and (ii) simple, interpretable proxy indicators that can be computed from the few quantities routinely measured at 33/11 kV substations.
Summary table: Why a physics guided proxy layer is needed
Need at 33/11 kV	What prior work offers	Remaining gap addressed by physics guided proxies	Citations
Many sites, few sensors	High dimensional or detailed harmonic models	Compress physics into low dimensional, monitor driven indicators	(Guan et al., 2025; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025; Plienis et al., 2023)
Operational, near real time use	Heavy off line studies	Fast metrics usable in routine operational analytics	(Guan et al., 2025; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Plienis et al., 2023)
Interpretability for operators	Black box AI or abstract safety indices	Mechanism linked proxies (e.g., resonance margin, harmonic stress)	(Zheng et al., 2025; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Veeramsetty et al., 2021; Plienis et al., 2023; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Yusuf et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Conceptual gap motivating physics-guided proxies.
Conclusion
A physics guided proxy framework is justified because current work either models substation physics in too much detail to be operationally scalable, or models data without encoding the harmonic and resonance mechanisms that matter for 33/11 kV risk, leaving a clear conceptual gap between full models and actionable, interpretable indicators.

2.8 Theoretical Basis for Physics-Guided Proxy Indicators
•	What physical principles support the use of busbar voltage depression, feeder loading, and incomer stress as indirect indicators of resonance vulnerability?
Busbar voltage depression, feeder loading, and incomer stress are indirect resonance indicators because resonance manifests as large harmonic currents flowing through finite impedances, causing localized voltage drops, current concentration, and thermal/electrical overstress.
Underlying physical principles
1.	Resonance, impedance, and voltage magnification
o	Parallel or series resonance between line inductance, shunt capacitors, and network/load impedances produces sharp peaks in driving point or transfer impedance at specific frequencies (Letha et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025).
o	When harmonic or supraharmonic currents from converters or non linear loads inject near these resonant frequencies, voltage at the resonant bus can be strongly magnified or distorted (Razmi et al., 2023; Ahsan et al., 2021; Letha et al., 2021).
o	Thus, busbar voltage depression or distortion under given harmonic injection reflects how close the effective Thevenin impedance is to a resonant condition: small additional current causing disproportionate voltage change indicates a weak, resonance prone bus (Razmi et al., 2023; Letha et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025).
2.	Feeder loading and damping of resonant circuits
o	Loads provide resistive damping to LC resonances; characteristic impedance at resonance corresponds to a particular equivalent load level at which the circuit is critically damped (Letha et al., 2021).
o	As feeder loading moves away from this characteristic value (e.g., very light loading), effective damping decreases and resonance peaks in system impedance become higher, increasing harmonic magnification risk (Letha et al., 2021).
o	Therefore, feeder loading is a proxy for the damping ratio of the line capacitor network and hence for its resonance vulnerability (Letha et al., 2021).
3.	Incomer stress as an indicator of harmonic energy concentration
o	At resonance, large harmonic currents can circulate between feeders and capacitor banks, concentrating through incomers and busbars, driving up RMS currents, losses, and thermal stress (Tahir et al., 2022; Letha et al., 2021; Hou et al., 2023).
o	Elevated incomer current, temperature, or voltage drop at specific frequencies implies that the network impedance seen from that point is close to a resonant peak and that harmonic power is being trapped there rather than dissipated uniformly (Tahir et al., 2022; Letha et al., 2021; Hou et al., 2023).
Summary table: indicator → physical link
Indicator	Physical link to resonance vulnerability	Citations
Busbar voltage depression/distortion	High local Thevenin impedance and magnified voltage response near resonant frequencies	(Razmi et al., 2023; Ahsan et al., 2021; Letha et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025)
Feeder loading	Changes effective resistance/damping of line–capacitor LC modes; light or specific loading increases Q and peak impedance	(Ahsan et al., 2021; Letha et al., 2021; Wanjekeche et al., 2025; Ansari et al., 2025)
Incomer electrical/thermal stress	Resonant circulation of harmonic currents through incomer paths, raising current density, losses, and temperature	(Tahir et al., 2022; Letha et al., 2021; Hou et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Physical mechanisms linking indirect indicators to resonance
Conclusion
These indirect indicators are grounded in basic circuit principles: resonance shapes frequency dependent impedance; loading sets damping; and harmonic energy at resonant peaks appears as abnormal voltage changes and concentrated current/thermal stress at busbars, feeders, and incomers.

•	How can operational variables be theoretically linked to changes in system stiffness, impedance interaction, or resonance susceptibility?
Operational variables affect resonance by reshaping the frequency dependent impedances of grids and converters, which changes effective “stiffness”, damping, and where/if impedance intersections (resonances) occur.
1. System stiffness and grid strength
•	Grid stiffness is essentially the inverse of the Thevenin impedance seen from a bus; weaker grids have larger equivalent impedance, especially at certain frequencies (Din et al., 2022; Zhao et al., 2024).
•	Operational variables such as topology (line switching), feeder loading, added converters or storage, and reactive support change the aggregate R–L–C seen at a node, shifting the magnitude and angle of the Thevenin/Short Circuit impedance and thus stiffness (Calero et al., 2023; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Din et al., 2022).
•	In converter dominated systems, control gains can act as virtual inductors or resistors, effectively modifying stiffness and damping at sub  and super synchronous frequencies (Zhu et al., 2024; Zhao et al., 2024).
2. Impedance interaction and resonance susceptibility
Resonance is governed by how converter and network impedances intersect:
•	A resonance occurs when inductive and capacitive reactances cancel, producing very high (parallel) or very low (series) equivalent impedance at a frequency (Sun et al., 2023; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021).
•	Operational variables reshape either side of the impedance pair: 
o	Control parameters (current loop PI gains, PLL, virtual impedance) directly change converter output impedance magnitude, phase, and resonant peaks, moving grid–converter intersection frequencies and altering phase margin (Zhu et al., 2024; Sun et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2025).
o	Filter/component settings (LCL values, capacitor banks, active dampers) shift natural frequencies and damping ratios of LC networks, creating or removing resonant modes (Zhu et al., 2024; Xie & Zhong, 2025; Tahir et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2024).
o	Loading and dispatch modify effective resistive damping and network R/X ratios, sharpening or flattening impedance peaks and thus Q factor of resonant modes (Xie & Zhong, 2025; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Din et al., 2022; Tahir et al., 2022).
Mapping operational levers to theoretical effects
Operational variable	Main theoretical effect on resonance	Citations
Grid topology, feeder status	Changes network R–L–C, grid stiffness, natural modes	(Calero et al., 2023; Sun et al., 2023; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Din et al., 2022)
Load level/location	Alters damping (R), Q factor, peak impedance	(Xie & Zhong, 2025; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Din et al., 2022; Tahir et al., 2022)
Converter control gains (PI, virtual R/L)	Shape converter impedance, add virtual stiffness/damping	(Zhu et al., 2024; Sun et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2025; Zhao et al., 2024)
Filters, capacitors, active dampers	Shift LC eigenfrequencies; add/remove resonant peaks	(Zhu et al., 2024; Xie & Zhong, 2025; Tahir et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Operational changes mapped to impedance and resonance
Conclusion
Theoretical links come from viewing any operational change as modifying the composite impedance (and damping) of interconnected RLC and control derived elements; this shifts eigenfrequencies, grid stiffness, and impedance intersections that determine resonance susceptibility.

•	What criteria should a valid physics-guided proxy indicator satisfy in engineering risk-screening studies?
A valid physics guided proxy indicator must be mechanistically justified, predictive of the target risk, robust across conditions, and practically observable with quantified uncertainty.
Core criteria for a physics guided proxy
1. Strong mechanistic linkage
•	The proxy must be monotonically or functionally related to the underlying physical failure or hazard mechanism (e.g., strain state → internal short circuit probability in Li ion cells) (Jia et al., 2021).
•	Feature engineering should encode known thresholds and invariants (e.g., above/below freezing, degree day proxies for permafrost thaw) so that the indicator reflects real physics rather than spurious correlations (Kriuk, 2025).
2. Predictive validity for risk outcomes
•	The proxy must reliably predict occurrence, severity, or progression of the risk (e.g., fracture growth and leakage in CO₂ storage; rockburst occurrence in mining) (Sekar et al., 2025; Khan et al., 2023).
•	Performance should be demonstrated against high fidelity simulations or historical events using appropriate metrics (R², RMSE, confusion metrics, calibrated probabilities) (Sekar et al., 2025; Bahrami et al., 2022; Abdollahfard et al., 2025; Kriuk, 2025; Jia et al., 2021).
3. Robustness, generalizability, and extrapolation
•	The indicator should remain valid over the screening design space (geologies, operating regimes, climates) and not fail catastrophically under extrapolation where physics is well understood (Bahrami et al., 2022; Jenkins et al., 2022; Kriuk, 2025).
•	Hybrid or multi fidelity approaches are favored when they retain the governing physics and can extrapolate beyond the training data while bounding errors (Bahrami et al., 2022; Jenkins et al., 2022; Kriuk, 2025).
4. Observability, timeliness, and operational usefulness
•	The proxy must be derivable from measurable or routinely simulated quantities (field pressures/temperatures, strain, climate variables), enabling real time or near real time screening (Sekar et al., 2025; Abdollahfard et al., 2025; Khan et al., 2023; Kriuk, 2025; Jia et al., 2021).
•	It should support fast, low cost evaluation relative to full physics models, with explicit uncertainty quantification for decision support (Sekar et al., 2025; Abdollahfard et al., 2025; Jenkins et al., 2022; Kriuk, 2025).
5. Transparency and risk aware calibration
•	Assumptions, input ranges, and uncertainty bounds must be documented so that decision makers understand where the proxy is trustworthy and where full physics analysis is required (Bahrami et al., 2022; Jenkins et al., 2022; Kriuk, 2025).
Conclusion
In engineering risk screening, a physics guided proxy is valid only if it is tightly tied to governing mechanisms, empirically validated against high fidelity or observed data, robust across relevant scenarios, operationally measurable, and delivered with quantified, transparent uncertainty so that its limitations are explicit in risk decisions.

3. Materials and Methods
3.1 Study Design and Methodological Framework
•	What research designs are appropriate for developing and validating a physics-guided proxy screening framework from substation operational data?
Designing and validating a physics guided proxy screening framework: appropriate research designs
Developing a physics guided proxy screening framework from substation operational data benefits from a staged design that combines simulation based development, offline validation on historical data, and limited online/field testing.
1. Simulation and digital twin–based development
•	Use high fidelity physics models or digital twins (e.g., AC power flow, dynamic models, or substation twins) to generate labeled scenarios for training and stress testing the proxy, analogous to CO₂ storage and geothermal proxy workflows and substation digital twin perception frameworks (Ribeiro et al., 2025; Khanal & Shahriar, 2022; Omosebi et al., 2022; Tian et al., 2025).
•	Design of experiments over operating conditions and contingencies to span the relevant state space, as in physics based proxy modeling and flexibility region characterization (Khanal & Shahriar, 2022; Omosebi et al., 2022; Li et al., 2023).
•	Train physics guided ML (e.g., physics informed GNNs or hybrid models) using these simulations as “ground truth,” as done for N 2 contingency screening and hybrid OPF/PIRL (Zheng et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Lu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023).
2. Retrospective observational studies on operational data
•	Conduct retrospective cohort / case control–style evaluations: apply the proxy to historical substation data with known events (violations, contingencies, failures) and compare to outcomes, mirroring condition monitoring PIML and wastewater plant validation (Koksal et al., 2024; Wu et al., 2024).
•	Use cross validation or rolling origin (temporal) splits to assess generalization under realistic non stationarity (Koksal et al., 2024; Wu et al., 2024).
•	Benchmark against naive or purely data driven baselines (e.g., random sampling, DC approximations, black box ML) as in contingency screening, flexibility region classifiers, and hybrid substation/district heating baselines (Zheng et al., 2025; Lu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023).
Example evaluation dimensions
Dimension	Typical choices for proxy screening	Citations
Predictive performance	ROC/AUC, precision–recall, false negative rate on unsafe events	(Zheng et al., 2025; Koksal et al., 2024; Wu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023)
Physics consistency	Power flow feasibility, constraint violation counts/margins	(Zheng et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Lu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023)
Computational efficiency	Speed up vs full simulation or full OPF	(Zheng et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Li et al., 2023)
Robustness/data scarcity	Performance under noise, missing data, reduced samples	(Omosebi et al., 2022; Koksal et al., 2024; Wu et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Core evaluation axes for proxy screening.
3. Prospective / online or hardware in the loop validation
•	Hardware in the loop or operator in the loop tests where the proxy runs in real time but decisions are shadowed, as in hybrid vehicle dynamics and HiL studies (Chen et al., 2022).
•	Limited scope field pilots or A/B style operational trials comparing operator decisions with and without proxy guidance, focusing on safety margins, alarms, and computational load (Zheng et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Wu et al., 2024).
Conclusion
A rigorous program would: (1) prototype and stress test the physics guided proxy in simulations/digital twins, (2) retrospectively validate on real substation data against baselines with multi axis metrics, and (3) cautiously progress to online or HiL trials to confirm reliability and operational value.

•	How should a methodological workflow be structured when combining data preprocessing, proxy engineering, clustering, risk labelling, and ordinal modelling?
A coherent workflow should be staged, iterative, and fully nested within resampling (CV) to avoid leakage, moving from preprocessing → proxy engineering → clustering → risk labelling → ordinal modelling, with feedback between steps.
Recommended staged workflow
1. Data preprocessing & basic feature engineering
•	Clean, impute, normalize/standardize, and handle outliers before any downstream step, guided by domain knowledge and target task (Chicco et al., 2022; Cofre-Martel et al., 2021; Mumuni & Mumuni, 2024).
•	Automate or systematically search preprocessing variants when possible (e.g., AutoML style pipeline search) (Koka et al., 2025; Siddiqi et al., 2023; Mumuni & Mumuni, 2024).
2. Proxy engineering (physics  or domain guided features)
•	Construct derived proxies (aggregates, indices, ratios, temporal features) based on expert knowledge; this is a distinct, documented stage after basic cleaning (Chicco et al., 2022; Cofre-Martel et al., 2021; Mishra, 2025; Bahrami et al., 2022).
•	Validate proxies via exploratory data analysis and simple association with known outcomes or covariates (Cofre-Martel et al., 2021; Mishra, 2025).
3. Unsupervised structure: clustering
•	Apply clustering on preprocessed + proxy features to reveal latent subgroups or regimes (Zhao et al., 2025; Gao et al., 2023; Dinh et al., 2024).
•	Place all pre clustering steps (scaling, dimensionality reduction) inside the CV or bootstrap loop to avoid information leakage (Gao et al., 2023; Dinh et al., 2024).
•	Evaluate clusters with internal validity (silhouette, stability) and domain interpretability (Zhao et al., 2025; Gao et al., 2023; Dinh et al., 2024).
4. Risk labelling strategy
•	Option A (cluster driven): map clusters to risk levels using external outcomes or expert rules (e.g., cluster outcome profiles, thresholds) (Cofre-Martel et al., 2021; Gao et al., 2023; Dinh et al., 2024).
•	Option B (outcome driven): define ordinal risk labels directly from continuous risk scores or time to event outcomes, then use clustering only for subgroup exploration (Koka et al., 2025; Cofre-Martel et al., 2021; Gao et al., 2023).
•	Document label rules and check class balance and plausibility (Chicco et al., 2022; Cofre-Martel et al., 2021).
5. Ordinal modelling and evaluation
•	Fit ordinal models (e.g., proportional odds, ordinal forests/boosting) using original + proxy features and, optionally, cluster indicators as predictors (Kumar et al., 2025; Yu et al., 2025; Onah et al., 2025).
•	Use appropriate metrics for ordered outcomes (ordinal c index, weighted kappa, macro averaged F1 across thresholds) and report uncertainty via CV/bootstrapping (Koka et al., 2025; Kumar et al., 2025; Yu et al., 2025; Onah et al., 2025).
6. Iteration and model selection
Loop focus	Typical adjustments	Citations
Data/proxies	Remove/reshape noisy features; refine proxies	(Chicco et al., 2022; Cofre-Martel et al., 2021; Mishra, 2025; Bahrami et al., 2022)
Clustering	Algorithm, k, distance, DR method	(Zhao et al., 2025; Gao et al., 2023; Dinh et al., 2024; Gutman et al., 2025)
Ordinal model	Model family, regularization, hyperparameters	(Koka et al., 2025; Kumar et al., 2025; Yu et al., 2025; Onah et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Nested, iterative refinement across pipeline stages.
Conclusion
A robust workflow treats preprocessing, proxy engineering, clustering, risk labelling, and ordinal modelling as sequential but tightly coupled modules, all evaluated within a unified resampling framework and iterated based on both statistical performance and domain interpretability.
