•	What validation logic is recommended for operational risk-screening frameworks in power systems?
Operational risk-screening frameworks in power systems should use multi-layer validation logic that combines scenario stress-testing, cost-/risk-weighted metrics, physics checks, uncertainty, and human oversight.
Core validation layers
1.	Multi-scenario technical validation 
o	Test across representative and extreme operating conditions (normal, high-load, limited/poor data, high RES penetration) to assess adaptability and resilience (Vychuzhanin & Vychuzhanin, 2025; Zhang et al., 2025)- Use realistic contingency sets (faults, generator trips, DC blockings, load rejections) and compare screening outputs to time domain simulation or digital twin “ground truth” (Moya et al., 2022; Zhang et al., 2025; Tan et al., 2025)2. Risk- and cost-aware performance metrics
o	Go beyond plain accuracy: emphasize recall on high risk events, risk weighted recall and F1, and cost adjusted precision to reflect asymmetric costs of false negatives vs false positives (Vychuzhanin & Vychuzhanin, 2025; Carrington et al., 2021; Abdelsattar et al., 2025; Ratigan et al., 2025)- Use ROC/AUC plus deeper ROC analysis (group wise AUC, stratified performance) to detect threshold sensitive behavior and subpopulation failures (Carrington et al., 2021; Abdelsattar et al., 2025)### Example validation metrics for screening frameworks
Aspect	Typical metric choices	Citations
Basic discrimination	ROC AUC, PR AUC, accuracy, F1	(Vychuzhanin & Vychuzhanin, 2025; Huang et al., 2025; Moya et al., 2022; Abdelsattar et al., 2025)
Safety / missed risks	Risk weighted recall (RecallR), class specific FN rates	(Vychuzhanin & Vychuzhanin, 2025; Abdelsattar et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025)
Alarm burden	Cost adjusted precision, false alarm rate, capacity-aware ROC	(Vychuzhanin & Vychuzhanin, 2025; Abdelsattar et al., 2025; Ratigan et al., 2025)
Stability & robustness	Cross validation SD, performance under noisy / sparse data	(Vychuzhanin & Vychuzhanin, 2025; Moya et al., 2022; Abdelsattar et al., 2025; Zhu et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Key metric families for validating risk screeners.
3.	Physics consistency and uncertainty checks 
o	Enforce or audit physical feasibility of predictions (frequency limits, power-flow consistency, operator thresholds) as an additional validation criterion, not just error size (Zhu et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Letzgus & Müller, 2023)- Incorporate uncertainty quantification (Bayesian or probabilistic models, PINNs, DeepONet-UQ) and validate calibration, ensuring high risk flags correspond to high predictive uncertainty or large impact (Moya et al., 2022; Zhu et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025)4. Workflow and human-in-the-loop logic
o	Deploy in “shadow mode” first (screening → benchmark/physics validation → operator decision), logging disagreements and near misses for post hoc review (Huang et al., 2025; Zhu et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025)- Encode domain rules (e.g., mandatory human review for certain equipment or conditions) as a safety layer on top of ML risk scores, and validate that this rule layer intercepts critical misclassifications (Huang et al., 2025; Zhu et al., 2025)## Conclusion
For operational risk screening in power systems, strong validation logic means: stress-test across scenarios, use risk- and cost-weighted metrics, verify physics and uncertainty, and embed the model inside a rule- and human supervised decision workflow before full operational use.

3.2 Study System and Dataset Description
•	What information should be reported to adequately describe a 33/11 kV substation case study for a Q1 power-systems journal?
A 33/11 kV substation case study for a Q1 journal should fully specify system context, equipment and data, modelling/experiment setup, and validation, so results are interpretable and reproducible.
1. System context and substation description
•	Geographic/utility context, role in the network (bulk supply point, injection, industrial hub) and demand characteristics (peak/base load, load mix, growth) (Norouzi et al., 2024; Neupane, 2024; Naruyinda et al., 2023).
•	Single line diagram with connection to higher voltage grid, number and ratings of transformers (MVA, voltage ratios, %Z, vector group), busbar arrangement, NOPs/rings, capacitor banks and DG/EG connections (Alahmad et al., 2024; Norouzi et al., 2024; Obied & Wahhab, 2021; F. et al., 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023).
•	Feeder inventory: number, lengths, conductor types, impedances, rated currents, and key large customers or embedded plants (Norouzi et al., 2024; Obied & Wahhab, 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; Naruyinda et al., 2023).
Example items to tabulate
Category	Key items to report	Citations
Transformers	Ratings, tap ranges, losses, loading levels	(Norouzi et al., 2024; Obied & Wahhab, 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Neupane, 2024)
Lines/feeders	Type, R/X per km, length, topology	(Norouzi et al., 2024; Obied & Wahhab, 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Neupane, 2024; Naruyinda et al., 2023)
Loads	Peak/average, PF, forecast horizon	(Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; Naruyinda et al., 2023)
Protection/automation	Relay types, settings basis, SC levels, SCADA	(Obied & Wahhab, 2021; F. et al., 2021; Aziz et al., 2025; Mohammed et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Core technical data typically reported in substation cases.
2. Data, modelling and scenarios
•	Data sources and periods (SCADA logs, outage records, measurements), quality checks, and any assumptions/imputations (Itua et al., 2025; Neupane, 2024; , 2023; Ogum et al., 2023).
•	Software/tools (ETAP, CYME, PSS/E, DIgSILENT, in house code) and model validation against measured voltages/flows or utility studies (Obied & Wahhab, 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023).
•	Scenario design: base case, contingency/fault cases, with/without DG, alternative operating modes, forecast years, parameter ranges (Alahmad et al., 2024; Obied & Wahhab, 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Aziz et al., 2025; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; Samten et al., 2025).
3. Performance indices, methods and uncertainty
•	Clearly defined metrics: technical losses, voltage profiles, reliability indices (SAIDI, SAIFI, ASAI, EENS), PQ indices, short circuit levels, protection margins, financial indices or payback where relevant (Itua et al., 2025; F. et al., 2021; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; Naruyinda et al., 2023; Mujika et al., 2025; Ogum et al., 2023).
•	Numerical methods (load flow type, fault algorithm, optimization approach, AI models) and key settings (tolerances, stopping criteria, hyperparameters) (Obied & Wahhab, 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Aziz et al., 2025; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; Naruyinda et al., 2023).
•	Sensitivity analyses or uncertainty ranges for key parameters (load growth, DG output, failure rates) and limitations of the case’s generalizability (Norouzi et al., 2024; Itua et al., 2025; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; , 2023; Mujika et al., 2025).
4. Reporting style for Q1 impact
•	Make the case study’s novelty and transferability explicit (what is generalizable beyond this substation) and link design/operational insights to standards or utility practice (Alahmad et al., 2024; Norouzi et al., 2024; Obied & Wahhab, 2021; F. et al., 2021; Sepasi et al., 2023; Mujika et al., 2025; Marin-Garcia et al., 2022).
•	Provide high quality figures (SLDs, TCC curves, loss/voltage maps, reliability plots) and well structured sections: system, data/model, scenarios, results, discussion, practical implications (Obied & Wahhab, 2021; F. et al., 2021; Bravo et al., 2022; Neupane, 2024; Mohammed et al., 2023; Mujika et al., 2025).
Conclusion
To satisfy a Q1 power systems journal, a 33/11 kV substation case study must read as a reproducible system study: fully specified network and equipment, transparent data and modelling choices, well defined scenarios and indices, explicit uncertainty and limitations, and clearly articulated contributions that extend beyond the single substation.

•	How should hourly operational datasets from substations be described in terms of variables, resolution, coverage, and context?
Hourly substation datasets should explicitly describe what is measured, at what granularity, for which assets and time span, and under which operational context and preprocessing rules.
Key elements to report
1. Variables (what is measured)
•	Core electrical quantities: active/reactive power, energy, voltage, current per phase, power factor, frequency where available (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Sănduleac et al., 2022; De Souza Duz et al., 2025).
•	Asset-/function-specific signals: transformer temperatures, DGA gases, FRA indices, tap positions, breaker/relay states, alarms, operator actions, fault labels, maintenance events (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Roelofs et al., 2025; Andrade et al., 2022).
•	Metadata per record: substation ID, feeder/asset ID, timestamp, measurement unit, quality flag (valid/estimated/missing), event labels where used (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Roelofs et al., 2025; Andrade et al., 2022; De Souza Duz et al., 2025).
2. Temporal resolution and aggregation
•	Native sampling rate (e.g., SCADA seconds, PMU milliseconds, AMI hourly) and how data were aggregated to hourly (mean, sum, max, min) (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Sănduleac et al., 2022; De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Fu et al., 2024).
•	Clock and synchronization details: time zone, daylight saving handling, alignment across devices, and any resampling/interpolation (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Sănduleac et al., 2022; Xu et al., 2022).
•	Coverage completeness (percent missing per variable/substation, criteria for discarding series) (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Li et al., 2022; Sănduleac et al., 2022).
3. Spatial/asset coverage
•	Number of substations and their types/voltage levels/regions, plus counts of monitored transformers, feeders, and bays (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Roelofs et al., 2025; Andrade et al., 2022; De Souza Duz et al., 2025).
•	Period covered (start–end dates, seasons/years), and whether data are continuous or event based only (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Roelofs et al., 2025; Andrade et al., 2022; Li et al., 2022).
4. Operational and use case context
•	Network and customer context (load mix, climate, RES penetration, tariff or control schemes) (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Sănduleac et al., 2022; Li et al., 2022; Fu et al., 2024).
•	Data origin (AMI, SCADA, PMU, edge IoT), system architecture, and any privacy or anonymization steps (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Sănduleac et al., 2022; Andrade et al., 2022; Lakshmi et al., 2022).
•	Preprocessing: filtering, outlier rules, labeling procedures, dimensionality reduction or feature construction (e.g., 24 dim daily load profiles) (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Andrade et al., 2022; Fu et al., 2024).
Example description structure
Aspect	Minimum description items	Citations
Variables	List, units, per asset scope	(Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Sănduleac et al., 2022)
Time	Native rate, hourly aggregation, gaps	(Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Sănduleac et al., 2022; Li et al., 2022)
Coverage	#substations/assets, geography, period	(Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Roelofs et al., 2025; Andrade et al., 2022)
Context	grid role, metering system, preprocessing	(Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Sănduleac et al., 2022; Lakshmi et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Core facets for documenting hourly substation data.
Conclusion
An adequate description of hourly substation datasets makes variables, temporal handling, spatial coverage, and operational context transparent, enabling reproducible analysis and fair benchmarking across studies.

•	What dataset characteristics are most important for studies on operational power-quality risk screening?
For operational power quality (PQ) risk screening, the most important dataset characteristics concern what is measured, how finely, under what conditions, and how representative and label rich the data are.
1. Signal resolution and fidelity
•	Sampling rate vs. disturbance spectrum: Sampling must be high enough to capture harmonics, notches and transients relevant to the risk being screened; studies show that classification accuracy and voltage event detection are very sensitive to sampling frequency choices (Pérez-Aragüés & Olivan, 2025; Medina-Molina et al., 2025; Dubey, 2022).
•	Windowing and aggregation: PQD methods depend on cycle level or sub cycle windows, not hourly averages, for accurate sag/swell, transient and flicker detection (Caicedo et al., 2023; Priyadarshini et al., 2025; Dubey, 2022).
•	Noise levels and SNR: Many algorithms are tested down to 20–30 dB SNR; robustness at those noise levels is crucial for realistic screening (Wang et al., 2025; Mishra et al., 2024; Priyadarshini et al., 2025; Khetarpal et al., 2023).
2. Variable set and labels
•	Core PQ variables: Voltage and current waveforms/phasors, RMS values, harmonic content, frequency, power factor, and event flags form the backbone of PQ monitoring datasets (Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Kumwenda, 2023; Priyadarshini et al., 2025; Dubey, 2022).
•	Physically meaningful disturbance descriptors: Magnitude, duration, phase angle jump, energy content and waveform shape (e.g., shape index, Lissajous features, wavelet energy bands) are key for risk relevant classification and severity assessment (Sinha et al., 2025; Caicedo et al., 2023; Kumwenda, 2023; Priyadarshini et al., 2025).
•	Ground truth labels: Clear labeling of disturbance type, cause, and location (when known) is critical for training and benchmarking ML based screening tools (Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Caicedo et al., 2023; Ahmed et al., 2024; Khetarpal et al., 2023; Khan et al., 2023).
Key dataset facets for PQ risk screening
Aspect	Why it matters for risk screening	Citations
Sampling & SNR	Captures fast PQDs; robustness to noise	(Pérez-Aragüés & Olivan, 2025; Wang et al., 2025; Mishra et al., 2024; Medina-Molina et al., 2025; Dubey, 2022)
Feature richness	Enables distinguishing overlapping/mixed events	(Sinha et al., 2025; Caicedo et al., 2023; Kumwenda, 2023; Priyadarshini et al., 2025; Khetarpal et al., 2023)
Labels & class balance	Reliable ML training, low false alarms	(Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Ahmed et al., 2024; Khetarpal et al., 2023; Khan et al., 2023)
Operating context	Links events to assets, RES, topology	(Wang et al., 2025; Hu et al., 2025; Shao et al., 2023; Wang, 2023)
Figure 1: Core dataset facets enabling PQ risk screening.
3. Coverage, diversity, and context
•	Event diversity and mixed PQDs: Datasets covering single and combined disturbances (sag swell, sag harmonics, etc.) are essential for realistic operational screening (Wang et al., 2025; Mishra et al., 2024; Dubey, 2022).
•	Spatial and temporal coverage: Multiple buses/substations, seasons, and operating conditions (renewables, switching, faults) improve generalizability and reduce bias (Wang et al., 2025; Hu et al., 2025; Shao et al., 2023; Wang, 2023).
•	Measurement and network context: Device type (PMU, PQ monitor), deployment topology, and RES penetration strongly influence PQ event characteristics and algorithm transferability (Hu et al., 2025; Shao et al., 2023; Wang, 2023).
Conclusion
For operational PQ risk screening, datasets are most valuable when they offer high fidelity waveform data, rich physically interpretable features, well balanced and reliable labels across diverse PQ events and conditions, and clear documentation of sampling, noise, network context and coverage.

3.2.1 Description of the 33/11 kV Substation Environment
•	What physical, operational, and network characteristics of a 33/11 kV substation are necessary to contextualize harmonic-risk screening?
To contextualize harmonic risk screening at a 33/11 kV substation, datasets should describe key physical design, operating conditions, and surrounding network configuration that shape harmonic generation, propagation, and resonance.
Physical / Design Characteristics
•	Transformer details: MVA rating, vector group (e.g., Dyn11), short circuit impedance and leakage reactance, tap changer range, and loading philosophy (e.g., 60% nominal with 1.25 future expansion factor) because these determine harmonic transfer, attenuation (e.g., triplen blocking by delta), and thermal margins (Oyeleye et al., 2021; Oyeleyea et al., 2021; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2022; Moradi et al., 2021).
•	Busbar and switchgear: Rated voltage, insulation level, breaker/fuse sizing, and protection coordination, since harmonic currents influence breaker stress and protection sensitivity (Oyeleye et al., 2021; Alahmad et al., 2024; Schräder et al., 2024).
•	Feeder construction: Conductor type, cross section, R/X ratio, cable length and layout, which affect harmonic voltage drops and resonance with line/cable capacitances (Naruyinda et al., 2023; Nta, 2022; Moradi et al., 2021; Pereira et al., 2021).
Operational Characteristics
•	Loading patterns: Daily/seasonal load curves, maximum demand, load factor, and overload history, as high loading increases harmonic losses and transformer heating (Gracheva et al., 2025; Abdullazyanov et al., 2023; Akpojedje et al., 2022; Nta, 2022).
•	Outage/tripping statistics: Feeder tripping profiles, failure rates, and reliability indices (SAIFI, SAIDI, ASAI), to link harmonic risk screening with existing reliability weaknesses (Akpojedje et al., 2022; Itua et al., 2025; , 2023).
•	Nonlinear load and converter mix: Presence and share of large rectifiers, drives, PV inverters, EV chargers, etc., since high penetration of converter based resources is a dominant harmonic source (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Moradi et al., 2021; Pereira et al., 2021).
Network / System Context
Context facet	Why it matters for harmonic risk	References
Upstream grid impedance & background distortion	Sets boundary conditions for harmonic voltages at 33 kV	(Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2022; Moradi et al., 2021; Schräder et al., 2024)
11 kV ring/open point scheme	Determines parallel paths, resonance conditions, fault levels	(Alahmad et al., 2024; Naruyinda et al., 2023; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2022)
PQ/monitor placement & resolution	Affects observability and estimation accuracy of harmonics	(Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2022; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Moradi et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Substation and network context shaping harmonic risk.
Conclusion
For meaningful harmonic risk screening at a 33/11 kV substation, datasets should capture transformer and feeder electrical parameters, non linear load and DER mix, loading and reliability behavior, and the surrounding network impedance and topology, all of which jointly determine harmonic levels and associated risk.

•	How do incomers, busbars, feeder arrangement, and urban loading environment affect substation behaviour in the literature?
Substation behaviour in the literature is strongly shaped by how incomers, busbars, feeder topology, and the urban load environment jointly determine reliability, loadability, and power quality.
Physical configuration: incomers, busbars, feeders
•	Busbar scheme and couplings
Urban studies show that single  and simple double busbar switchyards have the highest non attended demand risk (~50%), whereas double bus + bypass, double bus + ring, and coupled bus schemes markedly reduce unmet demand (≤20–25%) by improving contingency handling (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024). Busbar couplings increase the probability of withstanding N 1/N 2 events by providing alternative paths for transformer and feeder supply (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024).
•	Incomer and ring operation
Parallel or ringed feeder operation between 33/11 kV substations can improve load balancing and voltage profile but may create load flow and protection coordination problems if loops span different sources; closed rings are acceptable only when fed from the same substation/source (Alahmad et al., 2024).
•	Feeder arrangement and topology
Feeder length, number of feeders, and presence of normally open points directly affect voltage drops, losses, and restoration options (Zein et al., 2025; Alahmad et al., 2024). Optimized placement of tie lines and feeder interconnections can halve system losses and improve voltage margins under stressed conditions or cyber contingencies (Maghami et al., 2025).
Urban loading environment
Aspect of urban context	Effect on substation behaviour	Citations
High, peaky demand (seasonal/diurnal)	Overloading of transformers/busbars, higher unmet demand risk	(Silva-Ortega et al., 2024; Yao et al., 2023)
Dense EV / nonlinear loads	Increased harmonics, voltage unbalance, higher short circuit stress	(Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021; Kaya & Akar, 2024; Iranpour et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2025)
Low power factor / high reactive flow	Excessive losses, voltage drops, harmonic propagation	(Tavarov et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Configuration and urban loading shaping substation risk.
Conclusion
Across the literature, incomer configuration and busbar scheme set the structural resilience of an urban substation, feeder arrangement governs voltage profile, losses, and restoration capability, and the urban loading environment (density, EV/PV, PF) drives the operational stress, power quality issues, and actual risk levels experienced.

Transactions in GIS, 27, 1662 - 1679. https://doi.org/10.1111/tgis.13093
Maghami, M., Mutambara, A., & Gomes, C. (2025). Assessing cyber attack vulnerabilities of distributed generation in grid-connected systems. Environment, Development and Sustainability. https://doi.org/10.1007/s10668-024-05929-z
•	What level of substation environment description is expected in high-quality distribution-system case studies?
High quality distribution case studies typically give a concise but multi layered description of the substation environment, covering topology, equipment ratings, and the surrounding load/DER context, rather than just a single line “X bus, Y MVA” label.
1. How much structural detail is typical?
Review work on test feeders shows that “key descriptive components” are expected, not full engineering drawings (Marcos et al., 2017):
•	Voltage levels and role (e.g., primary vs secondary substation; HV/MV/LV interface) (Al‐Jaafreh & Mokryani, 2019; Marcos et al., 2017).
•	Topology (radial/looped, presence of tie switches, number of feeders per substation) (Sambaiah & Jayabarathi, 2020; Al‐Jaafreh & Mokryani, 2019; Marcos et al., 2017).
•	Equipment classes and ratings: transformer MVA ranges, number of transformers, presence of regulators and capacitor banks; often summarized in a table, not element by element inventories (Therrien et al., 2021; Sambaiah & Jayabarathi, 2020; Marcos et al., 2017).
•	Typical layouts (European vs US style, underground vs overhead feeders, urban vs rural variants) to clarify representativeness (Al‐Jaafreh & Mokryani, 2019; Marcos et al., 2017).
Typical substation environment descriptors
Dimension	Common level of detail in good case studies	Citations
Voltage & function	“132/33 kV BSP feeding n×33/11 kV primaries…”	(Al‐Jaafreh & Mokryani, 2019; Marcos et al., 2017)
Topology	Radial vs meshed, ties, inline regulators, no. of feeders	(Therrien et al., 2021; Sambaiah & Jayabarathi, 2020; Marcos et al., 2017)
Equipment	Range of transformer sizes, regulator types, LV layout	(Therrien et al., 2021; Al‐Jaafreh & Mokryani, 2019; Marcos et al., 2017)
Data environment	AMI/SCADA coverage, measurement granularity	(Ge et al., 2022; Therrien et al., 2021; Paul et al., 2023)
Context	Urban/rural, load classes, DER/EV penetration ranges	(Li & Jenn, 2024; Yuvaraj et al., 2024; Marcos et al., 2017)
Figure 1: Common dimensions of substation context description.
2. Surrounding load and DER environment
High quality EV and DER studies explicitly relate substations to:
•	Feeder count and customer mix (residential/commercial/industrial) and load density (Li & Jenn, 2024; Marcos et al., 2017).
•	Penetration ranges of EVs, PV, and other DERs and whether networks are real or “typical” synthetic models (Li & Jenn, 2024; Yuvaraj et al., 2024; Marcos et al., 2017; El‐Fergany, 2024).
•	Planning/operation focus (loss minimization, voltage regulation, congestion, resilience), which guides which substation attributes must be specified (e.g., voltage regulation schemes for DG hosting studies) (Iweh et al., 2021; Sambaiah & Jayabarathi, 2020; Paul et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Across high quality distribution system case studies, the expected substation description is a structured summary of voltage role, topology, key equipment ratings and control devices, measurement environment, and surrounding load/DER mix—enough to judge representativeness and to reproduce or adapt the study, but not a component by component asset register.

3.2.2 Operational Variables and Data Structure
•	How are busbar voltages, incomer currents, feeder currents, and total active power typically structured and reported in substation analytics studies?
How substation electrical variables are usually structured and reported in analytics studies
Across substation and feeder analytics, busbar voltages, incomer/feeder currents, and total active power are typically organized by device, phase, and time, and then summarized with statistical or power flow–based indicators.
Typical data structuring
By element and phase
•	Busbar voltages: Stored as per bus, per phase phasors (magnitude, angle) or RMS values; often aggregated into a bus voltage matrix or “power point voltage matrix” indexed by buses and time (Chen et al., 2025; Borkowski, 2023).
•	Incomer currents: MV/LV transformer or incoming line currents usually reported per phase (and sometimes zero/positive/negative sequence) to assess loading and thermal stress (Sănduleac et al., 2022; Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021).
•	Feeder currents: Per feeder, per phase currents, sometimes decomposed into harmonic spectra or sequence components for power quality and fault analysis (Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Chen et al., 2024; Xue et al., 2025).
•	Total active power: Often given as per phase and total P at the transformer or bus (P_a, P_b, P_c, P_s) over time, sometimes with associated Q and power factor (Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Sănduleac et al., 2022).
Example reporting patterns
Variable type	Common structure / metric	Citations
Busbar voltages	Time series per bus & phase; percent deviation, CDFs, p95/p99	(Sănduleac et al., 2022; Chen et al., 2025; Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Borkowski, 2023)
Incomer currents	Transformer/bus tie RMS, sequence components, thermal loading	(Sănduleac et al., 2022; Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Xue et al., 2025)
Feeder currents	Per feeder RMS, harmonic phasors, differential currents	(Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Chen et al., 2024; Xue et al., 2025)
Total active power	Phase and total P time series; min/max, percent of capacity	(Sănduleac et al., 2022; Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Lin, 2021)
Figure 1: Common organization of substation electrical measurements in studies
How results are reported in analytics papers
•	Time series plots and profiles: Daily/weekly profiles of MV/LV transformer P, Q, voltages at remote nodes, and critical line currents, often at multiple aggregation windows (1 s, 1–60 min) (Sănduleac et al., 2022; Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021).
•	Statistical summaries: Percentiles (p95, p99) of active power, voltage, and current; ranges as % of rated capacity; asymmetry and negative P occurrences by phase (Sănduleac et al., 2022; Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021).
•	Derived indicators: Voltage sensitivity ratios dV/dQ at busbars (Agustriadi & Radhitya, 2024), harmonic loss factors and additional loss coefficients from harmonic current spectra (Bialobrzheskyi et al., 2021; Chen et al., 2024).
•	Matrix/graph formulations: Use of adjacency, incidence, and topology matrices with node voltages and line currents attached to nodes/edges for topology learning and voltage monitoring algorithms (Xu et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2025; Deka et al., 2022; Chen et al., 2024).
Conclusion
In substation analytics literature, busbar voltages, incomer and feeder currents, and total active power are modeled as structured, time indexed, per element/per phase datasets, then reported through statistical descriptors, power flow–based indicators, and topology aware matrices to support monitoring, protection, and planning.
.

•	Which operational variables are most informative for capturing substation stress states and screening risk conditions?
Substation stress and risk are best captured by a focused set of electrical, thermal, and PQ variables rather than raw voltages/currents alone.
Most informative operational variables
1. Loading, voltage, and security margins
•	Bus voltages and line/transformer loading relative to thermal/voltage limits underpin static security indices (MW/MVA performance indices, voltage performance index, composite security/security indices) used to rank N 1 contingencies and stress levels (Hailu et al., 2023; Mokred et al., 2023).
•	Voltage stability indicators (e.g., collapse prediction indices like NCPI, FVSI) derived from bus voltages, line currents, and reactive power flows directly quantify proximity to voltage collapse under stressed conditions and outages (Mokred et al., 2023).
•	Reactive power flows and power factor at substations and feeders indicate margin for voltage control and overloading under high demand or contingencies (Tariq et al., 2022; Hailu et al., 2023; Siddiqui et al., 2024).
Illustrative variable categories for stress / risk
Dimension	Key variables / indices	Citations
Loading & limits	Line/transformer MVA, % loading, MW/MVA PIs, NCPI	(Hailu et al., 2023; Siddiqui et al., 2024; Mokred et al., 2023)
Voltage margins	Bus voltage magnitude, PIV/CSI, voltage collapse indices	(Hailu et al., 2023; Mokred et al., 2023)
Power quality & PQD	THD (V/I), sag/notch indicators, PQ composite indices	(Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021; Elsotohy et al., 2025; Diniș et al., 2023; Caicedo et al., 2023)
Imbalance & unbalance	Voltage/current unbalance factors, sequence components	(Elsotohy et al., 2025; Mahmoud, 2021; Diniș et al., 2023)
Thermal stress	Equipment/busbar temperature vs. ratings	(Yahya et al., 2024; Siddiqui et al., 2024)
Component condition	Transformer/circuit breaker condition metrics, alarms	(Swain et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2025; Jin et al., 2022; Xu et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Operational variable groups indicative of substation stress
Power quality and component health as stress signals
•	THD, voltage/current unbalance, and power factor are repeatedly treated as core PQ phenomena that affect overall system stress and sensitive loads; combined PQ indices weight V_un, I_un, V_THD, I_THD, and PF to create a single stress score (Elsotohy et al., 2025; Diniș et al., 2023; Caicedo et al., 2023).
•	Busbar/connector temperature rises sharply with harmonic current distortion, making temperature plus current THD a direct indicator of thermal overstress risk (Yahya et al., 2024).
•	Condition monitoring metrics (DGA, PD, thermal and vibration data for transformers; multi-parameter risk indices for SF₆ breakers) provide equipment level stress and failure risk beyond system electrical variables (Wang et al., 2025; Jin et al., 2022; Xu et al., 2022).
Conclusion
For screening substation stress states and risk conditions, the most informative variables are those that express margins to thermal/voltage/PQ limits and equipment health, not raw measurements alone: loading and voltage performance indices, voltage stability indices, PQ metrics (THD, imbalance, PF), thermal measurements, and condition monitoring indicators for critical assets.
References

•	How should the data schema and measurement hierarchy be presented for reproducible power-system analytics research?
For reproducible power system analytics, data schemas should follow an explicit, hierarchical model of the grid (system → asset → measurement) with machine readable metadata and clear time series organization.
Recommended hierarchy and schema elements
1. Explicit system / asset hierarchy
PowerSystems.jl defines an abstract type hierarchy (system → bus/branch → generator/load/monitor) with separate containers for static network data and time series data; this separation and explicit object model are central to reproducibility and reuse (Lara et al., 2021).
PSML similarly organizes data by grid level and function (transmission vs. distribution; PMUs vs. loads/renewables) and ties measurements to concrete network elements in a joint T+D model (Zheng et al., 2021).
For PV analytics, the dataset is organized as site → inverter → (panel/optimizer), with clear indication of which levels are measured vs. aggregated (Lin et al., 2025).
Example hierarchical schema pattern
Level	Recommended entities / keys	Citations
System	grid_id, version, base_MVA, base_kV	(Lara et al., 2021; Zheng et al., 2021)
Topology	buses, lines, transformers, connectivity, coordinates	(Lara et al., 2021; Xu et al., 2025; Zheng et al., 2021)
Asset	generators, loads, inverters, meters, sensors	(Lara et al., 2021; Lin et al., 2025; Kryltcov et al., 2021)
Measurement channel	variable_name, phase, unit, sampling_rate, quality flags	(Lara et al., 2021; Lin et al., 2025; Alves et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2021)
Time series	(timestamp, value) arrays linked by measurement_id	(Lara et al., 2021; Lin et al., 2025; Zheng et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Common hierarchical layers in power-system data models
Presentation for reproducibility
•	Separate static and dynamic data: Store network model (topology, parameters) distinctly from time series measurements but with stable IDs linking them (Lara et al., 2021; Zheng et al., 2021).
•	Document measurement provenance and resolution: For each channel, provide sensor location in the hierarchy, device type, units, sampling interval, and missing data rates or uncertainty (Alves et al., 2023; Lin et al., 2025; Kryltcov et al., 2021).
•	Use structured folders / tables: PV and PSML datasets use nested folders and tables (system type → site / bus → channel) with standardized column names and units to make schemas self describing (Lin et al., 2025; Zheng et al., 2021).
•	Include metadata schemas and validation rules: PowerSystems.jl uses schema based validation on import; plasma MDS style metadata schemas are recommended to encode preprocessing, uncertainty treatment, and statistical methods for reuse (Lara et al., 2021; Alves et al., 2023).
Conclusion
For reproducible power system analytics, data should be organized in a clear hierarchy from grid to asset to measurement channel, with standardized IDs, units, sampling specs, quality/uncertainty metadata, and a documented mapping between static network models and time series files.

3.2.3 Data Scope, Coverage, and Quality Constraints
•	What forms of incompleteness, sparsity, or inconsistency are common in utility operational datasets used for academic research?
Utility operational datasets used in research commonly suffer from structured missingness, extreme sparsity, misalignment across systems, and poor or inconsistent metadata.
Common forms of incompleteness and sparsity
•	Random and continuous gaps in time series: Sensor failures, communication losses, and device limitations create both isolated missing points and long contiguous gaps; realistic utility-style scenarios include missing voltage/current channels, full substation outages, and temporal communication losses of 5–45 ms (Oelhaf et al., 2025). Large monitoring datasets in analogous domains show mixed single and sequential gaps and zero stretches treated as missing (AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025).
•	High overall sparsity / low data density: Public power-consumption datasets used for research can have data densities below 7%, forcing heavy reliance on reconstruction methods (Shi & Xie, 2025; Zheng et al., 2024). Smart grid load datasets are explicitly treated as high dimensional and incomplete, with 5–10% or more missingness typical in benchmarks (Xie & Xie, 2025).
•	Irregular sampling and reduced temporal resolution: Legacy or bandwidth limited infrastructure yields lower or changing sampling rates, effectively discarding data and creating irregular grids in time (Oelhaf et al., 2025).
Typical data-quality issues in power/critical infrastructure time series
Issue type	Example manifestations in research datasets	Citations
Point gaps & zeros	Single NaNs or zeros flagged as missing sensor values	(Kim & Kim, 2025; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025)
Long contiguous gaps	Multi-day/year gaps, full substation or device outages	(Oelhaf et al., 2025; Bikše et al., 2023)
Channel loss	Missing one phase, voltage-only or current-only records	(Oelhaf et al., 2025)
Sparse coverage	Very low observation density across time × device tensors	(Shi & Xie, 2025; Zheng et al., 2024)
Edge gaps	Unfillable missing values at dataset start/end after interpolation	(AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025)
Figure 1: Frequent gap and sparsity patterns in operations-like data
Inconsistency, misalignment, and metadata gaps
•	Fragmented observability and misaligned systems: Realistic distribution system studies report incomplete GIS links, inconsistent device labels, and partial SCADA coverage, requiring data driven topology reconstruction (Sun et al., 2025).
•	Inconsistent or missing metadata: Reviews of ML for protection note that many papers omit basic attributes like voltage level (34.5% of studies) and sampling frequency (52.1%), and often lack clear information on fault types and simulation tools (Oelhaf et al., 2025).
•	Label and class imbalance: Faults and disturbance events are rare and geographically localized, producing sparse and highly imbalanced labeled datasets and sometimes missing entire fault types (Oelhaf et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Utility style operational datasets used in academic work are typically incomplete in time (gaps, sparsity, irregular sampling), incomplete in space (missing channels, devices, or substations), and inconsistent across information systems and metadata, all of which must be addressed or documented for reproducible power system analytics.
References

Impact of Data Sparsity on Machine Learning for Fault Detection in Power System Protection. 2025 33rd European Signal Processing Conference (EUSIPCO), 1997-2001. https://doi.org/10.23919/eusipco63237.2025.11226584

•	How should data scope and quality constraints be documented to support methodological transparency?
Data scope and quality constraints should be documented explicitly, using structured templates that describe inclusion, limitations, missingness, and QC procedures in a way that is understandable and comparable across studies.
1. Clearly define and justify data scope
•	Population, domain, and coverage: Specify what the data represent (e.g., voltage level, grid type, geography, period, device types) and what is not covered (Oelhaf et al., 2025; Pezzutto et al., 2025; Schwabe et al., 2024).
•	Selection criteria and exclusions: Provide an explicit variable and record selection justification, mirroring “data specification file” practices that log which sources, fields, and linkages were included and why others were excluded (O'Sullivan et al., 2025).
•	Intended use and constraints: As in Data Cards, describe intended tasks, known mismatch between data and task (e.g., simulated vs. real faults, rare events), and any prohibited uses (Pushkarna et al., 2022; Oelhaf et al., 2025).
Example scope elements to report
Dimension	Minimal items to document	Citations
Domain & period	Systems covered, time range, temporal resolution	(Oelhaf et al., 2025; Pezzutto et al., 2025)
Sampling frame	Sensors/sources, inclusion–exclusion rules	(O'Sullivan et al., 2025; Schwabe et al., 2024)
Task fit	Whether data were collected for this task; known gaps	(Pushkarna et al., 2022; Oelhaf et al., 2025)
Access limits	Legal/privacy constraints, redactions, aggregation	(Pezzutto et al., 2025; O'Sullivan et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Core elements to define dataset scope and limits
2. Make data quality constraints explicit and quantified
•	Missing data: Report proportion, patterns (point vs. block), variables affected, and, where possible, missingness mechanisms; poor reporting on these is a major reproducibility problem in ML and trials (Nijman et al., 2021; Xin et al., 2025; Ren et al., 2023; Srijan & Rajagopalan, 2023).
•	Quality dimensions: Use a framework (e.g., METRIC or FAIR/QC indicators) to report completeness, consistency, accuracy, and known biases, not just “cleaned” status (Schwabe et al., 2024; Pezzutto et al., 2025; Whang et al., 2021).
•	Handling methods: Describe preprocessing and imputation strategies, assumptions, and sensitivity analyses, not only the final dataset (Nijman et al., 2021; Woods et al., 2021; Ren et al., 2023; Srijan & Rajagopalan, 2023).
•	Provenance and decision logs: Record transformation steps, linkage logic, and QC checks (ranges, min/max, IDs, small cell suppression) in a provenance tool or decision log to support auditing (O'Sullivan et al., 2025; Ozonze et al., 2023).
Conclusion
To support methodological transparency, authors should use structured documentation (e.g., Data Cards style or QC frameworks) that spells out what the data do and do not cover, quantifies key data-quality limits (missingness, bias, consistency), and records how these constraints were handled and justified in the analysis.

•	What literature-guided criteria are used to determine whether available substation data are adequate for risk-screening studies?
Utility risk-screening studies typically deem substation data “adequate” when they cover key assets and hazards, have enough spatial/temporal resolution to distinguish relative risk, and support at least coarse sensitivity analysis of assumptions.
Core adequacy criteria seen in the literature
1. Coverage of relevant assets, configurations, and loads
Risk-screening methods usually require, at minimum:
•	Substation configuration and main components (buses, transformers, lines, breakers) to model connectivity and contingencies (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024; Liang et al., 2022; Maia et al., 2021; Kumpalavalee et al., 2025)- Basic load and capacity information (loadability, transformer ratings, typical loading levels) to estimate unmet demand or loss-of-function indices (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024; Liang et al., 2022; Maia et al., 2021)- Criticality of connected loads (e.g., hospitals, industrial zones, dense urban areas) to prioritize sites (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024; Maia et al., 2021; Mahato et al., 2025; Kumpalavalee et al., 2025)If these are missing for a large share of substations, studies often restrict analysis to a better-documented subset or rely heavily on expert elicitation (Maia et al., 2021).
2. Hazard and context data sufficient to differentiate risk
For physical or climate hazards, adequacy typically means:
•	Location data accurate enough to link each substation to hazard fields (seismic intensity, flood depth, storm surge, climate projections) (Shah & Ntakou, 2025; Liang et al., 2022; Mahato et al., 2025)- At least coarse hazard curves or scenario values across the study region so relative risk can be mapped, even if high-resolution projections are unavailable (Shah & Ntakou, 2025; Liang et al., 2022)### 3. Minimum quality for comparative screening
Even with sparse or uncertain data, screening is considered feasible if:
•	Key fields are available consistently enough to compute standardized indices (e.g., non attended demand risk, criticality indices, SCI/TVCI) across most substations (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024; Maia et al., 2021; Mahato et al., 2025; Kumpalavalee et al., 2025)- Uncertainties (e.g., fragility parameters, hazard thresholds) can be probed via sensitivity analysis to show robustness of rankings (Shah & Ntakou, 2025; Silva-Ortega et al., 2024; Liang et al., 2022)- Where operational/failure histories are lacking, structured expert judgment can be used and documented for scoring risk factors (Maia et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Across seismic, climate, operational, and security studies, substation data are judged adequate for risk screening when they reliably describe topology and loading, can be linked to relevant hazards, and support consistent index calculation and sensitivity analysis, even if detailed failure statistics are absent.
References
Shah, H., & Ntakou, E. (2025). Assessing Climate Vulnerability Risk for Substations in Massachusetts Via Sensitivity Analysis. 2025 57th North American Power Symposium (NAPS), 1-6. https://doi.org/10.1109/naps66256.2025.11272243
Silva-Ortega, J., Ortíz, J., & Candelo-Becerra, J. (2024). A New Method to Assess the Reliability and Security of Urban Electrical Substations. Electricity. https://doi.org/10.3390/electricity5040050
Liang, H., Blagojević, N., Xie, Q., & Stojadinović, B. (2022). Seismic risk analysis of electrical substations based on the network analysis method. Earthquake Engineering & Structural Dynamics, 51, 2690 - 2707. https://doi.org/10.1002/eqe.3695
Maia, W., Ekel, P., Vieira, D., De Castro, E., De Oliveira, M., Reis, I., & Santos, K. (2021). Evaluation of Operational Risk in Power Substations and Its Rational Reduction on the Basis of Multicriteria Allocating Resources. IEEE Access, 9, 149383-149397. https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2021.3124529

Kumpalavalee, S., Suwanasri, T., Suwanasri, C., & Phadungthin, R. (2025). Risk Assessment Framework for Power Circuit Breakers Based on Condition, Replacement, and Criticality Indices. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18133298
3.3 Data Preprocessing and Quality Control
•	What preprocessing workflows are recommended for multi-sheet, hourly substation operational datasets?
For multi-sheet hourly substation datasets, workflows emphasize standardization (timestamps, units), cleaning (missing/outliers), sheet integration, and feature construction before modeling.
Core preprocessing workflow
1. Standardize and integrate across sheets
•	Parse each sheet (e.g., feeders, transformers, environment) to a tidy table: one timestamped row, one variable per column (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Fan et al., 2021; De Souza Duz et al., 2025).
•	Convert all timestamps to a common timezone, resolve daylight-saving days (23/25 h) by dropping extra hours and linearly interpolating missing ones (Giacomazzi et al., 2023).
•	Enforce consistent units and naming conventions across sheets (kW vs MW, °C, IDs) before merging on substation ID + hour (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Hanžel et al., 2025).
Typical substation-hour preprocessing steps
Step	Recommended actions	Citations
Time alignment	Harmonize DST, resample to 1 h, ensure complete hourly index	(Giacomazzi et al., 2023; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Fan et al., 2021)
Sheet merging	Join by substation + time, resolve duplicates, drop impossible combos	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Sun et al., 2025)
Cleaning	Remove or flag null records, invalid flags, extreme outliers	(Giacomazzi et al., 2023; Mukkamala & Sunku, 2025; Liu et al., 2022)
Imputation	Use simple (linear, carry forward) or ML based imputation, document masks	(Giacomazzi et al., 2023; Fan et al., 2021; Liu et al., 2022)
Scaling	Normalize/standardize for ML models (e.g., Min–Max, Z score)	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Mukkamala & Sunku, 2025; Fan et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Key steps for hourly substation data preprocessing
2. Data cleaning, imputation, and reduction
•	Missing values: Identify gaps per substation; when sparse, use linear interpolation or similar; when extensive, consider discarding that substation or day (Giacomazzi et al., 2023; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Fan et al., 2021).
•	Outliers: Detect outages or sensor errors via IQR or domain limits, then remove and interpolate or mask for models (Giacomazzi et al., 2023; Mukkamala & Sunku, 2025; Liu et al., 2022).
•	Quality flags: Exclude or repair records tagged “provisional/defective/incorrect” before modeling (Giacomazzi et al., 2023).
•	Dimensionality reduction: For high dimensional or multi-sensor settings, apply PCA or feature selection before ML to improve stability (De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Haben et al., 2021; Fan et al., 2021; Sahu et al., 2021).
3. Feature engineering for risk / forecasting / detection
•	Aggregate hourly profiles to daily or typical load curves per substation for clustering or risk screening (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
•	Derive temperature signatures or heat-map representations when comparing efficiency/fault patterns across many substations (Weber et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Recommended workflows convert multi-sheet hourly substation data into a unified, time-aligned, quality-controlled table; handle missingness and outliers transparently; then scale, reduce, and engineer features appropriate to the target application (forecasting, risk screening, or fault detection).
References

•	How are data cleaning, validation, and quality control typically performed in power-system operational analytics?
Power-system operational analytics typically use rule-based filters plus statistical/ML anomaly detection, followed by targeted repair, with explicit quality metrics to validate cleaned data.
Typical data cleaning workflows
1. Rule-based and physics-based filtering
•	Remove impossible or non-physical values (negative power, wind speed, or rotation speed; power >110% rated; zero power above cut in speed) and rows with missing key fields (Martín-Calzada et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2025).
•	Enforce operational criteria (e.g., relationships between wind speed and power, turbine cut in rules, hydropower unit operating ranges) as first-stage filters (Zhang et al., 2024; Zhang et al., 2023; Martín-Calzada et al., 2025).
•	Apply signal filtering (low-pass, median) to reduce noise in voltage/current/temperature signals before feature extraction or modeling (Zhu, 2024; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025).
2. Statistical and ML-based anomaly detection
•	Use quartile/IQR, change-point, and other statistical tests for sparse outliers and stacked anomalies in SCADA data (Yang et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Li et al., 2025).
•	Apply clustering (k means, DBSCAN, HDBSCAN) or density-based methods to separate normal from abnormal operating regimes in wind, hydro, and PV datasets (Yang et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2023; Li et al., 2025).
•	Use one-class or unsupervised models (Isolation Forest, LOF, OC-SVM, ABOD, fuzzy-voting ensembles) for high-dimensional power-quality and IoT data streams (Patrizi et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2025; Lv et al., 2025; Muñoz et al., 2024; Martín-Calzada et al., 2025; Ogrizović et al., 2024).
Representative cleaning methods and targets
Target data	Main cleaning tools	Citations
Wind SCADA	Rules + quartiles + RANSAC / clustering	(Yang et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Martín-Calzada et al., 2025; Li et al., 2025)
PV plants	Interpolation, outlier detection, image/RDIP cleaning	(Zang et al., 2024)
Hydro units	HDBSCAN + GAN-based imputation	(Zhang et al., 2023)
Power quality / IoT	ML-based anomaly detection, noise filters	(Patrizi et al., 2024; Muñoz et al., 2024; Zhu, 2024; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Common cleaning tools by data/application type
Validation and quality control
•	Imputation/repair quality checked via RMSE/MAE between filled and true data, and distribution distances (KL, JS, Hellinger) to ensure repaired data match original statistics (Zhang et al., 2023; Tolnai et al., 2025; Lei et al., 2024; Lv et al., 2025).
•	Forecasting/analytics back-testing: evaluate how cleaning affects downstream models (NILM, wind/PV forecasting, early-warning), expecting reduced NMAE/MAE/RMSE and improved F1/accuracy (Tolnai et al., 2025; Morrison et al., 2021; Yang et al., 2025; Lei et al., 2024; Lv et al., 2025; Zang et al., 2024).
•	Quality metrics/scores: multivariate profiling and aggregated scores (e.g., Building Quality Score) quantify missingness, timestamp irregularities, and sensor inconsistencies, linking them to analytics performance (Tolnai et al., 2025; Goknil et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Across wind, PV, hydro, and power-quality applications, operational analytics rely on staged QC: physics-based rules, statistical/ML anomaly detection, and specialized imputation, with rigorous validation against both ground-truth where available and downstream model performance.

•	What preprocessing decisions most strongly affect downstream clustering and risk-modelling results in time-series engineering studies?
Preprocessing choices about scale, outliers/missingness, temporal structure, and class balance most strongly shape clustering structure and risk model performance in time series engineering studies.
1. Data cleaning and transformations
•	Outlier and anomaly handling: Order of operations matters. Automated pipeline reviews recommend detecting and treating isolated and consecutive anomalies before any scaling or stationarity transforms, because outliers can severely distort both (Meisenbacher et al., 2022).
•	Missing data imputation: Choice of imputer (e.g., k NN, EMAs, Bayesian ridge, KNN/DataWig) substantially changes forecast and risk metrics; some methods (e.g., cubic/spline interpolation) perform poorly and should be avoided in high stakes settings (Usmani et al., 2024).
•	Scale and distribution transforms: A multiverse analysis shows preprocessing (outliers, imputation, transformations) can explain up to 99.8% of variance in effect estimates, dwarfing model choice; standardization or log transforms can shrink estimated effects by >90% or, if omitted, inflate them by an order of magnitude (Veltri, 2025).
Key preprocessing levers
Decision type	Typical options	Effect on clustering / risk models	Citations
Outliers	Remove, winsorize, model explicitly	Changes cluster boundaries, tail risk estimates	(Meisenbacher et al., 2022; Usmani et al., 2024; , 2025)
Imputation	Mean/linear vs k NN/EMAs/ML	Alters dependence structure, volatility, risk	(Mei et al., 2025; Usmani et al., 2024; , 2025)
Transform	None vs standardize/log	Rescales distances, affects feature importance	(Usmani et al., 2024; Tiwary et al., 2025; Veltri, 2025; , 2025)
Noise/denoising	Filters, wavelets	Affects shape based clusters and anomalies	(Ao & Fayek, 2023; Sharma & Bandhu, 2025; Dong et al., 2025)
Class/label balance	Over/undersampling, SMOTE variants	Strongly affects rare event risk prediction	(De Vargas et al., 2022; , 2025)
Figure 1: Major preprocessing choices and their impacts
2. Temporal structure and representation
•	Windowing and feature engineering: Window length and lag/rolling feature design can let simple models outperform deep ones by exposing relevant dynamics, or hide key patterns if chosen poorly (Ao & Fayek, 2023; , 2025).
•	Resampling and alignment: Decisions on aggregation (e.g., hourly→daily), handling irregular timestamps, and detrending/seasonal adjustment strongly affect similarity measures, cluster stability, and extreme value behaviour crucial for risk (Ao & Fayek, 2023; Sharma & Bandhu, 2025; Meisenbacher et al., 2022; , 2025).
Conclusion
Across time series engineering studies, the dominant levers on clustering structure and quantitative risk estimates are how anomalies and missingness are treated, how scale/distribution are transformed, and how temporal structure is encoded. These preprocessing choices typically matter more than the specific clustering or modelling algorithm and should be stress tested via sensitivity or multiverse analyses.

3.3.1 Timestamp Harmonization and Monthly-Sheet Consolidation
•	What best practices exist for harmonizing timestamps and consolidating monthly substation records into a single analysis-ready time series?
Harmonizing timestamps and merging monthly substation files is best done by enforcing a single time standard, resolving clock offsets, and resampling all signals onto a common, regular grid before concatenation.
Core timestamp harmonization practices
1.	Adopt a single time reference and accuracy target
o	Use UTC with explicit offset and document the required accuracy class (e.g., IEC 61850 5 classes: ≥100 ms for SCADA logging, 10 ms for PQ, 1 ms–1 µs for protection/WAMPAC) (Macii & Rinaldi, 2022).
o	In modern substations, synchronize clocks via GPS or IEEE 1588 PTP; hardware timestamping can reach ≤1 µs, software PTP ≈10 µs (Khan & Hayes, 2023; Macii & Rinaldi, 2022; Liu et al., 2025; Jones et al., 2021).
2.	Correct clock drift and misalignment across devices
o	Systems integrating multi sensor streams (temperature, DGA, FRA, electrical quantities) use network protocol timestamping plus data fusion routines to align devices with heterogeneous sampling rates on a common time axis (De Souza Duz et al., 2025).
o	Fault diagnosis frameworks explicitly estimate and correct deviations between SCADA and IED clocks before aligning alarms and events (Zhou et al., 2025).
3.	Detect and fix bad timestamps
o	Check for duplicate timestamps, backward jumps, and large gaps; incorrect timing has been shown to corrupt phase angles and force operators to suspend synchrophasor applications until all devices agree on time (Jones et al., 2021; Macii & Rinaldi, 2022).
o	Use consistency checks against known event times or neighboring stations; where needed, interpolate or drop clearly corrupt segments (Macii & Rinaldi, 2022; Jones et al., 2021).
Building an analysis ready series from monthly files
Step	Practice	Citations
1	Normalize all timestamps to UTC, applying known offsets	(Macii & Rinaldi, 2022; Khan & Hayes, 2023; Liu et al., 2025)
2	Snap readings to a chosen resolution (e.g., 1 min, 15 min) and resample/aggregate within each month	(Macii & Rinaldi, 2022; De Souza Duz et al., 2025)
3	Align variables with different sampling periods via nearest neighbor or interpolation within a small time tolerance	(De Souza Duz et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2025)
4	Concatenate months, ensure continuous index, and record boundaries/metadata	(Zhang et al., 2021; Nascimento et al., 2025; De Souza Duz et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Typical pipeline from raw logs to unified series
Conclusion
Effective consolidation of monthly substation records hinges on unified time standards, explicit correction of clock offsets, rigorous detection of timestamp errors, and resampling all signals onto a single, well documented time grid before concatenation.

Zhou, C., Qi, P., Feng, Z., Liu, X., & Li, Y. (2025). Self-Learning Fault Diagnosis Method for Intelligent Substations Based on Large Models and Multi-Source Data Fusion. 2025 4th International Conference on Green Energy and Power Systems (ICGEPS), 198-201. https://doi.org/10.1109/icgeps65133.2025.11034431
•	How should inconsistencies in date formats, missing hour labels, or duplicated timestamps be treated in utility data?
Utility time-series data with dirty timestamps should be standardized to a single format, gap-filled as explicit missing times, and have duplicated timestamps either dropped or aggregated, depending on business logic.
Standardizing and validating timestamps
•	Normalize formats first: Convert all date–time strings (e.g., dd/mm/yyyy, mm/dd/yyyy, custom codes) into a single canonical format (e.g., POSIX/ISO 8601 with timezone) before any analysis (Faybishenko et al., 2021; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025).
•	Check temporal consistency: After conversion, sort by time and compute time differences to detect non consecutive order, negative intervals, or irregular spacing, then correct ordering and flag abnormal intervals (Faybishenko et al., 2021).
•	Align to a target frequency (e.g., 15 min, hourly) and insert missing timestamps as explicit rows with NaNs, preserving temporal structure for later imputation (AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Wang et al., 2025; Faybishenko et al., 2021).
Handling missing hour labels
•	If the clock code or hour label is wrong or missing but can be inferred (e.g., from adjacent regular 30 min readings), reconstruct timestamps and create a coherent series; otherwise, drop unusable records to avoid inconsistent alignment (Oprea et al., 2021; Hijazi et al., 2024; Faybishenko et al., 2021).
•	Insert any truly missing time slots as NA rows rather than silently skipping them, which improves downstream imputation and anomaly detection (AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Wang et al., 2025).
Treating duplicated timestamps
Research distinguishes two cases in smart/utility data (Kasaraneni et al., 2022; Oprea et al., 2021; Faybishenko et al., 2021):
1.	Same timestamp, same value
o	Typically treated as redundant duplicates; safe to keep one record and drop the rest after logging counts for QA/QC (Kasaraneni et al., 2022; Faybishenko et al., 2021).
2.	Same timestamp, different values
o	Indicates meter glitches, communication errors, or fraud attempts and is a high impact anomaly (Kasaraneni et al., 2022; Oprea et al., 2021).
o	Recommended actions: 
	Isolate these rows, inspect context (neighboring values, other meters), and either 
	aggregate (e.g., mean/sum) if domain rules justify it, or
	discard and mark as missing if the correct value cannot be reliably inferred (Kasaraneni et al., 2022; Oprea et al., 2021; Faybishenko et al., 2021).
Summary Table
Issue type	Recommended treatment	Citations
Inconsistent formats	Parse to unified POSIX/ISO with TZ; then sort	(Faybishenko et al., 2021; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025)
Missing hour/slots	Infer where safe; else insert NA rows	(AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Oprea et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2025; Faybishenko et al., 2021)
Duplicate, same value	Keep one, drop others	(Kasaraneni et al., 2022; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Faybishenko et al., 2021)
Duplicate, different values	Investigate; aggregate or set to missing	(Kasaraneni et al., 2022; Oprea et al., 2021; Faybishenko et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Standard treatments for common timestamp issues
Conclusion
For utility time series, rigorous timestamp standardization, explicit representation of missing hours, and principled handling of duplicates (drop redundant, investigate conflicting) are core best practices to maintain analysis-ready, trustworthy data.

Challenging problems of quality assurance and quality control (QA/QC) of meteorological time series data. Stochastic Environmental Research and Risk Assessment, 36, 1049 - 1062. https://doi.org/10.1007/s00477-021-02106-w
•	What methodological checks are recommended after temporal consolidation of operational power-system data?
After temporal consolidation, operational power system data should be checked for temporal coherence, statistical consistency, physical/network plausibility, and residual data quality issues before analysis or modelling.
1. Temporal and statistical consistency
•	Verify distributional properties over time: Compare probability density, autocorrelation function, and power spectral density before vs. after consolidation to ensure diurnal/seasonal structure and variability are preserved (Cramer et al., 2021).
•	For more complex patterns (peaks, bursts, plateaus), apply multifractal detrended fluctuation analysis (MFDFA) to confirm scaling behaviour is not distorted (Cramer et al., 2021).
•	Perform exploratory data analysis on the consolidated series (seasonality, trends, outliers, load–weather relationships) to confirm plausible temporal patterns for load, generation, or prices (Javed et al., 2021).
Example post consolidation checks
Check type	Purpose	Citations
PDF / ACF / PSD comparison	Preserve variability and memory	(Cramer et al., 2021)
MFDFA	Preserve bursts/peaks structure	(Cramer et al., 2021)
Seasonality & feature EDA	Confirm realistic temporal patterns	(Javed et al., 2021)
Missing data pattern analysis	Identify blocks vs random gaps	(Wang et al., 2025; Hu et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Key statistical checks after time aggregation
2. Data quality and anomaly checks
•	Run bad data detection on the unified series to catch remaining loss, corruption, synchronization anomalies, and noise; LSTM based detectors and other deep models are effective for mixed bad data patterns in power system time series (Qin & Tian, 2025; Li et al., 2021).
•	Apply signal quality indices (SQIs) to audit noise level, clipping, flat lining, and other “silent failures” in the consolidated signals (Gao et al., 2024).
•	Use multimodal or spatio temporal anomaly detection where SCADA, PMU, log, or frequency domain features are fused to spot cyber or operational anomalies (Qu et al., 2024; De Caro et al., 2023; Chen et al., 2025).
•	When imputation was used during consolidation, validate imputed segments with held out data or advanced imputation models (e.g., convolutional transformer, denoising autoencoders) and quantify reconstruction error (Hu et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2025).
3. Physical and network plausibility
•	Perform rule based sanity checks and offline power flow or hosting capacity studies to ensure that aggregated time series lead to physically consistent grid states and feasible voltages/flows (Ali et al., 2025).
•	For dynamic/security related data, verify that post consolidation trajectories support accurate dynamic security assessment and fault localization when used in data driven DSSA or GNN/FNO models (De Caro et al., 2023; Lu & Bu, 2025; Qu et al., 2024).
Conclusion
After consolidating utility time series, robust practice is to (1) confirm temporal/statistical structure is preserved, (2) re audit signal quality and anomalies with modern ML and SQIs, and (3) test physical/network plausibility via power system models, ensuring the dataset is trustworthy for downstream forecasting, security assessment, or planning.

3.3.2 Missing Data Handling and Exclusion Logic
•	What methods are recommended for handling missing values in hourly substation datasets used for operational modelling?
For hourly substation data, use time series–aware imputation that preserves seasonality and cross variable structure; simple interpolation is fine for short gaps, while KNN/seasonal or ML/deep models are preferred for longer or complex gaps.
Key method families
1. Simple and time series statistical methods (baseline)
•	Listwise/row deletion is strongly discouraged: it destroys temporal continuity and biases models for time dependent data (Li et al., 2023; Niako et al., 2024).
•	Linear / spline / Stineman interpolation work well for short, isolated hourly gaps, preserving continuity and giving high R² (≈0.9–0.97) in environmental time series and solar/irradiance applications (Ahn et al., 2021; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Denhard et al., 2021; García et al., 2022; Niako et al., 2024).
•	Kalman filtering/smoothing and EWMA / moving average interpolation are robust across a range of missingness levels and often give stable forecasting performance with ARIMA/LSTM models (Niako et al., 2024; García et al., 2022).
2. Seasonality  and structure aware methods (strong fit for load/substation data)
•	K nearest neighbour (KNN) imputation in the time domain is frequently a strong performer for multivariate time series forecasting tasks, including energy and air quality data (Emmanuel et al., 2021; Ahn et al., 2021; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; García et al., 2022).
•	For power demand, Seasonal KNN (SKNN) that augments features with day of week/year and other seasonal indicators substantially reduces MAE/RMSE versus plain KNN, especially under random and block clipping up to 70% missingness (Vasenin et al., 2024).
•	Historical data–informed regression (H DIRT) using past seasonal profiles gives similar accuracy to SKNN at lower computational cost when the missing share is moderate (Vasenin et al., 2024).
3. Machine learning and deep models (for large, multivariate grids)
•	Random forest–based missForest and other tree ensembles often outperform simpler methods on complex, nonlinear power/price or meteorological time series, including electricity prices and multivariate weather/energy data (Emmanuel et al., 2021; Li et al., 2023; Mari & Baldassari, 2021).
•	Attention based and transformer/DeepMVI style imputation models that jointly exploit temporal context and cross series correlations significantly reduce imputation error (often >50% over classical methods) and improve downstream analytics on multivariate time series (Bansal et al., 2021; Bülte et al., 2023; Kazijevs & Samad, 2023).
•	Benchmarks on smart meter and energy data show Kalman smoothing and advanced time series foundation models as top candidates; choice depends on the trade off between accuracy and computational cost (Sartipi et al., 2025; Bülte et al., 2023).
4. Practical guidance for operational substation modelling
Situation in hourly substation data	Recommended methods	Sources
Very short, isolated gaps (<3–4 h)	Linear/spline/Stine, Kalman smoothing	(AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Denhard et al., 2021; García et al., 2022; Niako et al., 2024)
Diurnal/weekly seasonal load, modest missingness	Seasonal KNN, H DIRT style regression, KNN	(Ahn et al., 2021; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Vasenin et al., 2024; García et al., 2022)
Long gaps, many variables (multi feeder, weather, prices)	missForest/RF, attention/transformer imputation, DeepMVI like models	(Emmanuel et al., 2021; Bansal et al., 2021; Li et al., 2023; Bülte et al., 2023; Kazijevs & Samad, 2023; Mari & Baldassari, 2021)
Figure 1: Imputation choices by gap type and complexity
Conclusion
For hourly substation datasets, avoid deletion and naive mean filling. Use interpolation or Kalman methods for short gaps; adopt seasonality aware KNN/regression for routine operational load data; and consider RF/attention based deep imputers when datasets are large, multivariate, and used for high stakes operational modelling.

Mari, C., & Baldassari, C. (2021). Ensemble Methods for Jump-Diffusion Models of Power Prices. Energies, 14, 2084. https://doi.org/10.3390/en14082084
•	How do previous studies justify interpolation, deletion, or imputation choices in engineering time-series analysis?
Engineering time-series studies typically justify missing-data handling by its impact on temporal structure and downstream task accuracy, not just raw imputation error.
Common justifications for avoiding deletion
•	Serial dependence and continuity: Deleting records breaks equal time steps and undermines ARIMA/LSTM type models that assume continuous, ordered data (Niako et al., 2024; Ahn et al., 2021).
•	Loss of temporal dependencies: Case deletion “is highly likely to fail in modeling the time dependencies” and distort autocorrelation in time series (Niako et al., 2024; Ahn et al., 2021).
•	Information loss: Reviews emphasize that deletion discards valuable dynamics, especially with longer gaps, and is used only as a baseline or for very small missingness (Petrusevich, 2021; Noufel et al., 2024).
How studies justify interpolation / simple imputation choices
•	Model feasibility: Many papers state that conventional time series models “cannot begin even when there is one single missing value,” so some imputation is required before ARIMA/LSTM/forecasting (Niako et al., 2024; Petrusevich, 2021; Bülte et al., 2023).
•	Preservation of basic structure: Linear/spline/Stineman, moving averages, and Kalman methods are recommended where they maintain distribution and autocorrelation reasonably while enabling forecasting (Niako et al., 2024; Petrusevich, 2021; Bülte et al., 2023; Ramadhani et al., 2024).
•	Empirical downstream performance: Several works benchmark interpolation methods by training forecasting or classification models on the imputed series and picking the method that gives best predictive accuracy, not lowest imputation error alone (Niako et al., 2024; Ahn et al., 2021; Kazijevs & Samad, 2023; Bülte et al., 2023).
•	Gap characteristics: Reviews and energy/PM2.5 studies justify interpolation for short, isolated gaps, showing low RMSE/SMAPE versus more complex methods, and warn it degrades with long or highly nonlinear gaps (Petrusevich, 2021; Bülte et al., 2023; Flores et al., 2023).
How studies justify advanced imputation / integrated models
•	Complex dependencies: Transformer, GAN, attention and diffusion models are motivated by the need to capture both temporal and cross series correlations that simple interpolation ignores, and are justified by large error reductions and better downstream analytics across many datasets (Yildiz et al., 2022; Bansal et al., 2021; Ni & Cao, 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Bülte et al., 2023; Tashiro et al., 2021).
•	Data centric selection: Benchmarking reviews argue that no single method dominates; choice should depend on missing pattern, rate, variable properties, and target task, evaluated via both imputation and predictive metrics (Niako et al., 2024; Kazijevs & Samad, 2023).
Summary Table: Typical Justification Logic
Choice	Main justification pattern	Citations
Deletion	Only as naive baseline; harms dependencies	(Niako et al., 2024; Ahn et al., 2021; Petrusevich, 2021)
Simple interpolation/Kalman	Enough to restore continuity; good for short gaps; validated via forecast error	(Niako et al., 2024; Petrusevich, 2021; Bülte et al., 2023; Ramadhani et al., 2024; Flores et al., 2023)
Advanced DL / GAN / diffusion	Needed for complex, multivariate, high missingness settings; shown to improve imputation + downstream tasks	(Yildiz et al., 2022; Bansal et al., 2021; Ni & Cao, 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Kazijevs & Samad, 2023; Bülte et al., 2023; Tashiro et al., 2021)
Figure 1: How prior work motivates missing-data strategies
Conclusion
Across engineering and related time series domains, researchers justify interpolation, deletion, or imputation primarily by: (1) theoretical compatibility with temporal dependence; (2) the gap pattern and series complexity; and (3) empirical impact on downstream forecasting or classification, rather than by interpolation error alone.

•	What exclusion logic should be applied to periods with structurally invalid, zero-filled, or incomplete substation records?
Exclude structurally invalid or clearly non-physical substation periods; treat zero filled or incomplete records as missing and only retain intervals where physical and structural checks pass.
Principles from prior work
1. Treat structural errors and zero fills as missing, not data
•	In multi sensor environmental time series, all zero readings are conservatively re coded as missing (NaN) when it is unclear whether they are true zeros or artifacts of sensor/telemetry failure, to avoid distorting later interpolation and analysis (AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025).
•	The handling is sequential: first convert structurally impossible/zero filled segments to missing, then impute or exclude depending on gap length and context (AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025).
•	Industrial energy monitoring studies explicitly categorize continuous vs random missing to reflect sensor/communication faults vs sporadic errors before imputation and downstream modelling (Wang et al., 2025).
Suggested exclusion logic for substation data
Record type / period	Recommended logic	Citations
Structurally invalid (schema errors, impossible states, unbalanced power, time misalignment)	Always exclude from modelling and flag; these violate physical/structural constraints and cannot be trusted.	(Sun et al., 2025; Goknil et al., 2023)
Zero filled blocks known to be placeholders (e.g., outage of RTU, communication drop)	Recode as missing; exclude from analyses that require actual load, or impute only if gaps are short and context supports it.	(AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Khan et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2025)
Long continuous gaps (sensor failure)	Mark as missing and exclude from training or high stakes operational use; only use if robust imputation model is validated on similar gaps.	(AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Khan et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2025)
Incomplete records (some key variables missing: power, voltage, status)	Use only if task allows partial information; otherwise treat hour as missing and drop for tasks needing full state.	(Khan et al., 2022; Goknil et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Heuristic exclusion rules by error pattern
Conclusion
For operational substation modelling, periods that are structurally invalid, schema corrupt, or clearly zero filled placeholders should be excluded outright and treated as missing. Zero filled or incomplete segments that plausibly reflect sensor/telemetry issues are first re coded as missing, then either imputed (for short gaps with sufficient context) or excluded from model fitting and evaluation when gaps are long or affect critical variables.
