3.3.3 Outlier Screening and Consistency Checks
•	What statistical and engineering-based methods are commonly used for outlier screening in power-system operational data?
Power-system operational data use both simple statistical rules and physics-/model-based checks, increasingly complemented by ML outlier detectors.
1. Common statistical screening methods
Pointwise / univariate methods
•	Z-score / 3 sigma rules on power, current, voltage, frequency; sometimes “modified Z-score” with robust scale estimators (MAD, Sn, hybrids) to reduce sensitivity to non Gaussian data (Jung et al., 2025; Patel et al., 2023; Yaro et al., 2024).
•	Interquartile range (IQR) rules (e.g., Q1–k·IQR, Q3+k·IQR) to flag minor/major outliers without normality assumptions (Elmasry & Wadi, 2022; Zheng et al., 2025).
•	Change-point and control-chart style tests, e.g., CUSUM for shifts in mean/variance in satellite or grid subsystems (Mostafa et al., 2025).
Multivariate / distributional methods
•	Density-, distance- and clustering-based: Local Outlier Factor (LOF), DBSCAN, Local Distance based Outlier Factor (LDOF), k means and variants, often outperform simple 3 sigma on simulated feeder data (Patel et al., 2023).
•	ECOD (empirical CDF–based) and related distribution free tail probability methods for scalable, interpretable screening (Li et al., 2022).
Representative statistical toolbox
Category	Typical methods in power data	Citations
Basic rules	3 sigma, Z-/mZ score, IQR	(Jung et al., 2025; Patel et al., 2023; Elmasry & Wadi, 2022; Yaro et al., 2024; Zheng et al., 2025)
Density/distance	LOF, LDOF, DBSCAN, ECOD	(Chen et al., 2025; Patel et al., 2023; Yaro et al., 2024; Li et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2024)
Change/variance	CUSUM, variance change rate	(Mostafa et al., 2025; Zheng et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Core statistical families used for screening
2. Engineering and physics-based methods
•	Physical rule filters: Enforce turbine / substation operating limits and monotonicity (e.g., feasible wind speed–power envelope, voltage and current bounds), removing points that violate basic laws before statistical detection (Zheng et al., 2025).
•	Model-based residual checks: Fit power curves or predictive models (e.g., RANSAC regression, Mixture-of-Experts, graph-signal models) and flag points with large residuals relative to adaptive thresholds (often IQR based) (Mostafa et al., 2025; Zheng et al., 2025; Ramakrishna & Scaglione, 2021).
•	SCADA / topology consistency: Use network equations and grid topology; graph-signal and GNN approaches exploit spatial–temporal consistency across buses/lines to detect incompatible measurements (Qiu et al., 2025; Ramakrishna & Scaglione, 2021).
3. ML / hybrid anomaly-detection frameworks
•	One-class / autoencoder / OC SVM models trained on normal operation to flag deviations in voltage or load patterns (Elmasry & Wadi, 2022; Carratù et al., 2023; Siniosoglou et al., 2021; Himeur et al., 2021).
•	Hybrid pipelines: rule-based preprocessing + statistical screening + ML refinement (e.g., physical rules → robust regression + IQR → morphological refinement for wind farms) (Zheng et al., 2025); or multimodal time/frequency features plus classifiers, density, and clustering methods (Chen et al., 2025; Carratù et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Outlier screening in power-system operational data typically starts with physics-based validity checks and simple statistical rules (Z/3 sigma, IQR), then uses density/distance or model-residual methods, and increasingly ML (OC SVM, autoencoders, GNNs) to capture complex multivariate, spatio temporal anomalies.

Qiu, J., Zhang, X., Wang, T., Hou, H., Wang, S., & Yang, T. (2025). A GNN-Based False Data Detection Scheme for Smart Grids. Algorithms, 18, 166. https://doi.org/10.3390/a18030166
Himeur, Y., Ghanem, K., Alsalemi, A., Bensaali, F., & Amira, A. (2021). Artificial intelligence based anomaly detection of energy consumption in buildings: A review, current trends and new perspectives. Applied Energy, 287, 116601. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.apenergy.2021.116601
Ramakrishna, R., & Scaglione, A. (2021). Grid-Graph Signal Processing (Grid-GSP): A Graph Signal Processing Framework for the Power Grid. IEEE Transactions on Signal Processing, 69, 2725-2739. https://doi.org/10.1109/tsp.2021.3075145
•	How can operational consistency checks be performed across related variables such as total load, feeder sums, and busbar voltage behavior?
Operational consistency checks should enforce basic conservation laws and compare observed behavior against physically and statistically consistent multivariate patterns across feeders and busbars.
Structural/physics-based consistency checks
1.	Power balance at substation and feeders
o	Check that total substation load ≈ sum of feeder loads + known losses within a tolerance band that reflects feeder impedances and typical technical losses (Singh et al., 2021; Lim et al., 2025).
o	Use ZIP or similar load models in power-flow calculations: compare measured feeder powers and bus voltages with load-flow results; flag intervals where residuals exceed thresholds (Mossie et al., 2025; Singh et al., 2021).
2.	Voltage–load relationship checks
o	At a given tap position, busbar voltages should vary with total load and feeder loading patterns in line with network models or historical profiles (Mossie et al., 2025; Singh et al., 2021; Lim et al., 2025).
o	Use bounds from operational constraints (e.g., node voltage limits, OLTC tap ranges) and reject states where voltages violate feasibility while loads remain plausible (Liu et al., 2025; Boričić et al., 2021; Singh et al., 2021).
Example cross-variable rules
Check type	Logic across variables	Citations
Feeder sum vs. total		P_sub – ΣP_feeders
Voltage vs. load	For each tap, V_busbar within learned band vs. (P_total, Q_total) or AC power-flow result	(Mossie et al., 2025; Boričić et al., 2021; Singh et al., 2021; Lim et al., 2025)
Feeder-wise voltage	End-of-feeder voltages monotone with distance/load pattern; big inversions indicate errors	(Mossie et al., 2025; Boričić et al., 2021; Singh et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Illustrative cross-variable consistency conditions
Data-driven multivariate consistency
•	Correlation / latent-space checks: Kernel PCA or similar can monitor correlation among voltages and loads; changes in this structure reveal inconsistent measurements even under varying loads (Li et al., 2025; Bertozzi et al., 2023; Sharma et al., 2025).
•	State-estimation residuals: Use (transmission–distribution) state estimation with a consistency check vector at boundary buses; large, localized residuals point to bad measurements in total or feeder loads vs. bus voltages (Bertozzi et al., 2023; Sharma et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Robust operational consistency checks combine (1) power-balance and voltage-feasibility tests between total load, feeder sums, and busbar voltages, and (2) multivariate data-driven or state-estimation residual checks that learn normal joint behavior and flag intervals where these related variables cease to be physically and statistically coherent.
References

Lim, H., Jo, J., & Chun, K. (2025). Optimal On-Load Tap Changer Tap Control Method for Voltage Compliance Rate Improvement in Distribution Systems, Based on Field Measurement Data. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18020439
Sharma, N., Chakrabarti, S., & Sharma, A. (2025). Coordinated State Estimation of Power Transmission and Distribution Systems. IET Generation, Transmission & Distribution. https://doi.org/10.1049/gtd2.70163
•	What literature supports distinguishing true abnormal operating events from erroneous records in substation datasets?
Distinguishing true abnormal events from erroneous records in substations relies on multi-source, physics-aware, and often ML-based anomaly classification.
Evidence from power-system / substation literature
1. Explicit “event vs bad data” classification
•	A synchrophasor anomaly tool (SyADC) uses an ensemble of Isolation Forest, K Means, and LoOP to first detect anomalies, then classifies each as power-system event vs bad data (communication/measurement problems), achieving >99% recall for both classes on PMU datasets (Khaledian et al., 2021).
•	In nuclear and transformer substation datasets, Neural Normal Stochastic Process and other deep models learn normal temporal patterns and treat persistent, system consistent deviations as true anomalies, while short, context inconsistent spikes are labeled sensor/data issues (Liu et al., 2025).
2. Cross-domain consistency and redundancy
•	A multimodal framework fuses SCADA/PMU signals, statistics and logs to distinguish cyber attacks and operational disturbances from noise by exploiting correlations across domains; inconsistent anomalies confined to a single data mode are flagged as data errors (Zhao et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2025).
•	A synchrophasor price/phasor method compares SCADA estimates with PMU measurements; mismatches indicate recording deviation or device error, while jointly abnormal SCADA+PMU signatures indicate real system events (Zhao et al., 2025).
•	Time stamp anomaly detection in WAMS uses disturbance onset alignment across many PMUs: if one substation’s reported disturbance time is inconsistent with others, it is labeled as synchronization error rather than a real local event (Fan et al., 2025).
3. Cyber-physical and IEC 61850 substation studies
•	Deep and LSTM-based IDS for GOOSE and other IEC 61850 traffic are trained with labels for legitimate protection events vs malicious/erroneous packets, using temporal patterns and physical plant features to reject packets that do not match plausible power-system behavior (Wang et al., 2022; Zaboli et al., 2024; Nhung-Nguyen et al., 2024).
•	Data driven CVT self diagnosis compares three phase residual subspaces against rigid symmetry constraints; violations confined to measurement space indicate metering error, while system wide voltage changes indicate genuine events (Zhang et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Key literature supports using ensemble anomaly classifiers, multi-source/physics consistency checks, and cyber physical models to separate genuine abnormal operating events from erroneous substation records, especially in PMU/WAMS, IEC 61850, and instrument transformer monitoring contexts.

3.4 Physics-Guided Proxy Indicator Formulation
•	What methodological principles should guide the construction of physically interpretable proxy indicators for harmonic resonance risk?
Physically interpretable proxy indicators for harmonic resonance risk should be grounded in power system physics, derived from measurable quantities, and validated against resonance behavior and consequences.
1. Grounding in physical mechanisms
•	Build indicators from quantities that directly characterize resonance: frequency dependent impedance/admittance, their local extrema, and associated mode shapes. Peaks in network impedance or modal impedances are natural proxies for resonance propensity (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Alashaary et al., 2025).
•	Include excitation–response linkage: combine infrastructure susceptibility (e.g., impedance peak) with actual or expected harmonic emission at that frequency, since overvoltage occurs only when both coincide (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Xie & Zhong, 2025).
•	Reflect damping and amplification: use sensitivity of modal impedance magnitude and resonant frequency to element parameters and damping (e.g., hydrodynamic damping in pumps, control gains in converters) as proxies for how strongly resonance will amplify responses (Lokar et al., 2021; Zhu et al., 2024; Zeng et al., 2022).
2. Constructing proxy metrics
•	Use dimensionally meaningful ratios such as |Z| relative to a non resonant baseline, voltage THD increases not explained by current distortion, or simple risk factors combining impedance peaks and emission magnitude (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Xie & Zhong, 2025).
•	Derive node  or branch level indices that summarize local resonance exposure (e.g., dominant mode participation at a bus, branch resistance vs grid connection indicating low damping paths) (Lokar et al., 2021; Plienis et al., 2023; Alashaary et al., 2025).
•	For system level prioritization, define composite indicators like a comprehensive resonance impact factor (CRIF) that weights modal participation, harmonic energy, and component criticality (Xie & Zhong, 2025).
Example proxy structure
Design principle	Proxy example	Citations
Susceptibility	Normalized peak impedance at node	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Alashaary et al., 2025)
Excitation link	Impedance peak × emitted current at f	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Xie & Zhong, 2025)
Consequence	Expected overvoltage / overcurrent factor	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Plienis et al., 2023; Zeng et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Key physical elements in proxy design.
3. Validation and robustness
•	Check that proxies track simulated or measured resonance events (overvoltages, oscillations) across scenarios, including uncertainty in load, PV, and configuration (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024; Plienis et al., 2023).
•	Prefer formulations that are computationally simple and stable for real time or large scale screening (e.g., STFT based spectra, modal indices) (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Lokar et al., 2021; Plienis et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Methodologically, proxy indicators for harmonic resonance risk should arise from explicit resonance physics (impedance modes, excitation, damping), be expressed as simple, measurable ratios or indices at nodes/branches, and be empirically calibrated so higher proxy values reliably correspond to stronger resonance amplification and more severe consequences.
References

Engineering Applications of Computational Fluid Mechanics, 16, 2176 - 2190. https://doi.org/10.1080/19942060.2022.2143902

•	How have engineering studies translated operational measurements into proxy variables for latent or unmeasured risk states?
Engineering studies typically map high dimensional operational data into low dimensional latent or composite indices that act as proxy variables for otherwise unmeasured risk or health states, using explicit measurement models and validation against adverse events.
Common methodological patterns
1. Latent-variable state–space and factor models
•	Driver stress and workload are modeled as latent states driven by environment and measured via heart rate, gaze, and facial action units; a state space model specifies how sensor channels load onto each latent construct and how latent states evolve over time (Tavakoli et al., 2022).
•	Plasma “disruptivity” is captured by a low dimensional latent space learned with multimodal VAEs, where trajectories approaching certain latent regions serve as continuous disruption risk indicators (Poels et al., 2025).
•	Proxy based causal inference in EHRs uses factor analysis on many clinical covariates and treatments to construct latent factors that stand in for unmeasured confounders (Kottler & Cochran, 2025).
2. Health indicators and degradation proxies
•	Machine Health Indicators (HIs) aggregate vibration, temperature, and other signals into scalar indices linked to remaining useful life; construction typically enforces monotonicity with degradation, high correlation with failure time, and robustness to operating variability (Bublil et al., 2025).
•	Hierarchical differential models (H CDE) explicitly separate fast operational dynamics from slow latent degradation, using neural controlled differential equations and monotonic constraints on the degradation component so it can be treated as an interpretable latent health/risk variable (Zhao & Fink, 2025).
Examples of “operational → proxy risk” mappings
Domain	Operational data	Proxy risk/latent variable	Citations
Bridges / machinery	Vibration, loads	Scalar HI, latent degradation trajectory	(Zhao & Fink, 2025; Bublil et al., 2025)
Autonomous driving	Vehicle & environment states	Latent ODC violation / failure boundary	(Xu et al., 2025)
Robotics safety	Image/latent features + epistemic uncertainty	Latent safety margin in augmented space	(Seo et al., 2025)
Software systems	Load, cache hit rate, dependency depth	Latent Risk Index (optimization induced fragility)	(Arafat et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Representative mappings from measurements to latent risk.
Validation and interpretability principles
•	Proxies are usually validated by correlation with incident severity, failures, or disruptions and by predictive performance in held out scenarios (Arafat et al., 2025; Poels et al., 2025; Zhao & Fink, 2025; Seo et al., 2025; Bublil et al., 2025; Xu et al., 2025).
•	Many methods constrain proxies to respect physics or domain knowledge (e.g., monotone degradation, operational limits, safety margins) to retain physical interpretability and reduce spurious risk patterns (Poels et al., 2025; Zhao & Fink, 2025; Seo et al., 2025; Bublil et al., 2025; Kong et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Across domains, studies translate raw operational measurements into proxy variables for latent risk by specifying measurement models from sensors to latent states, enforcing structural or physical constraints on those latent processes, and empirically validating that these proxies reliably track or predict adverse events or degradation.

Kong, X., Jiang, X., Zhang, B., Yuan, J., & Ge, Z. (2022). Latent Variable Models in the Era of Industrial Big Data: Extension and Beyond. Annu. Rev. Control., 54, 167-199. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.arcontrol.2022.09.005
•	What mathematical and physical criteria make a proxy indicator valid, robust, and useful for risk screening?
A proxy indicator is useful for risk screening only if it shows strong statistical validity, stability across conditions, and a defensible causal/physical link to the underlying hazard.
Mathematical criteria
1. Validity and calibration
•	Criterion/concurrent validity: Strong correlation or agreement with a gold standard or accepted outcome (e.g., malnutrition tools vs SGA/ESPEN; STRONGkids ORs 1.96–18.93 for adverse outcomes) (Cortés-Aguilar et al., 2024; Pereira et al., 2022).
•	Predictive validity: Proxy levels must predict future events or worsening (e.g., depression and violence risk tools predicting later incidents) (Han et al., 2025; Mallett-Smith et al., 2024).
•	Discrimination: Adequate AUC (often ≥0.70 for screening), with sensitivity/specificity tuned to context via ROC analysis and Youden’s J (Han et al., 2025; Panlilio et al., 2022; Ibáñez-Martínez et al., 2025; Mallett-Smith et al., 2024).
•	Calibration: Empirical vs “true” sensitivity/specificity should be understood and, where possible, related analytically (e.g., Markov models for cancer screening sensitivity) (Lange et al., 2023).
2. Reliability and robustness
•	Internal consistency and test–retest reliability above accepted thresholds (e.g., Cronbach’s α ≳0.7; ICC >0.75) ensure numerical stability (Pan et al., 2025; Phillips et al., 2021; Han et al., 2025; Mallett-Smith et al., 2024).
•	Performance should remain acceptable across prevalence and scenario changes (prevalence driven robustness of COPD tools; dynamic performance simulations) (Wang et al., 2025).
•	Low risk of bias in measurement and scoring (COSMIN, QUADAS 2, STARD assessments) strengthens robustness (Meng et al., 2025; Phillips et al., 2021; Cortés-Aguilar et al., 2024; Pereira et al., 2022).
Key mathematical properties for a valid screening proxy
Property	Typical quantitative targets	Citations
Discrimination	AUC ≥0.70; Se/Sp suited to use-case	(Cortés-Aguilar et al., 2024; Panlilio et al., 2022; Ibáñez-Martínez et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2025; Mallett-Smith et al., 2024)
Reliability	α, KR 20 ≳0.7; ICC >0.75	(Pan et al., 2025; Phillips et al., 2021; Han et al., 2025; Mallett-Smith et al., 2024)
Predictive power	OR or RR significantly >1	(Cortés-Aguilar et al., 2024; Han et al., 2025; Pereira et al., 2022; Mallett-Smith et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Core statistical requirements for proxy validity.
Physical / construct criteria
•	Causal or mechanistic link: Indicators must reflect physically or conceptually relevant drivers (e.g., loess slope indices tied to cohesion, rainfall, human activity; bridge flood risk via channel instability, structural condition, network criticality) (Liao et al., 2025; Alipour & Miner, 2024).
•	Feasibility and observability: Variables must be measurable with adequate precision and accessible data; principles of uniqueness, purpose, feasibility, observability reduce redundancy and maintain interpretability (Liao et al., 2025; Alipour & Miner, 2024).
•	Scope and limitations: Proxy must be framed as early risk signal, not full risk; missing mechanisms (e.g., fate, exposure in nanomaterial risk screening) should be explicitly acknowledged (Arvidsson et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Mathematically, a valid proxy for risk screening needs strong criterion and predictive validity, good discrimination and calibration, and high reliability under varying conditions. Physically, it must be grounded in mechanisms that plausibly generate the risk, rely on observable and non redundant quantities, and be explicit about what parts of the risk it does—and does not—capture.

3.4.1 Busbar Voltage Depression Index
•	How has busbar voltage depression been defined and used as an indicator of stress or vulnerability in distribution systems?
Busbar voltage depression is generally defined as sustained per unit voltage below planning/operational limits at a node, and is used as a local proxy for stress, weakness, or proximity to voltage instability in distribution networks.
How “voltage depression” is defined at busbars
Across contingency and planning studies, busbar stress is usually framed as deviation below an acceptable band, rather than with a unique “depression” formula:
•	Many works adopt a 0.95–1.05 p.u. acceptable band; a busbar is critical if its steady state voltage drops below 0.95 p.u. under N 1/N 2 contingencies or load growth (Lumina et al., 2024; Villarreal et al., 2025; Anggara et al., 2025).
•	In IEEE 9 bus contingency analysis, the “worst” busbar is the one whose post contingency voltage falls furthest below 0.95 p.u. (e.g., 0.748–0.759 p.u.), explicitly treated as a critical node for security (Lumina et al., 2024).
•	Expansion planning studies similarly require all load buses to remain ≥0.95 p.u.; buses that violate this bound under projected loading are flagged for conductor upgrades or reactive support (Villarreal et al., 2025).
•	Load growth studies report percentage voltage drop at each bus (e.g., 25.8% drop for the farthest bus when load reaches 150%), with larger drops interpreted as greater stress and vulnerability in the feeder (Anggara et al., 2025).
Use as an indicator of stress or vulnerability
Use case	How busbar voltage depression is interpreted	Citations
Contingency screening	Buses with the largest post contingency under voltage are labeled “critical” and guide reinforcement or reconfiguration	(Lumina et al., 2024; Farh et al., 2024)
Long term planning	Persistent low voltage buses under load growth trigger line upgrades to keep minimum V ≥0.95 p.u.	(Villarreal et al., 2025; Anggara et al., 2025)
Stability indices	Weak buses identified where voltage is low and voltage stability indices approach their limits	(Mossie et al., 2025; Mokred et al., 2025; Mokred & Wang, 2025)
Figure 1: Ways busbar under-voltage signals network vulnerability.
Relation to voltage stability risk
•	Voltage stability indices (e.g., FVSI, NDVSI) are evaluated bus wise under load scaling; weak buses tend to be those with both depressed voltage and an index value close to its collapse threshold (Mossie et al., 2025; Mokred et al., 2025; Mokred & Wang, 2025).
•	Buses farther from the substation in radial feeders show greater voltage depression as loading increases, and are therefore treated as natural stress points for monitoring and mitigation (Mossie et al., 2025; Villarreal et al., 2025; Anggara et al., 2025).
Conclusion
In distribution system studies, busbar voltage depression is operationalized as sustained under voltage (typically <0.95 p.u. or a large percentage drop) and is used to rank buses by weakness, identify critical nodes under contingencies or load growth, and, when combined with voltage stability indices, to localize proximity to voltage collapse.
References

Villarreal, M., Téllez, A., Krishnan, N., & García, M. (2025). Expansion Planning of Electrical Distribution Systems Considering Voltage Quality and Reliability Criteria. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18112822
Anggara, D., Danial, D., & Abidin, Z. (2025). Analysis of the Effect of Increasing Load on Voltage Profile in Electric Power Distribution System. Action Research Literate. https://doi.org/10.46799/arl.v9i9.3033
Mokred, S., Wang, Y., Alruwaili, M., & Ibrahim, M. (2025). A Novel Approach for Voltage Stability Assessment and Optimal Siting and Sizing of DGs in Radial Power Distribution Networks. Processes. https://doi.org/10.3390/pr13072239

•	What theoretical relationship exists between voltage depression, network weakness, and increased resonance susceptibility?
Voltage depression, weak networks, and resonance are linked through increased effective impedance and reduced damping, which heighten harmonic and control‐mode resonance risk.
Conceptual chain: from voltage depression to “weakness”
•	In distribution grids, voltage depression at a bus typically reflects high line impedance, heavy loading, or insufficient reactive support, all of which reduce short circuit capacity and make the bus “weak” in the voltage stability sense (Qazi et al., 2024; Mokred et al., 2025; Adeyemi & Adebayo, 2025; Lu et al., 2025).
•	Voltage stability indices (VCPI, NDVSI, BVSI, RPLI) formalize this: low voltage combined with large voltage sensitivity to power injections and high R/X or low SCR identifies weak buses/lines prone to collapse under disturbances (Qazi et al., 2024; Ismail et al., 2022; Mokred et al., 2025; Adeyemi & Adebayo, 2025; Lu et al., 2025).
Table: Theoretical link to resonance
Step	Physical/mathematical relation	Citations
1. Low V → high stress	Depressed V implies operation near reactive limits, high effective impedance	(Anggara et al., 2025; Qazi et al., 2024; Mokred et al., 2025; Lu et al., 2025)
2. Weak grid → high Z(s)	Weak/remote nodes show large driving point or transfer impedance across frequencies	(Saedabad et al., 2024; Ding et al., 2024; Letha et al., 2021; Kovernikova, 2023; Lokar et al., 2021; Lu et al., 2025)
3. High Z(s) → resonance	Parallel/series resonance when network L–C balance makes admittance small or impedance large at some ω	(Razmi et al., 2023; Ding et al., 2024; Letha et al., 2021; Kovernikova, 2023; Lokar et al., 2021)
4. Resonance → vulnerability	Harmonic/supraharmonic currents are amplified, causing overvoltages, distortion, equipment stress	(Razmi et al., 2023; Srivastava et al., 2023; Ding et al., 2024; Letha et al., 2021; Hou et al., 2024; Kovernikova, 2023; Lokar et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Voltage, weakness, and resonance linked via impedance.
Resonance susceptibility in weak / low voltage areas
•	High penetration of power electronic converters and capacitor banks in low voltage, weak feeders leads to numerous L–C combinations; where network impedance peaks (parallel resonance) or dips (series resonance), even modest harmonic injection can cause large voltage/current distortion (Razmi et al., 2023; Srivastava et al., 2023; Letha et al., 2021; Kovernikova, 2023; Lokar et al., 2021).
•	Studies of weak grid PV and PSC controlled converters show that larger grid impedance (typical at low voltage, distant buses) shifts resonant frequencies into the converter control bandwidth and reduces damping, provoking harmonic or synchronous frequency resonance unless extra virtual/physical damping is added (Saedabad et al., 2024; Hou et al., 2024; Xiong et al., 2023).
•	Supraharmonic studies in MV networks find that bigger, more weakly damped networks exhibit more resonant modes and strong dependence of resonance patterns on feeder length and configuration, especially toward remote ends—locations also most prone to voltage depression under load (Anggara et al., 2025; Letha et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Theoretically, bus voltage depression signals operation at high effective impedance and low short circuit strength. That same high, frequency dependent impedance creates sharp resonance conditions with line/cable inductance, capacitors, and converter filters, so weak, low voltage parts of the network are intrinsically more susceptible to harmonic and control mode resonances.

•	What normalization or reference-voltage formulations are recommended for constructing a busbar voltage depression index?
Busbar voltage depression indices are typically normalized to a nominal or acceptable minimum voltage (e.g., 1.0 or 0.95 p.u.) and expressed as a per unit or percentage deviation below that reference.
Common reference voltage choices
Because no paper in the results defines a “busbar voltage depression index” explicitly, practice is inferred from how voltages are assessed in power system and equipment studies:
1.	Nominal system voltage (rated kV/kVLL) → 1.0 p.u.
o	Voltages are typically converted to per unit on the nominal base; under voltage is then seen as deviation from 1.0 p.u.
o	This is standard in grid modeling and design studies, including switchgear and busbar thermal/short circuit analysis, where currents and losses are referred to rated conditions (Szulborski et al., 2021).
2.	Planning/operational lower limit (e.g., 0.95 p.u.) as the “no stress” reference
o	Distribution and equipment standards usually specify an allowable lower bound (e.g., 0.9–0.95 p.u.); violations indicate inadequate design or overload (Szulborski et al., 2021).
o	Using this limit as reference makes the index directly interpretable with respect to compliance.
Example index formulations (recommended patterns)
Index type	Mathematical form (for bus i)	Interpretation	Citations
Absolute p.u. shortfall vs. nominal	( D_i = 1 - V_i)	0 when at nominal; grows as V drops	(Szulborski et al., 2021)
Relative shortfall vs. nominal	( D_i = (1 - V_i)/1) or in %	Percent voltage depression from nominal	(Szulborski et al., 2021)
Shortfall vs. lower limit	( D_i = \max(0, V_{\min} - V_i)), where (V_{\min}=0.95)	Zero if acceptable; positive only when violating limit	(Szulborski et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Typical normalization choices for voltage indices.
Practical recommendations
•	Use per unit normalization on nominal bus voltage to keep the index dimensionless and comparable across buses and feeders.
•	For planning/security screening, prefer an index referenced to the minimum acceptable voltage, so that (D_i=0) implies “no problem” and (D_i>0) directly measures severity of under voltage.
•	Optionally scale or square the depression term (e.g., (D_i^2)) if emphasizing severe depressions in optimization or ranking.
Conclusion
A robust busbar voltage depression index should normalize voltages in per unit and reference either the nominal or, more usefully for vulnerability assessment, the minimum acceptable voltage, with the index defined as the (possibly thresholded) deviation below that reference.
References
Szulborski, M., Łapczyński, S., & Kolimas, Ł. (2021). Thermal Analysis of Heat Distribution in Busbars during Rated Current Flow in Low-Voltage Industrial Switchgear. Energies, 14, 2427. https://doi.org/10.3390/en14092427

3.4.2 Busbar Voltage Imbalance Index
•	How is busbar voltage imbalance measured and interpreted in substation and feeder studies?
Busbar voltage imbalance is quantified mainly by sequence component indices (VUF) or simpler magnitude based rates (e.g., NEMA/IEC), and interpreted against 1–2% limits at substations and a few percent at feeder ends.
How imbalance is measured
1. Sequence component (phasor based) metrics
•	The Voltage Unbalance Factor (VUF) is the IEC/“true” definition:
( \text{VUF} = |V_2|/|V_1| \times 100% ), where (V_1) and (V_2) are positive  and negative sequence busbar voltages derived from three phase phasors using Fortescue’s transformation (Gruosso et al., 2024; Naumov et al., 2021; Zabihi et al., 2025; Hashmi et al., 2022; Beneteli et al., 2021).
•	Many substation and feeder studies treat this as the reference metric; CIGRÉ’s index is an exact reformulation of VUF (Zabihi et al., 2025).
•	Some works define a complex VUF to capture both magnitude and angle of unbalance at each bus, useful for sensitivity and probabilistic studies with DER or PV (Gruosso et al., 2024).
2. Magnitude only (simplified) busbar indices
•	NEMA/IEEE style voltage unbalance rate (PVUR) uses phase to neutral magnitudes:
PVUR at a bus is the maximum deviation of any phase from the average, divided by the average, in % (Taleb et al., 2024; Zabihi et al., 2025; Hashmi et al., 2022).
•	Line voltage unbalance rate (LVUR) uses phase to phase voltages instead; it inherently includes some angle information and is often a good approximation to VUF at feeder buses (Zabihi et al., 2025).
•	Magnitude based indices approximate VUF very well in many LV feeders, especially with high R/X ratios, but tend to slightly underestimate true VUF and cannot see zero sequence components (Zabihi et al., 2025; Hashmi et al., 2022).
Example formulations at a busbar
Metric	Formula (conceptual)	Typical use	Citations
VUF	(	V_2	/
PVUR	max(	Vφ–Vavg	)/Vavg
LVUR	f(line voltage magnitudes)	When only line voltages available	(Zabihi et al., 2025; Hashmi et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Common unbalance metrics used at buses.
Interpretation in substation and feeder studies
•	Substation and MV/LV busbars are usually evaluated against 1–2% VUF limits from IEC/EN and NEMA; exceeding these indicates poor power quality and risk of motor heating and losses (Taleb et al., 2024; Naumov et al., 2021; Malonda et al., 2023; Ndwandwe et al., 2025).
•	Along feeders, voltage unbalance typically increases toward remote ends due to unbalanced single phase loads and DER; studies report busbar VUF values from ~1% at substations up to several percent at feeder termini (Möller & Meyer, 2022; Naumov et al., 2021; Gruosso et al., 2024; Hashmi et al., 2022).
•	Studies link higher busbar VUF or PVUR/LVUR to extra losses, cable overheating, and transformer derating, so indices are used both as PQ indicators and as optimization objectives for phase balancing or capacitor placement (Naumov et al., 2021; Beneteli et al., 2021; Montoya-Giraldo et al., 2024; Ndwandwe et al., 2025).
Conclusion
In practice, busbar voltage imbalance in substations and feeders is measured primarily by the sequence based VUF (often via complex VUF or CIGRÉ equivalents) and, where only magnitudes are available, by PVUR/LVUR type indices. These are interpreted against standard limits (≈1–2% at substations, a few percent at feeder ends), with higher values signaling increased losses, equipment stress, and the need for load or phase balancing interventions.

•	What operational or structural conditions cause measurable differences between busbar sections in distribution substations?
Key idea: Measurable differences between busbar sections usually arise from how they are energized/grounded, how loads/feeder circuits are distributed, and the specific busbar configuration and coupler status, rather than from the copper itself.
1. Operational conditions
•	Different energization/grounding arrangements
In impedance earthed MV substations, sections may be energized and grounded by different transformer/neutral resistor combinations (e.g., only Tr1+ZZ1, or Tr1+Tr2 with one or two grounding paths) (Jankovski et al., 2023). These lead to distinct zero sequence and negative sequence voltages/currents on each section, especially under single phase faults or earth fault conditions, which are directly measurable (Jankovski et al., 2023).
•	Bus coupler open vs. closed (busbar splitting)
Opening the bus coupler creates two electrically distinct sections; under load or contingency, this produces different voltage magnitudes and angles between sections due to changed power flows and voltage drops (Titz et al., 2025). Splitting is explicitly modeled as causing a measurable voltage and phase angle drop at the newly separated section (Titz et al., 2025).
•	Unequal loading and feeder mix
When one section supplies more heavily loaded feeders, long lines, or nonlinear/low power factor loads, it shows higher losses and larger voltage depression compared with the lighter section (Tavarov et al., 2022; Međugorac et al., 2022). High EV charging or other concentrated loads on one section increase current, losses, and voltage drop relative to the other section (Tavarov et al., 2022; Međugorac et al., 2022).
•	Presence/operation of local control devices
FACTS, capacitor banks, or STATCOMs applied at a “critical bus” or section improve voltage profile and reduce voltage drop there compared with sections without compensation (Chremk & Medhaffar, 2024).
2. Structural / configuration conditions
•	Busbar scheme and level of redundancy
Different layouts (single bar, double bar, double bar + bypass, ring) provide different levels of security and loadability, which translate into different overload risks and voltage profiles under contingencies for their associated sections (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024). Sections in more meshed/coupled configurations better withstand transformer or line outages, showing smaller voltage deviations and lower non attended demand risk than simple single bar sections (Silva-Ortega et al., 2024).
•	Number and type of connected elements
Sections with many connected branches/lines and binding voltage limits are more sensitive to topology changes and show larger variations in voltage when reconfigured or split, compared with sparsely connected bus sections (Bastianel et al., 2025; Titz et al., 2025).
Conclusion
In practice, measurable differences between busbar sections in distribution substations are driven by: (1) energization/grounding topology and bus coupler status, (2) asymmetric loading and feeder characteristics, (3) localized compensation or control devices, and (4) busbar configuration and connected element density. These factors manifest as different steady state voltages, angles, sequence components, losses, and reliability/risk indices between sections.
References

Silva-Ortega, J., Ortíz, J., & Candelo-Becerra, J. (2024). A New Method to Assess the Reliability and Security of Urban Electrical Substations. Electricity. https://doi.org/10.3390/electricity5040050
•	Why might voltage imbalance between busbars provide useful information for resonance-risk screening?
Voltage imbalance differences between busbars can act as a proxy for asymmetries and weak damping that often coincide with higher resonance risk.
Why busbar imbalance is informative for resonance screening
1. Imbalance and resonance share the same root causes
•	Voltage imbalance typically arises from unsymmetrical loads, nonlinear converters, and uneven phase use, which are also major sources and pathways for harmonics and resonances (Akbari et al., 2024; Elsotohy et al., 2025; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021; Taleb et al., 2024).
•	LCL filters, capacitor banks, and line/cable reactances that shape unbalanced fundamental voltages also create frequency selective impedance that can amplify specific harmonic orders (parallel/series resonance) (Akbari et al., 2024; Razmi et al., 2023; Xie & Zhong, 2025; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023; Prezotto et al., 2025).
•	Thus, a substation where one bus section shows consistently higher negative sequence or unbalance tends to be one where phase impedances and loading are more asymmetric, a condition that often correlates with stronger resonance peaks and localized “weak points” along lines (Razmi et al., 2023; Liu et al., 2022; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023).
2. Imbalance differences hint at spatially non uniform PQ and weak positions
•	Harmonic and resonant voltages can be higher along lines or at specific buses than at the main busbar; locations with larger asymmetry or different compensation often coincide with these weak points (Razmi et al., 2023; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023).
•	Studies on residential and traction networks show that buses or sections farther from the transformer or with more single phase / power electronic loading experience both higher unbalance and higher harmonic distortion or resonance driven overvoltages (Liu et al., 2022; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021; Taleb et al., 2024).
•	Comparing imbalance between busbar sections therefore helps flag which side of a split bus, or which feeder group, is closer to a resonance sensitive configuration (e.g., certain capacitor–transformer combinations, long cables) (Plienis et al., 2023; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023; Prezotto et al., 2025).
Illustrative linkage: unbalance, harmonics, resonance
Phenomenon at busbars	Underlying feature	Resonance risk relevance	Citations
Higher negative sequence voltage	Unsymmetrical Z, single phase loads	Indicates asymmetry, lower damping in some paths	(Akbari et al., 2024; Elsotohy et al., 2025; Taleb et al., 2024; Rodríguez-Pajarón et al., 2021)
Unequal THD/imbalance between sections	Different filters/capacitors/lines	Suggests different local resonant frequencies/Q factors	(Akbari et al., 2024; Razmi et al., 2023; Plienis et al., 2023; Tiguntsev & Shafarevich, 2023)
Figure 1: How imbalance differences point to resonance-prone areas.
Conclusion
Screening for resonance risk by looking at voltage imbalance differences between busbars is useful because it highlights asymmetries, loading patterns, and local compensation configurations that both distort balance and shape resonant modes. Sections with systematically higher imbalance are strong candidates for detailed harmonic and impedance frequency studies.

3.4.3 Incomer Utilization and Concentration Indices
•	How are incomer utilization and loading concentration quantified in substation operational studies?
Key idea: Incomer utilization and loading concentration are typically quantified using capacity based utilization factors and load distribution indices across feeders/sections.
Quantifying incomer utilization
At substation or transformer incomers, studies use standard asset utilization metrics:
•	Load factor – ratio of average load to rated capacity over a period; often used to flag under  or over utilized transformers and feeders (Xu et al., 2025).
•	Capacity–load ratio – installed capacity divided by maximum or typical loading; highlights oversizing or chronic overloading (Xu et al., 2025).
•	Life cycle utilization rate – cumulative energy or operating hours relative to design life, reflecting how intensively a unit is used over time (Xu et al., 2025).
•	Operational audits compute ranges of real power vs. rated kVA and compare efficiency at these loading levels to “maximum efficiency” points to judge whether transformers/incomers are optimally loaded (Siddiqui et al., 2024).
These indicators are evaluated over historical data (e.g., one year) and used to classify equipment into multi grade utilization classes (high, medium, low) for planning and retrofits (Xu et al., 2025).
Quantifying loading concentration
Loading concentration describes how demand is spatially or structurally clustered:
•	Share of capacity or share of energy per feeder/zone – fraction of total substation load carried by each transformer, feeder, or zone; high shares indicate concentration (Xu et al., 2025).
•	Idle rate / capacity utilization rate for reactive or other equipment – proportion of time or capacity not used, by zone or device, used to flag zones where compensation or loading is poorly matched (Xu et al., 2025).
•	In distribution networks, number of users, installed power, and activity types per substation are combined with load profiles to characterize where demand is concentrated across secondary substations (Bosisio et al., 2021).
Conclusion
In operational substation studies, incomer utilization is quantified with load factor, capacity load, and life cycle style indices, while loading concentration is captured via shares of load/capacity and utilization/idle rate metrics across feeders, zones, or equipment classes.
References
Siddiqui, M., Chattopadhyaya, S., Sharma, S., Li, C., Zhang, Y., Kumar, A., Kozak, D., Abbas, M., & Lozanović, J. (2024). Productivity enhancement of continuous miners packages of underground mines with energy conservation in industrial sectors. Energy Exploration & Exploitation, 43, 169 - 204. https://doi.org/10.1177/01445987241266084
Xu, M., Yao, W., Zeng, W., Li, T., Wang, Z., & Dou, Q. (2025). A multi-grade reactive power utilization evaluation method for distribution networks with high renewables. Frontiers in Energy Research. https://doi.org/10.3389/fenrg.2025.1540577
Bosisio, A., Moncecchi, M., Morotti, A., & Merlo, M. (2021). Machine Learning and GIS Approach for Electrical Load Assessment to Increase Distribution Networks Resilience. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en14144133
•	What is the engineering significance of uneven current concentration across incomers in relation to system stress or vulnerability?
Uneven current concentration across incomers signals localized thermal, protection, and fault level stress, and highlights structurally weak parts of the substation.
How uneven incomer loading relates to system stress
1. Thermal over stress and accelerated ageing
•	Heavily loaded paths experience higher I²R losses, leading to hotter conductors and transformers, which accelerates insulation ageing and can push equipment beyond thermal design limits (Anggara et al., 2025; Azam et al., 2021).
•	Studies on uneven load growth show that when some elements run closer to their limits while others are lightly loaded, losses and temperature hotspots rise disproportionately, degrading reliability (Anggara et al., 2025; Azam et al., 2021).
2. Reduced margin to faults and arc flash hazards
•	As “favored” incomers carry more current, prospective short circuit currents at those buses rise, stressing breakers and CTs and potentially exceeding interrupting ratings (Azam et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2025).
•	Higher normal loading can increase calculated arc flash incident energy and boundaries in switchgear, tightening maintenance safety margins (Azam et al., 2021).
3. Increased unbalance, losses, and PQ issues
•	Uneven current concentration often reflects uneven phase and feeder loading, which raises negative  and zero sequence currents, neutral current, and total losses (Anggara et al., 2025; Wesseling et al., 2025; Fajri & K, 2025; Orlov et al., 2024).
•	In LV grids, unbalanced loading leads to voltage asymmetry, overheating, and sub optimal capacity utilization, especially where new single phase LCTs (EVs, HPs, PV) are unevenly connected (Wesseling et al., 2025; Orlov et al., 2024).
Engineering implications in substation studies
Aspect	Effect of uneven incomer currents	Citations
Asset health	Localized overheating, faster ageing	(Anggara et al., 2025; Azam et al., 2021; Fajri & K, 2025)
Protection	Higher fault/arc flash levels on some sections	(Azam et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2025)
Power quality	More voltage unbalance, losses on stressed paths	(Anggara et al., 2025; Wesseling et al., 2025; Orlov et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Key stress mechanisms from uneven incomer loading.
Conclusion
Uneven current concentration across incomers is not just an efficiency issue; it is a diagnostic for where the system is thermally, electrically, and protection wise most stressed and therefore more vulnerable to faults, voltage quality problems, and accelerated asset degradation.

Wesseling, Y., Damianakis, N., Mouli, G., & Bauer, P. (2025). Grid Impact of Unbalanced Phase Integration of PV Generation, Electrified Mobility, and Heating in LV Distribution Grids. IEEE Access, 13, 56887-56907. https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2025.3555169

Sun, S., Zhou, G., Song, Y., Tang, X., Zhou, Y., & Yuan, Z. (2025). Research on Measures to Limit Short-Circuit Current by Changing the Structure of the Power Grid. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18082098

•	What mathematical formulations, including ratio-based or entropy-based measures, are suitable for incomer concentration analysis?
Ratio and entropy formulations for income concentration: core options and formulas
1. Ratio based measures
These rely on income shares or relative positions:
a. Concentration ratios and tail ratios
•	k share (top k) concentration ratio
(CR_k = \sum_{i=1}^{k} s_i), where (s_i) is income share of unit (i) sorted descending (Tran et al., 2021; Kvålseth, 2022).
•	Tail (percentile) ratios (e.g., P90/P10, P99/P50):
(TR_{a,b} = q_a / q_b), where (q_a) is the a th income quantile (Tran et al., 2021).
b. Inequality ratios using mean or median
Many indices are ratio functionals of incomes to a central value μ (mean) or m (median), e.g. generalized entropy, Theil, Atkinson, Hoover, CV (Cowell & Flachaire, 2023; Kawakubo & Kakamu, 2025; Kim et al., 2024).
Cowell & Flachaire analyze median normalized generalized entropy and Gini to avoid anomalies of mean normalized ratios (Cowell & Flachaire, 2023).
c. Market style concentration indices (if units are firms/regions)
For income shares (p_i):
•	Herfindahl–Hirschman index (HHI): (HHI = \sum_i p_i^2) (Tran et al., 2021; Kvålseth, 2022).
•	Rosenbluth / Hall–Tideman, Linda, dominance indices: weighted sum ratios emphasizing large shares (Tran et al., 2021; Kvålseth, 2022; Evren et al., 2021; Hambardzumyan, 2023).
2. Entropy based and generalized entropy measures
Let (p_i) be income shares (or normalized incomes):
a. Shannon entropy and entropy concentration
•	Entropy: (H = -\sum_i p_i \log p_i).
Low H ⇒ high concentration. Used directly on income distributions or income shares (Tran et al., 2021; Koutsoyiannis & Sargentis, 2021).
•	Normalized entropy / “entropy concentration”:
(U = H/H_{\max}), with (H_{\max} = \log n); concentration coefficient (C = 1-U) on [0,1] scale (Kvålseth, 2022; Koutsoyiannis & Sargentis, 2021).
b. Generalized entropy (GEα) family (includes Theil)
For incomes (y_i), mean μ:
[ GE_\alpha = \begin{cases} \frac{1}{\alpha(\alpha-1)}\left[\frac{1}{n}\sum_i\left(\frac{y_i}{\mu}\right)^\alpha -1\right], & \alpha\neq 0,1\[4pt] \frac{1}{n}\sum_i \frac{y_i}{\mu}\ln\frac{y_i}{\mu}, & \alpha=1\ (\text{Theil }T)\[4pt] \frac{1}{n}\sum_i \ln\frac{\mu}{y_i}, & \alpha=0\ (\text{MLD}) \end{cases} ] These are entropy type indices, additively decomposable by group/region (Tran et al., 2021; Kawakubo & Kakamu, 2025; Yuan et al., 2025; Carrazana-Escalona et al., 2022).
c. Cumulative entropy and related curves
Cumulative entropy of income links directly to Lorenz curve, Gini, Bonferroni, and Zenga curves, giving an alternative entropy style concentration functional over the income survival function (Nair & Vineshkumar, 2022).
Conclusion
For income concentration, standard toolkits combine top share ratios, tail ratios, and HHI type indices with Shannon and generalized entropy (Theil/MLD) measures, possibly normalized to a 0–1 “entropy concentration” coefficient, plus cumulative entropy when a Lorenz curve link is desired.

3.4.4 Feeder Utilization, Coincidence, and Imbalance Indices
•	How are feeder utilization, feeder coincidence, and feeder imbalance defined in distribution-system operational analytics?
Feeder utilization, coincidence, and imbalance are commonly defined as normalized metrics describing how heavily, how simultaneously, and how unevenly feeders are loaded.
Core Definitions in Operational Analytics
Feeder utilization
•	What it is: Degree to which a feeder or transformer is loaded relative to its thermal or apparent power rating.
•	Typical form:
[ \text{Utilization} = \frac{\text{actual or forecast feeder loading}}{\text{feeder thermal or kVA rating}} ]
•	Used widely in hosting capacity and PV impact studies as “line/transformer loading” or “thermal limit” utilization when checking congestion, overloading risk, and headroom for new DERs (Islam & Hossain, 2023; Chihota et al., 2021; Lotfi & Shojaei, 2022; Mahmoud & Lehtonen, 2021).
Feeder coincidence
•	What it is: A coincidence factor expressing how often multiple customer or device peaks occur at the same time at the feeder level.
•	For a group of loads or EV chargers, coincidence factor (CF) is typically
[ CF = \frac{\text{simultaneous feeder peak of the group}}{\text{sum of individual peak demands}} ]
•	CF decreases with aggregation size and depends strongly on connection count and operating behavior (e.g., EV charging), making it a key feeder level diversity measure for planning and operational forecasting (Bollerslev et al., 2022).
Feeder imbalance
•	What it is: Degree of phase unbalance in currents or voltages along a feeder.
•	Commonly quantified via a voltage  or current unbalance factor (VUF/IUF), e.g. ratio of negative sequence to positive sequence components, or magnitude only approximations when phasors are unavailable (Hashmi et al., 2022; Beneteli et al., 2021).
•	Feeder level imbalance metrics are used to evaluate losses, neutral currents, and operational efficiency, and to assess benefits of re phasing or capacitor placement (Firdaus & Budisusila, 2025; Hermawan et al., 2024; Hashmi et al., 2022; Beneteli et al., 2021).
Summary Table
Concept	Typical metric form / focus	Citations
Feeder utilization	Load or current / thermal or kVA rating	(Islam & Hossain, 2023; Chihota et al., 2021; Lotfi & Shojaei, 2022; Mahmoud & Lehtonen, 2021)
Feeder coincidence	Simultaneous peak / sum of individual peaks	(Bollerslev et al., 2022)
Feeder imbalance	VUF/IUF, phase magnitude or sequence component ratio	(Firdaus & Budisusila, 2025; Hermawan et al., 2024; Hashmi et al., 2022; Beneteli et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Operational metrics describing feeder loading, simultaneity, and phase balance.
Conclusion
In distribution system analytics, utilization captures how close feeders are to capacity, coincidence captures diversity and simultaneity of underlying loads at the feeder peak, and imbalance captures three phase asymmetry using unbalance factors derived from currents or voltages.

•	What do simultaneous feeder loading and uneven feeder stress reveal about network operating states?
Simultaneous loading & uneven stress as state indicators
High simultaneous feeder loading and unevenly shared stress together signal stressed, low margin operating states with elevated reliability and power quality risk.
1. High simultaneous feeder loading
When many feeders or key feeders are near thermal or voltage limits at the same time, it indicates:
•	Low operational headroom: Overlapping peaks raise risk of load shedding or curtailment if a fault or contingency occurs, since few alternative paths or reserves are available (Yin et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2022; Haider et al., 2021; Chihota et al., 2021).
•	Greater contingency impact: Under N 1 events, heavily and concurrently loaded feeders force reconfiguration to higher network levels (transformer/substation) and still may not avoid shedding (Gao et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2021).
•	Tight flexibility margins: Dynamic adjustable capacity of feeder loads, ESS, and flexible loads shrinks when base loading is high, constraining participation in demand response, VPPs, and DOE flexibility markets (Zheng et al., 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Lankeshwara et al., 2024).
2. Uneven feeder stress (imbalanced loading across feeders/phases)
Substantial differences in loading across feeders or phases reveal:
•	Structural or operational imbalance: Spatially uneven development, DG siting, or switching patterns that leave some feeders or transformers persistently “heavy” while others are lightly used (Gao et al., 2022; Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2021; Thavaratnam & Venkatesh, 2025; Luo et al., 2024).
•	Inefficient and risky operation: Overstressed feeders suffer higher losses, more voltage violations, and accelerated aging of OLTCs, transformers and cables, while capacity on other corridors is underutilized (Mohare & Singh, 2025; Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Kljajić et al., 2024; Kisuule et al., 2023; Ivanov et al., 2025).
•	Need for reconfiguration or coordinated control: Many works explicitly use load balancing indices and switching/ESS/FL coordination to move from uneven stress towards more uniform loading as a hallmark of a secure state (Gao et al., 2022; Yuechao et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2021; Thavaratnam & Venkatesh, 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Ivanov et al., 2025).
3. Interpreting both together
Pattern observed	Implied operating state / risk focus	Citations
All feeders highly and simultaneously loaded	System wide scarcity; high risk under contingencies	(Yin et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2022; Haider et al., 2021; Chihota et al., 2021)
Some feeders very heavy, others light	Topology/dispatch sub optimal; loss and aging hotspots	(Gao et al., 2022; Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2021; Thavaratnam & Venkatesh, 2025; Luo et al., 2024)
Persistent phase or branch imbalance on feeders	Elevated losses, neutral currents, voltage asymmetry	(Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Kundankar et al., 2025; Ivanov et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Feeder loading patterns as proxies for system stress.
Conclusion
In distribution system analytics, patterns of simultaneous high loading and uneven stress across feeders/phases are widely used as fingerprints of stressed, low resilience operating states, guiding reconfiguration, flexibility activation, and reinforcement planning.

•	Which mathematical measures are most appropriate for capturing aggregate feeder stress, coincidence, and dispersion?
Simultaneous loading & uneven stress as state indicators
High simultaneous feeder loading and unevenly shared stress together signal stressed, low margin operating states with elevated reliability and power quality risk.
1. High simultaneous feeder loading
When many feeders or key feeders are near thermal or voltage limits at the same time, it indicates:
•	Low operational headroom: Overlapping peaks raise risk of load shedding or curtailment if a fault or contingency occurs, since few alternative paths or reserves are available (Yin et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2022; Haider et al., 2021; Chihota et al., 2021).
•	Greater contingency impact: Under N 1 events, heavily and concurrently loaded feeders force reconfiguration to higher network levels (transformer/substation) and still may not avoid shedding (Gao et al., 2022; Zhou et al., 2021).
•	Tight flexibility margins: Dynamic adjustable capacity of feeder loads, ESS, and flexible loads shrinks when base loading is high, constraining participation in demand response, VPPs, and DOE flexibility markets (Zheng et al., 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Lankeshwara et al., 2024).
2. Uneven feeder stress (imbalanced loading across feeders/phases)
Substantial differences in loading across feeders or phases reveal:
•	Structural or operational imbalance: Spatially uneven development, DG siting, or switching patterns that leave some feeders or transformers persistently “heavy” while others are lightly used (Gao et al., 2022; Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2021; Thavaratnam & Venkatesh, 2025; Luo et al., 2024).
•	Inefficient and risky operation: Overstressed feeders suffer higher losses, more voltage violations, and accelerated aging of OLTCs, transformers and cables, while capacity on other corridors is underutilized (Mohare & Singh, 2025; Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Kljajić et al., 2024; Kisuule et al., 2023; Ivanov et al., 2025).
•	Need for reconfiguration or coordinated control: Many works explicitly use load balancing indices and switching/ESS/FL coordination to move from uneven stress towards more uniform loading as a hallmark of a secure state (Gao et al., 2022; Yuechao et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2021; Thavaratnam & Venkatesh, 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Ivanov et al., 2025).
3. Interpreting both together
Pattern observed	Implied operating state / risk focus	Citations
All feeders highly and simultaneously loaded	System wide scarcity; high risk under contingencies	(Yin et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2022; Haider et al., 2021; Chihota et al., 2021)
Some feeders very heavy, others light	Topology/dispatch sub optimal; loss and aging hotspots	(Gao et al., 2022; Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Zhou et al., 2021; Thavaratnam & Venkatesh, 2025; Luo et al., 2024)
Persistent phase or branch imbalance on feeders	Elevated losses, neutral currents, voltage asymmetry	(Bat-Orgil et al., 2025; Kundankar et al., 2025; Ivanov et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Feeder loading patterns as proxies for system stress.
Conclusion
In distribution system analytics, patterns of simultaneous high loading and uneven stress across feeders/phases are widely used as fingerprints of stressed, low resilience operating states, guiding reconfiguration, flexibility activation, and reinforcement planning.

3.4.5 Load Ramp and Feeder Ramp Dispersion Indices
•	How have load ramp rates and feeder ramp dispersion been used to characterize dynamic operating stress in distribution systems?
Load ramp rates and feeder ramp dispersion are used as quantitative “stress indicators” linking fast net-load changes to voltage, thermal, and stability margins in distribution systems.
How ramp rates quantify dynamic operating stress
•	DER flexibility envelopes. In nodal operating envelope (NOE) frameworks, ramp capability is an explicit dynamic flexibility metric: the feasible set of active power deviations over a response time τ defines how fast net feeder power can change without violating network constraints (Riaz & Mancarella, 2021). Ramp NOEs are computed via OPF under voltage/thermal limits, so tighter envelopes directly signal higher dynamic stress for given ramps (Riaz & Mancarella, 2021).
•	Ramp rate limits as a stress constraint. Distribution level studies treat ΔP/Δt at the transformer or feeder head as a controllable stress variable. Central BESSs are sized so that the aggregated feeder ramp is constrained below code limits; higher natural PV/load ramps imply larger required storage energy/power and thus higher inherent dynamic stress (Gkavanoudis et al., 2023).
•	Ramp rate limitation against rapid voltage changes. Experimental work on CIRESs shows that high active power ramp rates cause rapid voltage changes (RVCs); applying ramp rate limitation (RRL) with storage measurably reduces RVC indices and restores voltage quality on benchmark feeders, directly using ramp magnitude as a voltage stress proxy (Dimoulias et al., 2025).
•	Load side flexibility events. In large scale EWH control, ramp up and ramp down rates (e.g., %/min) during rebound events are defined as key characterization parameters; high aggregate ramps are associated with risks of feeder overloading and voltage deviations, guiding when and how much flexibility can be activated safely (Lakshmanan et al., 2021).
Feeder ramp dispersion and spatial/temporal stress
•	Studies of flexibility and hosting capacity treat feeder level variation in ramps (across buses or scenarios) as a way to map where dynamic headroom is tightest, via stress functions on line flows and voltages under different DER/load patterns (Riaz & Mancarella, 2021; Mulkin et al., 2025; Wu et al., 2023). Larger dispersion in local ramps for a given aggregate profile indicates more localized thermal/voltage stress concentration.
Conclusion
Across flexibility, RRL, and demand response studies, load ramp rates at feeder or transformer level are directly tied to voltage, thermal, and stability margins, while dispersion of ramps across feeders and buses is used to locate and quantify how unevenly that dynamic stress is distributed.
References
Riaz, S., & Mancarella, P. (2021). Modelling and Characterisation of Flexibility From Distributed Energy Resources. IEEE Transactions on Power Systems, 37, 38-50. https://doi.org/10.1109/tpwrs.2021.3096971
Mulkin, O., Heleno, M., & Ludkovski, M. (2025). Selecting Critical Scenarios of DER Adoption in Distribution Grids Using Bayesian Optimization. ArXiv, abs/2501.14118. https://doi.org/10.48550/arxiv.2501.14118
Dimoulias, S., Malamaki, K., Gross, A., García-López, F., Kryonidis, G., & Barragán-Villarejo, M. (2025). Experimental Evaluation and Coordinated Deployment of Ramp-Rate Limitation Against Rapid Voltage Changes in Distribution Systems. IEEE Transactions on Industry Applications, 61, 1839-1853. https://doi.org/10.1109/tia.2024.3523456
Wu, J., Yuan, J., Weng, Y., & Ayyanar, R. (2023). Spatial-Temporal Deep Learning for Hosting Capacity Analysis in Distribution Grids. IEEE Transactions on Smart Grid, 14, 354-364. https://doi.org/10.1109/tsg.2022.3196943

Journal of Modern Power Systems and Clean Energy. https://doi.org/10.35833/mpce.2022.000595

•	What evidence links rapid load transitions or synchronized feeder ramps to system sensitivity or instability-related risks?
Rapid, large or synchronized ramps in load or DER output are consistently linked to higher risk of frequency and voltage instability, especially in low inertia grids.
Mechanisms: Why rapid/synchronized ramps matter
•	Act as large disturbances in low inertia systems: Reduced inertia and short circuit strength mean a given power imbalance (ΔP) produces faster frequency excursions and higher RoCoF, shrinking the time for corrective action and increasing risk of cascading outages and blackouts (Ahmed et al., 2023; Sauhats et al., 2024; Gu & Green, 2023; XiaoxinZhouQ. et al., 2023).
•	Abrupt operating point shifts: Fast load increases/decreases or sharp ramps in large data center loads cause eigenvalues of the system state matrix to move quickly, temporarily reducing damping and creating poorly damped oscillatory modes that may be missed by traditional snapshot stability checks (Kwon et al., 2025).
•	Short term voltage stability stress: Momentary cessation or disconnection of DERs during faults, which is effectively a rapid net load ramp, is strongly correlated with short term voltage instability; dynamic RMS studies show DER tripping is roughly “twice as detrimental” to STVS as the benefit from equivalent ride through support, for given MW levels (Boričić et al., 2021).
•	Sustained oscillatory or step like ramps: In simulations with large, fast ramping data center loads, rapid oscillatory ramps produce persistent frequency fluctuations and can drive outright collapse when ramp amplitude is increased (frequency divergence, huge “energy flow” stress metrics) (Kwon et al., 2025). Slow stair step ramps avoid sharp transients but still excite slow system wide oscillatory modes, indicating latent stress (Kwon et al., 2025).
Evidence for synchronized or net system ramps
•	Wind/solar ramp events: Large, time correlated wind ramps (30 min–5 h) substantially increase operational risk indices relative to “normal” fluctuations, particularly when negative ramps coincide with rising system load, leading to higher risk of frequency violations, load shedding, and curtailment (Wang et al., 2025).
•	Clustered DER effects on bulk STVS: High penetrations of dynamically modeled DERs show that widespread tripping during faults (synchronized effective net load increase) significantly worsens short term voltage stability margins in bulk networks (Boričić et al., 2021).
Summary table
Phenomenon	Observed risk linkage	Citations
Fast LDDL/data center ramps	High freq transients, eigenvalue shifts, collapse under amplified ramps	(Kwon et al., 2025)
DER tripping / momentary cessation	Strongly correlated with STVS deterioration; needs ~2× MW of ride through DER to offset	(Boričić et al., 2021)
Large wind ramps	Higher frequency related risk indices vs. normal variability; worst when aligned with load rise	(Wang et al., 2025)
Low inertia + large ΔP	Faster frequency fall, more ROCOF, higher blackout risk	(Ahmed et al., 2023; Sauhats et al., 2024; Gu & Green, 2023)
Figure 1: How different ramp types map to instability risk
Conclusion
Across bulk and distribution level studies, rapid or synchronized ramps in load or DER output increase sensitivity to both frequency and short term voltage instability, mainly by accelerating dynamics, abruptly shifting operating points, and compounding with low inertia or coincident load growth. Operational risk frameworks now explicitly model ramp timing, magnitude, and duration to capture these instability related risks.

•	What formulations are recommended for measuring temporal change and dispersion in feeder-level operational time series?
Feeder-level time series are best described by combining simple dispersion statistics with change- and complexity-focused metrics that separate amplitude from timing.
1. Basic dispersion for operational spread
For daily / seasonal spread in feeder loading, voltage, or flows, standard variance and standard deviation are the canonical measures of dispersion; they quantify consistency and risk around the mean and are easy to interpret and compare across feeders or time periods (Rakrak, 2025). Range is useful for extreme excursions but is highly sensitive to outliers (Rakrak, 2025).
Recommended basic formulations
•	Rolling mean and standard deviation over operational windows (e.g., 5–60 min, day, week) to measure short-term volatility and diurnal/seasonal dispersion.
•	Normalized dispersion (coefficient of variation = SD/mean) to compare feeders with different absolute levels (Rakrak, 2025).
Example: basic dispersion metrics
Purpose	Metric	Comment	Sources
Spread in operating levels	Rolling SD, variance	Core feeder variability measure	(Rakrak, 2025)
Relative variability	Coefficient of variation	Compares across feeders/periods	(Rakrak, 2025)
Figure 1: Core dispersion metrics for feeder time series
2. Temporal change and regime shifts
To characterize temporal change (level or pattern shifts) in operational series:
•	Change-point / breakpoint methods: optimization over segmented cost functions (e.g., likelihood or regression error) with penalties for many breaks, suitable for detecting structural changes in load or DER behavior (Ghaderpour et al., 2021; Apostol et al., 2021).
•	Trend/seasonal break frameworks such as BFAST or related breakpoint-in-trend tools identify when long-term feeder loading or PV output patterns reconfigure (Ghaderpour et al., 2021).
These can be applied on aggregated feeder metrics (e.g., daily peak, minimum voltage) to mark operational regime changes.
3. Temporal dispersion and complexity
To go beyond variance and capture irregularity and multi-scale variability:
•	Entropy-based complexity: sample entropy, dispersion entropy, fuzzy-dispersion entropy and their multiscale variants quantify irregularity and nonlinearity in operational time series (Kamali et al., 2025; Blanks & Brown, 2024; Rostaghi et al., 2023; Jiang & Shang, 2024; Chen et al., 2023).
•	Methods that separate timing vs amplitude contributions (e.g., ExSEnt) provide distinct measures of variability in event durations vs magnitudes, which is useful for distinguishing frequent small vs rare large changes in feeders (Kamali et al., 2025).
Conclusion
In operational feeder analyses, dispersion is typically measured with rolling variance/standard deviation (often normalized), while temporal change is captured via breakpoint/segmentation methods, and richer temporal dispersion is captured with entropy-based complexity measures that can explicitly separate timing from amplitude variability.
References

Kamali, S., Baroni, F., & Varona, P. (2025). ExSEnt: Extrema-Segmented Entropy Analysis of Time Series. ArXiv, abs/2509.07751. https://doi.org/10.48550/arxiv.2509.07751

3.4.6 Voltage-to-Load Sensitivity Proxy
•	How is voltage-to-load sensitivity estimated in operational power-system studies using rolling or local models?
Operational studies typically estimate voltage–load sensitivity with local linear models updated online via recursive or windowed regression, sometimes enhanced by ML.
Core estimation setup (local/rolling models)
Most operational methods assume a locally linear relation between voltage magnitudes and net injections around the current operating point, e.g.
|ΔV| ≈ KᴾΔP + KQΔQ, where Kᴾ,KQ are voltage-to-active/reactive power sensitivities (Gupta et al., 2022; Ye et al., 2021; Gupta & Paolone, 2023; Henry & Gupta, 2023).
Key steps:
1.	Data windowing / locality
o	Use short windows (e.g. 1–5 min) where the network and sensitivities are assumed constant (Gupta et al., 2022; Gupta & Paolone, 2023).
o	Some methods adapt the window based on observed voltage–power variation or “similar sensitivity” criteria to ensure sufficient excitation and avoid mixing regimes (Rizvi et al., 2022; Rizvi et al., 2021).
2.	Offline initialization
o	Initial sensitivities from: 
	LS fit on large historical measurement sets (Gupta et al., 2022; Gupta & Paolone, 2023), or
	Model derived Jacobian based sensitivities when a rough network model exists (Wang et al., 2024; Nowak et al., 2022; Ye et al., 2021).
3.	Online recursive updating (rolling model)
o	Recursive least squares (RLS) or robust/weighted variants update K at each time step using the newest (V,P,Q) samples, often with a forgetting factor to track slow drifts (Gupta et al., 2022; Picallo et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2024; Nowak et al., 2022; Rizvi et al., 2021; Gupta & Paolone, 2023).
o	Robust RLS and covariance reconfiguration address low excitation, multicollinearity, and measurement noise (Gupta et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Rizvi et al., 2021).
Representative approaches
Approach	Locality mechanism	Robustness features	Citations
LS + RLS two-stage	Fixed short window + exponential forgetting	Regularization; covariance tracking; uncertainty estimation	(Gupta et al., 2022; Picallo et al., 2021; Nowak et al., 2022; Gupta & Paolone, 2023)
Robust RLS for LSF	Rolling window + scenario-based chance constraints	Outlier/noise handling; feeds chance-constrained dispatch	(Wang et al., 2024)
Adaptive-window ZIP / load tracking	Window selected by sensitivity & feature distance	Handles regime switches; mitigates ill-conditioning	(Rizvi et al., 2022; Rizvi et al., 2021)
NN (FF/LSTM) estimators	Trained on rolling windows of V,P,Q	Better noise & multicollinearity tolerance than regression	(Henry & Gupta, 2023)
Figure 1: Common rolling/local schemes for sensitivity estimation
Use in operational control
The estimated local sensitivities feed:
•	Online feedback OPF / DER dispatch linearized around current state (Picallo et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2024; Nowak et al., 2022; Ye et al., 2021).
•	Robust or chance constrained voltage control, where uncertainty in K is explicitly modeled from RLS covariance (Gupta et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Gupta & Paolone, 2023).
Conclusion
Operational studies estimate voltage to load sensitivity by fitting local linear V–P–Q models over short, rolling windows, initialized from historical data or approximate models, and continuously updated via (robust) RLS or, more recently, neural networks, with adaptive windowing and covariance handling to maintain reliability in noisy, time varying grids.
References
Gupta, R., Sossan, F., & Paolone, M. (2022). Model-less Robust Voltage Control in Active Distribution Networks using Sensitivity Coefficients Estimated from Measurements. ArXiv, abs/2201.04192. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.epsr.2022.108547

Rizvi, S., Sajan, K., & Srivastava, A. (2022). Synchrophasor Based ZIP Parameters Tracking Using ML With Adaptive Window and Data Anomalies. IEEE Transactions on Power Systems, 37, 3-13. https://doi.org/10.1109/tpwrs.2021.3088903
Nowak, S., Chen, Y., & Wang, L. (2022). Distributed Measurement-Based Optimal DER Dispatch With Estimated Sensitivity Models. IEEE Transactions on Smart Grid, 13, 2197-2208. https://doi.org/10.1109/tsg.2021.3139450
Rizvi, S., Sadanandan, S., & Srivastava, A. (2021). Real-Time ZIP Load Parameter Tracking Using Sensitivity-Based Adaptive Window and Variable Elimination With Realistic Synchrophasor Data. IEEE Transactions on Industry Applications, 57, 6525-6536. https://doi.org/10.1109/tia.2021.3105078

Gupta, R., & Paolone, M. (2023). Experimental Validation of Model-less Robust Voltage Control using Measurement-based Estimated Voltage Sensitivity Coefficients. 2023 IEEE Belgrade PowerTech, 1-8. https://doi.org/10.1109/powertech55446.2023.10202696
Henry, R., & Gupta, R. (2023). Measurement - Based/Model-Less Estimation of Voltage Sensitivity Coefficients by Feedforward and LSTM Neural Networks in Power Distribution Grids. 2024 IEEE Texas Power and Energy Conference (TPEC), 1-6. https://doi.org/10.1109/tpec60005.2024.10472179
•	Why is voltage sensitivity to load variation a useful indicator of network stiffness or vulnerability?
Voltage sensitivity to load is a proxy for how “stiff” the grid is: large voltage changes for small load changes indicate a weak, vulnerable network; small changes indicate a stiff, robust one.
Physical intuition
•	In steady state, bus voltages are determined by the balance between power injections and the network’s impedance/reactive support. Sensitivity coefficients (e.g., ∂V/∂P, ∂V/∂Q) measure how much voltage moves when load changes. Large sensitivities mean small load increases push voltages far from nominal, signalling weak voltage control and low stability margin (Mogaka et al., 2021; Alzaareer et al., 2021).
•	As the system approaches its voltage stability limit, the power flow Jacobian becomes nearly singular; elements of the associated sensitivity matrices grow large, so V–P or V–Q sensitivity is a direct early warning signal of proximity to collapse (Gao et al., 2021; Mokred et al., 2023; Mogaka et al., 2021; Alzaareer et al., 2021).
Relation to stiffness and vulnerability
•	Stiff (strong) networks: high short circuit capacity, abundant reactive support, low R/X paths. These show low voltage sensitivity to power changes; indices built from sensitivities or VSIs remain far from their critical values (Mokred et al., 2023; Mogaka et al., 2021; Lu et al., 2025).
•	Weak or vulnerable areas: long radial feeders, high impedance, or limited VAR support show high |∂V/∂P| or |∂V/∂Q|, and line/bus stability indices (FVSI, NCPI, VSF, etc.) approach their collapse thresholds as loading rises (Rehman et al., 2023; Mossie et al., 2025; Mokred et al., 2023; Mokred & Wang, 2025; Guo et al., 2025).
•	Sensitivity based methods consistently identify weak buses/lines and regions most susceptible to instability or voltage violations, guiding placement of reactive compensation, DG, or storage (Badrudeen et al., 2022; Mogaka et al., 2021; Lu et al., 2025; Adeyemi & Adebayo, 2025; Wang et al., 2024).
Example: weak node identification
Simplified or Jacobian based voltage sensitivities are explicitly used as a core indicator of nodal disturbance resistance; larger sensitivity values flag nodes more prone to over/undervoltage and thus more “fragile” under PV or load fluctuations (Lu et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Because it is mathematically tied to the power flow Jacobian and practically correlated with short circuit strength and VAR margin, voltage sensitivity to load variation is a compact, interpretable indicator of network stiffness and vulnerability to voltage instability or collapse.
