•	What modelling approaches are most defensible for constructing a voltage-to-load sensitivity proxy from hourly data?
Constructing Voltage–Load Sensitivity Proxies from Hourly Data
For hourly-resolution data, the most defensible approaches treat voltage–load sensitivity as a locally linear but time varying relationship, estimated with regularized regression or surrogate models, and anchored where possible to physical ZIP type load models.
1. Physics Informed Static/Quasi Static Models
•	ZIP / composite ZIP–electronic models: Represent active/reactive power as polynomial functions of voltage and estimate coefficients from data windows with sufficient voltage variation (Parker et al., 2021; Rizvi et al., 2022). 
o	Approaches include constrained least squares, sensitivity driven constrained optimization, and hybrid distance–based windowing to enforce near constant sensitivity within a window (Rizvi et al., 2022).
o	These can be aggregated by end use to give time  and voltage dependent building or feeder load profiles (Parker et al., 2021).
Example modelling choices (hourly context)
Goal	Approach	Notes	Citations
Simple linear proxy dP/dV	Windowed OLS / RLS on P–V	Assume piecewise stationarity over hours–days	(Gupta et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Gupta & Paolone, 2023)
Robust to noise & collinearity	Ridge / weighted LS, robust RLS	Explicitly model uncertainty in sensitivity	(Gupta et al., 2022; Gupta & Paolone, 2023; Henry & Gupta, 2023)
Capture nonlinearity	Static ZIP or CEZLM fit in windows	Physical interpretability; supports CVR/CVRF	(Parker et al., 2021; Rizvi et al., 2022; Zheng et al., 2025; Rizvi et al., 2022)
Higher order/global effects	Surrogate models (GP/ANOVA Kriging) from P,Q,V	Enables Sobol type global sensitivity indices	(Ye et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Representative modelling options for voltage–load sensitivity proxies
2. Purely Data Driven Sensitivity Estimation
Where physical parameters/topology are unknown:
•	Recursive least squares (RLS) on measured V, P, Q to obtain local sensitivity factors (voltage w.r.t. nodal injections), updated on rolling time windows (Gupta et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Gupta & Paolone, 2023).
•	Neural network estimators (feedforward, LSTM) mapping time series of V, P, Q to sensitivity coefficients, shown more robust than regression under high noise and multicollinearity (Henry & Gupta, 2023).
•	Gaussian process / ANOVA kernel surrogates trained on historical P,Q,V samples to approximate the full nonlinear voltage response surface, from which analytical or Monte Carlo based sensitivities are derived (Ye et al., 2021).
3. Practical defensibility considerations
•	Temporal stationarity assumption: Choose window lengths where load composition and network topology are approximately constant; adaptive window selection based on observed voltage–power variation improves identifiability (Rizvi et al., 2022).
•	Uncertainty quantification: Treat sensitivity estimates as random variables and propagate their uncertainty into any control/assessment (e.g., chance constrained DER dispatch, robust voltage control) (Gupta et al., 2022; Wang et al., 2024; Gupta & Paolone, 2023).
•	Anchoring to physical load types (ZIP/end use categories) improves interpretability and transferability compared with black box time series forecasting models (Parker et al., 2021; Ye et al., 2021; Rizvi et al., 2022).
Conclusion
For hourly data, the most defensible voltage to load sensitivity proxies combine windowed, regularized regression or surrogate modelling in V–P–Q space with ZIP inspired structure and explicit uncertainty quantification, optionally enhanced by NN based estimators when measurement noise and collinearity are severe.

3.5 Operating-State Reconstruction and Clustering
•	What methodological approaches are used to reconstruct substation operating states from multivariate hourly data?
Substation operating states are typically reconstructed from multivariate hourly data using feature engineered load profiles plus clustering, and complemented by state estimation and generative/ML models.
Main methodological approaches
1. Feature based daily profiles + clustering
•	Hourly measurements (e.g., active power per substation) are aggregated into 24 dimensional mean daily load profiles per substation (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
•	Dimensionality reduction (PCA, t SNE, UMAP) is used to obtain compact embeddings while preserving dominant consumption patterns (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
•	Clustering algorithms (k means, k medoids, adaptive k means) on these profiles identify recurrent “operating states” or typical demand types per substation (Prompook et al., 2025; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
•	Cluster quality is evaluated with DBI, CHI, Silhouette and custom variability metrics; spatial mapping links clusters to customer mix and regions (Prompook et al., 2025; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
2. State estimation from sparse multivariate measurements
•	Classical and enhanced weighted least squares (WLS) state estimation reconstruct bus voltages/angles from SCADA/AMI hourly data and pseudo measurements (Dandea et al., 2025; Dabush et al., 2023; Pan et al., 2024).
•	Graph signal processing regularized WLS exploits spatial smoothness of voltages to reconstruct states in partially observable systems using limited hourly measurements (Dabush et al., 2023).
•	Data driven estimators (deep ensembles, Kalman filter–based system identification) learn mappings from multivariate measurements (V, P, Q, angles) to system states over time (Bhusal et al., 2021; Hossain & Naeini, 2022).
•	Hierarchical graph neural networks use node/edge features (loads, flows, taps) to estimate substation level voltages under low observability (Za’ter & Hodge, 2025).
3. Generative and forecasting models as state reconstructors
•	Conditional variational autoencoders generate realistic multivariate load states conditioned on system context, filling gaps in historical hourly data and exploring rare operating conditions (Wang et al., 2021).
•	Short term load forecasting models (ensembles, TFT, CNN LSTM) at substation level reconstruct expected future operating states (hourly load trajectories) from past multivariate time series and exogenous variables (Giacomazzi et al., 2023; Giamarelos et al., 2023; Syed et al., 2021; Mukkamala & Sunku, 2025).
4. Equipment centric and harmonic state evaluation
•	For equipment states, Gaussian mixture models + EM, association rules, and Naive Bayes classify substation equipment condition from multidimensional operational data (Dang et al., 2024).
•	Multi task Gaussian process regression models multivariate harmonic currents/voltages at substations, reconstructing probabilistic harmonic “states” over time (Zheng et al., 2025).
Conclusion
In practice, substation operating states from multivariate hourly data are reconstructed by: (1) building daily or multi day feature vectors and clustering them into typical operating regimes; (2) applying enhanced state estimation (WLS, GSP, data driven, GNN) to infer unmeasured electrical variables; and (3) using generative/forecasting and equipment/harmonic models to complete and refine these state representations.

•	How have clustering methods been applied to identify representative power-system operating regimes?
Clustering is widely used to compress time series data into a small set of representative operating regimes for planning, operation, and analysis.
How clustering is used to derive representative regimes
1. Time series load and net load regimes
•	Daily / hourly profiles as regimes: Substation or customer 24 h load curves are clustered so that each cluster represents a typical operating regime (e.g., base load, evening peak, PV driven reverse flow) (Pelekis et al., 2023; Prompook et al., 2025; Cen et al., 2022).
•	Feature extraction first: Methods use wavelet transforms, PCA, or other compression before clustering to stabilize groups and highlight patterns (Cen et al., 2022; Gao et al., 2024).
•	Choice of algorithm & distance: 
o	k means / k medoids, agglomerative hierarchical clustering, DBSCAN, and SOM based hybrids are most common (Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Pelekis et al., 2023; Prompook et al., 2025; Benítez & Díez, 2022; Michalakopoulos et al., 2023; Cen et al., 2022).
o	Euclidean distance emphasizes magnitude regimes, cosine or DTW emphasize shape/temporal regimes (Pelekis et al., 2023; Prompook et al., 2025).
•	Representative profiles (centroids/medoids): Cluster centroids or medoids are used as representative operating days for tariff design, DR targeting, or reduced order studies (Pelekis et al., 2023; Prompook et al., 2025; Aschidamini et al., 2024; Benítez & Díez, 2022; Cen et al., 2022).
2. Scenario reduction for operational and planning models
•	Representative periods for system/IES models: Time series of demand and renewables are clustered to select a small set of representative periods (RPs) used in unit commitment or integrated energy system optimization (Gao et al., 2024; Neustroev et al., 2025).
•	k medoids and hierarchical clustering are standard; newer approaches (e.g., density canopy + k medoids, hull clustering with extreme point RPs) better capture rare but critical regimes while keeping model size small (Gao et al., 2024; Neustroev et al., 2025).
3. Operating condition regimes beyond load
•	Power quality & virtual power plant states: PQ indicators over weeks are clustered (k means, Ward’s method) to identify distinct PQ operating conditions of a VPP and assess compliance with standards (Aksan et al., 2021).
•	Protection / fault regimes: Voltage–current features or network conditions are clustered to define fault/operating regimes for adaptive protection settings or unsupervised fault pattern discovery (Matschinske et al., 2025; Alasali et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Across applications, clustering converts large multivariate time series into a limited set of representative operating regimes (profiles or periods), typically via feature extraction, choice of task appropriate distance, and selection of cluster centroids/medoids as the regimes used in planning, operation, and analysis.

•	What literature supports the use of representative states to reduce complexity in screening or simulation studies?
Representative operating states (periods, scenarios, profiles) are widely used to cut computation while preserving accuracy in screening and simulation studies.
Power  and energy system planning & screening
•	Representative periods/days/weeks for capacity expansion and planning
Capacity expansion models and integrated energy systems often replace full year hourly data with a limited set of representative periods selected by clustering or optimization, then validate errors in costs, dispatch, and storage sizing against full resolution runs (Barbar & Mallapragada, 2022; Liu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2024; Raya-Tapia et al., 2024; Neustroev et al., 2025).
•	Barbar & Mallapragada use autoencoder based dimensionality reduction plus clustering to select representative periods and show that reduced models closely match full space CEM outcomes while greatly lowering computational burden (Barbar & Mallapragada, 2022).
•	Liu et al. propose a closed loop representative day framework for generation and transmission expansion with demand response; 8 representative days reduce renewable energy error and total cost error versus full year simulation, improving planning accuracy and computational efficiency (Liu et al., 2024).
•	Li et al. select representative weeks and hours in an electricity–hydrogen integrated system, cutting life cycle cost error (>10% lower average error vs conventional RTP methods) while enabling detailed multi timescale storage modeling with far fewer operating points (Li et al., 2024).
•	Comparative work on representative weeks (k means, DTW, combinatorial) quantifies trade offs between net load representation error and objective function error, explicitly justifying representative week use for tractable optimization (Raya-Tapia et al., 2024).
Scenario reduction / smart sampling
Purpose	Method	Complexity benefit	Citations
Screening power flow / planning scenarios	Hierarchical Latin Hypercube “smart sampling” of representative scenarios	Maintains operation envelope with small subset of cases (Sun et al., 2021)	(Sun et al., 2021)
Stochastic IES planning	k means scenario reduction for wind/solar/load	Reduces scenario count while preserving cost optimal configs	(Lin et al., 2022; Zeng et al., 2021)
Representative wind power trajectories	Two stage generation & reduction to daily scenarios	Fewer time series scenarios with accurate stochastic behavior	(He et al., 2022)
Power flow screening	k means to pick average and outlier “representative points”	Targets both typical and stress conditions for simulation	(Cook et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Examples of representative-state methods reducing simulation size.
Advanced representative state selection
•	GAN based and LSTM GAN methods learn latent clusters and generate representative days for multi series renewable datasets, achieving 40–70% lower error vs conventional aggregation while greatly improving planning tractability (Rastgoo et al., 2024).
•	Hull clustering with extreme point representative periods plus blending improves regret and runtime versus standard centroid based RPs in energy system optimization (Neustroev et al., 2025).
•	Reduced order and component based ROMs similarly replace full high fidelity dynamics with low dimensional state surrogates, enabling many query simulations of complex multiphysics systems with orders of magnitude speedups (Kim & Kramer, 2025; Huang et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Across planning, stochastic optimization, and dynamic simulation, clustering, scenario reduction, representative period selection, and reduced order modeling all support using representative states to screen operating conditions or run tractable simulations, with numerous studies quantifying small cost/accuracy loss for large computational gains.

3.5.1 Feature Standardization and State-Space Definition
•	What best practices exist for feature scaling and standardization before clustering in engineering multivariate datasets?
Feature scaling is usually essential before distance based clustering, with Z score or min–max scaling as the main defaults, chosen based on units, outliers, and algorithm.
Core best practices before clustering
1. Always address heterogeneous units and ranges
•	When features use different units or ranges, scaling markedly improves k means clustering quality (accuracy, precision, recall, F1) compared with raw data (Wongoutong, 2024; Starovoitov & Golub, 2021).
•	If all features share the same unit and similar range, scaling changes results little for k means, but is still benign (Wongoutong, 2024).
•	Reviews recommend transforming all quantitative features to a common scale before any distance based clustering (k means, k medoids, hierarchical with Euclidean, fuzzy c means) (Wongoutong, 2024; Hashemi et al., 2023; Wani, 2024; Starovoitov & Golub, 2021).
2. Choose scaling to match data characteristics
Situation / goal	Recommended practice	Citations
Mixed units, roughly Gaussian, moderate outliers	Z score standardization (zero mean, unit variance) is consistently strong for k means (Wongoutong, 2024; Demir & Şahin, 2024; Starovoitov & Golub, 2021)	
Strong skewness / heavy tails	Use log, Box–Cox, Yeo–Johnson before or alongside scaling to stabilize variance (Demir & Şahin, 2024)	
Bounded features or non Gaussian	Min–max or percentile/quantile scaling works well for distance based models (Wongoutong, 2024; Ahsan et al., 2021; Hasan et al., 2025)	
Extreme outliers	Robust scalers (median/IQR) or percentile transforms improve robustness (Wongoutong, 2024; Ahsan et al., 2021; Demir & Şahin, 2024)	
Figure 1: When to favor different scaling schemes.
3. Integrate scaling with feature engineering
•	For engineered multivariate data (e.g., sensor or geo engineering variables), preprocessing pipelines often combine outlier filtering, normalization, and distributional transforms to stabilize clustering and subsequent analysis (Zhang & Parnell, 2022; Shahri et al., 2024).
•	Reviews emphasize there is no universally best normalization, so comparing several scalers and validating via internal indices (Silhouette, Davies–Bouldin) is recommended (Ahsan et al., 2021; Wani, 2024; Starovoitov & Golub, 2021).
Conclusion
For engineering multivariate datasets, cluster only after: (1) harmonizing units and ranges, (2) selecting Z score, min–max, or robust/transform based scaling according to skewness and outliers, and (3) empirically checking clustering validity across alternative scalers rather than assuming one method is universally optimal.

•	How should the state space be defined when combining voltage, current, loading, and derived proxy variables?
Defining a joint state space for voltage, current, loading, and proxies
The key is to define a physically meaningful, low redundancy state vector that respects network structure and the intended use (estimation, control, or clustering).
1. Start from a physically coherent base state
Two common, well founded choices:
•	Bus centric state: voltage magnitudes/angles at buses, with currents and loading treated as measurements/features derived from them (Bhusal et al., 2021; Dabush et al., 2023; Nguyen et al., 2021).
•	Branch centric state: node voltages plus branch active/reactive currents as state variables, which greatly simplifies measurement functions and avoids transforming voltage/current measurements (Choe et al., 2025).
Branch centric state vectors of the form
x = [U_bus, I_P, I_Q]
naturally combine voltage, current and loading information and work well even with many branches (Choe et al., 2025).
2. Add derived “proxy” variables as features, not new states
Proxy variables (e.g., loading indices, voltage deviation indices, power factors, congestion or stress indicators) should generally be:
•	Algebraic functions of the physical state and topology, e.g. line loading = |S_ij|/S_ij,max, voltage deviation = |V_i – V_ref| (Wei et al., 2025; Li et al., 2023; Nguyen et al., 2021).
•	Included in an augmented feature vector for learning or clustering, while keeping the underlying dynamical state minimal to retain identifiability and numerical robustness (Cecati et al., 2022; Li et al., 2023; Nguyen et al., 2021).
For example, a clustering/operating state feature vector might be:
s = [U_bus, I_P, I_Q, line_loading, voltage_deviation, N 1 indices, topology embeddings] (Wei et al., 2025; Li et al., 2023; Nguyen et al., 2021).
3. Respect topology and structure
•	Encode network connectivity explicitly (e.g., via graph based embeddings over buses/lines) so that the state captures spatial relationships between voltages, currents, and loading (Wei et al., 2025; Dabush et al., 2023).
•	Descriptor or modular state space formulations can preserve subsystem states (converter, line, load) while connecting them consistently, which helps interpret which components drive a given operating state (Li et al., 2023; Cecati et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Define the true state in terms of voltages and currents (bus and/or branch), derive loading and proxy indices from that state and topology, and then build an augmented feature vector for clustering or learning—carefully avoiding redundant or non identifiable additions.
References

Cecati, F., Zhu, R., Liserre, M., & Wang, X. (2022). Nonlinear Modular State-Space Modeling of Power-Electronics-Based Power Systems. IEEE Transactions on Power Electronics, 37, 6102-6115. https://doi.org/10.1109/tpel.2021.3127746

•	What criteria are used to choose features that preserve physical interpretability while supporting stable clustering?
Choose features that are physically meaningful, low redundancy, and stably discriminative across resamples to support interpretable clustering.
1. Physical interpretability criteria
•	Direct physical meaning: Prefer raw or simply derived variables (e.g., load factor, voltage deviation, power factor) whose effect can be explained to domain experts, rather than opaque embeddings (Bonifati et al., 2022; Bruce, 2023).
•	Low dimensionality: Interpretable clustering systems explicitly constrain the number of features used so humans can inspect and reason about cluster definitions (Bonifati et al., 2022; Shati et al., 2025).
•	Diversity, not duplication: Interpretable feature sets should cover diverse aspects of the system while avoiding near duplicates; diversity is emphasized as a core interpretability property (Bonifati et al., 2022; Kuzudisli et al., 2023).
•	Domain-relevant structure: Features that encode meaningful temporal or spectral patterns (e.g., dominant cycles, envelopes) are favored over generic statistics in time series clustering (Bonifati et al., 2022; Bruce, 2023; López-Oriona et al., 2021).
2. Criteria for stable, cluster supporting features
•	Cluster discriminativeness: Features should yield well separated clusters (high AMI/NMI, or clear separation in feature space); methods like SC FS select variables with high R² w.r.t. provisional cluster labels (Liu et al., 2022).
•	Stability across resampling and hyperparameters: 
o	Stability based feature selection retains variables that are consistently weighted as important across distance metrics or repeated runs (Zhang & De Amorim, 2025; Bodinier et al., 2025).
o	Stability measures (e.g., intersection/overlap of selected sets, “stably selected” features) are used to quantify robustness (Huang, 2021; Faletto & Bien, 2022; Bodinier et al., 2025).
•	Robustness to noise and correlations: 
o	Grouping correlated features and choosing representative ones improves stability and reduces redundancy (Huang, 2021; Kuzudisli et al., 2023; Dökeroğlu et al., 2022).
o	Random subspace/ensemble methods and consensus criteria can identify stable clusters under feature perturbations (Belucci et al., 2025; Bodinier et al., 2025).
Example selection workflow
1.	Start from physically meaningful raw/derived variables.
2.	Remove highly correlated or redundant features via clustering or correlation analysis, keeping representatives (Huang, 2021; Kuzudisli et al., 2023; Jung et al., 2023; Dökeroğlu et al., 2022).
3.	Run clustering + feature selection; retain features that both maximize cluster quality and are stably selected across resamples/parameter settings (Bonifati et al., 2022; Zhang & De Amorim, 2025; Huang, 2021; Faletto & Bien, 2022; Bodinier et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Features should be chosen for clear physical meaning, diversity and low redundancy, and empirically demonstrated discriminative power and selection stability, balancing human interpretability with robust clustering performance.

3.5.2 Selection of Clustering Algorithm and Number of Clusters
•	What clustering algorithms are most suitable for robust and interpretable operating-state identification in power-system data?
For operating-state identification, robust and interpretable clustering typically relies on medoid /hierarchical methods, Ward/k means for time series states, and GMMs when probabilistic states are needed.
Algorithm families most used in power system operating state/state like tasks
1. K means / Ward for typical system states and profiles
•	For temporal aggregation into typical hourly system states, a systematic benchmark finds k means and Ward hierarchical clustering to be the most effective across realism and model error metrics, compressing time series to <1% of points while preserving system behavior (Kittel et al., 2022).
•	Similar conclusions appear in large bibliometric reviews: k means (plus variants and BIRCH) is the dominant workhorse in modern power system applications due to scalability and simplicity (Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Pelekis et al., 2023).
•	Ward’s method offers a dendrogram plus good compactness–separation trade off, which aids visual interpretability of operating states (Kittel et al., 2022).
2. K medoids and medoid based variants for robustness
•	K medoids is repeatedly preferred where outliers, discrete events, or mixed uncertainties (e.g., outages + loads + wind) are present, because clusters are represented by real scenarios/periods and the method is less sensitive to extremes (Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2024).
•	In probabilistic power flow and OPF, k medoids based scenario reduction provides high accuracy with much lower computational cost than Monte Carlo, while handling correlated and discrete variables (Martins et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023).
•	Medoid representatives (actual days or scenarios) make state clusters physically interpretable to operators.
3. Gaussian mixture models (GMMs) for probabilistic operating states
•	For load/asset stratification, GMMs capture heterogeneous, overlapping regimes and provide soft membership probabilities, which are valuable for borderline or transitional operating states (Mendoza-Vitonera et al., 2025; Meng et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2022).
•	In distribution system load stratification, GMMs outperform k means and DBSCAN in modeling irregular patterns and yield more coherent, stable strata (Mendoza-Vitonera et al., 2025).
4. Hybrid and advanced frameworks focused on interpretability
•	PCA + (density canopies + k medoids) or similar hybrids improve scenario representativeness and clustering quality for integrated energy system operation, with much lower optimization error vs full year simulations (Gao et al., 2024).
•	Ensemble clustering with explainable AI has been used for load profiling, explicitly trading some “pure” optimality for clusters that match utility operational needs (Michalakopoulos et al., 2023; Sarmas et al., 2024).
Summary table
Goal in operating state ID	Best suited approaches	Citations
Fast, scalable typical states	k means, Ward	(Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Pelekis et al., 2023; Kittel et al., 2022)
Robust to outliers, real representative days/scenarios	k medoids, medoid based fuzzy/entropy methods	(Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2024; D’Urso et al., 2023)
Probabilistic / overlapping states	GMMs, mixture model clustering	(Mendoza-Vitonera et al., 2025; Meng et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2022)
High fidelity scenario reduction with interpretability	PCA/feature reduction + k medoids or Ward hybrids	(Martins et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Kittel et al., 2022; Gao et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Mapping operating-state goals to clustering families.
Conclusion
For robust and interpretable operating state identification in power system data:
•	use k means or Ward where speed and simple, crisp typical states suffice;
•	prefer k medoids/medoid based hierarchical or fuzzy methods when robustness to outliers and physically meaningful representative periods/scenarios are critical;
•	adopt GMMs or mixture models when probabilistic, overlapping operating states are needed for downstream risk aware decisions.

•	How do studies justify the choice between k-medoids, k-means, hierarchical clustering, or Gaussian mixture models in similar applications?
Most studies justify the choice empirically (via benchmarks and validity indices) plus data/goal driven arguments (distributional assumptions, robustness, interpretability, and speed).
Common Justifications by Method Class
1. K means vs k medoids (PAM)
•	Speed & scale: K means is preferred when data are large, numeric, roughly spherical, and speed is critical; it is simple and highly scalable (Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Herman et al., 2022; Muthmainah et al., 2024; Aljohani, 2024).
•	Robustness & interpretability: K medoids is chosen when outliers, non spherical clusters, or mixed distances matter, since it uses real data points as centers and can plug into arbitrary dissimilarities (Herman et al., 2022; Buaton & Solikhun, 2024; Coombes et al., 2021; Pakpahan et al., 2025).
•	Empirical comparison: Many papers run both and pick the one with better internal indices (Silhouette, DBI, CH) or external metrics (ARI, purity) (Herman et al., 2022; Muthmainah et al., 2024; Buaton & Solikhun, 2024; Perveen et al., 2025; Pakpahan et al., 2025). Often k means wins on compact, well separated numeric data, whereas k medoids gives more balanced clusters and is less sensitive to extremes (Herman et al., 2022; Muthmainah et al., 2024; Buaton & Solikhun, 2024; Pakpahan et al., 2025).
2. Hierarchical clustering
•	Performance on mixed/clinical data: Simulation benchmarks on heterogeneous clinical data show agglomerative hierarchical clustering with Ward’s linkage often outperforms k medoids and SOM in ARI and silhouette width, especially with appropriate mixed type distances (e.g., DAISY, Gower) (Preud'homme et al., 2021; Coombes et al., 2021).
•	Justification: Chosen when cluster tree structure, flexibility in k, and strong performance on mixed data are desired; authors justify by benchmark dominance and visual interpretability of dendrograms (Preud'homme et al., 2021; Coombes et al., 2021).
3. Gaussian mixture models (GMMs)
•	Probabilistic / soft clustering: GMMs are preferred when clusters are assumed (or observed) to be approximately elliptical with overlapping membership, and probabilities or likelihood based model selection (AIC/BIC) are needed (Preud'homme et al., 2021; Ghorvei et al., 2024; Santoso & Haw, 2023; De Santi et al., 2025).
•	Comparative evidence: Studies show GMMs or related model based methods can outperform distance based methods in ARI when the data follow (or approximate) the assumed distribution (Preud'homme et al., 2021; Pal & Heumann, 2022; Ghorvei et al., 2024; Santoso & Haw, 2023), and they are explicitly chosen to overcome k means’ hard assignments in overlapping or multimodal settings (Santoso & Haw, 2023; De Santi et al., 2025).
4. Multi criteria, application driven choice
Criterion used in papers	Typical method justification	Citations
Distributional assumptions	GMM or other model based if (approx.) Gaussian/normal multinomial	(Preud'homme et al., 2021; Pal & Heumann, 2022; Ghorvei et al., 2024; Santoso & Haw, 2023; De Santi et al., 2025)
Data type / distance	k medoids/HC with flexible dissimilarities for mixed or time series data	(Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Preud'homme et al., 2021; AlMahamid & Grolinger, 2022; Buaton & Solikhun, 2024; Coombes et al., 2021; Pakpahan et al., 2025)
Robustness to outliers	k medoids or HC over k means	(Herman et al., 2022; Buaton & Solikhun, 2024; Coombes et al., 2021)
Need for soft membership	GMM (or fuzzy c means) over k means/k medoids/HC	(Ghorvei et al., 2024; Santoso & Haw, 2023; De Santi et al., 2025)
Computational load	k means (or k means++/mini batch) for very large n	(Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Aljohani, 2024; Ling & Weiling, 2025)
Empirical validation	Choose algorithm with best ARI, silhouette, DBI, purity on the task	(Preud'homme et al., 2021; AlMahamid & Grolinger, 2022; Muthmainah et al., 2024; Coombes et al., 2021; Ghorvei et al., 2024; Perveen et al., 2025; Pakpahan et al., 2025; De Santi et al., 2025)
Figure 1: How studies map data and goals to clustering choices.
Conclusion
Across applications, authors rarely justify clustering choice on theory alone; they typically combine assumptions about data (geometry, noise, variable types) and task needs (soft labels, interpretability, speed) with head to head comparisons using validity indices and, when available, external labels or domain performance metrics.
References

•	What statistical and engineering criteria are commonly used to select the optimal number of clusters?
Cluster number is usually chosen by combining internal validity indices with problem specific engineering criteria, rather than any single “optimal” statistic.
1. Statistical / Data Driven Criteria
Internal validity indices (distance based)
Commonly run over a range of k, then pick k that optimizes the index:
•	Silhouette coefficient (maximize) – balances within cluster cohesion and between cluster separation; widely used and often more informative than many alternatives for convex clusters (Ashari et al., 2023; Chicco et al., 2025; Hassan et al., 2024).
•	Davies–Bouldin index (minimize) – lower values indicate better separation and compactness (Ashari et al., 2023; Chicco et al., 2025; Oyewole & Thopil, 2022; Andayani et al., 2025).
•	Calinski–Harabasz index (maximize) – ratio of between cluster to within cluster dispersion, frequently found effective and robust (Ashari et al., 2023; Sowan et al., 2022; Oyewole & Thopil, 2022; Hassan et al., 2024; Karna & Gibert, 2021).
•	Dunn, C index, Shannon entropy, Gap statistic, WB, DS, etc. – used as complementary checks, with differing sensitivity to shape, density, and overlap (Chicco et al., 2025; Ashari et al., 2023; Chicco et al., 2025; Oyewole & Thopil, 2022; Awong & Zielińska, 2023; Hassan et al., 2024; Saidah et al., 2022; Karna & Gibert, 2021).
Model based and information criteria
•	AIC/BIC, ICL, MICL for Gaussian mixtures or latent class models; lower values indicate better trade off between fit and parsimony (Li et al., 2021; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022).
•	Eigen gap or multimodality gap in spectral methods (jump in Laplacian eigenvalues) (Li et al., 2021; Yu et al., 2022).
Stability based / ensemble criteria
•	Resampling or perturbation to choose k giving most stable partitions (Miller & Alexander, 2025; Yu et al., 2022; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022).
•	Ensembled indices (e.g., ECVI combining Silhouette, DB, CH, Gap) to reduce dependence on any single metric (Sowan et al., 2022; Zhipeng et al., 2025).
2. Engineering / Domain Driven Criteria
In engineering and applied settings, k is further constrained by:
•	Interpretability and use case: clusters must be distinguishable and actionable for operators; extremely small or tiny clusters are often rejected despite index scores (Miller & Alexander, 2025; Oyewole & Thopil, 2022; Awong & Zielińska, 2023; Karna & Gibert, 2021; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022).
•	Parsimony and complexity trade off: prefer simpler solutions beyond which gains in indices plateau (elbow heuristics on WSS, BIC, or other scores) (Ashari et al., 2023; Li et al., 2021; Kallel et al., 2025; Karna & Gibert, 2021; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022).
•	Cluster size and practicality: minimum population share per cluster for reliable statistics and control actions (Awong & Zielińska, 2023; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022).
Example: Combined selection
Criterion type	Example metrics / rules	Role in choosing k	Citations
Internal indices	Silhouette, DB, CH, Gap, WB	Rank k by cohesion/separation	(Chicco et al., 2025; Ashari et al., 2023; Chicco et al., 2025; Sowan et al., 2022; Oyewole & Thopil, 2022; Hassan et al., 2024; Saidah et al., 2022)
Model fit	BIC, AIC, ICL/MICL	Penalize overfitting; elbow on fit	(Li et al., 2021; Yu et al., 2022; Karna & Gibert, 2021; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022)
Stability	Resampling, ensemble ECVI	Prefer most stable k	(Miller & Alexander, 2025; Sowan et al., 2022; Yu et al., 2022; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022)
Engineering	Interpretability, min cluster size, operational usefulness	Final adjustment of k	(Miller & Alexander, 2025; Oyewole & Thopil, 2022; Awong & Zielińska, 2023; Karna & Gibert, 2021; Lezhnina & Kismihók, 2022)
Figure 1: Key statistical and domain criteria for k selection.
Conclusion
Across domains, the “optimal” number of clusters is usually selected by scanning k with multiple validity indices, model fit and stability checks, then finalizing k based on interpretability, parsimony, and engineering usefulness rather than statistics alone.

3.5.3 Interpretation of Representative Operating-State Medoids
•	How should cluster medoids or representative states be interpreted and reported in power-system operational studies?
Cluster medoids/representative states should be treated and reported as compressed, physically interpretable operating scenarios with clear probabilistic weights and limits of representativeness.
Conceptual Interpretation
•	In operational or probabilistic studies, K medoids/representatives are scenarios that best summarize groups of similar operating points, preserving joint behavior of loads, RES, outages, or frequency patterns while reducing scenario count (Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024).
•	Each medoid is not an “average day” in a statistical sense, but the actual sampled state that minimizes within cluster dissimilarity, including discrete features (e.g., outage status) and mixed data types (Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Andrews & Jain, 2022).
How to Report Them
1. Link to original space and physics
•	Report for each representative state: underlying variables (power injections, outages, RES outputs, frequency indices, etc.), and any derived system indicators (OPF cost, losses, reliability indices) (Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024).
•	Explain the distance metric and feature scaling so readers know which aspects of operation drive similarity (e.g., Gower/COMB distance, correlation based metrics, frequency quality indices) (Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024; Andrews & Jain, 2022).
2. Attach probabilities and coverage
•	Provide cluster sizes or scenario weights so that each medoid is clearly associated with a probability mass and can be used in reliability, OPF, or benchmarking calculations (Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024).
•	Quantify approximation error vs. full scenario set (e.g., CDFs of losses or flows, reliability indices) as cluster number varies, to justify that medoids preserve key statistics (Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024).
3. Interpret behaviorally
•	Translate medoids into human readable operating narratives, e.g., “high wind/low load with one major line out,” “medium fluctuations in short term frequency,” or “inefficient, inflexible building group” (Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024; Andrews & Jain, 2022; Michalakopoulos et al., 2024).
•	Use tables or rule based/xAI models to express which features distinguish clusters, improving interpretability for operators and policymakers (Kallel et al., 2025; Andrews & Jain, 2022; Michalakopoulos et al., 2024).
Example reporting structure
Element to report	Purpose	Citations
Feature set & distance metric	Defines what “similar” means	(Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024; Andrews & Jain, 2022)
Medoid state vector & derived KPIs	Physical meaning	(Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024)
Cluster weight / frequency	Link to probabilities	(Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024)
Error vs. full set	Defend reduction	(Kiel et al., 2024; Badoozadeh et al., 2023; Martins et al., 2024; Mohammadi et al., 2024)
Narrative / rules for cluster	Operational insight	(Kallel et al., 2025; Martins et al., 2024; Andrews & Jain, 2022; Michalakopoulos et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Key elements for reporting representative operating states.
Conclusion
In Q1 level operational studies, cluster medoids should be presented as physically grounded, probabilistically weighted operating scenarios, with transparent feature choices, distance metrics, and validation against the unreduced scenario set, and interpreted in plain operational language rather than left as opaque cluster IDs.

3.6 Reduced-Order Resonance Risk Screening Layer
•	What reduced-order or simplified physical models have been used to screen resonance or power-quality risk when full EMT simulation is unavailable?
Screening resonance and power quality risk without full EMT is commonly done using reduced order converter, network, and admittance/impedance models tailored to the frequency band of interest.
Main classes of simplified physical models
1. Reduced order converter / IBR models
•	Average or controlled current / controlled voltage source models: Replace switching with a time averaged current or voltage source plus fundamental frequency control dynamics; preserve fault and low frequency behavior with much larger time steps (Muñoz et al., 2022; Sano et al., 2022).
•	Model order reduced VSC models from EMT to RMS: Start from detailed averaged EMT model, then systematically discard fast states based on AC side admittance sensitivity; produce a small set of standard reduced order VSC models valid over specified frequency ranges for small signal and resonance screening (Grdenić et al., 2023).
•	Generic IBG EMT models using controlled current sources: Omit detailed switching but keep key control functions (PLL, current limits, FRT, negative sequence injection) to study short circuit and short term stability in EMT resolution with low computational load (Muñoz et al., 2022).
2. Network and line equivalents
•	Frequency dependent line/cable equivalents: Multi conductor transmission line models with simplified parameter calculation and frequency dependent modifications (e.g., improved Bergeron type models) capture broadband resonance while remaining suitable for real time or large system simulation (Cao et al., 2025).
•	Reduced AC/DC distribution system circuits: Parameter equivalent transformations aggregate lines, filters, and constant power loads into a low order circuit that preserves high frequency and DC bus dynamics for stability and oscillation assessment (Jiang et al., 2024; Liu et al., 2023).
3. Admittance/impedance based reduced networks for harmonic & resonance scans
•	Aggregated admittance based models (ABM): Represent each converter, load, and line as a frequency domain I/ABM, aggregate them, then apply model order reduction in the frequency domain, enabling efficient small signal and resonance analysis of large converter dominated grids (Safavizadeh et al., 2025).
•	Frequency dependent IBR admittance in reduced transmission–distribution models: Use a reduced UK wide network with IBRs modeled by frequency dependent impedances plus probabilistic harmonic injections to screen harmonic amplification and local resonances before detailed EMT (Deng & Todeschini, 2024).
4. System strength / overvoltage screening indices
•	Generalized short circuit ratio for TOV (gSCR TOV): An RMS level equivalent that characterizes converter–grid interaction and provides a scalar screening metric for identifying buses at high temporary overvoltage risk, to be followed by targeted EMT studies (Xin et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Across studies, reduced order converter admittance models, frequency dependent line/circuit equivalents, and system strength indices are key tools to pre screen resonance and power quality risk when full, system wide EMT simulation is impractical, reserving detailed EMT only for the highest risk locations.

•	How can operational stress variables be translated into a physically grounded harmonic resonance risk screening layer?
Operational stress variables can be mapped to harmonic resonance risk by linking them to changes in network impedance/admittance and excitation strength, then using simplified spectral or state estimation layers to screen risk.
1. Physically meaningful risk drivers
Operational stress variables (e.g., feeder loading, switching state, capacitor status, DG output) influence two physical levers:
•	Network resonant characteristics: Topology and component states change modal impedances and resonance frequencies; resonance severity indices can be defined from modal analysis across load profiles and configurations (Ansari et al., 2025).
•	Excitation strength at critical frequencies: Nonlinear loads and converters increase harmonic current where network impedance is high, driving overvoltages and equipment stress (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Shen et al., 2024).
A screening layer should therefore map stress variables → (a) approximate impedance peaks, (b) expected harmonic injection, then combine them into a resonance severity metric.
2. Example architectures for a screening layer
A. Impedance / mode-based layer
•	Use offline modal or frequency scan studies under representative loading and switching scenarios to build a resonance severity index as a function of operating point (Ansari et al., 2025).
•	In operation, approximate the current mode (e.g., feeder loading pattern, capacitor/transformer tap state) and interpolate the pre computed index to flag “high gain” frequency ranges and buses.
B. Measurement-enhanced, physically grounded layer
•	Use spectral indicators from voltage/current at a few points (STFT, grouped harmonics) to detect when distortion rises at frequencies coinciding with known network impedance peaks, confirming resonance rather than source distortion alone (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021).
•	Physics aware harmonic state estimation can then infer unmeasured harmonic voltages/currents across the grid using network structure and sparse PQ meters, enabling system wide risk maps in near real time (Mack et al., 2024).
C. Probabilistic / fuzzy risk mapping
•	Historical harmonic data and operational states can feed probability distributions of harmonic indices, with fuzzy risk membership functions (low/medium/high) to translate predicted PQ indices into actionable risk levels (Shen et al., 2024).
Table: From stress variables to resonance risk
Layer step	Example implementation	Sources
Map state → impedance/modes	Offline modal analysis vs. loading/topology	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Ansari et al., 2025; Mack et al., 2024)
Map state → harmonic injection	Profiles of nonlinear loads/DG vs. output	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Shao et al., 2023)
Combine into risk index	Severity / observability / fuzzy indices	(Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Shen et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025; Mack et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Structuring a resonance risk screening pipeline.
Conclusion
A physically grounded resonance screening layer treats operational stress variables as proxies for where the system’s impedance peaks move and how strongly they’re excited, using pre computed modal behavior, sparse harmonic measurements, and probabilistic or fuzzy indices to provide fast, system wide risk flags before detailed EMT studies.

Shao, H., Henriques, R., Morais, H., & Tedeschi, E. (2023). Power quality monitoring in electric grid integrating offshore wind energy: A review. Renewable and Sustainable Energy Reviews. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.rser.2023.114094


•	What methodological justifications are needed to defend a reduced-order resonance screening approach in a Q1 journal?
To defend a reduced order resonance screening method in a Q1 journal, it must be positioned as a credible, validated approximation with clearly bounded accuracy, not as a surrogate for full EMT.
1. Explicit modelling assumptions and reduction rationale
•	Clearly state why the full model is intractable (size, time steps) and how the reduced model preserves the physics relevant to resonance: frequency band, dominant modes, and coupling mechanisms (Gu et al., 2018; Touz'e et al., 2021; Opreni et al., 2021).
•	Justify the chosen reduction technique (e.g., static/implicit condensation, modal/invariant manifold reduction, normal forms) with references showing when these methods are accurate (slow/fast separation, moderate amplitudes, limited internal resonances) (Touz'e et al., 2021; Xiao et al., 2023; Opreni et al., 2021; Nicolaidou et al., 2021).
•	Discuss limitations: which nonlinearities, switching effects, or higher order resonances are intentionally neglected and under what operating range this is acceptable (Touz'e et al., 2021; Opreni et al., 2021; Abuishmais et al., 2023; Nicolaidou et al., 2021).
Example justification elements (to make explicit)
•	“Number of master modes chosen to cover the excitation frequency band” (Touz'e et al., 2021).
•	How approximate internal resonances are handled or explicitly excluded (Touz'e et al., 2021; Opreni et al., 2021).
2. Verification, validation, and error quantification
•	Provide systematic verification/validation (V&V): 
o	Compare ROM predictions of resonance frequencies, modal impedances, and risk indices against full models or measurements under multiple conditions (Plienis et al., 2023; Cartiel et al., 2025; Gu et al., 2018; Zeng et al., 2022; Gobat et al., 2022).
o	Use targeted metrics: frequency error, amplification ratio error, missed/false resonances, computational speed up.
•	Use established ROM validation concepts: ensuring accurate static behavior as a prerequisite for dynamic accuracy (Xiao et al., 2023), error metrics over an energy/displacement range (Xiao et al., 2023), or indicators detecting when important modal interactions are not captured (Nicolaidou et al., 2021).
•	Situate this within general V&V/UQ frameworks, explaining how uncertainties in parameters and operating conditions are treated or bounded (Riedmaier et al., 2020; Li et al., 2018).
Example evidence structure
Requirement	How to justify in paper	Citations
Physical fidelity	Show ROM captures key modes, resonance bands, sensitivities	(Touz'e et al., 2021; Gu et al., 2018; Lokar et al., 2021; Li et al., 2018)
Accuracy bounds	Quantify errors vs. full model/measurements across scenarios	(Plienis et al., 2023; Gobat et al., 2022; Cartiel et al., 2025; Zeng et al., 2022; Xiao et al., 2023; Abuishmais et al., 2023)
Robustness to uncertainty	Probabilistic or parametric studies on resonance indices	(Li et al., 2018; Riedmaier et al., 2020)
Efficiency	Demonstrate orders of magnitude speed up vs. full analysis	(Gobat et al., 2022; Touz'e et al., 2021; Cartiel et al., 2025; Gu et al., 2018; Xiao et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Core methodological pillars for Q1 level ROM justification.
3. Positioning as a screening tool, not a final predictor
•	Frame the method explicitly as a screening layer that narrows down scenarios/buses for subsequent detailed EMT or FE analysis, similar to modal/harmonic resonance mode analysis that accelerates but does not replace full simulations (Cartiel et al., 2025; Lokar et al., 2021; Li et al., 2018; Cartiel et al., 2023).
•	Demonstrate that all “high risk” cases identified by the full model are detected by the screening method (very low false negative rate), even if amplitudes are approximate (Plienis et al., 2023; Zeng et al., 2022; Li et al., 2018).
Conclusion
For a Q1 journal, a reduced order resonance screening approach must rest on transparent physical assumptions, state of the art reduction theory, rigorous V&V with quantified errors and uncertainty treatment, and a clearly delimited role as a computationally efficient screening layer feeding higher fidelity EMT or FEA studies.

3.6.1 Physical Rationale of the Reduced-Order Screening Model
•	What physical assumptions allow harmonic resonance risk to be screened using reduced-order representations instead of full waveform simulation?
Physical assumptions enabling reduced order screening of harmonic resonance risk
Harmonic resonance can be screened with reduced order, frequency domain or modal models instead of full waveform simulation when the system is approximately linear, time invariant, and dominated by a few resonant modes over the frequency band of interest.
1. Linearity and time invariance
•	Power system harmonic studies typically assume small signal operation around a steady operating point, so converters, lines, and filters can be cast as linear, time invariant (LTI) impedance/admittance models in the frequency domain (Peiris et al., 2025; Sakinci et al., 2022; Deng & Todeschini, 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2022).
•	Nonlinear switching and control are represented via averaged or small signal models; harmonics are treated as independent sinusoidal perturbations (superposition holds), allowing frequency scans or modal analysis rather than time domain EMT simulation (Peiris et al., 2025; Sakinci et al., 2022; Lokar et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2022).
2. Dominance of a few resonant modes (low effective order)
•	Network resonance behavior is governed by a small number of critical eigenmodes of the nodal admittance (or loop impedance) matrix; these modes identify dominant buses and parameters for amplification (Lokar et al., 2021).
•	In mechanical systems, spectral submanifold and nonlinear modal ROMs rely on the assumption that the dynamics near resonance live on a low dimensional invariant manifold spanned by a few interacting modes, even if the full FE model is very high dimensional (Li et al., 2021; Li & Haller, 2021; Touz'e et al., 2021).
•	Model reduction via static condensation or SSMs assumes time scale separation or at least that non retained modes remain quasi statically slaved to retained ones, except near internal resonances that must be explicitly included (Nicolaidou et al., 2021; Li et al., 2021; Li & Haller, 2021; Touz'e et al., 2021).
3. Narrowband and periodic excitation
•	When disturbance spectra are narrowband or discrete (integer harmonics) of a fundamental, resonance risk can be assessed by scanning impedances/eigenvalues at those discrete frequencies, avoiding broadband transients (Peiris et al., 2025; Deng & Todeschini, 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Chen et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2022).
•	For periodically forced nonlinear structures, periodic and quasi periodic steady responses can be obtained from the reduced dynamics without integrating full transient waveforms (Li et al., 2021; Li & Haller, 2021).
4. Weak coupling and aggregation assumptions
•	Harmonic contributions from many inverters/loads are often treated as independent injections into a linear network, enabling reduced network equivalents and aggregated impedance or harmonic source models (Peiris et al., 2025; Deng & Todeschini, 2024; Zheng et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2022).
Conclusion
Under small signal LTI behavior, dominance of a few resonant modes, narrowband harmonic forcing, and weakly coupled/aggregatable sources, harmonic resonance risk can be reliably screened using reduced order modal or impedance based models instead of full time domain waveform simulations.

•	How can system stiffness, loading stress, voltage sensitivity, and feeder interaction be combined into a resonance susceptibility concept?
Combining stiffness, stress, sensitivity, and interaction into a resonance susceptibility index
A practical resonance susceptibility concept can be built as a composite, mode specific risk index that weights how strongly a bus/feeder mode will be excited and how badly that excitation propagates and affects voltages.
Conceptual structure
For a given mode (frequency, eigenvector) or resonance condition:
1.	System stiffness term
Use a normalized inverse of voltage (or angle) stiffness, e.g. from multi bus strength or admittance eigenvalues: weak buses (low stiffness) get higher scores (Zhuang et al., 2025).
2.	Loading stress term
Represent as a loading ratio or FVSI type index at that bus/feeder: high loading, high reactive demand or proximity to voltage stability limit → higher stress (Sun et al., 2025; Mossie et al., 2025).
3.	Voltage sensitivity term
Use modal or sensitivity factors: e.g. ∂|V|/∂Q or participation factors of each bus in the resonant eigenmode; larger magnitude implies higher local amplification if resonance is excited (Mossie et al., 2025; Hatziargyriou et al., 2021; Zhuang et al., 2025).
4.	Feeder interaction term
Capture how easily energy/oscillations spread along and between feeders, for example through:
o	topological fragility / low redundancy metrics (Sun et al., 2025)- coupling or “energy flow” interaction factors between buses over time windows (Kwon et al., 2025)- zone level risk propagation weights (Sun et al., 2025)## Example normalized index
A mode specific susceptibility score at bus i could be:
[ RS_i = w_1 S_i + w_2 L_i + w_3 V_i + w_4 F_i ]
where each component is normalized to [0,1]:
•	(S_i): inverse stiffness (from multi bus strength or admittance eigenvalues) (Zhuang et al., 2025)- (L_i): loading stress index (e.g., load/rating, FVSI) (Sun et al., 2025; Mossie et al., 2025)- (V_i): voltage sensitivity/participation in the resonant mode (Mossie et al., 2025; Hatziargyriou et al., 2021; Zhuang et al., 2025)- (F_i): feeder interaction/spread potential from topology based risk metrics (Sun et al., 2025; Kwon et al., 2025)Weights (w_k) can be tuned empirically or via scenario based risk optimization (Sun et al., 2025).
Tabular view
Component	Example metric	Role in resonance susceptibility	Citations
Stiffness (S_i)	Multi bus strength, small signal eigenvalue	How easily voltages move	(Hatziargyriou et al., 2021; Zhuang et al., 2025)
Loading (L_i)	Load/rating, FVSI like index	How stressed local operating point is	(Sun et al., 2025; Mossie et al., 2025)
Sensitivity (V_i)	Modal voltage participation, ∂V/∂Q	Local amplification if excited	(Mossie et al., 2025; Hatziargyriou et al., 2021; Zhuang et al., 2025)
Interaction (F_i)	Topology risk, energy flow coupling	Propagation to other feeders	(Sun et al., 2025; Kwon et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Decomposition of a resonance susceptibility index into four physical factors.
Conclusion
By defining each of stiffness, loading stress, voltage sensitivity, and feeder interaction as normalized, mode aware terms and aggregating them in a weighted index, a coherent resonance susceptibility measure can be constructed that is physically interpretable and compatible with existing voltage stability and risk zoning methodologies.

•	What prior literature supports reduced-order risk screening models in power-system quality or stability studies?
Reduced-order methods are widely used and supported as effective tools for screening power quality and small signal stability risks in large power systems.
Key strands of supporting literature
1. Reduced-order modal analysis for oscillatory stability
•	A reduced order modal method is proposed for systems with thousands of wind turbines, limiting the matrix dimension to either the number of WTGs or the order of the individual WTG model, while still detecting risk and tracing instability sources (Du et al., 2021).
•	Distributed, reduced order eigen analysis using area level models and PMU data is used for inter area oscillation monitoring, instead of full system order models (Gusrialdi, 2025).
•	Impedance based modal analysis (MAI) replaces full state space models with port impedance matrices, yet recovers eigenvalues, sensitivities, and participation factors for high IBR systems (Zheng et al., 2025; Zheng et al., 2024; Zheng et al., 2025).
2. Impedance based and spectral methods as screening tools
•	System level SISO impedance based stability analysis translates multi port interactions into decoupled minor loops and uses Nyquist plots plus participation factors to identify risky modes without full EMT models (Yang & Sun, 2022).
•	Spectral impedance based probabilistic oscillatory stability analysis computes impedance based margin indices and their probability distributions, avoiding repeated eigenvalue solutions for wideband risk screening (Chen et al., 2025).
•	Extended impedance modal analysis decomposes inverter dynamics into a few equivalent admittance components (PLL vs current control) to locate critical loops without full converter state space models (Zheng et al., 2025).
3. Harmonic resonance and power quality screening
•	Harmonic resonance mode analysis (RMA) uses eigenpairs of the nodal admittance matrix to identify critical resonance modes and dominant buses, avoiding full waveform simulations; accelerated variants (MSIPI) make this practical for many test systems (Lokar et al., 2021; Cartiel et al., 2023; Shakeri et al., 2024).
•	Frequency dependent network equivalents provide reduced order harmonic models focused on selected regions, balancing accuracy and efficiency vs full network representation (Verduzco-Durán et al., 2024).
•	National scale harmonic studies (U.K. future grid) employ reduced transmission distribution equivalents with aggregated frequency dependent IBR models and probabilistic harmonic load flow for high level screening (Deng & Todeschini, 2024).
Conclusion
Across oscillatory stability and harmonic power quality studies, reduced order modal and impedance based methods are well established for early stage risk screening, consistently shown to approximate full waveform or full order analyses with far lower computational and modeling burden.

3.6.2 Construction of Ordinal Risk Labels
•	How are low-, medium-, and high-risk classes typically defined in engineering risk-screening frameworks?
Engineering risk-screening frameworks typically define low/medium/high risk using quantitative indices mapped to discrete bands, often calibrated to data, standards, and risk tolerance.
Common ways low / medium / high classes are defined
1. Direct thresholds on a composite risk index
Many frameworks compute a single risk index from severity, likelihood, and exposure, then partition it into 3 bands:
•	Road work zone safety in Sri Lanka: ML-derived risk index clustered with K means; resulting empirical bands: low (0–0.204), medium (0.204–0.298), high (0.306–1) (Madushanka et al., 2025).
•	Social-engineering vulnerability: a Risk Index (RI) from behavioral and network factors; high-risk defined as RI above a chosen threshold driven by the organization’s risk distribution and risk tolerance (Mondal, et al., 2025).
Thresholds are typically set by:
•	Unsupervised clustering (e.g., K means) to find natural groupings (Madushanka et al., 2025; Meng et al., 2025)- Or chosen quantiles (e.g., top 25% as high risk, bottom 25% low risk) (Phung et al., 2025)### 2. Banding of physical or operational metrics Some frameworks classify risk directly from key engineering quantities:
•	Drilling safety: three grade thresholds in friction and torque:
o	Grade I (low): friction 0–100 kN, torque 0–10 kN·m
o	Grade II (medium): 100–200 kN, 10–20 kN·m
o	Grade III (high): >200 kN, >20 kN·m (Meng et al., 2025).
•	UAV safety: low/medium/high risk assigned from kinetic energy and injury indices (AIS), with low-risk drones having low KE and AIS<2, medium at moderate KE/AIS, and high risk at AIS≥6 (Savaş, 2025).
3. Matrix of probability vs. consequence
Risk matrices combine likelihood (low/medium/high) and consequence (negligible–high):
•	Nuclear pipeline RI ISI: 7 matrix categories grouped into high (1–3), medium (4–5), low (6–7) risk regions (Wang et al., 2025).
•	Information security under ISO 27001: high-risk values are “unacceptable,” while low and medium risk are treated as acceptable (Chandra et al., 2022).
4. Percentile-based statistical classification
•	Software risk: modules ranked by risk ratio; 
o	High: RR ≥ 75th percentile (Q3)
o	Medium: Q1 ≤ RR < Q3
o	Low: RR < Q1 (Phung et al., 2025).
•	Soil heavy-metal pollution: thresholds set via mixture models; areas above pollution thresholds and with high exceedance probability classified as higher risk zones (Fei et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Across engineering domains, low/medium/high risk classes are typically defined by: (1) bands of a composite index; (2) bands on key physical metrics; (3) probability–consequence matrices; or (4) percentile cuts. Thresholds are often data-driven (clustering, quantiles, statistical models) and/or aligned with standards and explicit risk-tolerance policies, rather than purely ad hoc.

Wang, M., Zhang, B., Xu, J., & Chen, S. (2025). A Pilot Application Study on Risk-Informed In-Service Inspection Methods for Pipelines in HPR1000 Nuclear Power Plants: A Case Study of the RCV System. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18174753
Savaş, T. (2025). A Risk-Based Analysis of Lightweight Drones: Evaluating the Harmless Threshold Through Human-Centered Safety Criteria. Drones. https://doi.org/10.3390/drones9080517
•	What criteria should be used to construct ordinal resonance-risk labels from reduced-order physical screening outputs?
Ordinal resonance-risk labels should be based on physically grounded severity and likelihood metrics, calibrated and validated rather than chosen ad hoc.
Core criteria for constructing ordinal resonance-risk labels
1. Define risk as a function of severity × likelihood
From reduced-order screening outputs, use physically interpretable indicators such as:
•	Severity proxies: expected overvoltage/overcurrent, energy amplification factor, duration of resonance.
•	Likelihood proxies: distance to resonance in frequency space, damping margin, parameter uncertainty ranges (e.g., “variability” or “amplification factor” analogs) (Kumar et al., 2025; Cai et al., 2024).
Combine them into a composite risk index (e.g., weighted product or MCDM score) before banding into ordinal classes (Kumar et al., 2025; Cai et al., 2024).
2. Data  and expert driven banding into ordinal labels
Evidence from risk-screening frameworks suggests three complementary approaches:
•	Percentile/quantile cuts: Low/medium/high based on distribution of the composite index (e.g., tertiles or quartiles), tuned for predictive performance via cross validation when prediction is the goal (Pudjihartono et al., 2022).
•	Fuzzy linguistic levels: Map continuous scores to labels (Very Low…Very High) via overlapping membership functions, then aggregate with expert defined rules (Kumar et al., 2025).
•	Rule based criticality bins: Express criticality as percentage increases or normalized scores and assign bands like Very Low (0–25%), Low (25–50%), Moderate (50–75%), High (75–100%), Very High (>100%) (Kumar et al., 2025).
3. Calibration and validation criteria
•	Choose band thresholds to optimize screening goals (e.g., maximize sensitivity to true high risk cases, while keeping false positives manageable), using ROC or similar metrics (Zuo et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2024).
•	Validate that “high risk” labels correlate with observed adverse outcomes or higher failure/event rates in a reference set, analogous to how medical and hearing risk models confirm that high-risk groups carry multiples of baseline risk (Zuo et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2024).
4. Transparency and reproducibility
•	Document the exact index definition, threshold values, and any fuzzy sets or rules (Kumar et al., 2025; Cai et al., 2024).
•	Assess robustness: ensure that modest shifts in thresholds do not radically reshuffle elements between low/medium/high classes (Pudjihartono et al., 2022; Cai et al., 2024).
Example structure
Step	Criterion	Output	Sources
Compute index	Severity × likelihood metrics	Continuous risk score	(Kumar et al., 2025; Pudjihartono et al., 2022)
Map to labels	Quantiles / fuzzy sets / % bins	Very Low–Very High	(Kumar et al., 2025; Pudjihartono et al., 2022; Cai et al., 2024)
Validate	Outcome correlation, ROC/cross val	Tuned thresholds	(Zuo et al., 2023; Wang et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Key steps for ordinal resonance-risk labelling.
Conclusion
Construct resonance-risk labels by (1) defining a composite, physically meaningful risk index from reduced-order outputs, (2) mapping this index to ordered categories via data- and expert-informed thresholds or fuzzy sets, and (3) calibrating and validating those thresholds against performance and robustness criteria.
References

Zuo, D., Yang, L., Jin, Y., Qi, H., Liu, Y., & Ren, L. (2023). Machine learning-based models for the prediction of breast cancer recurrence risk. BMC Medical Informatics and Decision Making, 23. https://doi.org/10.1186/s12911-023-02377-z

Advancing Alzheimer’s disease risk prediction: development and validation of a machine learning-based preclinical screening model in a cross-sectional study. BMJ Open, 15. https://doi.org/10.1136/bmjopen-2024-092293

Artificial intelligence in the risk prediction models of cardiovascular disease and development of an independent validation screening tool: a systematic review. BMC Medicine, 22. https://doi.org/10.1186/s12916-024-03273-7
Wang, Y., Yao, X., Wang, D., Ye, C., & Xu, L. (2024). A machine learning screening model for identifying the risk of high-frequency hearing impairment in a general population. BMC Public Health, 24. https://doi.org/10.1186/s12889-024-18636-1
•	How have previous studies justified ordinal rather than binary risk categorization in infrastructure risk analytics?
Ordinal (multi-level) risk scales are favored because they capture gradations of risk needed for prioritization, resource allocation, and uncertainty handling that binary labels cannot provide.
Why move beyond binary risk in infrastructure analytics?
1. Prioritization and resource allocation across large asset stocks
Infrastructure managers rarely need only “act / don’t act”; they must rank many assets for inspection, detailed analysis, or intervention.
•	Italian bridge guidelines define multiple “classes of attention” (not just safe/unsafe) to prioritize which bridges move to higher assessment levels and funding, acknowledging limited resources and differing criticalities (Santarsiero et al., 2021).
•	A building-level flood index (FRIB) uses four levels (“very low”–“very high”) so low risk buildings can be excluded and high risk ones targeted, reducing unnecessary interventions while focusing on hotspots (Park et al., 2025).
2. Reflecting multi-dimensional, graded risk rather than a single threshold
Risk often integrates hazard, vulnerability, exposure, and importance; collapsing this into binary loses information.
•	FRIB combines hazard, exposure and vulnerability into a quantitative index, then uses natural breaks clustering to define several ordered levels that better discriminate complex patterns than a single depth threshold (Park et al., 2025).
•	Italian bridge classes of attention differentiate structural, seismic, flooding and landslide risks qualitatively, recognizing varying contributions and uncertainties that do not map cleanly to a yes/no decision (Santarsiero et al., 2021).
3. Operational decision rules and policy constraints
Guidelines often encode different management actions by risk band:
•	In the Italian bridge system, even relatively low degradation bridges can fall into intermediate classes triggering preliminary or detailed assessments, while only the highest classes push toward immediate intervention, giving a graded management ladder (Santarsiero et al., 2021).
Illustrative comparison
Aspect	Binary labels	Ordinal labels	Citations
Decision options	Act / no act	Tiered actions, triage	(Santarsiero et al., 2021; Park et al., 2025)
Info content	Single threshold	Preserves rank & gradation	(Santarsiero et al., 2021; Park et al., 2025; Angelelli et al., 2023)
Match to practice	Poor for portfolios	Suits budgeting & staging	(Santarsiero et al., 2021; Park et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Reasons ordinal scales outperform binary risk labels.
Conclusion
Prior studies justify ordinal instead of binary risk categories in infrastructure mainly to support ranking and triage of many assets, preserve nuanced information from multi-factor risk indices, and align risk levels with a spectrum of management actions under uncertainty and resource constraints.
References
Santarsiero, G., Masi, A., Picciano, V., & Digrisolo, A. (2021). The Italian Guidelines on Risk Classification and Management of Bridges: Applications and Remarks on Large Scale Risk Assessments. Infrastructures. https://doi.org/10.3390/infrastructures6080111
Park, H., Song, S., Kim, D., & Lee, S. (2025). Study on the Effectiveness of Multi-Dimensional Approaches to Urban Flood Risk Assessment. Applied Sciences. https://doi.org/10.3390/app15147777
Angelelli, M., Arima, S., Catalano, C., & Ciavolino, E. (2023). A robust statistical framework for cyber-vulnerability prioritisation under partial information in threat intelligence. Expert Syst. Appl., 255, 124572. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.eswa.2024.124572

3.6.3 Engineering Assumptions and Threshold Selection
•	What types of engineering assumptions are acceptable when setting thresholds in reduced-order screening models for power systems?
Acceptable assumptions are those that preserve the phenomena of interest, are explicitly stated, and are validated against higher fidelity models or data.
Categories of acceptable engineering assumptions
1. Time scale and bandwidth simplifications
•	Representing slow electromechanical dynamics with quasi static phasor or averaged models while neglecting fast electromagnetic or switching transients is acceptable if the study phenomena are low frequency and this scope is stated (Lara et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2021).
•	Narrow band or dynamic phasor–type assumptions (e.g., focusing on specific oscillatory modes) are acceptable when supported by prior detailed simulations showing that off band dynamics are negligible for the questions posed (Lara et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023).
2. Structural/model reduction assumptions
•	Aggregating many similar devices (e.g., multiple grid following inverters into an equivalent unit) or lines into equivalent admittance/impedance elements is acceptable when done via systematic reduction (frequency decoupling, participation factors, balanced truncation) and shown to reproduce key impedance and stability characteristics (Duddeti et al., 2025; Yue et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2021; Grdenić et al., 2023).
•	Reducing converter and PV models by neglecting dc side or secondary control loops is acceptable for low frequency oscillation or short term stability analysis if comparisons against EMT or full models show close trajectories, admittance curves, and eigenvalues in the frequency band of interest (Lara et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2021; Yue et al., 2025).
3. Uncertainty and robustness assumptions
•	Assuming bounded but unknown parameters (interval models) or inhomogeneous initial conditions is acceptable when explicit error bounds or a priori error estimates for the reduced model are provided (Wang et al., 2021; Fan & Yang, 2025).
•	Screening thresholds (e.g., for mode participation, line importance, or scenario selection) are acceptable when they are: 
o	derived from systematic metrics (participation factors, Hankel singular values, error indices) rather than arbitrary choices, and
o	stress tested via sensitivity analysis to show that classification of “critical” vs “screened out” elements is stable over a reasonable threshold range (Duddeti et al., 2025; Fan & Yang, 2025; Roald et al., 2023; Grdenić et al., 2023).
4. Transparency requirements
Aspect	Good practice for “acceptable” assumptions	Sources
Scope	State which dynamics/controls are neglected and why they are irrelevant for the screening task	(Lara et al., 2023; Yue et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2021)
Validation	Compare ROM vs full model on time domain, frequency domain and stability metrics; report errors	(Duddeti et al., 2025; Lara et al., 2023; Yue et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2021; Zhang et al., 2021; Grdenić et al., 2023)
Thresholds	Tie screening cut offs to modal/energy metrics and show sensitivity	(Duddeti et al., 2025; Fan & Yang, 2025; Roald et al., 2023; Grdenić et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Key criteria for acceptable screening-model assumptions.
Conclusion
In reduced order screening for power systems, assumptions are acceptable when they (1) discard only dynamics proven non influential for the target phenomena, (2) rely on systematic reduction/importance metrics rather than ad hoc rules, (3) provide error or stability guarantees, and (4) are fully documented and validated against higher fidelity models or data.

•	How should threshold values be justified using standards, literature, operating practice, or sensitivity analysis?
Justifying threshold values: linking them to evidence, context, and robustness
Across fields, best practice is to avoid “arbitrary” cut offs and to explicitly tie thresholds to standards, data, operating realities, and sensitivity analyses.
1. Use standards and guidelines where they exist
•	Regulatory or professional guidelines should be the primary anchor (e.g., TLVs for formaldehyde exposure, IFCC 99th percentile URLs for troponin) with a clear statement of which document, version, and value were used and why it is appropriate for the setting (Russ & Hammer, 2025; Aakre et al., 2022).
•	For Bayesian decision thresholds, systematic review work stresses defining posterior probability cut offs a priori and justifying them with methodological or regulatory norms (e.g., 95% for efficacy) (Barret et al., 2024).
2. Ground thresholds in prior literature and data
•	Reviews repeatedly criticise thresholds that are copied from earlier papers without reconciling differences in design or parameters, urging authors to adapt and justify thresholds using study specific characteristics and statistical theory rather than citation alone (Yeung, 2024; Guenther et al., 2023; White et al., 2023).
•	In extreme value and environmental analyses, thresholds are derived from diagnostics (mean residual life plots, parameter stability, long range correlation structure) and then supported by goodness of fit checks and uncertainty assessment (Liu et al., 2023).
3. Reflect operating practice and risk trade offs
•	Decision threshold work and CAD silicosis analyses recommend choosing cut offs based on explicit tolerance for false positives/negatives, prevalence, and operational costs, sometimes using different thresholds for different use cases (Scarffe et al., 2024; Barker et al., 2025).
•	Environmental management papers emphasise that acceptable thresholds must balance societal harm tolerance, precaution, and operational practicality, with stakeholder input (Hitchin et al., 2023).
4. Demonstrate robustness via sensitivity analysis
•	Threshold sensitivity studies show that small threshold shifts can materially change rankings, model performance, or design estimates; authors are urged to report how conclusions change over a plausible threshold range, not just at a single chosen value (Liu et al., 2023; Tsvetkova & Ouarda, 2021; Kelemen et al., 2024).
•	Meta analytic and diagnostic work recommends reporting threshold specific sensitivity/specificity and avoiding sole reliance on global summaries (e.g., AUC) (Khatami et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2024).
Conclusion
In Q1 level work, thresholds should be: (1) anchored in formal standards when available, (2) quantitatively derived and checked against the data, (3) justified in terms of operating risk trade offs, and (4) stress tested with sensitivity analysis, with all assumptions and diagnostics transparently reported.
