•	What transparency is required when reporting assumptions and thresholds in Q1 engineering papers?
Q1 engineering papers are expected to report assumptions and thresholds with enough detail that methods are reproducible, justified, and open to scrutiny.
What “transparency” typically entails
1. Explicit reporting of thresholds and cut offs
High rank journals expect all analytical thresholds to be:
•	Stated numerically (e.g., loading ≥ 0.708; AVE ≥ 0.50; HTMT ≤ 0.85–0.90) rather than implied or hidden in software defaults (Guenther et al., 2023).
•	Linked to citation-backed rationale (e.g., why a particular reliability or validity cut off was chosen) (Guenther et al., 2023).
•	Evaluated and reported (how many indicators/variables met each threshold; what was done when they did not) (Guenther et al., 2023).
2. Justification of methodological assumptions
Best practice guidelines emphasise:
•	Explaining why a given method/model is appropriate (e.g., sample size, prediction focus, model complexity in PLS SEM) instead of generic claims (Guenther et al., 2023).
•	Declaring key model assumptions (additivity, linearity, causal interpretation) and noting when they may be problematic, especially in complex or heterogeneous data (Hettikankanamage et al., 2025; Guenther et al., 2023).
•	Avoiding uncritical use of “standard” criteria that may not fit the context (e.g., arbitrary indicator reporting or impact indicators mistaken for causal evidence) (Reed et al., 2021; Guenther et al., 2023).
3. Coherence and detail in reporting
Methodology papers stress:
•	Methodological coherence: thresholds and assumptions must align with the stated epistemology and analytic approach; one-size-fits-all checklists can create incoherence if applied blindly (Braun & Clarke, 2024).
•	Providing enough procedural detail that another researcher could reproduce the analysis and understand limitations and potential biases (Hettikankanamage et al., 2025; Reed et al., 2021; Braun & Clarke, 2024; Guenther et al., 2023).
4. Reflexivity about limitations
Transparent reporting also includes:
•	Acknowledging where assumptions are strong or unverifiable (e.g., XAI additivity and causal assumptions) and the implications for interpreting results (Hettikankanamage et al., 2025).
•	Clarifying where thresholds are conventional/heuristic rather than empirically optimized (Reed et al., 2021; Guenther et al., 2023).
Conclusion
For Q1 engineering outlets, transparency means stating and justifying all assumptions and thresholds, aligning them with the chosen methods, reporting how well data met them, and openly discussing their limitations so results are interpretable and reproducible.

3.7 Ordinal Risk Modelling and Benchmark Methods
•	What statistical models are most appropriate for linking proxy indicators to ordinal engineering risk classes?
For linking proxy indicators to ordinal risk classes, ordinal regression models (especially cumulative/proportional-odds and related generalized ordinal models) are generally most appropriate, with more flexible ML-based ordinal methods when relationships are complex.
Core modeling families
1. Classical ordinal regression (recommended default)
•	Cumulative / proportional-odds models (POLR, CPMs) directly model cumulative probabilities of ordered classes and are the most widely used, interpretable choice for ordered risk ratings (James et al., 2021; Tutz, 2021; Pujol‐Rigol et al., 2025).
•	Variants include: 
o	Cumulative logit/probit and log cumulative probability models (PPM/LCPM) when probabilities rather than odds are preferred (James et al., 2021; DePratti & Singh, 2025).
o	Partial proportional odds and heteroscedastic or category specific effect models when the proportional odds assumption fails (Chen, 2025; James et al., 2021; Tutz, 2021; Liu et al., 2023).
•	These models work well for engineering fragility/risk (e.g., seismic damage classes) and support rigorous diagnostics and Bayesian or frequentist inference (Chen, 2025; James et al., 2021).
2. Alternative ordinal link structures
•	Sequential and adjacent category ordinal models can better capture stepwise deterioration or stage wise failure mechanisms and sometimes outperform standard cumulative models in fragility/risk contexts (Chen, 2025; Tutz, 2021).
•	A structured taxonomy clarifies when to use cumulative vs adjacent vs sequential formulations based on whether risks are understood as thresholds, neighboring transitions, or staged processes (Tutz, 2021).
3. Flexible and ML-based ordinal models
•	Ordinal extensions of ML (ordered probit BART, ordered random forest, deep TabNet with proportional odds link, neural ordinal classifiers) capture nonlinearities and interactions between proxy indicators and risk classes while respecting order (Nxumalo et al., 2025; Lee & Hwang, 2024; Zhu et al., 2021; Lázaro & Figueiras-Vidal, 2023; Nxumalo et al., 2025).
•	In head to head comparisons, properly specified proportional odds regression often matches or exceeds tree based ML in out of sample discrimination for ordinal clinical risk, with better parsimony and interpretability (Qu et al., 2025).
4. Practical guidance
Situation	Prefer these models	Citations
Need interpretability, modest nonlinearity	Cumulative/proportional odds (possibly partial PO)	(Chen, 2025; James et al., 2021; Tutz, 2021; Qu et al., 2025)
Stage wise failure or transitions	Sequential / adjacent category ordinal models	(Chen, 2025; Tutz, 2021)
Strong nonlinearity, many proxies	OP BART, ordered RF, deep ordinal nets	(Nxumalo et al., 2025; Lee & Hwang, 2024; Zhu et al., 2021; Lázaro & Figueiras-Vidal, 2023; Nxumalo et al., 2025; Kim et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Model choices for ordinal engineering risk classes
Conclusion
For mapping proxy indicators to ordinal engineering risk classes, start with cumulative/proportional odds–type ordinal regression (and its generalized extensions), moving to more flexible ML based ordinal models when relationships are highly nonlinear or interaction rich, while always preserving the ordered nature of the risk scale.

3.7.2 Baseline Threshold and Non-Physics Screening Comparators
•	What baseline screening approaches are commonly used for comparison in power-system risk screening studies?
Most power system risk screening studies benchmark new methods against Monte Carlo–based, OPF based, and heuristic contingency screening baselines.
Common Baseline Screening Approaches
1. Monte Carlo–based exhaustive or semi exhaustive risk evaluation
•	Many works treat crude Monte Carlo (MC) simulation without screening as the gold standard, then compare faster screening methods by their ability to match MC risk estimates with fewer simulations (Sabot et al., 2025; Che et al., 2023).
•	MC is often combined with risk indices (e.g., load loss, instability events) and serves as the reference for missed risk and speed up factors when adding contingency or state screening (Sabot et al., 2025; Che et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2025).
2. Deterministic N 1 / N k security checks and OPF based screens
•	Deterministic N 1 (and sometimes N k) security assessment using standard power flow or dynamic simulations is widely used as a baseline to show the added value of probabilistic or data driven risk screening (Draz et al., 2024; Sabot et al., 2025).
•	Optimal power flow (OPF)–based methods: simplified OPF formulations minimizing imports, losses, or costs under constraints are used as baseline tools to identify stressed states, then compared with more advanced probabilistic or ML aided screens (Draz et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2021).
3. Heuristic or rule based contingency / fault set screening
•	Simple rule based or threshold screens (e.g., using critical clearing time margins, basic stability indicators, or pre defined “key” faults) are used as baselines for more sophisticated segmentation or Huffman based state screening methods (Sabot et al., 2025; Che et al., 2023).
•	In disaster oriented studies, uniform or purely random fault sampling serves as a baseline against improved, risk weighted fault set generation and classification schemes (Huang et al., 2025; Che et al., 2023).
4. Traditional expert / index based risk scoring
•	Some equipment and system level studies benchmark against classical index or matrix based risk assessment that relies on expert assigned likelihood–consequence scores, AHP style weighting, or simple composite risk indices (Koval et al., 2025; Tang et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2025).
Summary Table of Baselines
Baseline type	Typical role in studies	Citations
Crude MC without screening	Accuracy benchmark for new screening	(Sabot et al., 2025; Che et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2025)
Deterministic N 1 / N k security	Legacy planning/operations comparator	(Draz et al., 2024; Sabot et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2021)
Simple rule / threshold screens	Speed baseline, highlight missed risk	(Sabot et al., 2025; Che et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2025)
Expert / index risk scoring	Legacy planning / asset risk benchmark	(Koval et al., 2025; Tang et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Overview of baseline risk-screening approaches in power grids.
Conclusion
Across power system risk screening, new probabilistic, ML, or segmentation methods are usually validated against crude Monte Carlo, deterministic N 1/N k and OPF based checks, simple rule based or key fault screens, and traditional expert/index based risk scoring.

•	How should simple threshold methods and non-physics data-driven comparators be defined for fair benchmarking?
Fair benchmarking requires defining baselines that are simple, transparent, capacity matched, and trained/evaluated under the same data, costs, and constraints as the proposed method.
1. Simple threshold methods (rule based baselines)
For risk screening tasks (e.g., medical or engineering risk models), threshold baselines should:
•	Be derived from explicit utility or cost assumptions, not arbitrary cut points. Simple decision analytic frameworks can turn test/treatment costs and effectiveness into an optimal risk threshold or dual thresholds for screening vs. no action (Katki & Bebu, 2021; Alharbi, 2025; Brentnall et al., 2023).
•	Use the same predicted risk scale as complex models, with thresholds chosen by: 
o	maximizing net benefit or incremental net benefit (Katki & Bebu, 2021),
o	targeting clinically/operationally meaningful event probabilities (e.g., P(event)=5%, 20%) (Alharbi, 2025; Brentnall et al., 2023).
•	Be fully specified and auditable: fixed single or dual thresholds, plus clear rules for gray zones and follow up testing, to avoid hidden tuning advantages (Alharbi, 2025; Katki & Bebu, 2021; Brentnall et al., 2023).
These designs make threshold rules strong, interpretable baselines rather than “straw men.”
2. Non physics, data driven comparators
To define fair ML comparators without domain physics:
•	Include a broad, diverse baseline set: linear/logistic regression, random forests, gradient boosting, and simple statistical models, avoiding “stereotype bias” that assumes traditional methods are weak (Qiu et al., 2024; Paz et al., 2025).
•	Use a unified, reproducible pipeline: identical preprocessing, train/validation/test splits, hyperparameter search space, and evaluation metrics for all models (Qiu et al., 2024).
•	Match model capacity and data access: no method may use extra features, labels, or future data unavailable to others; all baselines should be trained on the same datasets with consistent sampling schemes (Bao et al., 2021; Qiu et al., 2024; Ghosh et al., 2025).
•	Document dataset provenance and label limitations, especially in socio technical settings where “ground truth” is contested; benchmarks should not silently inherit biased datasets as unquestioned gold standards (Bao et al., 2021; Ghosh et al., 2025; Conahan et al., 2025).
Example structure
Component	Fair definition principle	Citations
Threshold rules	Utility-based, fixed, transparent	(Alharbi, 2025; Katki & Bebu, 2021; Brentnall et al., 2023)
ML baselines	Diverse, capacity-matched, unified pipeline	(Qiu et al., 2024; Paz et al., 2025)
Data/labels	Same splits, documented bias/limits	(Bao et al., 2021; Ghosh et al., 2025; Conahan et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Design principles for fair threshold and ML baselines.
Conclusion
Simple threshold and non physics data driven baselines should be strong, clearly specified, and evaluated under identical data and cost assumptions to provide a genuinely fair performance benchmark.

•	What evaluation frameworks have been used to demonstrate the added value of physics-guided methods over naive alternatives?
Evaluation frameworks for showing the value of physics-guided ML
Across applications, added value of physics-guided methods is usually demonstrated via structured comparisons to naive or purely data driven baselines under a shared experimental protocol, using task specific metrics plus physics consistency measures.
Benchmarking setups and baselines
•	Direct model comparisons on identical tasks/datasets 
o	PV power forecasting: physics-informed feature expansion and feature selection are tested against the same ML models trained on “intrinsic-only” and non physics feature sets, using identical data splits and hyperparameter search (Pombo et al., 2022).
o	Chaotic systems: Physics Guided Learning (PGL) is compared with standard data driven predictors across six chaotic dynamical systems using the same training regimes (Feng et al., 2025).
o	Nonlinear dynamics: physics guided neural networks are compared to vanilla DNNs on several canonical ODE systems (Lotka–Volterra, Lorenz, etc.) with identical architectures/training budgets (Robinson et al., 2022).
o	Landslide susceptibility: a physics informed RF pipeline (Scoops 3D FS filtering) is benchmarked against a “pure RF” classifier (Liu et al., 2023).
Typical metrics and evaluation axes
Dimension	How added value is measured	Citations
Predictive accuracy	RMSE/MAE, correlation, AUC vs naive/data only baselines	(Pombo et al., 2022; Liu et al., 2023; Feng et al., 2025; Robinson et al., 2022)
Long horizon/generalization	Multi step forecast error, degradation over time	(Park et al., 2025; Feng et al., 2025; Robinson et al., 2022)
Data efficiency	Performance vs training set size (especially in low data regime)	(Fu et al., 2024; Feng et al., 2025; Robinson et al., 2022)
Physics consistency / plausibility	PDE/ODE residuals, stability, factor of safety, forces used	(Pombo et al., 2022; Liu et al., 2023; Fan et al., 2023; Thuerey et al., 2021)
Computational efficiency	Error vs wall clock or iteration count (convergence speed)	(Zhou et al., 2021; Fan et al., 2023; Dolean et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Common axes to benchmark physics-guided models.
Structured benchmark frameworks
•	PINNacle: standardized suite of 20+ PDE problems with shared code and metrics (solution error vs analytic or numerical reference, scaling tests) to compare different PINN variants and architectures (Hao et al., 2023).
•	Comparative PINN studies: pressure reconstruction benchmarked against two classic numerical integration schemes using the same synthetic and experimental flows (Fan et al., 2023).
•	Physics-guided SSL and pretraining: DSSL in materials science compares to non physics SSL and supervised baselines across 10 property datasets, reporting percent improvement and data efficiency curves (Fu et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Frameworks combine common protocols (same data, architecture class, and budgets) with multi axis metrics—accuracy, generalization, data efficiency, and physics consistency—to quantify how much physics guidance outperforms naive or purely data driven alternatives.

3.8 Uncertainty Quantification, Sensitivity Analysis, and Validation Metrics
•	What uncertainty quantification methods are most suitable for proxy-based and ordinal risk models in engineering applications?
For proxy-based and ordinal risk models, UQ methods that give calibrated probabilities cheaply—especially Bayesian/probabilistic surrogates, ensembles, and non probabilistic set based formalisms—are most suitable.
1. Proxy based (surrogate / reduced order) risk models
For engineering settings where high fidelity simulators are replaced by proxies, recommended UQ approaches are:
Probabilistic surrogates (best when full risk curves are needed)
•	Gaussian process (GP) surrogates / kriging provide a predictive distribution at each input, enabling direct propagation to risk metrics (failure probability, fragility) and adaptive sampling (Marrel & Iooss, 2024; Birrell et al., 2025).
•	Polynomial Chaos Expansions (PCE) and Low Rank Approximations efficiently approximate output distributions and moments; multi fidelity and low rank variants mitigate curse of dimensionality in high dimensional risk problems (Azarhoosh & Ghazaan, 2025; Wang et al., 2022; Yildiz et al., 2022; Bahrami et al., 2022).
•	Multi fidelity surrogates (GP, PCE, LRA) combine cheap/accurate models to support large UQ campaigns in early design and performance based engineering (Azarhoosh & Ghazaan, 2025; Wang et al., 2022; Giovanis et al., 2024; Bahrami et al., 2022; Yildiz et al., 2023).
Bayesian / data driven surrogates
•	Bayesian learning and MCMC around ML proxies (e.g., SVR, neural nets) quantify parameter and function uncertainty, producing predictive intervals for safety critical responses (He et al., 2025; Nemani et al., 2023; Marelli et al., 2025; Ghahari et al., 2022).
•	Ensemble based UQ (neural network ensembles, ARDCW, Monte Carlo dropout) gives empirically calibrated predictive distributions suitable when model form is complex and data rich (Nemani et al., 2023; Shahri et al., 2022; Xiang et al., 2023).
Non probabilistic / hybrid when information is scarce
•	Fuzzy and rough set based UQ capture bounded but poorly known epistemic uncertainty, often combined with Kriging surrogates for propagation (Guo et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2022).
2. Ordinal and category based risk models
For models outputting damage states, risk classes, or ordered categories:
•	Bayesian networks and dynamic/fuzzy Bayesian networks naturally handle discrete/ordinal states and propagate probability ranges (including imprecise probabilities via fuzzy sets) through time and causal structure (Guo et al., 2021).
•	Ordinal/probabilistic classifiers with UQ (Bayesian NNs, GP classification, calibrated ensembles) provide class probabilities and uncertainty, enabling risk aware decisions in health prognostics and structural assessment (Nemani et al., 2023; Xiang et al., 2023).
•	Fragility based frameworks treat damage states as ordered thresholds and use parameterized probabilistic models with sensitivity/UQ around fragility curves (Fu et al., 2025; Giovanis et al., 2024; Birrell et al., 2025).
Conclusion
For proxy based engineering risk models, probabilistic surrogates (GP, PCE/LRA, multi fidelity) and Bayesian/ensemble ML provide the most suitable and widely used UQ; for ordinal risk, Bayesian network–style models and probabilistic ordinal classifiers give coherent uncertainty on class probabilities, with fuzzy/rough set hybrids valuable under severe epistemic uncertainty.

•	How have bootstrap analysis, perturbation tests, and global sensitivity analysis been applied in power-system analytics studies?
Bootstrap, perturbation, and global sensitivity methods are mainly used to quantify uncertainty, rank drivers, and stress test power system models under renewables and converter dominance.
Global sensitivity analysis (GSA)
•	Variance based GSA (Sobol/ANCOVA indices) is widely used to rank uncertain inputs (e.g., renewable outputs, converter parameters) by their contribution to variance of reliability or security indicators such as EENS or load supply capability (Zhang et al., 2021; Wang et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2025).
•	Polynomial chaos expansion (PCE) surrogates make GSA tractable in large grids; PCE based ANCOVA was compared for correlated inputs, showing that building PCE directly with correlated variables can yield more accurate indices than decorrelation approaches (Wang et al., 2023).
•	In renewable dominated systems, GSA is used to: 
o	Identify the most critical converter uncertainties for system reliability (Zhang et al., 2021)- Rank renewable plants by impact on probabilistic LSC, guiding operational strategies and reinforcement planning (Zhang et al., 2025)- Analyze over limit risks (voltage, temperature, pressure) in integrated electric heat gas systems (Wang et al., 2023).
Rare event–focused GSA
•	New rare event GSA (REGSA) frameworks couple subset simulation with sparse PCE to assess sensitivity of tail probabilities (e.g., over limit or blackout like events), overcoming the limitations of standard GSA that targets the whole PDF (Li et al., 2025).
Bootstrap analysis
•	Bootstrap resampling appears mainly in data driven stability studies as part of ML pipelines for smart grid stability prediction, where it is used to generate balanced training sets (“bootstrapping” within bagged trees) and improve robustness of classification accuracy estimates under varying operating conditions (Önder et al., 2023).
Perturbation tests
•	Perturbation based analyses are used to probe small signal or frequency domain stability by injecting small disturbances or parameter variations: 
o	Frequency domain perturbation scans in EMT tools (e.g., DIgSILENT) obtain impedance characteristics for inverter based resources, then evaluate stability margins via Nyquist criteria across operating points (Mukundan, 2025).
o	Lyapunov based perturbation analysis is applied to derive algebraic robustness conditions for frequency stability under bounded active power disturbances, linking maximum power perturbation, damping, and frequency limits without full time domain Monte Carlo (Alves et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Across power system analytics, global sensitivity (including rare event variants) is the main tool for ranking uncertainty sources and guiding control/planning; perturbation tests probe stability margins in converter rich grids; and bootstrap is primarily embedded in ML based analytics to stabilize and validate data driven stability predictions.

•	Which validation metrics are most appropriate for ordinal classification, calibration, robustness, and interpretability assessment?
Ordinal models should be judged with distance aware accuracy metrics, ordinal-aware probabilistic scores for calibration, stress tests for OOD/adversarial robustness, and task specific metrics for explanation fidelity and simplicity.
1. Ordinal classification performance
For hard (class) predictions, metrics must respect ordering:
•	Distance sensitive error: mean absolute error (MAE) on class indices, and its class-balanced variants AMAE and MMAE, penalize distant misorders more than adjacent ones (Nxumalo et al., 2025; Binotto & Delgado, 2025).
•	Agreement measures: quadratic weighted kappa (QWK) and related weighted kappa/Scott’s π measures are highly recommended; a 2023 comparison of 37 metrics finds weighted Scott’s π especially reliable and sensitive under imbalance, outperforming plain Cohen’s κ, F1 and MCC for ordinal tasks (Yılmaz & Demirhan, 2023; Nxumalo et al., 2025; Sakai, 2021).
•	Interval-aware extensions: when labels are ordered intervals (e.g., risk bands with unequal width), interval-scale adaptations of MAE type metrics using interval distances and normalization are proposed to better reflect real costs (Binotto & Delgado, 2025).
Recommended: QWK or weighted Scott’s π + an MAE type metric.
2. Calibration of ordinal probabilistic predictions
•	For ordinal probabilities, Ranked Probability Score (RPS), a proper scoring rule that accumulates CDF errors across ordered classes, better reflects ordinal miscalibration than standard Brier score or nominal ECE (Galdrán, 2023).
•	General calibration tools—ECE and its improved estimators (Posocco & Bonnefoy, 2021), cumulative difference and kernel smoothed reliability diagrams (Ibarra et al., 2022; Blasiok & Nakkiran, 2023)—remain useful, but should be interpreted alongside RPS for ordinal tasks (Galdrán, 2023).
•	Application studies also use Brier score, log loss, ECE, Spiegelhalter’s test, and reliability diagrams for probability quality assessment (Odesola et al., 2025; Suster et al., 2023).
Recommended: RPS as primary, with ECE/Brier + reliability diagrams for diagnostics.
3. Robustness metrics
Robustness should be evaluated with:
•	Accuracy / ordinal metrics under distribution shift and common corruptions, since in distribution error is a strong predictor of OOD error (Wenzel et al., 2022; Corso et al., 2023).
•	Adversarial robustness metrics: adversarial error rates under norm bounded attacks and richer robustness suites that examine worst case performance and decision boundary behavior (Zhang et al., 2022; Liu et al., 2021).
4. Interpretability assessment
Explainability quality is multi faceted:
•	Surveys highlight quantitative metrics for fidelity, parsimony/simplicity, stability, and human centred measures like trust and usefulness (Zhou et al., 2021; Nauta et al., 2022; Alangari et al., 2023).
•	Task specific metrics comparing explanations to human judgments or ground truth features (e.g., recall of important features) are proposed for local explainers like LIME and time series attribution (, 2023; Turbé et al., 2022).
Conclusion
For ordinal risk models, use distance aware ordinal metrics (QWK/weighted π + MAE/AMAE/MMAE), RPS based calibration with modern ECE/diagram variants, robustness audits via shifted/adversarial test sets, and explanation metrics that jointly capture fidelity, simplicity, stability, and alignment with human judgments.

4. Results
4.1 Dataset Characteristics and Operational-State Distribution
•	What result dimensions are typically reported when describing the statistical structure of cleaned substation operational datasets?
Cleaned substation datasets are typically described along dimensions of completeness, distributional statistics, temporal structure, dependence, and residual anomalies.
Typical Result Dimensions
1. Completeness and basic quality
•	Missingness and gaps: proportion of missing points, length and frequency of gaps, and data-quality “grades” or labels (e.g., A–D based on % outliers, missing points, and gaps) (Lindig et al., 2021).
•	Outlier burden: share of points flagged/removed as anomalies and residual anomaly rate after cleaning (Morrison et al., 2021; Shi et al., 2025; Olteanu et al., 2023; Pimentel et al., 2024).
2. Univariate statistical structure
For each key signal (e.g., power, voltage, current, temperature):
•	Central tendency & spread: mean, median, variance/standard deviation, often per hour or per operating mode (Dang et al., 2024; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Lindig et al., 2021).
•	Shape: skewness, kurtosis, empirical distribution or density estimates to capture non Gaussian behavior (Dang et al., 2024; Shi et al., 2025; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
•	Extreme behavior: quantiles (e.g., P1, P99), IQR-based ranges, and thresholds used for outlier rules (Morrison et al., 2021; AlSalehy & Bailey, 2025; Lindig et al., 2021).
3. Temporal structure
•	Autocorrelation and seasonality: autocorrelation plots and identified daily/weekly cycles (e.g., 24 hour profiles, weekly dependence) (Nespoli et al., 2021; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024; Lindig et al., 2021).
•	Aggregated profiles: average hourly load curves (24 dim vectors) per substation or class of substations (Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
4. Multivariate dependence and clustering
•	Correlation and association structure: correlation matrices, association rules between measurements and equipment states (Dang et al., 2024; Kuang et al., 2021).
•	Clustering / mixture structure: number of clusters or mixture components, cluster centers, and within/ between cluster variance for different operating regimes (Dang et al., 2024; Shi et al., 2025; Bustos-Brinez et al., 2024).
5. Residual anomaly and model-related metrics
•	Outlier-detection quality: silhouette scores, separation scores, precision/recall/F1 of anomaly labels where ground truth or proxies exist (Shi et al., 2025; Pimentel et al., 2024).
•	Impact on downstream models: changes in forecast or classification error (e.g., NMAE, NRMSE, EF) before vs. after cleaning (Nespoli et al., 2021; Pimentel et al., 2024).
Conclusion
To describe the statistical structure of cleaned substation operational datasets, studies typically report completeness/quality indicators, per-variable distributional statistics, temporal dependence, multivariate structure, and residual anomaly or model-performance metrics, often stratified by operating mode or substation type.

•	How should the temporal distribution and coverage of operating states be summarized in power-system analytics papers?
Temporal coverage of operating states should be summarized with explicit time fractions, scenario grids, and imbalance metrics across all relevant regimes.
Core dimensions to report
1. Operating state taxonomy and counts
•	Clearly define the set of discrete states or regimes (normal, emergency, restorative; pre fault, fault, post fault; different loading/RES levels, etc.) and report sample counts per state (Li et al., 2023; Khalid et al., 2023; Bhusal et al., 2021).
•	For synthetic scenario studies, tabulate the number of simulations per combination of load level, DER penetration, fault type/location, and clearing time (Hijazi et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023; Huang et al., 2022; Bhusal et al., 2021).
2. Temporal distribution and coverage
•	Report time fractions spent in each state over the simulation or dataset horizon (e.g., % of time in normal vs emergency, % in high RES volatility periods) (Li et al., 2023; Iliadis et al., 2025; Khalid et al., 2023).
•	For time series windows, specify window length, stride, and horizon, and give the distribution of event start times within the day/season (e.g., peak vs off peak, seasonal split) (Li et al., 2023; Wu et al., 2023).
•	When using randomized scenario generation (load/PV scaling, EV trips), describe the sampling law and ranges (e.g., P,Q scaled 0.8–1.2; EV traffic/charging states per hour) and show histograms or kernel densities over time (Iliadis et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2024; Liu & Liu, 2023).
3. Imbalance and representativeness
•	Quantify class imbalance between stable/unstable, attacked/normal, or fault/no fault operating states (ratios such as 100:1; percentage unstable, attacked, etc.) (Qu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023).
•	Report coverage across topologies and time scales (number of distinct network configurations; counts per topology; share of PMU/SCADA/slow measurements) (Hijazi et al., 2024; Iliadis et al., 2025; Liu et al., 2023; Haug & Goebel, 2025).
Example reporting structure
Dimension	Recommended summary	Citations
State set	List states + counts per state	(Li et al., 2023; Khalid et al., 2023; Bhusal et al., 2021)
Time coverage	% of total hours/samples per state, per season/peak	(Li et al., 2023; Iliadis et al., 2025; Liu & Liu, 2023)
Scenario grid	Table of load/RES/fault/topology combinations	(Hijazi et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023; Iliadis et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2024)
Imbalance	Ratios (e.g., stable:unstable, normal:attack)	(Qu et al., 2024; Li et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Key elements of temporal state coverage reporting.
Conclusion
To make temporal distribution and coverage of operating states clear in power system analytics papers, authors should define states explicitly, quantify their time fractions and scenario combinations, and report imbalance and topology/time scale coverage, rather than only giving total sample counts.

•	What result presentation practices best show that the dataset supports valid state-based risk screening?
To show that a dataset supports valid state based risk screening, results should explicitly link state coverage, label quality, and screening performance across all relevant risk states.
1. Show state coverage and representativeness
•	Tabulate state definitions, counts, and time fractions (e.g., normal/risk/fault; stable/unstable; different disaster intensities) and highlight class imbalance (e.g., 9.09% unstable, 100:1 imbalance) (Li et al., 2023; Song et al., 2025; He et al., 2025; Qian et al., 2025).
•	For scenario driven datasets (faults, typhoons, earthquakes, wildfires), present a scenario grid over operating conditions and hazard levels (e.g., load levels, RES penetration, fault location, hazard intensity) with samples per cell to demonstrate coverage of the relevant state space (Huang et al., 2025; Li et al., 2023; Ti et al., 2022; Umunnakwe et al., 2022; Zhou & Li, 2025).
Recommended table
Aspect	What to report	Citations
State taxonomy	Names, criteria, thresholds	(Li et al., 2023; Li et al., 2025; He et al., 2025; Qian et al., 2025)
Counts & fractions	Samples and % per state	(Li et al., 2023; Song et al., 2025; He et al., 2025)
Scenario grid	Load/RES/hazard × fault/topology	(Huang et al., 2025; Li et al., 2023; Ti et al., 2022; Umunnakwe et al., 2022; Zhou & Li, 2025)
Figure 1: Core coverage descriptors for state-based screening datasets.
2. Demonstrate label validity and risk ordering
•	Explain state/risk labelling rules (e.g., voltage stability indices, failure probabilities, resilience metrics, ALARP risk classes) and show that higher states correspond to worse physical or economic outcomes (Huang et al., 2025; Li et al., 2021; Ti et al., 2022; Li et al., 2025; He et al., 2025; Qian et al., 2025).
•	When labels come from clustering or semi supervised methods, report cluster separation and how clusters map to physically interpretable risk levels (Li et al., 2021; Li et al., 2023; Li et al., 2025).
3. Report screening performance by state and operating condition
•	Provide confusion matrices and per state metrics (precision, recall, F1, MCC, G mean) for the risk screening model, not just overall accuracy, to show reliable detection of rare high risk states (Li et al., 2021; Li et al., 2023; Sahu et al., 2021; Song et al., 2025; Zhou & Li, 2025; Wang et al., 2025).
•	Break down performance across key regimes (e.g., high RES, extreme weather, cyber attack scenarios) to demonstrate robustness where risk screening is most critical (Lin et al., 2024; Ti et al., 2022; Sahu et al., 2021; Umunnakwe et al., 2022; Lin et al., 2024).
Conclusion
A dataset can be credibly claimed to support state based risk screening when papers clearly document state coverage, justify and validate risk labels, and report regime specific screening performance for all states—especially rare but high consequence ones.

Wang, S., Liu, D., Su, W., Zhang, X., & Hui, S. (2025). Power system security and protection considering the integration of new energy power plants. Scientific Reports, 15. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-025-19149-6
Qian, G., Dai, W., Zou, D., Sun, H., Zhang, H., & Hao, J. (2025). Risk Assessment Model for Converter Transformers Based on Entropy-Weight Analytic Hierarchy Process. Energies. https://doi.org/10.3390/en18071757
4.2 Descriptive Behavior of Voltage, Load, Incomer, and Feeder Variables
•	How are descriptive operating patterns of busbar voltage, substation load, incomers, and feeders typically presented in the literature?
Descriptive operating patterns are usually shown as time series plots, profiles, and distributions by asset (busbar, feeder, substation) and operating condition.
Common presentation formats
1. Time series and daily profiles
•	Voltage and load trajectories over hours/days, often averaged in 15 min or hourly bins, to show typical diurnal behaviour at busbars, substations, and feeders (Lim et al., 2025; Sirisumrannukul, 2023; Gilbert et al., 2022).
•	Separate curves per feeder or bus to highlight differences in variability and limit violations (e.g., average voltage per feeder over time, with statutory bands) (Lim et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2023).
2. Profiles along the feeder or across buses
•	Voltage profile vs. bus index or distance from substation to show spatial drop or rise; used both for base case and under scenarios like high PV or EV penetration (Mossie et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2023; Sirisumrannukul, 2023; , 2023; Maribe, 2025).
•	Loading profiles of transformers, incomers, and feeders versus time or peak level (light/medium/peak) to show margins and overloads (Sirisumrannukul, 2023; De Negreiros et al., 2023; , 2023).
3. Histograms, distributions, and compliance metrics
•	Histograms or kernel densities of busbar/feeder voltages by feeder or by time of day, overlaid with regulatory limits, to summarise spread and compliance (Lim et al., 2025).
•	Summary statistics: mean, standard deviation, min/max voltage, percentage of samples within allowed band (VCR, compliance rate) per feeder or per busbar (Lim et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2023; Tavarov et al., 2022).
4. Scenario and state comparisons
•	Tables or multi panel figures comparing no load vs peak, different PV/EV penetrations, or cyber attack/contingency states, listing buses/feeders with violations and their duration or severity (Maghami et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2023; Sirisumrannukul, 2023; De Negreiros et al., 2023).
•	Case study diagrams (single line plus color coded voltages or loading) for substations and feeders to visualise stressed sections (Mossie et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2024; Sirisumrannukul, 2023; , 2023).
Example structure
Asset / level	Typical pattern views	Citations
Busbars	Time series voltage, histograms, profiles vs bus	(Maghami et al., 2023; Lim et al., 2025; , 2023)
Substations	Incomer & transformer loading over time, peak tables	(Sirisumrannukul, 2023; Tavarov et al., 2022; Urquizo et al., 2023)
Feeders	Voltage along feeder, load curves, violation tables	(Mossie et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2025; Maghami et al., 2023; De Negreiros et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Typical visual and tabular pattern summaries.
Conclusion
To convey valid operating patterns, papers usually combine time series, spatial profiles, and distribution plots, with per asset statistics and scenario based tables that jointly describe how busbar voltage, substation load, incomers, and feeders behave across time and conditions.

•	What daily, weekly, or peak/off-peak behaviors are usually important in substation operational characterization?
Substation characterization usually focuses on diurnal load shape, weekday–weekend/seasonal shifts, and peak vs off peak timing and magnitude.
Key daily behaviours
•	Diurnal load curve shape: morning ramp up, midday/high load plateau, afternoon setback, and night base load are core descriptors for substations and their downstream buildings/feeders (Pinheiro et al., 2023; Li et al., 2021; Hu et al., 2021).
•	Peak timing windows: many substations peak in early evening (e.g., 18:00–22:00) in residential contexts, with alternative morning/afternoon peaks for some feeders or climates (Prompook et al., 2025; Kyriakou et al., 2025; Hutabarat & Zambak, 2022; Gilbert et al., 2022).
•	Peak/base metrics: peak load, base load, peak–base ratio, and duration of “high load” period (hours above a threshold) are standard time domain features (Li et al., 2021; Pinheiro et al., 2023).
•	Voltage and harmonic responses to load: higher daytime/evening load corresponds to higher harmonic currents and distinct voltage/OLTC behaviour (e.g., tap changes following 24 h load pattern) (Zheng et al., 2025; Jia et al., 2025; Lim et al., 2025).
Typical temporal features used
Timescale / regime	Commonly used behaviours	Citations
Intra day	Morning rise, high load start/finish, fall time, daily peak time & intensity	(Gilbert et al., 2022; Li et al., 2021; Hu et al., 2021)
Peak vs off peak	Peak window(s), off peak base level, peak duration, peak shifting via control	(Pinheiro et al., 2023; Prompook et al., 2025; Kyriakou et al., 2025; Jia et al., 2025)
Weekly	Weekday vs weekend load factors, holiday effects, “empty” days	(Pinheiro et al., 2023; Gilbert et al., 2022; Moon et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Core daily and weekly behaviours in substation use.
Weekly and seasonal patterns
•	Weekday–weekend differences in magnitude and shape are routinely modelled for substations and LV hierarchies; weekends often show lower or shifted peaks (Pinheiro et al., 2023; Gilbert et al., 2022; Haben et al., 2021).
•	Seasonal shifts in daily peak time and level (e.g., midday peaks in cooling seasons, evening in heating seasons) are highlighted for substations and transformers (Gilbert et al., 2022; Kyriakou et al., 2025; Pinheiro et al., 2023).
Peak/off peak behaviour for operational use
•	Daily peak intensity and timing at substation, feeder, and household levels are treated as separate stochastic processes for forecasting and control (Gilbert et al., 2022; Amara-Ouali et al., 2021).
•	Peak metrics are used for constraint management (overloading, voltage violations), OLTC schedule design, peak shaving, and storage/control strategies (Pinheiro et al., 2023; Kyriakou et al., 2025; Jia et al., 2025; Lim et al., 2025).
Conclusion
For substation operational characterization, literature emphasizes diurnal load shape parameters, precise daily peak timing and magnitude, weekday–weekend and seasonal shifts, and how voltage, harmonics, and control devices track these peak/off peak behaviours.

•	How should descriptive results be interpreted so that they motivate later proxy and risk-model findings?
Descriptive results should be read as hypotheses generators and design constraints that directly shape proxy inputs, targets, and risk metrics.
1. From description to proxy model design
Descriptive analyses (patterns, correlations, clusters) are most useful when explicitly translated into variables, structures, and objectives for proxy models:
•	Use descriptive patterns to select features and encode domain knowledge. For instance, knowledge graphs and incident statistics are first organized descriptively, then used to define entities, relations, and text fields that feed the ERNIE CNN risk proxy for secondary operations (Huang et al., 2025).
•	Treat strong descriptive correlations (e.g., between wind fields and outages, or typhoon metrics and failures) as candidate predictors and verify them in the proxy model via feature importance or ablation (Zhou & Li, 2025; Baembitov et al., 2023).
•	Descriptive clustering of substations by incident profiles provides risk classes that later become labels or priors in GNN based predictive maintenance proxies (Kazim et al., 2025).
Example linkage
Descriptive result type	How it motivates proxies/risk models	Citations
Spatio temporal hazard maps, ignition patterns	Define spatial–temporal risk fields and scenario generators feeding outage loss proxies	(Zhou & Li, 2025; Umunnakwe et al., 2022)
Fragility/damage statistics	Shape functional form or priors of fragility based risk models	(Karagiannakis et al., 2024; Stanković et al., 2023)
Expert weighted criteria and risk rankings	Calibrate multi criteria risk indices and resource allocation models	(Maia et al., 2021; Judy et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Mapping descriptive findings to modeling choices.
2. From description to risk metrics and scenarios
Descriptive results should also be interpreted as evidence about what “matters” for impact:
•	Incident frequencies, loss-of-load distributions, and cascading-path statistics motivate which risk indicators (e.g., unserved energy, cascading index, resilience curves) are prioritized in later models (Zhang et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2023; Stanković et al., 2023).
•	Descriptive surveys of past failures and climate hazards guide the scenario space for model driven and data driven risk simulations (which hazards, intensities, and asset types to include) (Karagiannakis et al., 2024; Umunnakwe et al., 2022; Stanković et al., 2023).
•	Expert based descriptive scoring of consequence and probability provides weights and thresholds for systemic risk indices and multicriteria resource allocation (Maia et al., 2021; Judy et al., 2021).
Conclusion
To make descriptive results motivate proxy and risk models, interpret them as structured guidance for: (i) choosing inputs, outputs, and architecture; (ii) defining risk indicators and scenarios; and (iii) setting priors, weights, and constraints—then explicitly show this traceability from descriptive patterns to modeling choices.

4.3 Behaviour of Physics-Guided Proxy Indicators Across Operating Windows
•	How should proxy indicators be evaluated across different operational periods such as peak, transitional, and low-load windows?
Proxy indicators should be stress tested separately in peak, transitional, and low load windows using regime specific error, bias, and risk metrics.
1. Segmenting operating regimes
•	Define peak, transitional, and low load windows from the empirical load shape (e.g., percentiles or daily key times like high load start/finish, rise/fall times) so regimes reflect actual operation rather than clock time (Li et al., 2021; Treutlein et al., 2025).
•	Use LV hierarchy (household → feeder → secondary → primary substation) to check how regime definitions scale with aggregation level (Gilbert et al., 2022; Pinheiro et al., 2023).
Suggested evaluation table
Regime	Focus for proxy indicators	Typical metrics	Sources
Peak window	Magnitude, timing, and shape of daily peaks	MAE/RMSE on peak size; timing error; peak shape score	(Treutlein et al., 2025; Gilbert et al., 2022; Li et al., 2021)
Transitional	Ramps, volatility, and mis timed peaks	ramp error; forecast skew; ramp specific CRPS	(Li et al., 2021; Kwon et al., 2025; Habbak et al., 2023)
Low load window	Baseload level, noise, and false alarms	bias, variance, false event rate	(Li et al., 2021; Pinheiro et al., 2023; Habbak et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Regime-specific focus for proxy evaluation.
2. Regime conditioned accuracy and calibration
•	Evaluate proxies conditioned on regime: separate error distributions for peak hours vs off peak, rather than a single global score (Gilbert et al., 2022; Pinheiro et al., 2023).
•	For peak critical tasks, combine general forecasts with peak focused proxies and compare probabilistic scores (e.g., CRPS) specifically during peak hours and days with high stress (Gilbert et al., 2022; Treutlein et al., 2025).
•	For indicators tied to system adequacy or reliability (e.g., LOLE, EUE), assess how regime specific proxy errors shift frequency and severity of near shortfall states (Peña et al., 2025; Leibowicz et al., 2024).
3. Operational and risk relevance
•	Weight regime specific metrics by operational criticality: peak window performance is more important for congestion, transformer sizing, and flexibility assessment (Guo et al., 2025; Sudhoff et al., 2022; Su et al., 2023).
•	For flexibility and resilience studies, test how proxy errors in each regime affect peak shaving, storage scheduling, and risk indices (unserved energy, overload frequency) (Guo et al., 2025; Sudhoff et al., 2022; Abantao et al., 2024).
Conclusion
Interpreting proxies through regime specific segmentation, accuracy and timing metrics, and their impact on operational risk ensures that indicators proven robust in peak, transitional, and low load windows genuinely support later risk model conclusions.

•	What result patterns would indicate that the proposed proxies are physically meaningful and operationally discriminative?
Physically meaningful, operationally discriminative proxies should track core physics, separate high  vs low risk states, and improve decision relevant metrics beyond simpler baselines.
1. Patterns showing “physical meaningfulness”
Evidence that proxies encode the right physics includes:
•	Strong, stable fit to physics based or high fidelity models, not just raw data (e.g., proxy ML models reproducing compositional reservoir simulations of CO₂ trapping with R² ≈ 0.999 across trapping mechanisms and cross validation sets) (Khanal & Shahriar, 2022).
•	Feature importance aligned with known physical drivers: key inputs such as monitoring time, permeability, porosity, and relative permeability hysteresis emerging as dominant predictors of trapping is consistent with reservoir physics (Khanal & Shahriar, 2022).
•	Correct qualitative response to parameter changes (monotonicity, saturation, thresholds), mirroring known behavior of the underlying system across scenarios and stress levels (Khanal & Shahriar, 2022; Djeridi et al., 2025).
These patterns indicate the proxy is more than a statistical fit; it respects governing mechanisms.
2. Patterns showing “operational discriminativeness”
Operationally useful proxies must distinguish states that lead to different management actions:
•	Clear separation of operational regimes or groups: proxy based indicators that differentiate stock status zones (sustainable vs overfished) in Kobe plots, yielding tighter confidence regions and less temporal volatility than catch only methods, show real discriminative power for harvest decisions (Wang et al., 2025).
•	Reduced uncertainty and error in key decision metrics: proxy CPUE cutting variance of biomass and exploitation rate estimates by ~50% under high observation error relative to catch only models demonstrates added robustness where it matters operationally (Wang et al., 2025).
•	Predictive links to real performance outcomes: in other domains, tests are considered discriminative when they both differentiate groups (e.g., age bands) and correlate with match critical metrics such as high speed running or line breaks (Carron et al., 2025); analogous patterns would validate technical risk proxies that predict overloads, shortfalls, or failures.
Conclusion
Result patterns that support the proxies are: (i) close, mechanism consistent agreement with physics based or high fidelity models, and (ii) robust, uncertainty reducing discrimination between operationally different states, directly tied to improved management or risk decisions.

•	How have prior studies visualized and interpreted derived indicators across time windows or operating conditions?
Derived indicators are typically visualized as regime segmented trajectories or fields, then interpreted via contrasts between time windows or operating conditions.
Ways indicators are visualized across windows/conditions
1. Time windowed performance and behaviour plots
•	Metric vs. window length/phase: VALIO plots F1, precision, recall as a function of time window size (10–75 s) to show when OOTL indicators stabilize and how performance changes with longer histories (Lyu et al., 2024).
•	Per window pattern classes: Neonatal “pain sign” is visualized as a continuous probability time series with segments labeled as stable, irregular, unstable, or indeterminate, making temporal dynamics of the indicator explicit for clinicians (Ferreira et al., 2025).
•	Window based variability metrics: GlucoStats segments CGM into windows and visualizes distributions and trends of derived glucose features per window to reveal diurnal patterns and anomalies (Peiro-Corbacho et al., 2025).
2. Regime  or condition segmented plots
•	Operating regime trajectories: Multimodal VAE latent spaces show 2 D trajectories colored by disruption risk and regime, visually separating normal vs disruptive operating conditions and different disruption types (Poels et al., 2025).
•	Load shape indicators by cluster: Building load studies first cluster daily profiles, then plot distributions of derived shape metrics (morning rise time, peak/base ratio) per cluster/season, allowing interpretation of how operations differ across conditions (Li et al., 2021).
•	Grid peak contribution by time/temperature subsets: Correlation based indicators are computed and then visualized for each time–temperature subset to reveal which user categories contribute most to peak loads under specific conditions that would be hidden in aggregate views (Flygare et al., 2023).
3. Spatial–temporal fields of indicators
•	Contour/heat maps: Distributed fiber sensing in beams uses contour plots of strain derived deflection/crack indicators over space and load stages to interpret evolving structural condition (Berrocal et al., 2021).
•	Time–frequency indicator maps: Short time FDD for railway sleepers produces time–frequency mode indicator maps, highlighting mode emergence and evolution under different train speeds/loads (Zeng et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Across domains, derived indicators are usually visualized as time windowed curves, regime segmented distributions/trajectories, or space–time fields, and then interpreted by contrasting levels, trends, and patterns across windows or operating conditions to reveal meaningful regime differences and risk states.
References

4.4 Clustered Operating States and Representative Medoids
•	What kinds of operating-state clusters are commonly reported in substation or distribution-system data analytics?
Substation and feeder data are most often clustered into load profile, risk/reliability, and event/condition based operating states.
Common operating state cluster types
1. Load profile / operating pattern clusters
Based on 24 h or daily active/reactive power shapes at substations or transformers:
•	Low, medium, high, and highly dynamic demand substations, often linked to rural/agricultural vs. urban/industrial/tourist areas (Prompook et al., 2025).
•	Magnitude oriented vs. shape oriented clusters (e.g., Euclidean distance for total consumption vs. cosine/DTW for temporal shape) that distinguish stable profiles from sharply peaked or highly variable ones (Prompook et al., 2025; Pelekis et al., 2023).
•	Classes of electric distribution substations with similar load profiles used to select “pilot” SCADA equipped substations for state estimation (Dandea et al., 2025).
•	In forecasting, clusters of transformers with similar consumption patterns so one model can represent each operating pattern class (Syed et al., 2021).
These reflect “normal” operating regimes under different customer mixes and utilization levels.
2. Reliability / risk and condition clusters
Formed from outage, incident, or multivariate condition data:
•	Resilience oriented risk groups of substations with distinct outage frequency and recovery time (e.g., low risk groups vs. a high risk group with ~6× higher incident rates) (Kazim et al., 2025).
•	Outlier / health states in multivariate monitoring (e.g., normal vs. various anomalous operational modes in converter transformers) (Shi et al., 2025).
•	Relay protection system states or defect categories clustered from protection metrics and alarms (Wang et al., 2024; Andrade et al., 2022).
These define operating states in terms of failure likelihood and recovery behavior.
3. Event and alarm pattern clusters
From SCADA or high resolution monitoring streams:
•	Alarm/event pattern clusters such as SF₆ low pressure, abnormal auto reclosing sequences, or blocks with time delay issues in primary substations (Andrade et al., 2022).
•	Voltage anomaly clusters (normal behavior vs. dips, spikes, and site specific patterns) in solar farms connected to distribution grids (Dey et al., 2021).
These represent transient or abnormal operating states superimposed on the underlying load regimes.
Example mapping of cluster types
Cluster family	Typical labels/examples	Main use	Sources
Load profile	Rural low/stable, urban peaky, industrial high	Planning, siting, DSSE	(Dandea et al., 2025; Prompook et al., 2025; Syed et al., 2021; Brash et al., 2025)
Risk/condition	Low–medium–high outage risk, normal vs. outlier	Maintenance, resilience	(Wang et al., 2024; Kazim et al., 2025; Shi et al., 2025)
Event/alarm pattern	SF₆ issues, failed reclosing, voltage dips	Protection, diagnostics	(Andrade et al., 2022; Dey et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Typical cluster families and their operational meaning.
Conclusion
Across substation and distribution system analytics, operating states are most commonly clustered as (i) distinct load profile regimes, (ii) reliability or health/risk levels, and (iii) characteristic alarm or disturbance patterns, each tied to specific planning, control, or maintenance decisions.

•	How should cluster separability, engineering plausibility, and medoid representativeness be discussed in results sections?
Reporting cluster quality and interpretability in results
Results sections should treat separability, engineering plausibility, and medoid representativeness as three complementary lenses on cluster quality, each backed by specific evidence.
1. Cluster separability
Describe both numeric indices and visual assessment:
•	Report internal validity indices (e.g., Silhouette, Dunn, Davies–Bouldin, Calinski–Harabasz), explaining what “higher” or “lower” means for cohesion vs. separation and noting that thresholds are context dependent (Kallel et al., 2025; Hassan et al., 2024; Lenssen & Schubert, 2023; Ikotun et al., 2025).
•	Show 2D/3D projections (PCA, t SNE, UMAP) and comment on whether clusters overlap or form distinct regions, emphasizing that visual validation complements indices (Gao et al., 2023; Ullmann et al., 2021; Nakuci & Rahnev, 2025).
•	Acknowledge that some indices can favour pathological or overly fragmented solutions and should not be the sole selection criterion (Hassan et al., 2024; Gagolewski et al., 2021; Lenssen & Schubert, 2023).
2. Engineering plausibility of clusters
Link clusters to domain knowledge and external variables:
•	Describe how clusters differ on external, engineering meaningful variables (e.g., operating regimes, failure rates, environmental conditions) and test these differences with appropriate post clustering inference that accounts for data driven groups (Gao et al., 2023; Hivert et al., 2022).
•	Discuss whether patterns align with prior theory or expected subgroups; guidelines explicitly recommend reporting the theory or rationale supporting discovered subgroups and evaluating “cluster meaningfulness” against external criteria (Gao et al., 2023; Ullmann et al., 2021; Nakuci & Rahnev, 2025; Preud'homme et al., 2021).
•	Note any clusters that are statistically distinct yet lack plausible engineering interpretation, treating them cautiously as provisional or artefactual (Ullmann et al., 2021; Gagolewski et al., 2021; Nakuci & Rahnev, 2025).
3. Medoid representativeness
For k medoids or medoid based methods:
•	State that medoids are observed exemplars and justify their use for interpretability (Condino et al., 2025; Yousif & Yan, 2021; D’Urso et al., 2023; Ribeiro & Cezar, 2025).
•	Quantify representativeness (e.g., average intra cluster distance/RMSD to the medoid, or within cluster variance) and discuss how changes in thresholds or k trade off between medoid fidelity and over fragmentation (Kallel et al., 2025; Lenssen & Schubert, 2023; Ribeiro & Cezar, 2025).
•	Show exemplar medoids (plots, waveforms, profiles, shapes) and explain how well they summarize each group, including cases where large within cluster variability makes medoids less faithful (Yousif & Yan, 2021; Ribeiro & Cezar, 2025).
Conclusion
In results, separability should be supported by indices and visuals, plausibility by engineering relevant external comparisons and theory, and medoid quality by quantitative dispersion metrics plus qualitative inspection of exemplar profiles, with explicit discussion of trade offs and limitations.

•	What visual and numerical evidence best supports the validity of the selected operating-state clusters?
The validity of operating-state clusters is best supported by combining internal cluster indices, external engineering metrics, and clear visualizations of both.
1. Numerical evidence
Internal validity indices (cohesion/separation)
•	Use standard indices such as Silhouette coefficient, Davies–Bouldin Index (DBI) and Calinski–Harabasz Index (CHI) to show that the chosen k (and method) yields dense, well-separated clusters (Prompook et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025; Aksan et al., 2021).
•	Report values and interpret them (e.g., high Silhouette ≈0.6 and low DBI≈0.5 indicate well-separated, coherent substation risk clusters) (Kazim et al., 2025; Aksan et al., 2021).
Task- or domain-specific metrics
•	For load-profile or flexibility clusters, include shape-focused metrics (e.g., peak match score, peak performance score, time-warped Silhouette) to verify that clusters capture timing and shape, not just magnitude (Prompook et al., 2025; Pelekis et al., 2023).
•	For resilience/risk clusters, compare incident rates, recovery times, and risk scores across clusters; statistical tests (e.g., ANOVA with p<0.0001) strengthen claims of distinct operating-risk states (Kazim et al., 2025; Yu et al., 2024).
Example table of numerical support
Evidence type	What it shows	Citations
Silhouette, DBI, CHI	Intra-cluster compactness, separation	(Prompook et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025; Aksan et al., 2021)
Shape-based scores (PPS)	Distinct daily/temporal operating patterns	(Prompook et al., 2025; Pelekis et al., 2023)
Outage/voltage metrics + tests	Engineering distinctness of states	(Kazim et al., 2025; Weber et al., 2024; Yu et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Key numerical indicators for cluster validity.
2. Visual evidence
•	Cluster prototypes/centroids as curves or heatmaps: show 24 h load shapes or temperature/voltage signatures for each cluster to illustrate distinct operating regimes and anomalies (Prompook et al., 2025; Weber et al., 2024).
•	Low dimensional embeddings (PCA, UMAP, t SNE): plot points colored by cluster to confirm that groups occupy largely separate regions, while noting that apparent separation is supportive but not definitive (Kazim et al., 2025; Cai & Rong, 2021).
•	Boxplots or bar charts of key engineering variables (incidents/year, recovery minutes, PQ indices) by cluster to make operational differences immediately visible (Kazim et al., 2025; Aksan et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Strong support for operating-state clusters comes from: (i) good internal indices, ideally extended with shape-aware metrics; (ii) statistically significant differences in engineering outcomes across clusters; and (iii) visuals of both the data embedding and physically meaningful prototypes that clearly distinguish the operating regimes.

4.5 Reduced-Order Risk Labels and Screened High-Risk Operating Conditions
•	How should reduced-order risk labels be interpreted and presented as evidence of screened resonance vulnerability?
Reduced order risk labels should be framed as screening flags that summarize complex resonance evidence, not as precise quantitative risk estimates.
How to interpret reduced-order risk labels
1. As ordinal, not metric, risk information
•	Treat labels such as low / medium / high or screened in / screened out as ordered categories derived from a more detailed underlying model, analogous to “risk strata” in medical prediction tools rather than exact probabilities (Cai et al., 2024; Dritsas & Trigka, 2022).
•	Emphasize that each label corresponds to a range of predicted risk or a set of physical conditions (e.g., proximity to resonant frequency, damping ratio, excitation level), not a single numeric value.
2. As summaries conditional on a validated model
•	The credibility of labels relies on the underlying model having demonstrated good discrimination and calibration in validation data (e.g., c index/AUC, calibration plots) (Cai et al., 2024; Dritsas & Trigka, 2022).
•	Present labels explicitly as: “Given the screened conditions and the validated model, this component falls in the X risk tier for resonance response.”
How to present labels as evidence of screened resonance vulnerability
1. Tie each label to physical criteria
•	Document the mapping: e.g., High resonance vulnerability if predicted response exceeds a design limit in ≥Y% of simulated operating conditions; Moderate if near threshold; Low otherwise.
•	Provide examples of representative operating points (frequency, amplitude, damping) that fall into each tier to make the physics link transparent.
2. Report validation alongside the labels
Evidence type	What to show for resonance screening	Citations
Predictive validity	Confusion matrix, AUC, sensitivity/specificity of labels vs. ground truth failures/over response	(Cai et al., 2024; Dritsas & Trigka, 2022)
Calibration	Observed event rates vs. label categories (e.g., % of “high” that show high response)	(Cai et al., 2024)
Transparency	Clear rules or surrogate model description used to derive labels	(Cai et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Structuring label meaning and supporting evidence.
Conclusion
To use reduced order risk labels as evidence of screened resonance vulnerability, define them as ordinal summaries of a validated physics guided model, anchor each label to explicit physical response criteria, and present standard validation (discrimination, calibration, examples) so that the labels are seen as trustworthy screening flags rather than standalone quantitative risk numbers.
References
Cai, Y., Cai, Y., Tang, L., Wang, Y., Gong, M., Jing, T., Li, H., Li-Ling, J., Hu, W., Yin, Z., Gong, D., & Zhang, G. (2024). Artificial intelligence in the risk prediction models of cardiovascular disease and development of an independent validation screening tool: a systematic review. BMC Medicine, 22. https://doi.org/10.1186/s12916-024-03273-7
Dritsas, E., & Trigka, M. (2022). Stroke Risk Prediction with Machine Learning Techniques. Sensors (Basel, Switzerland), 22. https://doi.org/10.3390/s22134670
•	What operating characteristics are typically associated with high-risk classes in power-quality screening studies?
High-risk power quality classes are typically linked to operating points with large, long, or recurrent deviations of voltage/current from sinusoidal norms under stressed or converter dominated conditions.
Typical high risk operating characteristics
Across PQ monitoring, microgrid, and wind/PV studies, high risk classes (for equipment damage, protection mis operation, or customer impact) are most often associated with the following conditions:
1. Large magnitude deviations
•	Deep voltage sags/swells or interruptions beyond EN 50160/IEEE limits (e.g., sag depth, swell magnitude, complete loss) are central high severity indicators (Caicedo et al., 2023; Akkaya, 2023; Sepasi et al., 2023; Akbari & Seyyedi, 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Rashmi, 2025).
•	High total harmonic distortion (THD) and interharmonics from power electronic interfaces (PV, wind, EV chargers, drives) mark nodes at elevated thermal and insulation stress risk (Akkaya, 2023; Sepasi et al., 2023; Akbari & Seyyedi, 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Ali et al., 2024).
2. Adverse temporal characteristics
•	Long duration events (sags, notches, transients) and frequent repetition increase risk to industrial drives and sensitive loads even when individual events remain within short term limits (Caicedo et al., 2023; Akkaya, 2023; Shao et al., 2023; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
•	Fast, high frequency transients and notches from switching operations or converters raise risk of insulation stress and control malfunctions (Caicedo et al., 2023; Akkaya, 2023; Shao et al., 2023; Akbari & Seyyedi, 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
3. System and operating state factors
•	High loading / stressed voltage profiles in load nodes, especially under uncertainty, are linked to elevated risk of voltage quality decline and collapse (Hovorov et al., 2025; Gjorgiev & Sansavini, 2022).
•	Converter dominated, weak microgrids or offshore wind grids, with low short circuit capacity and many power electronic devices, show higher incidence of sags, flicker, harmonics and thus more high risk PQ classifications (Sepasi et al., 2023; Shao et al., 2023; Akbari & Seyyedi, 2024; Rashmi, 2025).
•	Disturbance prone conditions (frequent switching maneuvers, faults, lightning, geomagnetic storms) correlate with more PQ events and unexplained outages, effectively pushing nodes into higher risk tiers (Caicedo et al., 2023; Shao et al., 2023; Figueroa et al., 2025).
4. Multi disturbance or combined events
•	Simultaneous or rapidly alternating combinations of sag/swell, harmonics, and transients are often treated as high risk classes because they defeat simple protection and increase cumulative stress (Akkaya, 2023; Varbella et al., 2023; Akbari & Seyyedi, 2024; Kumar & T., 2025; Jiang et al., 2025; Mishra et al., 2024).
•	Jan 2021
o	2 papers: (Zografopoulos et al., 2021; Zhao et al., 2021)- Jan 2022
o	1 paper: (Gjorgiev & Sansavini, 2022)- Jan 2023
o	1 paper: (Caicedo et al., 2023)- May 2023
o	1 paper: (Varbella et al., 2023)- Jul 2023
o	4 papers: (Akkaya, 2023; Sepasi et al., 2023; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Hafidz et al., 2023)- Nov 2023
o	1 paper: (Shao et al., 2023)- Feb 2024
o	1 paper: (Akbari & Seyyedi, 2024)- Jul 2024
o	2 papers: (Ali et al., 2024; Mishra et al., 2024)- Jan 2025
o	2 papers: (Priyadarshini et al., 2025; Kumar & T., 2025)- Feb 2025

o	1 paper: (Rashmi, 2025)- Jul 2025
o	1 paper: (Figueroa et al., 2025)- Oct 2025
o	1 paper: (Wang et al., 2025)Figure 1: Evolution of PQ risk related operating conditions research.
Conclusion
In power quality screening studies, high risk classes generally correspond to operating states with deep or frequent voltage deviations, high harmonic/ transient content, stressed or weak network conditions, and combined disturbances—especially in converter rich microgrids and renewable dominated systems.

•	How can screened high-risk states be linked back to physical intuition and operational context?
Screened high risk states can be re anchored to intuition by making explicit which physical drivers, limits, and scenarios push them into a high risk class.
1. Tie risk labels to physically meaningful features
•	Map features to equipment and phenomena, e.g., load stress ratio, redundancy, asset aging, and topology fragility are combined into a zone level risk index that is then fuzzily classified into low/mid/high risk, with membership functions and thresholds chosen on physical grounds (Sun et al., 2025).
•	In oscillation or security screening, inputs such as power flows, voltages, generator outputs, and contingency patterns are derived from power flow/ dynamic models, so a “high risk” label corresponds to recognizable stressed or weakly damped conditions rather than abstract vectors (Wang et al., 2025; Varbella et al., 2023; De Caro et al., 2023).
•	For environmental or weather driven risk, link high risk states to concrete hazards such as extreme wind speed, precipitation, or low temperature relative to tower design strength or historical failure envelopes (Zhou & Li, 2025; Lv et al., 2026; Li et al., 2025).
Example linkage table
Screened high risk state	Physical / operational interpretation	Citations
High nodal/zone risk index	Node with high load–capacity ratio, weak redundancy, aging feeder, often near bottlenecks	(Sun et al., 2025)
High cascading failure risk	Operating point where specific line outages trigger widespread overloads in simulations	(Varbella et al., 2023; De Caro et al., 2023)
High weather fault risk	Corridor exposed to typhoon wind circles, extreme rain or drought beyond design thresholds	(Zhou & Li, 2025; Li et al., 2025)
High cyber physical risk	Configuration where compromise of key assets leads to large AGC or T&D deviations	(Zografopoulos et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Connecting categorical risk to physical drivers.
2. Use contextual artifacts and explanations
•	Knowledge graphs and ontologies can encode relationships between devices, operating actions, and risk clauses so that each high risk classification is accompanied by the specific violated standard or unsafe operation pattern (Huang et al., 2025).
•	Spatial and operational visualizations (risk maps, switching heatmaps, congestion weighted flow diagrams) show where high risk states cluster in the network and which reconfiguration actions relieve them, giving operators a geographic and procedural context (Zografopoulos et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2025).
•	Interpretable or post hoc tools (feature importance, rule extraction) help expose which measurements or conditions (e.g., specific loads, switches, or meteorological variables) most strongly drove a state into the high risk class (Caicedo et al., 2023; Gao et al., 2025; Chen et al., 2023; Tu et al., 2024; Tang et al., 2024).
Conclusion
To link screened high risk states back to physical intuition and operations, define risk classes over physically grounded features and limits, and accompany each high risk label with: (i) the dominant physical drivers, (ii) the relevant scenarios or standards, and (iii) spatial/operational visualizations or rule like explanations that show how operators can act on the identified vulnerability.

4.6 Performance of the Ordinal Risk Model
•	What performance measures and diagnostic outputs are most commonly reported for ordinal engineering risk models?
Ordinal engineering risk models typically report discrimination, calibration, and ordinal-specific accuracy, plus residual/diagnostic plots.
Common performance measures
Most applied risk prediction work (including engineering like credit and medical risk) reports a set of metrics rather than a single one (Cabot & Ross, 2023; Noriega et al., 2023; Owusu-Adjei et al., 2023; Shah et al., 2025; Dong et al., 2024):
1. Discrimination (ranking / classification ability)
•	AUC/ROC (or C statistic) is the most commonly reported metric across risk and credit models (Carrington et al., 2021; Cabot & Ross, 2023; Noriega et al., 2023; Owusu-Adjei et al., 2023; Shah et al., 2025; Dong et al., 2024).
•	Accuracy, precision, recall, F1, and sometimes G mean and KS statistic are frequently added, especially in imbalanced or high stakes settings (Jowf & Kolhar, 2025; Noriega et al., 2023; Xenopoulos et al., 2022; Owusu-Adjei et al., 2023; Shah et al., 2025; Dong et al., 2024; Dritsas & Trigka, 2023; Oise et al., 2025).
•	For ordinal outcomes, some studies use these on collapsed binary contrasts (e.g., high vs non high risk) rather than a dedicated ordinal index.
2. Calibration and probability quality
•	Reporting calibration curves / reliability diagrams and Brier score is increasingly recommended and used in risk models (Carrington et al., 2021; Cabot & Ross, 2023; Sun et al., 2024; Tu et al., 2024; Belsti et al., 2023; Barreñada et al., 2025).
•	More detailed work also reports expected calibration error (ECE), log loss, and sometimes formal tests (e.g., Spiegelhalter’s Z) for probability quality (Odesola et al., 2025; Tu et al., 2024).
•	Recent guidelines emphasize that discrimination plus calibration should be reported, not AUC alone (Carrington et al., 2021; Cabot & Ross, 2023; Barreñada et al., 2025).
3. Clinical/engineering utility
•	Decision-curve analysis (net benefit) and lift curves are used to express usefulness across thresholds or targeting levels (Carrington et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2024; Tu et al., 2024; Belsti et al., 2023).
•	Confusion matrices at chosen cut points (e.g., defining high risk ordinal categories) are common (Cabot & Ross, 2023; Sun et al., 2024; Tu et al., 2024; Shah et al., 2025).
4. Model diagnostics for ordinal/discrete outcomes
•	Residuals and goodness of fit tools (Pearson/deviance residuals, newer probability integral transform–based residuals) and QQ plots are recommended for ordinal regression diagnostics (Yang, 2023).
Summary table
Aspect	Typical outputs reported	Citations
Discrimination	AUC/ROC, accuracy, precision, recall, F1	(Carrington et al., 2021; Cabot & Ross, 2023; Noriega et al., 2023; Owusu-Adjei et al., 2023; Shah et al., 2025; Dong et al., 2024; Dritsas & Trigka, 2023)
Calibration	Brier, calibration/reliability plots	(Carrington et al., 2021; Cabot & Ross, 2023; Sun et al., 2024; Odesola et al., 2025; Tu et al., 2024; Belsti et al., 2023; Barreñada et al., 2025)
Utility	Decision curves, lift, confusion matrix	(Carrington et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2024; Tu et al., 2024; Shah et al., 2025; Belsti et al., 2023)
Diagnostics	Residuals, QQ/ordered curves	(Yang, 2023)
Figure 1: Key measure families for risk models.
Conclusion
For ordinal engineering risk models, best practice is to report discrimination metrics (especially AUC and class-wise performance), calibration metrics and plots, decision/utility analyses, and residual-based diagnostics tailored to discrete/ordinal outcomes.

•	How should model coefficients, odds ratios, or partial effects be interpreted when predictors are physics-guided proxy indicators?
Coefficients from physics guided proxy predictors should be read as effects of the proxy signal on risk, not as direct causal effects of the underlying physical quantity.
Core interpretation principles
1. Start with the statistical meaning (on the model scale)
•	In linear models, a coefficient is the expected change in outcome per unit increase in the predictor, holding others fixed (Achilonu et al., 2025).
•	In logistic/Poisson/other GLMs, coefficients are changes in log odds or log rates; exponentiating gives odds ratios or incidence rate ratios, which describe multiplicative changes in odds/rates, not probabilities or risks (Achilonu et al., 2025; Voas & Watt, 2024; Halvorson et al., 2021).
When predictors are proxies (e.g., sensor based surrogates of stress, loading, or degradation), this interpretation applies to the proxy variable itself.
Practical implication
•	“An OR of 1.5 for proxy X” means: for a one unit increase in that proxy, the odds of the event are 50% higher, conditional on covariates, given how X summarizes the underlying physics (Achilonu et al., 2025; Voas & Watt, 2024; Halvorson et al., 2021).
•	It does not mean a 50% increase in failure probability, and does not identify the causal effect of the true physical driver (Xiao et al., 2021; Voas & Watt, 2024).
Using partial/marginal effects
For GLMs and nonlinear ML models, partial effects (marginal effects, predicted probabilities) better communicate impact on quantities of interest (McCabe et al., 2021; Voas & Watt, 2024; Halvorson et al., 2021):
•	Compute changes in predicted probability or expected count when moving the proxy from low to high (e.g., 10th→90th percentile), holding other variables at realistic values (McCabe et al., 2021; Voas & Watt, 2024; Halvorson et al., 2021).
•	Present these as: “When the proxy increases from A to B, the predicted failure probability rises from p₁ to p₂,” clarifying that A,B are levels of a physics guided indicator, not of the latent physical stressor.
Recommended reporting format
Quantity	How to phrase it for proxies	Sources
Coefficient / OR	Effect of 1 unit change in proxy on log odds / odds	(Achilonu et al., 2025; Košuta et al., 2025; Voas & Watt, 2024; Halvorson et al., 2021)
Marginal effect	Change in probability/count across realistic proxy range	(McCabe et al., 2021; Voas & Watt, 2024; Halvorson et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Mapping coefficients on proxies to outcomes.
Conclusion
For physics guided proxy indicators, interpret coefficients, odds ratios, and partial effects strictly as associations between that proxy and the risk metric on the chosen scale, then translate to changes in predicted probabilities or counts over meaningful proxy ranges—while avoiding claims that they are direct causal effects of the underlying physical quantities.
References

McCabe, C., Halvorson, M., King, K., Cao, X., & Kim, D. (2021). Interpreting Interaction Effects in Generalized Linear Models of Nonlinear Probabilities and Counts. Multivariate Behavioral Research, 57, 243 - 263. https://doi.org/10.1080/00273171.2020.1868966
