•	What evidence best demonstrates that the model is both predictive and physically interpretable?
Evidence of being both predictive and physically interpretable should combine strong validation metrics with explicit alignment to known physics and mechanistic reasoning.
1. Evidence the model is predictive
Across engineering and risk modeling, accepted evidence of predictive ability includes (Edlinger et al., 2021; Van Den Goorbergh et al., 2022; Alemu et al., 2025; Van Amsterdam, 2024; Jacobs et al., 2022; El-Hay et al., 2025; Blackman et al., 2025):
•	Internal validation with proper metrics 
o	High discrimination/fit: R², c statistic/AUC, correlation, low RMSE/MAE, high NSE, low Brier score (Elbeltagi et al., 2025; Edlinger et al., 2021; Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Alemu et al., 2025; Jacobs et al., 2022; Blackman et al., 2025).
o	Calibration: calibration plots, calibration intercept/slope≈1, calibration index (Edlinger et al., 2021; Van Den Goorbergh et al., 2022; Alemu et al., 2025; Jacobs et al., 2022; El-Hay et al., 2025; Blackman et al., 2025).
•	External or scenario based validation 
o	Performance reproduced on independent datasets, different case mixes, or deployment scenarios, showing transportability and robustness (Zhang et al., 2024; Alemu et al., 2025; Kartoun et al., 2022; Vernooij et al., 2023; El-Hay et al., 2025; Blackman et al., 2025; Saeheaw, 2025).
•	Robustness checks 
o	Sensitivity to noise, missing data, or class imbalance, with clear evidence that corrections do not harm calibration or utility (Van Den Goorbergh et al., 2022; Kartoun et al., 2022; El-Hay et al., 2025; Blackman et al., 2025).
Example
High R² (~0.97), low RMSE, and good calibration for glass density prediction across large datasets demonstrate strong predictive capability (Stoch, 2025). Similarly, soil moisture and concrete-strength models benchmarked against standard baselines confirm competitive accuracy (Zhang et al., 2024; Saeheaw, 2025).
2. Evidence the model is physically interpretable
Physical interpretability is demonstrated when model structure and attributions reflect known physics, not just statistical patterns (Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Takeishi & Kalousis, 2021; Rudin et al., 2021; Mao et al., 2024; Saeheaw, 2025):
•	Physics informed features or architecture 
o	Use of descriptors that correspond to physical quantities (e.g., mean atomic weight → molar mass; optical bands at water absorption peaks) and showing that performance improves as physical realism of features increases (Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Takeishi & Kalousis, 2021; Rudin et al., 2021; Saeheaw, 2025).
•	Feature importance consistent with physical laws 
o	SHAP/Sobol/LIME or symbolic regression identifying variables and interactions that match established mechanisms (e.g., lagged crop coefficient and solar radiation driving Kc; curing-age × modulus consistent with hydration–microstructure) (Elbeltagi et al., 2025; Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Rudin et al., 2021; Saeheaw, 2025).
•	Model form resembling known equations 
o	Learned equations or turbulence closures that reduce to, or closely resemble, accepted physical models (e.g., density equation, modified eddy-viscosity forms, ODEs matching true dynamics) (Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Takeishi & Kalousis, 2021; Jigjid et al., 2025; Golder & Hasan, 2025).
•	Physically meaningful latent states 
o	In hybrid or world models, latent variables shown to correspond to real physical states and parameters, with dynamics constrained by known equations and recovered parameter values close to ground truth (Takeishi & Kalousis, 2021; Jigjid et al., 2025; Mao et al., 2024).
Illustrative evidence bundle
Dimension	Strong evidence looks like	Citations
Predictive	High discrimination + good calibration, confirmed on external/scenario data	(Elbeltagi et al., 2025; Edlinger et al., 2021; Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Alemu et al., 2025; Kartoun et al., 2022; Vernooij et al., 2023; Jacobs et al., 2022; El-Hay et al., 2025; Blackman et al., 2025; Saeheaw, 2025)
Physical interpretability	Attributions, structure, or latent variables aligning with known physics; learned equations similar to physical laws	(Elbeltagi et al., 2025; Stoch, 2025; Zhang et al., 2024; Takeishi & Kalousis, 2021; Jigjid et al., 2025; Rudin et al., 2021; Golder & Hasan, 2025; Mao et al., 2024; Saeheaw, 2025)
Figure 1: Joint evidence for prediction and physical interpretability.
Conclusion
The best demonstration that a model is both predictive and physically interpretable combines (i) rigorous internal and external validation with discrimination and calibration metrics, and (ii) explicit, quantitative evidence that features, parameterizations, or learned structures reproduce known physical mechanisms and variables rather than arbitrary correlations.

4.7 Comparison with Benchmark Screening Methods
•	How are proposed risk-screening frameworks typically compared against simpler threshold-based or non-physics baselines?
Proposed risk-screening frameworks are typically benchmarked against simple thresholds or non-physics rules using discrimination, clinical utility, cost effectiveness, and implementation complexity.
How comparisons are usually structured
1. Direct performance vs. simple threshold rules
•	Cancer and lung screening frameworks compare optimized or dynamic risk policies to single fixed risk thresholds or guideline cut points (e.g., USPSTF criteria, one risk cut-off from a prediction model) using life years, QALYs, mortality reduction, false positives, and screens per death avoided (Toumazis et al., 2023; Toumazis et al., 2021; Yala et al., 2021).
•	Risk-based frameworks are judged better if they achieve more benefit at similar or fewer screenings, or similar benefit at lower burden, than the fixed-threshold or “treat-all/treat-none” baselines (Brentnall et al., 2023; Quintana et al., 2025; Toumazis et al., 2023; Toumazis et al., 2021; Yala et al., 2021).
2. Net benefit / cost-effectiveness vs. non-physics baselines
•	Decision-analytic measures such as net benefit and incremental net benefit compare complex policies to: 
o	doing nothing,
o	screening everyone,
o	and simple risk-threshold rules, all under explicit cost and effectiveness assumptions (Quintana et al., 2025).
•	Cost-effectiveness frontiers identify whether the new framework lies on or below the frontier defined by current guideline strategies (Toumazis et al., 2023).
3. Operational and fairness comparisons
•	Frameworks are often compared to widely used score-based or rule-based tools (HOSPITAL, LACE, WHO algorithms) using discrimination plus fairness metrics or misclassification costs, to show added value over simple scoring or threshold heuristics (Wang et al., 2024; Quintana et al., 2025).
•	In engineering risk screens, ML based probabilistic thresholds are distilled back into chart-like dual thresholds and then contrasted with traditional single safety factors to show improved calibration and screening precision while preserving familiarity (Alharbi, 2025).
4. Complexity and robustness
•	Studies explicitly discuss implementation burden, interpretability, and robustness (sensitivity analyses, bootstrapping) relative to simple baselines to argue that extra complexity is justified only if performance gains are substantial and stable (Brentnall et al., 2023; Toumazis et al., 2023; Alharbi, 2025; Yala et al., 2021; Katki & Bebu, 2021).
Conclusion
Comparisons typically pit new risk-screening frameworks against simple threshold or non-physics baselines on discrimination, net clinical benefit, cost-effectiveness, error trade-offs, and practicality, with an emphasis on whether added complexity delivers robust, policy-relevant gains.
References

Optimizing risk-based breast cancer screening policies with reinforcement learning. Nature Medicine, 28, 136 - 143. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41591-021-01599-w

•	What constitutes meaningful improvement over benchmark screening methods in operational engineering studies?
Meaningful improvement is defined by practically significant, statistically supported gains in accuracy, efficiency, or decision quality over benchmark screening methods, not just small metric bumps.
Core Criteria for “Meaningful” Improvement
1. Substantial performance gains on key metrics
Operational studies usually require clear, multi-metric gains, not marginal differences:
•	ML-enhanced virtual screening was judged meaningfully better because AUC-ROC improvements over consensus strategies were both statistically significant and large enough to matter for hit discovery (Ricci-Lopez et al., 2021).
•	A knowledge-graph ERNIE CNN risk model was considered superior by achieving notable precision/recall/F1 improvements over BERT, verified via ablation to show the added modules’ contribution (Huang et al., 2025).
•	Hybrid BN–logistic models in cybersecurity risk assessment had marked jumps in accuracy and AUC over single models (≈9–10 percentage points), interpreted as operationally relevant for vulnerability prioritization (Wei & Dong, 2025).
2. Demonstrated operational or economic value
Improvements must translate into better decisions, fewer simulations, or lower cost:
•	The Impact Indices screening method is meaningful because it cuts simulation effort while reliably flagging influential occupant behaviors, thus saving time and guiding where detailed models are needed (Zambrano et al., 2023).
•	Indicator screening frameworks in slope and combat capability assessment are valued when they sharply reduce indicator sets (≈50%+) while preserving ≥80% of information, improving evaluation efficiency without loss of interpretability (Li et al., 2025; Liao et al., 2025).
3. Robustness, generality, and interpretability
Gains are more convincing when they:
•	Hold across datasets, imbalance levels, or time (e.g., robust tree boosting for imbalanced risk data) (Velarde et al., 2024).
•	Preserve or enhance engineering interpretability (e.g., validity/reliability checks on index systems; causal structure in BN-based methods) (Wei & Dong, 2025; Liao et al., 2025; Rahim et al., 2024).
Example benchmarks and improvements
Dimension	Typical benchmark	What counts as meaningful	Sources
Predictive accuracy	Simpler ML, rule-based, or CPT/EDAS	Clear AUC/F1/accuracy lift, statistically and practically large	(Huang et al., 2025; Velarde et al., 2024; Wei & Dong, 2025; Ricci-Lopez et al., 2021; Chang et al., 2025)
Computational effort	Full SA / exhaustive indicators	Major reduction in runs or variables at similar fidelity	(Li et al., 2025; Liao et al., 2025; Zambrano et al., 2023)
Decision quality	Traditional risk matrices / static rules	Better risk ranking, fewer incidents or misclassifications	(Wei & Dong, 2025; Rahim et al., 2024; Chang et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Typical axes for judging screening improvement.
Conclusion
In operational engineering, improvement over benchmark screening is “meaningful” when it yields clearly better predictive or screening performance, demonstrable savings in effort or cost, and robust, interpretable decisions—validated with appropriate statistical tests and sensitivity/ablation analyses.

•	How should comparative results be framed to highlight methodological contribution rather than only numerical gain?
Comparative results should be framed to show how and why a method improves inference, transparency, or decision support, not just that it improves a metric.
1. Make the methodological aim explicit
•	State what problem the new method solves: e.g., handling complex configurational causation (fsQCA), integrating qualitative and quantitative evidence, or representing uncertainty rather than point estimates (Kumar et al., 2022; Prado et al., 2022; Kutej et al., 2025; Sherif et al., 2024).
•	Situate it among alternatives by contrasting assumptions, data needs, and use-cases, not just accuracy scores (Khodabakhshian et al., 2023; Hajiyev et al., 2025; Kumi et al., 2024).
Example elements to emphasize
•	Type of causality handled (multiple conjunctural causation vs single-effect models) (Kumar et al., 2022; Sherif et al., 2024).
•	Ability to incorporate uncertainty, expert judgement or graphical communication vs fixed-value risk matrices (Prado et al., 2022; Kutej et al., 2025).
•	Mixed-methods synthesis capability vs purely quantitative synthesis (Kolaski et al., 2023; Sherif et al., 2024).
2. Report full patterns, not only “winning” numbers
•	Show variation and uncertainty (e.g., discernibility vs mere percentage differences; Monte Carlo distributions) to avoid overstating small gains (Prado et al., 2022; Kolaski et al., 2023).
•	Use visualizations that present the full result space and then highlight regions of interest, instead of hiding non-significant results (Taylor et al., 2022; Prado et al., 2022).
Comparative framing table
Focus	How to frame comparisons	Citations
Assumptions	Explain what the method assumes about data structure, causality, uncertainty	(Kumar et al., 2022; Kutej et al., 2025; Khodabakhshian et al., 2023; Sherif et al., 2024)
Robustness	Show sensitivity to thresholds, baseline imbalance, data quality	(Prado et al., 2022; Plass et al., 2022; Ammar et al., 2025; Kolaski et al., 2023)
Interpretability / XAI	Highlight gains in explainability, transparency, or reproducibility	(Şahin et al., 2024; Turbé et al., 2022; Taylor et al., 2022; Kumi et al., 2024)
Practical utility	Compare decision relevance, not just effect size (e.g., risk ranking, scenario exploration)	(Prado et al., 2022; Kutej et al., 2025; Hajiyev et al., 2025; Sattar et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Dimensions for framing methodological contributions.
3. Tie comparisons to good-practice criteria
•	Anchor claims in methodological standards: clarity of analytic approach, alignment with theory, handling of bias/heterogeneity, and reporting completeness (Peters et al., 2021; Thomann et al., 2022; Kolaski et al., 2023).
•	Discuss when a “worse” metric may still be preferable because of lower complexity, better ESG alignment, or easier implementation, as in cost–accuracy–ESG model selection (Sattar et al., 2025).
Conclusion
To foreground methodological contribution, comparisons should explain what new kinds of questions, evidence integration, or decisions the method enables, how it manages uncertainty and complexity, and why these properties matter for practice—using numerical gains as supporting evidence rather than the main story.

4.8 Sensitivity, Uncertainty, and Robustness Results
•	What result forms are commonly used to present bootstrap uncertainty, perturbation robustness, and sensitivity-analysis findings?
Bootstrap, robustness, and sensitivity results are most often presented as confidence intervals, summary tables, and visual effect plots that highlight variability and stability.
Common result forms for bootstrap uncertainty
•	Numerical CIs around key estimates (e.g., mean, indices, mediation effects), often 95% intervals reported in text or tables, using normal, percentile, BC, BCa, or bootstrap t methods (Mokhtar et al., 2023; Tibbe & Montoya, 2021; Ouyang et al., 2023; Mokhtar et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2025; Panichkitkosolkul, 2023).
•	Simulation summary tables listing coverage probability, average interval width, and sometimes power or bias across scenarios (sample sizes, distributions, methods) (Tibbe & Montoya, 2021; Ouyang et al., 2023; Mokhtar et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2025; Panichkitkosolkul, 2023).
•	Graphs of performance vs. design factors, such as power/type I error vs. log(sample size), or coverage vs. skewness, with separate curves for alternative bootstrap methods (Tibbe & Montoya, 2021; Mokhtar et al., 2024).
•	Density/histogram plots of bootstrap distributions to illustrate skewness or variability of resampled estimates (Chen et al., 2025; Panichkitkosolkul, 2023).
Example formats
Aim	Typical result form	Citations
Report uncertainty on one parameter	Numeric CI and/or forest plot–style intervals	(Mokhtar et al., 2023; Ouyang et al., 2023; Chen et al., 2025; Panichkitkosolkul, 2023)
Compare CI methods	Tables of coverage, width, power; method by scenario figures	(Tibbe & Montoya, 2021; Ouyang et al., 2023; Mokhtar et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Standard formats for reporting bootstrap outputs.
Result forms for perturbation robustness & sensitivity analysis
•	Vector field or heatmap plots of local sensitivities over parameter space, showing gradient magnitude/direction and regions of high sensitivity (Machado et al., 2025).
•	Global sensitivity summaries (Sobol’ first/total indices) reported as barplots with bootstrap CIs or in tables, sometimes up to higher order effects (Borgonovo & Rabitti, 2022; Puy et al., 2021; Lo Piano et al., 2021; Most, 2024).
•	Robustness bounds/interval bands over time or frequency: e.g., shaded envelopes showing min–max or vertex based bounds of system responses under parameter hyper rectangles or periodic perturbations (Blanchini et al., 2022).
•	Indices or robustness metrics as distributions, e.g., histograms of amplification factors or robustness measures across sampled parameters (Machado et al., 2025; Blanchini et al., 2022; Svitelman et al., 2024; Bosman et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Across these literatures, uncertainty and robustness are most commonly communicated through confidence intervals (often bootstrap based), tabular summaries of coverage/width or indices, and plots (bars, vector fields, envelopes, histograms) that visually convey variability, sensitivity structure, and stability under perturbations.

Panichkitkosolkul, W. (2023). Bootstrap Confidence Intervals for the Parameter of the Poisson-Prakaamy Distribution with Their Applications. WSEAS TRANSACTIONS ON MATHEMATICS. https://doi.org/10.37394/23206.2023.22.45
•	How can robustness results be used to strengthen confidence in proxy definitions and model conclusions?
Robustness analyses can justify proxies and conclusions when they show results persist across plausible alternative specifications, assumptions, and perturbations.
Using robustness to support proxy definitions
1. Varying proxy operationalizations and coding choices
•	Multiverse/specification-curve analyses show how effect estimates change across many defensible operationalizations of key variables; if results are stable across these choices, confidence in the proxy definitions increases (Rijnhart et al., 2021; Schweinsberg et al., 2021).
•	Crowdsourced re-analyses demonstrate that much dispersion in results comes from how constructs are operationalized; explicitly mapping and showing robustness across these choices is key to arguing that a chosen proxy is not idiosyncratic (Schweinsberg et al., 2021).
2. Proxy-based causal methods with stress tests
•	Proxy-based causal frameworks (e.g., factor-analytic proxies for latent confounders, proxy pattern–mixture models) use simulations and applied examples to show that treatment effects remain approximately correct under misspecification, misclassified proxies, and non-normality, bolstering the credibility of the proxy strategy itself (Andridge et al., 2025; Kottler & Cochran, 2025).
•	When robustness checks show correct sign and order of magnitude even under violated structural assumptions, authors argue that conclusions are qualitatively reliable while transparently flagging remaining bias risk (Kottler & Cochran, 2025).
Example ways to present this
Aim	Robustness use	How it strengthens claims	Citations
Validate proxy construct	Compare many proxy codings/specifications	Stable effect size/sign → proxy captures construct, not coding artifact	(Rijnhart et al., 2021; Schweinsberg et al., 2021)
Validate proxy as confounder surrogate	Stress-test proxy method under misspecification	Small bias across scenarios → proxy approach reasonable	(Andridge et al., 2025; Kottler & Cochran, 2025)
Figure 1: Roles of robustness checks in validating proxies.
Using robustness to qualify model conclusions
•	Sensitivity indices or robustness values (e.g., R²-based measures, confounding-strength parameters) quantify how strong violations must be to overturn conclusions; large required perturbations support stronger, but still conditional, claims (Huang & Pimentel, 2022; Zheng et al., 2022; Yin et al., 2021).
•	Robust statistical methods and pre-specified sensitivity analyses are framed as showing where conclusions do and do not hold, encouraging “bounded” claims rather than over-generalization (Borgonovo et al., 2022; Sladekova & Field, 2025).
Conclusion
Robustness results strengthen confidence when they demonstrate that main findings persist across reasonable alternative proxies, analytic choices, and assumption violations, and when the analysis makes explicit how extreme a deviation would be required to change conclusions—thereby justifying both the proxy definitions and the scope of the model’s claims.

•	What discussion points are expected when uncertainty and sensitivity analyses reveal both stable and unstable aspects of a framework?
When uncertainty and sensitivity analyses show both stable and unstable aspects, the discussion should clearly separate what is well supported from what is fragile, explain why, and spell out implications for use and future work.
1. Clarify which parts of the framework are reliable
•	Identify robust outputs/relationships: Highlight predictions or indices whose uncertainty bands are narrow and sensitivity is low or stable over time; e.g., age based EC concentration model parameters with small parameter uncertainty and time invariant sensitivity are argued to be more reliable than alternatives (Riazi & Western, 2024).
•	Emphasize robust subsystems or regimes: Note conditions where simplified or idealized models remain valid (e.g., certain thermodynamic regimes where ideal-fluid approximations give similar stability growth factors (Ren et al., 2025), or TAVI deployment diameter predictions that meet validation thresholds despite uncertainty (Scuoppo et al., 2025)).
•	Use robustness to justify design choices: Robust topology optimizations that remain stable across multiple uncertain sources can be discussed as evidence that the structural layout is inherently reliable, in contrast to unstable deterministic designs (Feng et al., 2025).
2. Diagnose and interpret unstable aspects
•	Locate main sources of uncertainty: Explain whether instability comes from parameters, inputs, or structure; for instance, EC modeling where input data and structural choices dominate total uncertainty, and validation-period coverage is poor (Riazi & Western, 2024), or iFR predictions dominated by geometry and flow uncertainties rather than pressures (Dalmaso et al., 2025).
•	Explain regime  or context dependence: Discuss how sensitivity shifts with conditions (e.g., slip depth thresholds where control of slope stability uncertainty switches between roots and soil (Xian et al., 2025), or fluid regimes where some property uncertainties become critical and others can be idealized away (Ren et al., 2025)).
•	Flag underestimation or limits of the framework: Note where prediction intervals miss observations or where models show regime specific lack of robustness, indicating structural inadequacy or missing processes (Riazi & Western, 2024; Mosso et al., 2023).
Suggested discussion structure
Element	Focus when both stable & unstable	Citations
“What is trusted”	Robust parameters, regimes, outputs	(Ren et al., 2025; Riazi & Western, 2024; Feng et al., 2025; Dalmaso et al., 2025; Scuoppo et al., 2025)
“What is fragile”	High sensitivity components, regimes with poor fit	(Ren et al., 2025; Riazi & Western, 2024; Xian et al., 2025; Dalmaso et al., 2025; Mosso et al., 2023)
Implications	When/where framework can guide decisions; where caution, extra data, or alternative models are needed	(Bi et al., 2023; Herrera et al., 2021; Scuoppo et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Structuring discussion of mixed robustness findings.
Conclusion
When analyses reveal both stability and fragility, the discussion should (i) delineate robust components and the conditions under which they hold, (ii) unpack the drivers and regimes of instability, and (iii) translate both into practical guidance, caveats, and priorities for refining the framework and targeting new data or model improvements.

5. Discussion
5.1 Interpretation of Key Findings in Relation to Resonance Theory
•	How should empirical findings on voltage depression, feeder loading, and risk classes be interpreted through established harmonic resonance theory?
Observed voltage depression and loading-dependent risk classes can be explained as manifestations of how feeder impedance and loading shift harmonic resonance conditions.
Linking empirical patterns to resonance theory
1. Voltage depression vs. harmonic resonance
•	Harmonics create additional reactive power flows and losses that increase effective feeder impedance and cause voltage drops, especially where 3rd, 5th, and 7th harmonics are strong (Pereira et al., 2021; Michalec et al., 2021).
•	Near a parallel resonance between feeder inductance and shunt capacitance (e.g., cables, capacitors, inverter filters), small harmonic currents can produce large voltage distortion or localized voltage depression/overvoltage, depending on where the resonance lies relative to the spectrum (Xie & Zhong, 2025; Elazim et al., 2025).
•	Empirical findings of feeders with both higher THD and worse voltage profiles are consistent with operation close to resonance bands where voltage distortion is amplified even if overall THD remains within limits (Ahsan et al., 2021; Pereira et al., 2021; Michalec et al., 2021).
Empirical patterns and resonance-informed interpretation
Empirical pattern	Resonance-theory interpretation	Citations
Worse PQ / lower voltage on certain heavily loaded feeders	Loading changes R–X ratio and shifts resonance frequencies, bringing dominant harmonic orders (3rd, 5th, 7th, 11th) closer to parallel/series resonances (Xie & Zhong, 2025; Elazim et al., 2025; Pereira et al., 2021; Michalec et al., 2021)	(Xie & Zhong, 2025; Elazim et al., 2025; Pereira et al., 2021; Michalec et al., 2021)
Feeder “risk classes” aligned with certain topology / capacitor placements	Different L–C configurations move resonance bands, explaining systematic feeder-to-feeder risk differences (Xie & Zhong, 2025; Elazim et al., 2025; Michalec et al., 2021; Aldosary et al., 2025)	
Higher-order harmonics decay with distance but some orders remain persistent	Network impedance profile attenuates most high orders; persistent ones indicate proximity to a resonance band (Elazim et al., 2025; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Michalec et al., 2021)	
Figure 1: Mapping observed risk patterns to resonance.
Interpreting risk classes
•	Feeders with high loading, significant capacitive elements, and strong nonlinear or inverter-based sources are higher resonance risk classes, since both excitation (harmonic injection) and amplification (L–C resonance) are present (Ahsan et al., 2021; Xie & Zhong, 2025; Elazim et al., 2025; Pereira et al., 2021; Michalec et al., 2021; Aldosary et al., 2025).
•	“Low-risk” classes likely correspond to feeders where resonance frequencies are far from dominant emission orders or where damping (R) is high enough to limit amplification (Xie & Zhong, 2025; Elazim et al., 2025; Michalec et al., 2021; Aldosary et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Empirical gradients in voltage depression, feeder loading, and PQ “risk classes” can be coherently viewed as different operating points relative to feeder resonance conditions: loading and topology shift resonance frequencies and damping, which in turn determines how strongly typical harmonic spectra are converted into voltage distortion and reliability risk.

•	What mechanisms from the literature help explain why certain operational proxies align with higher resonance vulnerability?
Operational proxies align with resonance vulnerability when they track how loading, topology, and controls shift system impedance toward resonant conditions.
Mechanisms linking proxies to resonance risk
1. Proxies that indicate “where” resonance can form (topology, capacitors, long lines)
•	Modal and frequency scan studies show that nodes near shunt capacitors/reactive compensators have the highest resonance sensitivity; small parameter changes there strongly shift resonance frequency and impedance peaks (Lokar et al., 2021; Buzo et al., 2025; Govorun et al., 2021).
•	Long feeders and cable rich networks have higher capacitance, moving series/parallel resonance to lower frequencies where common harmonics exist, raising vulnerability (Lokar et al., 2021; Bardadze & Gordiashvili, 2025; Shen et al., 2023).
•	Thus, operational proxies such as “feeder has many/large capacitor banks,” “long cable feeder,” or “voltage support devices present” align with higher resonance vulnerability because they correlate with high L/C nodes and low resonance frequencies (Lokar et al., 2021; Bardadze & Gordiashvili, 2025; Buzo et al., 2025; Govorun et al., 2021; Shen et al., 2023; Alashaary et al., 2025).
2. Proxies that indicate “how strongly” resonance is excited (loading, harmonic sources)
•	Studies show that nonlinear and inverter based resources inject harmonic spectra that can intersect the network’s resonant frequencies, especially as grid impedance and loading vary (Xu et al., 2024; Sun et al., 2023; Gu & Green, 2023).
•	Reactive power compensation degree and load level determine when current resonance in capacitor banks occurs over wide frequency ranges; certain loading/compensation combinations systematically overload capacitors at or near resonance (Govorun et al., 2021).
•	Operational proxies such as high feeder loading, high nonlinear/IBR penetration, or large reactive compensation ratios therefore align with resonance vulnerability because they indicate stronger harmonic excitation of existing resonant modes (Xu et al., 2024; Govorun et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2023; Gu & Green, 2023).
3. Proxies that reflect “observed consequences” (distortion, sensitive bus indices)
•	Harmonic mode–based severity indices and bus “dominance” metrics identify locations where small harmonic currents produce large voltage amplification (Lokar et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025; Shakeri et al., 2022).
•	Elevated local THD, persistent specific harmonic orders, or repeated PQ alarms at particular buses function as empirical proxies for high modal impedance and low damping, i.e., strong resonance amplification (Lokar et al., 2021; Othman et al., 2025; Kannan et al., 2022; Shakeri et al., 2022).
Summary Table
Proxy type	Physical link to resonance	Citations
Capacitor/line configuration	Sets L–C ratios, resonance frequencies	(Lokar et al., 2021; Bardadze & Gordiashvili, 2025; Buzo et al., 2025; Govorun et al., 2021; Shen et al., 2023; Alashaary et al., 2025)
Loading/IBR share	Changes grid impedance, harmonic injection	(Xu et al., 2024; Govorun et al., 2021; Sun et al., 2023; Gu & Green, 2023)
Local THD / PQ alarms	Indicates high modal impedance, low damping	(Lokar et al., 2021; Othman et al., 2025; Kannan et al., 2022; Shakeri et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Operational proxies mapped to resonance mechanisms.
Conclusion
Operational proxies align with higher resonance vulnerability when they correlate with (1) network structures that lower resonance frequencies, (2) operating points that increase harmonic excitation, or (3) measured symptoms of high modal impedance and low damping.

•	How can the discussion connect reduced-order screening results to known physical behavior without overstating direct harmonic measurement?
Connecting reduced-order screening to physical behavior
Reduced-order resonance screening (e.g., modal/harmonic-mode analysis, SSM/ROM approaches) can be tied to known physical behavior by emphasizing impedance/mode shaping and validation against full/few measurements, rather than claiming it is equivalent to dense harmonic monitoring.
1. Emphasize what the reduced model actually predicts
•	Power-system resonance mode analysis and frequency scans identify where impedance peaks occur and which nodes/modes dominate, not real-time harmonic levels (Lokar et al., 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025). These tools reveal physical mechanisms (L–C configurations, controller gains, line lengths) that create amplification, so screening results should be framed as “locations and frequencies where resonance would strongly amplify harmonics if excited”, not as measured distortion.
•	In mechanical and MEMS ROM work, spectral submanifolds, POD, and invariant-manifold ROMs reconstruct forced response curves and internal/superharmonic resonances of the full model, but only after careful consistency checks against full-order simulations or limited experiments (Li et al., 2021; Gobat et al., 2021; Gobat et al., 2022; Frangi et al., 2023; Dyn et al., 2023). Similarly, grid screening outputs should be positioned as surrogates for full-model frequency-response behavior, not direct PQ data.
2. Use limited measurements as consistency checks, not ground-truth surrogates
•	Rail and distribution studies detect resonances by combining structurally informed impedance criteria with selective voltage/current spectra at a few points, using rules to distinguish infrastructure resonances from source harmonics (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Sharma et al., 2022; Xie & Zhong, 2025).
•	PQ monitor placement work explicitly notes that resonance assessment is based on network data and calculated impedance, with measurement noise having minor effect on the identification of very high resonance values (Shakeri et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025). This supports wording such as: “screening results are consistent with available harmonic measurements at key buses,” not “we directly measured these resonances.”
3. Suggested discussion framing
Goal	Recommended phrasing strategy	Citations
Link to physics	Describe how screening shifts resonance frequencies/impedances in line with known L–C and control effects	(Xie & Zhong, 2025; Zhu et al., 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024)
Relate to data	Say results “predict high amplification potential at frequencies where measurements show elevated components,” avoiding claims of full spectrum reconstruction	(Sharma et al., 2022; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Shakeri et al., 2024; Ansari et al., 2025)
Figure 1: How to phrase links between ROM screening and data.
Conclusion
To avoid overstating, present reduced-order screening as a physics-based predictor of where and how resonance is likely, then use sparse harmonic measurements only as qualitative validation that observed distortions occur in the predicted bands and locations, not as evidence that the screening directly measures harmonics.

5.2 Significance of Proxy Indicators for Utility Practice
•	What practical value do proxy-based screening indicators provide for utilities with limited harmonic instrumentation?
Proxy-based indicators give low-cost, system-wide “early warning” for harmonics when meters are scarce.
Practical value for under-instrumented utilities
1.	Network-wide screening and prioritization
o	With only a few PQ monitors, utilities struggle to know where to invest in detailed harmonic studies or mitigation (Shao et al., 2023; Ali et al., 2024).
o	Physics-guided proxies (e.g., selected bus voltages, loading levels, low-rate smart meter data) can flag likely hotspots or problematic feeders, similar to how compressive-sensing and AI methods reduce the need for dense harmonic measurements (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Chandran et al., 2025).
2.	Reduced dependence on expensive monitors and models
o	Traditional harmonic estimation often assumes many monitors plus detailed impedance/admittance models (Taghvaie et al., 2023).
o	Proxy based screening can work with partial models and sparse data, analogous to low-cost sensor frameworks that trade a few reference instruments for many cheap, calibrated surrogates (Nalakurthi et al., 2024).
3.	Faster, lighter monitoring for operations
o	Full-space vector or synchronized waveform platforms provide rich harmonic detail but are data  and bandwidth intensive (Kryltcov et al., 2021; Shao et al., 2023).
o	Proxy indicators can be computed at the edge (e.g., from existing SCADA or smart meter streams) to trigger more detailed measurements or inverter control actions only when risk is elevated (Kryltcov et al., 2021; Shao et al., 2023; Chandran et al., 2025).
4.	Support for planning and risk management
o	Reviews highlight overheated equipment, misoperation, and reduced lifetime as key harmonic risks demanding proactive management (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Ali et al., 2024).
o	Screening scores at feeders or nodes help planners rank locations for filters, rephasing, or connection limits, even before full PQ logging is available (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Ali et al., 2024; Chandran et al., 2025).
Example positioning sentence
“For utilities with sparse PQ meters, physics-guided proxy indicators offer low-cost, system-level harmonic risk screening, directing limited instrumentation and mitigation resources to the most suspicious locations, much as compressive sensing and IoT frameworks do for power quality detection and low cost sensing in other domains (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Nalakurthi et al., 2024; Kryltcov et al., 2021; Chandran et al., 2025).”
Summary Table: Key Practical Benefits
Utility need	Proxy-based value	Citations
Identify harmonic hotspots	System-wide risk maps from sparse data	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Shao et al., 2023; Chandran et al., 2025)
Stretch limited PQ hardware	Fewer high-end meters, more virtual sensors	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Nalakurthi et al., 2024; Kryltcov et al., 2021)
Enable timely operational response	Lightweight, edge-computable indicators	(Kryltcov et al., 2021; Shao et al., 2023; Chandran et al., 2025)
Guide planning & investment	Prioritized feeders/assets for mitigation	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Ali et al., 2024; Chandran et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Operational benefits of proxy harmonic indicators.
Conclusion
Proxy-based screening indicators let under-instrumented utilities move from reactive, case-by-case harmonic checks to proactive, system-wide risk management, concentrating scarce meters and mitigation devices where they are likely to matter most.

Chandran, L., Karuppasamy, I., Nair, M., Sun, H., & Krishnakumari, P. (2025). Compressive Sensing in Power Engineering: A Comprehensive Survey of Theory and Applications, and a Case Study. J. Sens. Actuator Networks, 14, 28. https://doi.org/10.3390/jsan14020028
•	How have similar screening approaches informed monitoring prioritization, maintenance planning, or operational decision-making in utilities?
Screening-style indicators have guided utilities to focus monitoring, maintenance, and operations on the highest risk assets, feeders, and locations.
Monitoring & Instrumentation Prioritization
•	Risk- and value-based planning: Reliability/risk simulators combined with Value of Lost Load (VoLL) are used to rank assets and corridors where interruptions are most costly, guiding where to install advanced monitoring and where to tolerate lower observability (Gaha et al., 2021).
•	System-wide PQ assessment as a screening layer: Compound PQ indices and multi-criteria assessment frameworks summarize many disturbances into a single health score, which can be used to benchmark feeders or plants and decide where detailed, synchronized waveform monitoring is justified (Shao et al., 2023; Caicedo et al., 2023; Elsotohy et al., 2025).
•	Harmonic “zoning” / hotspot narrowing: Clustering of harmonic measurements into zones reduces the suspected area for dominant harmonic sources, so detailed meters and investigations are deployed only in the most polluted zones (Zhang et al., 2024).
Examples of Monitoring-Driven Prioritization
Approach	How it informs prioritization	Citations
Risk + VoLL simulator	Choose lines/areas for enhanced monitoring and reinforcement	(Kisuule et al., 2021; Gaha et al., 2021)
Combined PQ indices	Rank feeders/plants for PQ meters or campaigns	(Shao et al., 2023; Elsotohy et al., 2025)
Harmonic zoning via clustering	Focus harmonic analyzers on a few “polluted” zones	(Zhang et al., 2024)
Figure 1: How screening metrics guide monitoring focus.
Maintenance Planning & Operational Decisions
•	Maintenance-preventable failure screening: Mining outage records by cause (e.g., vegetation) identifies a small set of dominant, maintenance preventable drivers; refocusing preventive work on one cause yielded ~6–9% reductions in interruption indices, directly reshaping vegetation management plans (Da Silva et al., 2025).
•	Risk based feeder maintenance ranking: Multi-criteria methods (TOPSIS, simulated annealing) rank feeder–cause pairs by contribution to interruptions and cost, producing minimum risk preventive maintenance portfolios and helping decide where to intensify work vs. accept risk (Popović et al., 2025; Luz et al., 2024; Enjavimadar & Rastegar, 2022).
•	Asset management and resilience: Integrated reliability–VoLL frameworks generate monetized risk maps that support choices between run to failure, preventive maintenance, and capital renewal on high impact assets (Kisuule et al., 2021; Gaha et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Across reliability, PQ, and asset-management studies, screening-type indicators are used as a first layer to rank feeders, nodes, and failure modes, then drive targeted deployment of monitors, focused preventive maintenance, and risk-informed operational choices under constrained budgets.

•	What operational benefits and implementation constraints should be discussed for real-world utility adoption?
For real utility adoption, emphasize clear operational gains but also data, integration, and governance constraints.
Operational benefits to highlight
1. System reliability, safety, and power quality
•	Better harmonic estimation and power quality prediction reduce equipment overheating, misoperation, and outages, especially as grids integrate more non linear and renewable sources (Daniel et al., 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023; Al-Sharif et al., 2022).
•	Hybrid AI/controls can adapt filters or control settings in real time when rising harmonics or PQ disturbances are detected, improving stability and efficiency under changing conditions (Daniel et al., 2024; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025).
2. Targeted monitoring, maintenance, and planning
•	Proxy indicators and advanced estimation reduce dependency on dense instrumentation and full impedance models, enabling utilities with limited monitors to still identify high risk locations and prioritize mitigation investments (Daniel et al., 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
•	ESG style proxy metrics in water utilities show how infrastructure and operational indicators can stand in for harder to measure governance quality, supporting strategic planning in data constrained contexts (Błażewicz, 2025).
3. Operational efficiency and scalability
•	AI assisted PQ management demonstrates higher accuracy in identifying complex PQ issues than traditional methods, which can support more automated decision support for operators (Daniel et al., 2024; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025).
•	Proxy CPUE in fisheries shows that simple, scalable ratios built from routinely collected data can materially improve status estimates where direct measurements are sparse, an analogous benefit for utilities relying on limited PQ data (Wang et al., 2025).
Key implementation constraints and risks
1. Data, models, and computational limits
•	Many traditional harmonic and AI methods assume substantial measurement coverage, synchronized data, or complex models that are hard to maintain in practice (Daniel et al., 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
•	Deep learning–based PQ tools can be computationally heavy and sensitive to class imbalance, which complicates real time deployment and reliability (Daniel et al., 2024; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025).
2. Integration into workflows and IT systems
•	Large organizations adopting new digital tools must budget for IT integration, cybersecurity, training, and workflow redesign; burdening many roles with complex outputs can slow or derail adoption (Marwaha et al., 2022).
•	Tools that only touch a narrow workflow with clear, actionable outputs are easier to sustain operationally than broad, abstract “risk scores” (Marwaha et al., 2022).
3. Organizational readiness, governance, and regulation
•	Enterprise adoption hinges on executive sponsorship, alignment with institutional priorities, and clear value justification (e.g., avoided VoLL, ESG goals) (Marwaha et al., 2022; Błażewicz, 2025; Yang et al., 2024).
•	AI/PQ systems face regulatory and ethical constraints; gaps in standards or guidance can slow or limit deployment even where technical performance is strong (Yang et al., 2024; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025).
Conclusion
For real-world utilities, proxy-based and AI-enhanced screening can credibly promise better risk targeting, PQ control, and planning under sparse measurements, but only if constraints around data quality, model complexity, IT integration, staff training, and governance/regulatory alignment are explicitly addressed in any adoption proposal.

5.3 Methodological Contribution Relative to Existing Literature
•	How should the novelty of a physics-guided proxy indicator framework be positioned relative to existing harmonic and power-quality literature?
A physics-guided proxy indicator framework is novel when it links indirect, easy-to-measure quantities to harmonics through explicit power-system physics, beyond conventional indices or black-box ML.
1. Locate it within existing harmonic / PQ indicator work
Most current work introduces new indices but still ties them directly to waveform distortion measures (THD, individual harmonics, Lissajous features, combined PQ indices) rather than to proxy variables:
•	New PQ indices based on power decomposition for nonlinear loads remain grounded in harmonic powers of voltage/current waveforms themselves, not in other system states (De Almeida Coelho et al., 2024).
•	Dynamic Lissajous-based indices derive from geometric properties of v–i trajectories, again using directly measured PQ waveforms (Sinha et al., 2025).
•	Combined or compound PQ indices (AHP–CRITIC, multifactor or dynamic indices) aggregate classical PQ quantities (THD, unbalance, PF, sags) but still operate on primary PQ measurements, not on proxies (Elsotohy et al., 2025; Beridze et al., 2025).
•	Novel THD factors and DC-grid PQ metrics extend frequency ranges (up to supraharmonics) or refine grouping, but remain distortion-centric and measurement-based (Mariscotti, 2021; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021).
•	Physics-aware neural networks for harmonic state estimation embed network topology/impedance into the estimator, yet still aim to reconstruct full harmonic states rather than define task-specific proxy indicators (Mack et al., 2024).
2. Where a physics guided proxy framework differs
You can position your framework as novel if it:
•	Uses explicit circuit / network models (impedances, coupling paths, conservation laws) to select and mathematically justify proxy variables that are not standard PQ indices (e.g., selected bus voltages, device states, control signals).
•	Provides closed-form or learnable mappings from these proxies to harmonic/PQ indices, analogous to physics aware state estimation but simplified for monitoring or control at uninstrumented locations (Kryltcov et al., 2021; Mack et al., 2024).
•	Targets cost-effective monitoring or responsibility allocation where full harmonic metering is impractical, going beyond current contribution indices at the PCC that still rely on harmonic current measurement (Shklyarskiy et al., 2021).
3. Suggested positioning sentence
“In contrast to existing harmonic and power quality indices, which are built directly on detailed harmonic measurements or extended THD-type factors (De Almeida Coelho et al., 2024; Mariscotti, 2021; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021), the proposed framework introduces physics guided proxy indicators: physically interpretable, indirectly measured quantities rigorously linked to harmonic impacts via network models, thus enabling low cost, scalable monitoring where full PQ sensing is infeasible (Kryltcov et al., 2021; Mack et al., 2024; Shklyarskiy et al., 2021).”
Conclusion
Position the novelty as a shift from “better ways to measure distortion” to “physics justified indirect indicators” that trade dense metering for model-based inference, while remaining more interpretable and standards-aligned than purely data-driven ML.

•	What methodological features distinguish this study from direct-measurement, simulation-heavy, or purely data-driven approaches?
Hybrid, physics-informed designs distinguish this study from “pure” measurement, simulation, or black box data driven work.
Methodological contrasts
1. Versus direct measurement
•	Direct measurement approaches (e.g., heat flux meters, infrared thermography, battery SOH sensors) aim to observe the quantity of interest as directly as possible, with limited modeling and little generalization beyond the measurement setup (Gong et al., 2025; Kim et al., 2023).
•	In contrast, hybrid models estimate unobservable or hard to measure states (inner temperatures, structural nonlinearities, turbine power residuals) by combining sparse measurements with learned or simulated relationships, enabling soft sensing and extrapolation to new conditions (Kačur et al., 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2022; Gij'on et al., 2025).
2. Versus simulation heavy / pure physics models
•	Pure physics approaches rely on detailed first principles or finite element/CFD models and heavy computation, often assuming complete knowledge of parameters and boundary conditions (Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Kačur et al., 2025; Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2022).
•	The hybrid methodology reduces simulation burden by: 
o	Using physics models as encoders, surrogates, or assistants rather than full simulators (residual learning, surrogate models, feature learning) (Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Wenzel et al., 2024; Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2022; Yang et al., 2022).
o	Calibrating or correcting physics outputs with data driven components to capture unmodelled effects and reduce the performance gap (Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Wenzel et al., 2024; Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2022; Gij'on et al., 2025).
3. Versus purely data driven models
•	Pure ML models depend on large labeled datasets and often ignore known structure, leading to black box behavior and poor extrapolation or physical inconsistency (Deb & Schlueter, 2021; Pollice et al., 2021; Gong et al., 2025; Azevedo et al., 2024; Lin et al., 2025).
•	Hybrid methods: 
o	Embed physics-based constraints, features, or residual structures into the learning problem, improving sample efficiency, physical plausibility, and robustness to distribution shift (Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Wenzel et al., 2024; Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2022; Gij'on et al., 2025).
o	Explicitly handle uncertainty (e.g., probabilistic or conformal quantile prediction), which is uncommon in standard deterministic ML (Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Gij'on et al., 2025).
Summary Table
Approach type	Core idea	Typical limitation it overcomes	Sources
Direct measurement	Measure target directly	Limited generalization, instrumentation cost	(Gong et al., 2025; Kim et al., 2023)
Simulation-heavy	Detailed physics simulation	High computation, parameter uncertainty	(Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Kačur et al., 2025; Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2022)
Pure data-driven	Learn from data only	Data hunger, weak physical guarantees	(Deb & Schlueter, 2021; Pollice et al., 2021; Gong et al., 2025; Azevedo et al., 2024; Lin et al., 2025)
Hybrid (this study)	Physics + ML integration	Balances accuracy, cost, interpretability	(Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2025; Luo et al., 2024; Wenzel et al., 2024; Von Krannichfeldt et al., 2024; Yang et al., 2022; Gij'on et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Comparison of methodological families and hybrids.
Conclusion
Methodologically, the study is distinguished by its explicit fusion of physics and data—using simulations and theory as structured priors or residual baselines—rather than relying solely on dense instrumentation, exhaustive simulation, or unconstrained black box learning.

•	How do Q1 journal discussions typically articulate theoretical, methodological, and practical contributions simultaneously?
Q1 journal discussions usually weave contributions together by showing how new theory, methods, and implications co produce a clearer understanding of a problem.
Typical rhetorical pattern in Q1 discussions
1. Theoretical contribution
•	Authors position findings against dominant frameworks or constructs, showing how results extend, refine, or challenge them (e.g., motivational self systems, identity, capability approach, intersectionality) (Ghanbar et al., 2024; Mahmoodi & Yousefi, 2021; Bauer et al., 2021; Boni et al., 2025).
•	Reviews and scoping studies identify gaps in existing theories and propose integrative or revised conceptual models (e.g., for green innovation, women’s financial well being, CDST in language learning, multi system innovation) (Gonçalves et al., 2021; Oduro et al., 2021; Hiver et al., 2021; Bakhuis et al., 2024).
•	Introductions and discussions explicitly frame how the work “adds to theory” (new concepts, mechanisms, or typologies), often using a dedicated subsection or paragraph (Tu et al., 2025; Bispo, 2022; Figueredo et al., 2022).
2. Methodological contribution
•	Many Q1 articles highlight novel designs, measures, or analytic strategies (e.g., new coding schemes, multiscale GWR, stochastic frontier models, dynamic systems methods, improved saturation practice) as standalone contributions and as enablers of the theoretical insight (Ghanbar et al., 2024; Daher, 2023; Hiver et al., 2021; Bakhuis et al., 2024).
•	Systematic reviews stress methodological trends and deficiencies (e.g., over reliance on simple regressions in intersectionality research, dominance of surveys, lack of effect sizes) and translate these into concrete methodological recommendations (Gonçalves et al., 2021; Oduro et al., 2021; Mahmoodi & Yousefi, 2021; Bauer et al., 2021; Figueredo et al., 2022).
•	Editorial or didactic pieces explicitly classify work by type of contribution and argue that rigorous methodological innovation and clarity are prerequisites for credible theory and practice contributions (Bispo, 2022; Hiver et al., 2021).
3. Practical / societal contribution
•	Discussions typically map findings onto policy, professional practice, or design: e.g., tailoring financial education for women, guiding green innovation strategies, urban big data planning, leadership for creativity or digital transformation (Gonçalves et al., 2021; Oduro et al., 2021; Tu et al., 2025; Figueredo et al., 2022; Grabocka & Ndoka, 2025; Gustiawan et al., 2025).
•	Reviews synthesize evidence into frameworks or roadmaps that practitioners can apply (e.g., knowledge maps for COVID 19 tourism impacts, SME digital transformation models) (Figueredo et al., 2022; Gustiawan et al., 2025).
4. Integrating all three
•	High impact papers often close discussions by explicitly linking the three levels: a new or refined theory, enabled by particular methodological choices, leads to specific, context sensitive recommendations for practice and future research (Gonçalves et al., 2021; Oduro et al., 2021; Bauer et al., 2021; Figueredo et al., 2022; Hiver et al., 2021).
Conclusion
In Q1 journals, simultaneous articulation usually follows a clear logic: define what is new for theory, show how the study’s design or methods make that advance possible, and translate both into concrete implications for policy, practice, or further empirical work.

5.4 Transferability to Similar 33/11 kV Substations
•	What factors influence whether a proxy-based risk-screening framework can be transferred to other 33/11 kV substations?
Transferability of proxy-based risk screening in 33/11 kV substations
Whether a proxy-based framework can be safely transferred to other 33/11 kV substations depends on how closely the new sites match the original in network role, asset population, operating environment, and data quality.
Key factors determining transferability
1. System function and network criticality
•	Substations differ in function (load vs switching/terminal), bus arrangement, number of circuits/transformers, redundancy, and loading; these strongly affect criticality and risk patterns (Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021).
•	A proxy set calibrated on lightly loaded, radial, rural substations will not directly apply to heavily loaded urban nodes, breaker and a half schemes, or highly meshed areas (Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021).
2. Asset mix, age, and condition data
•	Transfer is more defensible when equipment types, ratings, technologies (e.g., GIS vs AIS), and age distributions are similar, and when condition/health indices are built from comparable tests and diagnostics (Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021).
•	Differences in maintenance practice, repair quality, and monitoring capabilities (e.g., absence of real time data) change failure patterns and weaken proxy validity (Itua et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2025).
3. Operating and environmental context
•	Loading level, climate, pollution, and surrounding land use (rural vs industrial/tourist/urban) materially influence failure rates, safety consequences, and public impact (Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021).
•	Cyber–physical exposure (communication architecture, DG penetration, cyber attack surfaces) also alters risk structure for modern active networks (Boakye-Boateng et al., 2023; Ouyang et al., 2025).
4. Data quality, expert judgment, and calibration
•	Frameworks that rely on expert scoring or fuzzy/multicriteria aggregation require re eliciting or at least checking weights and thresholds with local experts before reuse (Maia et al., 2021).
•	Demonstrating that proxy scores correlate with observed SAIFI/SAIDI or incident statistics in new substations strengthens claims of transferability (Itua et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025).
Illustrative factors for transfer assessment
Dimension	Example questions for a new 33/11 kV substation	Sources
Network role	Same bus scheme, redundancy, loading band?	(Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021)
Assets	Similar breaker/transformer technologies and ages?	(Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Wang et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021)
Context	Comparable climate, pollution, urbanisation, safety context?	(Kumpalavalee et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021)
Cyber/DER	Similar cyber architecture, DG/control schemes?	(Boakye-Boateng et al., 2023; Ouyang et al., 2025)
Data & experts	Local failure data and experts available to reweight proxies?	(Itua et al., 2025; Kazim et al., 2025; Maia et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Dimensions that shape framework transferability
Conclusion
Transferability is highest when other 33/11 kV substations share similar topology, loading, equipment, environment, and cyber/operational context, and when proxy weights are recalibrated or validated against local data and expert judgment rather than assumed universal.

•	How have previous power-system analytics studies argued for transferability from single-case studies to similar networks?
Power-system analytics studies typically justify transferability by aligning physics, structure, and data between the test case and target networks.
How single-case studies argue “this will work elsewhere”
1. Physics- and structure-based reasoning
•	Studies using graph or GNN models argue that methods generalize because they encode network physics and topology, not case specific features; the same relational inductive bias is expected to hold on other grids of different size/configuration (Lopez-Garcia & Domínguez-Navarro, 2023; Solheim et al., 2024; Kaseb et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2025).
•	Cascading-failure and resilience work on benchmark and real grids claim transferability by showing that vitality/centrality metrics track vulnerability in multiple systems, implying that the concepts (not just numbers) are reusable (Amani & Jalili, 2021; Stanković et al., 2023).
2. Cross-system experiments and benchmarks
•	Many methods are first validated on standard IEEE test systems (e.g., RTS 96, RBTS, 4 bus/33 bus, multiple IEEE feeders) and then applied to a single “real” or larger grid; similar performance across these cases is used as evidence of transferability to other, comparable networks (Lopez-Garcia & Domínguez-Navarro, 2023; Beyza & Yusta, 2021; Sharma & Karki, 2025; Ali et al., 2022; Petridis et al., 2025; Jain & Verma, 2024; Kaseb et al., 2023; Zhang et al., 2025; Bhusal et al., 2021).
•	Transfer-learning studies explicitly train on one system and test on others (e.g., PV plants in the same area, multiple feeders, different solar plants), showing accuracy gains over from scratch models; this is presented as proof that learned representations capture general patterns (irradiance–power, fault signatures) rather than site idiosyncrasies (Miraftabzadeh et al., 2023; Sarmas et al., 2022; Ali et al., 2025; Petridis et al., 2025).
3. Similarity and domain-knowledge arguments
•	Authors often restrict claimed transferability to networks with similar operating regimes, climate, and technology mix, or where nodal load/renewable patterns are known to be correlated, arguing that under these conditions scenario reduction or risk models remain valid (Beyza & Yusta, 2021; Sharma & Karki, 2025; Mu et al., 2022; Jain & Verma, 2024; Draz et al., 2024).
•	Data knowledge fusion approaches argue generalization when domain constraints (e.g., frequency response limits) are enforced, and then demonstrate high accuracy in untrained operating scenarios (e.g., different renewable penetrations) within the same regional grid (Zhang et al., 2025).
4. Acknowledged limits
•	Several works explicitly note that large topology changes, different protection schemes, or very different generation mixes would require retraining or recalibration, limiting transfer to “similar but not arbitrary” systems (Beyza & Yusta, 2021; Jain & Verma, 2024; Kaseb et al., 2023; Bhusal et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Across power-system analytics, transferability from a single (or few) case studies is mainly argued by (i) embedding general physics/topology, (ii) demonstrating performance on multiple test networks, and (iii) bounding claims to grids with similar structural and operational characteristics, while acknowledging retraining needs when these assumptions break.

•	What limitations and boundary conditions should be stated when claiming broader applicability?
Claims of broader applicability should be tightly bounded by system similarity, data limits, modeling assumptions, and tested scenario space.
Key limitations to state explicitly
1. System and topology similarity
•	Specify that results were derived on a particular voltage level, topology class, and DER penetration (e.g., radial distribution with moderate DER) and may not hold for meshed, heavily DER dominated, or very different grids (Sun et al., 2025; Barrett et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023).
•	Note any enforced structural constraints (e.g., thermal limits, voltage bounds, GIS-consistency constraints) that assume “well behaved” power flows and valid asset records (Sun et al., 2025; Roald et al., 2023).
2. Data quality, observability, and cyber context
•	Clarify observability conditions: completeness of SCADA/AMI/PMU data, degree of missing/misaligned GIS/asset metadata, and whether performance degrades outside the tested sparsity levels (Sun et al., 2025).
•	For cyber risk or trust frameworks, restrict transferability to similar protocol stacks, traffic patterns, and attack models, and acknowledge that unique substation environments and limited testbeds leave complex or unseen attacks underexplored (Zhao et al., 2022; Boakye-Boateng et al., 2023).
3. Uncertainty modeling and scenario coverage
•	State how uncertainty was represented (scenario sets, distributions, robust bounds) and that conclusions are conditioned on that choice and on the sampled range of operating conditions/extreme events (Sun et al., 2025; Barrett et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023).
•	Discuss computational tractability trade offs (reduced number of scenarios, simplified power flow models) and how they may bias risk estimates or omit rare but critical events (Sun et al., 2025; Barrett et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023).
4. Validation scope
•	Distinguish between validation on synthetic vs. real systems, single vs. multiple test networks, and short vs. long time horizons, and caution against extrapolation beyond these regimes (Sun et al., 2025; Barrett et al., 2023; Boakye-Boateng et al., 2023).
Conclusion
When claiming broader applicability, boundary conditions should transparently cover where the model was tested, how the system, data, uncertainty, and cyber/physical context may differ elsewhere, and under what deviations retraining, recalibration, or revalidation becomes necessary.
References

5.5 Limitations of the Reduced-Order Screening Framework
•	What are the standard limitations of reduced-order or proxy-based screening frameworks in power-system risk studies?
Core limitations of reduced-order / proxy screening in power system risk
Reduced-order and proxy-based frameworks greatly accelerate risk studies, but they introduce structural, statistical, and decision relevance limits that can bias risk estimates.
1. Structural/modeling limitations
•	Loss of physics and state detail: ROMs and simple data fit proxies neglect “irrelevant” states or coarsen physics; this can miss localized or rare behaviors that drive risk, especially in high dimensional network models and cascading failure studies (Bahrami et al., 2022; Yi et al., 2025; Dehghani et al., 2021).
•	Intrusiveness and code dependence: Projection based ROMs require access to and modification of underlying simulation codes; in complex or proprietary power models this is hard, constraining how faithfully dynamics can be reduced (Bahrami et al., 2022).
•	Limited extrapolation ability: Data fit surrogates (e.g., ANN, GPR) interpolate well within the training domain but are unreliable for extreme events or new operating regimes, which are precisely what risk studies target (Bahrami et al., 2022; Marrel & Iooss, 2024; Wang et al., 2022; Angione et al., 2022).
2. Statistical and validation issues
•	High dimensional input challenges: Many network level risk problems mix continuous and discrete variables; standard surrogate techniques struggle with dimensionality and mixed inputs, leading to poor failure probability estimates without adaptive sampling (Wang et al., 2022; Dehghani et al., 2021; Viana et al., 2021).
•	Overreliance on global accuracy metrics: Proxies can show good RMSE/R² while misrepresenting the tails relevant for optimization and risk (e.g., different optima or wrong reliability indices), highlighting the gap between surrogate “fit” and decision quality (Bahrami et al., 2022; Abdollahfard et al., 2025).
•	Uncertain error quantification: While GP-based surrogates provide predictive distributions, covariance estimation and validation for rare-event reliability remain delicate, and mis specified uncertainty leads to over  or under confident risk bounds (Marrel & Iooss, 2024; Qin et al., 2023).
3. Operational and interpretability constraints
•	Data requirements and quality: Many proxy frameworks assume abundant, clean simulation or historical data; in practice data scarcity, bias, and changing system conditions limit generalization and robustness (Wang et al., 2022; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Zhou & Li, 2025; Huang et al., 2025).
•	Limited interpretability: Black box surrogates used for risk screening (e.g., deep nets, boosted trees) can hinder understanding of causal drivers of risk and make regulatory acceptance difficult in safety critical power applications (Wang et al., 2022; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Zhou & Li, 2025; Huang et al., 2025).
Conclusion
In power system risk studies, reduced order and proxy-based screening is mainly limited by loss of critical physics, weak tail extrapolation, challenging uncertainty validation, and data/interpretability constraints, all of which can distort conclusions about low probability, high impact events.
References

•	How should the absence of continuous harmonic measurements or full EMT simulation be discussed without undermining the contribution?
Absence of continuous measurements or full EMT: how to frame it
It is common in harmonic and stability studies that continuous field monitoring and full EMT simulation are not feasible, mainly due to data, time, and computational constraints (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Subedi et al., 2021; Sidwall & Forsyth, 2022; Xiong et al., 2024; Lara et al., 2023). This can be discussed transparently without undermining the work by emphasizing scope, feasibility, and validation.
1. Position these as reasonable, standard trade offs
•	EMT simulations are recognized as the most accurate but also computationally intensive, and thus not routinely used for all planning/operational studies (Subedi et al., 2021; Sidwall & Forsyth, 2022; Xiong et al., 2024; Lara et al., 2023).
•	Best practice reviews explicitly note that RMS/phasor or simplified EMT models are acceptable when the study focuses on specific bandwidths or phenomena, provided the modeling choice is justified against the question being asked (Sidwall & Forsyth, 2022; Lacerda et al., 2021; Lara et al., 2023).
•	Harmonic studies often rely on snapshot or campaign-based measurements instead of continuous monitoring, and use indices or aggregated models to characterize distortion because wide area continuous monitoring is rarely available (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021).
Suggested rhetorical strategy
Element	How to phrase it	Citations
Justify scope	“Full EMT and continuous monitoring are standard only when high frequency or switching level phenomena are the primary focus; here the objective is X, which lies in Y frequency range, for which method Z is widely used.”	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Subedi et al., 2021; Sidwall & Forsyth, 2022; Lacerda et al., 2021; Lara et al., 2023)
Acknowledge limitation	“The absence of continuous recordings and full EMT means rare or fast transients outside the studied cases may not be captured.”	(Subedi et al., 2021; Sidwall & Forsyth, 2022; Xiong et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Guerreiro et al., 2025)
Mitigation/validation	“Model assumptions were checked by [modal analysis/sensitivity tests/limited EMT cases/comparisons to typical THD patterns or standards], which is consistent with current practice.”	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Xiong et al., 2024; Lokar et al., 2021; Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Yoon et al., 2022; Guerreiro et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Framing standard modeling and data trade-offs
2. Turn the gap into a clear future work path
•	Note that ongoing work in accelerated EMT, co simulation, and real time simulation aims specifically to make broader EMT usage practical (Subedi et al., 2021; Sidwall & Forsyth, 2022; Xiong et al., 2024; Qiao et al., 2023; Lara et al., 2023).
•	For harmonics, emphasize that emerging AI and improved distortion indices try to compensate for limited measurements and imperfect instruments (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021).
Conclusion
Present the lack of continuous harmonics data and full EMT not as flaws, but as standard, acknowledged constraints. Clearly delimit your study’s validity range, show that methods align with accepted practice, describe how you partially validated results, and state that broader monitoring or EMT analysis is an important direction for future refinement rather than a prerequisite for your current contribution.

•	What methodological limitations are most important to acknowledge to satisfy rigorous peer review?
Key methodological limitations to acknowledge for rigorous peer review
To satisfy demanding reviewers, the most important limitations are those that affect validity, generalizability, and operational relevance of your risk results.
1. Data and evidence limitations
•	Data scarcity / subjectivity: Many power risk studies rely on expert judgment or limited incident data, which introduces subjectivity and constrains quantitative inference (Wang et al., 2024; Li et al., 2025; Liang et al., 2025).
•	Simulated vs. real world data: Heavy reliance on simulations or custom datasets, without field validation or benchmark sets, limits external validity and reproducibility (Maican et al., 2025; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Li et al., 2025).
•	Measurement and uncertainty handling: Idealized or noise free inputs, no missing data treatment, and weak uncertainty modeling are recurring weaknesses in diagnostic and ML based risk work (Maican et al., 2025; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Navarro et al., 2021).
2. Modeling and methodological simplifications
•	Reduced physics / proxy models: Reduced order and proxy frameworks trade fidelity for tractability; they may miss nonlinear interactions, cascading effects, or rare extremes, affecting tail risk estimates (Chen et al., 2023; Du et al., 2021; Duddeti et al., 2025; Zhou & Beerten, 2023; Bahrami et al., 2022; Liang et al., 2025).
•	Structural assumptions: Simplified hazard, fragility, or damage models (e.g., binary states, independence of failures, coarse hazard description) can bias system level risk assessments (Barrett et al., 2023; Liang et al., 2025).
•	Algorithmic choices: Metaheuristics, ML, and fuzzy/Bayesian models often depend on ad hoc hyperparameters, priors, or search spaces; reviewers expect acknowledgment that results may change under alternative choices (Duddeti et al., 2025; Zhou & Beerten, 2023; Li et al., 2025; Bahrami et al., 2022).
Example: Common limitations in diagnostic / ML studies
Limitation type	Typical issue	Citations
Overfitting / sample size	Too few events per predictor, no overfitting check	(Maican et al., 2025; Navarro et al., 2021)
Lack of external validation	Only internal cross validation or single test system	(Chen et al., 2023; Maican et al., 2025; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Navarro et al., 2021)
Interpretability	Black box models without explanation or sensitivity analysis	(Maican et al., 2025; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Zhou & Beerten, 2023; Bahrami et al., 2022)
Figure 1: Methodological weaknesses frequently flagged in reviews
3. Scope, generalizability, and implementation gaps
•	Single case or test system focus: Results on one network, region, or plant type may not transfer to other topologies, climates, or regulatory contexts (Aniebonam et al., 2025; Hovorov et al., 2025; Barrett et al., 2023; Tang et al., 2024; Koval et al., 2025; Liang et al., 2025).
•	Partial pipeline coverage: Many studies stop at detection/screening or risk quantification and do not model localization, control actions, or economic/operational feasibility, limiting implementation relevance (Aniebonam et al., 2025; Maican et al., 2025; Li et al., 2025).
Conclusion
For rigorous peer review, explicitly acknowledge: (i) limits of data and uncertainty treatment, (ii) key modeling and algorithmic simplifications (especially reduced order/proxy choices), and (iii) constraints on generalizability and practical deployment. Link each limitation to its likely effect on risk estimates, and state how you partially mitigate it (validation, sensitivity analysis) and how future work can relax it.

5.6 Implications for Future Harmonic Monitoring and Modelling Research
•	What future research directions are suggested in the literature for integrating operational analytics with harmonic monitoring?
Future work should connect harmonic monitoring with real time, data driven operational analytics, moving from offline diagnosis to predictive and autonomous control.
Key Future Research Directions
1. From standalone monitors to system wide, data driven platforms
•	Develop holistic PQ frameworks that fuse synchronized waveform/harmonic data with SCADA, EMS/DMS and asset data for system level situational awareness and decision support (Shao et al., 2023; Zahid et al., 2025; Alam et al., 2025).
•	Standardize communication, data models, and storage for high resolution harmonic data to support long horizon analytics and fleet wide learning (Shao et al., 2023; Serdenko et al., 2025).
2. Advanced AI/ML for predictive and prescriptive operational analytics
•	Use deep learning and hybrid architectures (CNN RNN/LSTM, transformers) for forecasting THD and harmonic events under varying operating regimes, not just classifying past disturbances (Dehaghani et al., 2025; Eslami et al., 2022; Nishad et al., 2025; Hoffmann et al., 2022; Panoiu et al., 2025; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
•	Integrate reinforcement learning and DRL so controllers (inverters, filters, FACTS, converters) respond to predicted harmonic states rather than thresholds, optimizing cost–PQ trade offs in real time (Gautam et al., 2025; Nishad et al., 2025; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025).
3. Closing the loop: monitoring driven control and optimization
•	Co design monitoring + control, e.g., harmonics aware OPF, converter dispatch, and active/hybrid filter coordination using continuous harmonic indicators as operational constraints (Dehaghani et al., 2025; Gautam et al., 2025; Nishad et al., 2025; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Stopkin et al., 2025).
•	Explore edge/IoT based implementations where local devices run lightweight analytics on harmonic data for fast, hierarchical control actions in microgrids, PV plants, and industrial systems (Rashmi, 2025; Meenakshi et al., 2023; Hu et al., 2023; Aiman et al., 2025).
4. Robustness, explainability, and standards
•	Address data quality, imbalance, and cybersecurity for harmonic data streams and AI models used in operations (Dehaghani et al., 2025; Meenakshi et al., 2023; Nishad et al., 2025; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Alam et al., 2025).
•	Develop interpretable analytics and operator friendly indicators linking harmonic patterns to actionable grid decisions (Zahid et al., 2025; Hoffmann et al., 2022; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
Example Research Themes
Theme	Example question	Sources
Predictive harmonics OPF	How to include forecast THD/individual harmonics in real time dispatch?	(Dehaghani et al., 2025; Gautam et al., 2025; Zahid et al., 2025; Hoffmann et al., 2022; Panoiu et al., 2025)
Edge analytics	What tasks should run at sensor/IED vs control center?	(Rashmi, 2025; Meenakshi et al., 2023; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Hu et al., 2023; Aiman et al., 2025)
DRL for PQ	Can DRL use harmonic states to co optimize PQ and cost?	(Gautam et al., 2025; Nishad et al., 2025; Chinthaginjala et al., 2025; Stopkin et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Main research avenues linking harmonics and operations.
Conclusion
The literature points toward integrating high resolution harmonic monitoring with AI based forecasting, optimization, and control across centralized and edge platforms, with strong needs in standards, data/ML robustness, and interpretable, operator oriented analytics.
