•	How could future studies extend proxy-based screening toward richer measurement environments, adaptive models, or real-time implementation?
Proxy-based screening can be extended by adding multimodal data, online error learning, and ML-accelerated control so it works in richer, adaptive, real time settings.
Richer Measurement Environments
•	Use multimodal sensing (time- and frequency-domain, different device classes, topology, weather) so proxies capture diverse system states rather than a single channel (Chen et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Singh et al., 2025; He et al., 2025).
•	Explicitly model heterogeneous and time-varying measurement errors, learning their distributions from SCADA/PMU chains instead of assuming fixed Gaussian noise (Cheng & Lin, 2024).
•	Embed proxy screening inside digital twins and real-time simulators, where high-fidelity twins provide labels or confidence measures for proxy performance across scenarios (Arraño-Vargas & Konstantinou, 2023).
Example directions for richer measurement design
Direction	Concrete idea	Citations
Multimodal proxies	Combine waveform, spectral, topology, and context data in proxy scores	(Chen et al., 2025; Zhang et al., 2025; Singh et al., 2025; He et al., 2025)
Error-aware proxies	Learn measurement error statistics online and fold into screening weights	(Cheng & Lin, 2024)
Twin-based validation	Use digital twin to stress test and recalibrate proxy rules	(Arraño-Vargas & Konstantinou, 2023)
Figure 1: Paths to richer, error-aware proxy environments.
Adaptive Proxy Models
•	Adopt adaptive state estimation / observers that update proxy–truth relationships as measurement statistics drift, rather than fixed screening thresholds (Cheng & Lin, 2024; Yu et al., 2025).
•	Borrow latent-variable proxy theory: quantify proxy quality (e.g., efficiency-like metrics) and adaptively decide when to trust or ignore specific proxies, or reweight them via maximum-likelihood refinement (Deutsch & Katsevich, 2025).
•	Use federated and hierarchical learning to adapt proxy models across heterogeneous sites while respecting local differences (Luo et al., 2025; Rojek et al., 2025; Recalde et al., 2024).
Real-Time Implementation
•	Combine proxy screening with real-time MPC or adaptive control, where proxies pre-filter scenarios or states to keep optimization feasible on PLCs and embedded controllers (Chen et al., 2023; Hossain & Kumar, 2023; Saxena et al., 2024).
•	Apply ML-accelerated controllers and edge computing so proxy evaluation and model updating can run at millisecond–second scales in emergency or high-speed applications (Chen et al., 2023; Singh et al., 2025; Sengeni et al., 2024).
•	Design lightweight surrogate or proxy models (reduced-order, distilled NN surrogates) specifically for low-latency environments (Chen et al., 2023; Luo et al., 2025; Hossain & Kumar, 2023; Tian et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Future work can move proxy-based screening from static, single-source setups to multimodal, error-aware measurement environments, with online-adaptive weighting and calibration, and ML/MPC-accelerated real-time implementations deployed on edge and control hardware.

•	What unresolved research questions remain in harmonic resonance screening for medium-voltage substations?
Key open questions in harmonic resonance screening for MV substations
Harmonic resonance mechanisms are well understood, but there is no mature, standardized way to screen medium voltage (MV) substations for resonance risk under realistic, time varying, converter rich conditions.
1. Modeling & prediction gaps
•	Aggregated converter rich loads: Traditional fixed-spectrum harmonic models struggle with the strongly time varying, coupled harmonics from aggregated MV loads; probabilistic or ML approaches (e.g., adaptive multi task GPR) are only starting to be explored and not yet integrated into routine screening workflows (Zheng et al., 2025; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
•	Supraharmonic range (2–150 kHz): MV resonance studies show many feeder dependent resonant points and time varying supraharmonic emissions, but general design/screening rules for large MV networks are missing (Letha et al., 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
•	Wideband measurement constraints: Instrument transformers are poorly characterized above 2–9 kHz, so the accuracy of any screening based on measured impedance/voltage in this band is uncertain, and suitable calibration methods are still under development (Dewayalage et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Key open issues by frequency band
Aspect	0–2 kHz (classical)	2–150 kHz (supraharmonics)	Citations
Load models	Need stochastic/ML models for MV substations	Almost no validated MV models	(Zheng et al., 2025; Letha et al., 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024; Taghvaie et al., 2023)
Network models	Modal analysis exists but not automated for many states	Resonance structure of large MV cable networks not generalized	(Letha et al., 2021; Lokar et al., 2021)
Measurement	Standard PQ meters adequate	IT accuracy, calibration, algorithms unresolved	(Dewayalage et al., 2024; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024)
Figure 1: Frequency-dependent gaps in MV resonance screening
2. Screening, monitoring & diagnosis
•	Multi state screening: Harmonic resonance mode analysis and PQ meter placement methods start to consider load variation and topology changes, but scalable approaches tailored to real MV distribution/substation environments are lacking (Ansari et al., 2025; Lokar et al., 2021).
•	Real time resonance detection: Methods based on spectra/impedance trajectories exist in rail and MV networks, yet robust criteria to discriminate genuine infrastructure resonance from strong emission sources, at MV buses and in the supraharmonic range, remain open research problems (Mariscotti & Sandrolini, 2021; Letha et al., 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
•	Data infrastructures: Novel fast PQ data collection concepts for MV grids (space vector methods, IoT style nodes) are promising, but how to turn such data into automated resonance screening and alarms is unresolved (Kryltcov et al., 2021; Taghvaie et al., 2023).
3. Prevention, standards & risk metrics
•	Design time “resonance robust” criteria: Case studies show cable related and supraharmonic resonances leading to MV cable termination failures, yet general planning indices, safety margins, and screening limits for MV substations are not standardized (Letha et al., 2021; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
•	Risk based indicators: New indices for resonance severity, cable failure risk, and monitoring observability exist, but validation across diverse MV grids and their incorporation into PQ standards and grid codes remain open (Letha et al., 2021; Ansari et al., 2025; Mariscotti & Mingotti, 2024).
Conclusion
Unresolved work centers on realistic harmonic/supraharmonic modeling of MV substations, scalable multi state screening and real time detection, and translating these into robust risk metrics and planning/operational standards.
References

Mariscotti, A., & Sandrolini, L. (2021). Detection of Harmonic Overvoltage and Resonance in AC Railways using Measured Pantograph Electrical Quantities. Energies. https://doi.org/10.20944/preprints202107.0345.v1

IEEE Access, 11, 31417-31442. https://doi.org/10.1109/access.2023.3260768

6. Conclusion
6.1 Summary of Study Objectives and Findings
•	What is the most effective way to synthesize objectives and principal findings in conclusions of Q1 engineering papers?
Effective Q1 conclusions tightly restate the objective and then foreground a small set of clearly linked, novel findings.
Structural patterns in strong conclusions
Genre analyses of stand alone conclusion sections across high impact journals identify a dominant three move pattern: (1) summarize the study, (2) state contributions/implications, (3) point to future work/limitations (Kanoksilapatham, 2023). Within Move 1, restating objectives and principal findings together is the most frequent and obligatory element, especially “restating findings” directly tied to the stated aims (Kanoksilapatham, 2023).
Guides to research writing similarly emphasize that conclusions should:
•	Answer the initial research questions/objectives explicitly,
•	Highlight the distinct findings vs. previous work,
•	Explain how these findings advance knowledge in the field, and
•	Briefly note implications, strengths, limitations, and future directions (Budhathoki, 2023; Sun et al., 2021).
Practical synthesis pattern
A concise Q1 style paragraph often follows this logic:
1.	Objective anchor: “This work aimed to [core objective].” (Budhathoki, 2023; Kanoksilapatham, 2023)2. Linked principal findings (2–4 items): “We showed that (i) …, (ii) …, and (iii) …, thereby addressing [objective].” Each clause should map back to the objective rather than listing results in isolation (Budhathoki, 2023; Sun et al., 2021; Kanoksilapatham, 2023).
2.	Value statement: “These findings provide [type of contribution: method/framework/evidence] for [domain/task].” (Escudero-Mancebo et al., 2023; Budhathoki, 2023)4. Scoped implications and future work: One or two sentences on practical/theoretical implications and targeted future research, not generic “more research is needed” (Yusuf et al., 2025; Budhathoki, 2023).
Typical move structure in high impact conclusions
Move	Function in synthesis	Citations
1. Summarize study	Restate aim + key findings as a unified answer	(Budhathoki, 2023; Kanoksilapatham, 2023)
2. State contributions	Make explicit what is new and why it matters	(Escudero-Mancebo et al., 2023; Yusuf et al., 2025; Budhathoki, 2023)
3. Look forward	Brief, specific implications and next steps	(Yusuf et al., 2025; Budhathoki, 2023; Kanoksilapatham, 2023)
Figure 1: Three-move template for concise, impactful conclusions.
Conclusion
For Q1 engineering papers, the most effective synthesis explicitly reconnects the original objective to a short, integrated set of principal findings, followed immediately by a clear statement of contribution and scoped implications, all in a compact, single coherent narrative.

Budhathoki, M. (2023). Writing Better Research Articles for Publication. Cognition. https://doi.org/10.3126/cognition.v5i1.55411
Sun, Q., Chen, T., Liu, S., Miao, J., Chen, J., Yu, H., & Yu, B. (2021). Correlated Multi-objective Multi-fidelity Optimization for HLS Directives Design. 2021 Design, Automation & Test in Europe Conference & Exhibition (DATE), 46-51. https://doi.org/10.1145/3503540
Kanoksilapatham, B. (2023). Stand-Alone Conclusion Section in Open-Access Research Articles: Organizational Structure. Journal of Language and Education. https://doi.org/10.17323/jle.2023.16907
•	How should conclusions restate the central evidence supporting the usefulness of the proposed proxy indicators?
Conclusions should briefly re anchor the study aim, then restate the key validation evidence (correlations, performance gains, validity metrics) that shows the proxy indicators are useful, while noting limits to that usefulness.
What to Restate About Evidence
When claiming usefulness of proxy indicators, Q1 level conclusions typically pull through:
•	Strength of association with the target construct/outcome 
o	E.g., report that proxies show moderate–strong correlations with gold standard measures or outcomes (e.g., r≈0.6, good AUC, correct classification rates) (López-Díaz et al., 2025; Azizi et al., 2021)or, conversely, that correlations are weak/inconsistent, limiting usefulness (Salampessy et al., 2021; Alcocer et al., 2022; Verger et al., 2021).
•	Comparative performance vs. not using the proxy 
o	Emphasize reductions in error or variance, improved robustness, or better model fit when proxies are added (e.g., 50% reduction in estimate variation vs. catch only methods; lower bias and higher precision than previous models) (Wang et al., 2025; Ashe et al., 2022; Zoltán et al., 2023; Azizi et al., 2021).
•	Breadth of validation 
o	Mention multiple lines of validity: criterion/content/discriminant/external validity and reliability (e.g., good to excellent validity and ICC≈0.88) to support the proxy’s practical applicability (López-Díaz et al., 2025; Lopez et al., 2023).
Example Structure for Restating Central Evidence
Function in conclusion	How to phrase it	Citations
Link to aim	“This study proposed [proxy X] to approximate [target Y].”	(Wang et al., 2025; López-Díaz et al., 2025; Ashe et al., 2022; Alcocer et al., 2022)
Core evidence	“Proxy X showed [strength] association with Y / improved [metric] by Z% / produced estimates within ±Δ of reference…”	(Wang et al., 2025; López-Díaz et al., 2025; Ashe et al., 2022; Zoltán et al., 2023; Alcocer et al., 2022; Verger et al., 2021; Azizi et al., 2021)
Bound usefulness	“Thus, proxy X provides a practical indicator of Y under [conditions], but [limitations: weak for some outcomes, context dependence].”	(Salampessy et al., 2021; Ashe et al., 2022; Zoltán et al., 2023; Alcocer et al., 2022; Verger et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Key elements to restate proxy validation evidence.
Conclusion
In conclusions, restate in one compact paragraph: the original purpose of the proxy, the quantitative evidence that it tracks the target (effect sizes, error reduction, validity metrics), and a scoped statement of usefulness, explicitly flagging where performance is weak or context limited.

Lopez, A., Tinella, L., Caffò, A., & Bosco, A. (2023). Measuring the reliability of proxy respondents in behavioural assessments: an open question. Aging Clinical and Experimental Research, 35, 2173 - 2190. https://doi.org/10.1007/s40520-023-02501-z
•	What balance of brevity and specificity is recommended when summarizing findings in power-systems journal conclusions?
For Q1 power-systems journals, conclusions should be very brief but highly specific, focusing on a few quantified, objective-linked findings.
Recommended brevity–specificity balance
1. Keep the conclusion compact and selective
Premier quantitative journals emphasize conciseness: only the most important findings tied to the main objective(s) should appear, not a replay of the Results section (Christensen et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024). Conclusions that succinctly synthesize key insights and contributions, rather than list details, are viewed as stronger and more impactful (Christensen et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024).
In practice, this typically means:
•	1–2 short paragraphs (or 4–7 sentences total) for full papers.
•	No re-reporting of all case studies, scenarios, or tables—only the central patterns and contributions.
2. Maintain high technical specificity where it matters
Guides for quantitative premier journals stress that the summary should move beyond vague claims and clearly indicate what improved, by how much, and under which conditions (Christensen et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024). For power-systems work, this usually includes:
•	Stating the main performance metrics and direction/magnitude of change (e.g., “reduced expected operating cost by ~8% while keeping N-1 security” rather than “improved efficiency”) (Christensen et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024).
•	Indicating the context/scope (system type, uncertainty model, test system scale) so readers know where the results apply (Alajrash et al., 2024; Roald et al., 2023).
3. Link findings tightly to aims and avoid overgeneralization
High-quality conclusions explicitly connect summarized findings back to the stated aim/hypotheses and avoid claims not directly supported by data (Christensen et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023). Overly generalized or hype-like statements are discouraged in premier venues (Christensen et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024).
Practical template
Aspect	Recommended practice in conclusions	Citations
Length	1–2 concise paragraphs, no result dumping	(Christensen et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024)
Specificity	Quantified, context-bound key metrics only	(Christensen et al., 2023; Alajrash et al., 2024; Roald et al., 2023; Lim & Koay, 2024)
Scope	Explicit limits, no broad, unsupported claims	(Christensen et al., 2023; Roald et al., 2023; Peters & Chin‐Yee, 2025; Lim & Koay, 2024)
Figure 1: Concise, metrics-focused yet scoped conclusion guidance.
Conclusion
For power-systems Q1 journals, aim for a short, highly selective conclusion that states only a few quantified, context-specific findings directly answering the paper’s objectives, while clearly signaling the limits of generalization.
References
Christensen, R., Ranstam, J., Overgaard, S., & Wagner, P. (2023). Guidelines for a structured manuscript: Statistical methods and reporting in biomedical research journals. Acta Orthopaedica, 94, 243 - 249. https://doi.org/10.2340/17453674.2023.11656

6.2 Main Methodological and Practical Contributions
•	How should a conclusion clearly distinguish methodological contributions from applied utility contributions?
Conclusions should explicitly separate “what is new in method” from “what this enables or improves in practice,” often in different sentences or subparagraphs.
Structuring the distinction
A clear way to distinguish contributions is to mirror typologies that separate methodological, technical, and applied work:
•	Methodological contribution: new designs, tools, models, or analytic procedures, or novel combinations/validations of existing ones (Bergh et al., 2022; Hiver et al., 2021; Fish et al., 2025).
•	Applied (utility) contribution: insights, decisions, or performance gains in a specific domain, context, or user community (e.g., MSMEs, public health, credit scoring, LPBF manufacturing) (Sekty & As’ari, 2023; Li & Niyomsilp, 2024; Kamimura et al., 2023; Sarkar et al., 2024; Nutbeam & Milat, 2025).
Conclusions can therefore use parallel, labeled sentences or bullets, for example:
•	“Methodologically, this study introduces X/extends Y/compares Z in ways that… (generalizable beyond this case).” (Bergh et al., 2022; Hiver et al., 2021; Cabitza & Campagner, 2021; Fish et al., 2025)- “In applied terms, the findings show that in context C, stakeholders can do A and B to achieve D… (domain-specific implications).” (Sekty & As’ari, 2023; Li & Niyomsilp, 2024; Kamimura et al., 2023; Sarkar et al., 2024; Nutbeam & Milat, 2025)### Example layout for a conclusion
Section element	Focus	Typical phrasing	Citations
“Methodological contributions” subheading	New methods, designs, tools, taxonomies	“This work contributes a new framework/algorithm/checklist…; it advances rigor, reproducibility, or evaluation of X…”	(Bergh et al., 2022; Hiver et al., 2021; Kolaski et al., 2023; Cabitza & Campagner, 2021; Fish et al., 2025)
“Practical / applied implications” subheading	Domain effects, decisions, policies	“For practitioners in domain D, results indicate that doing Y improves Z; policymakers should…”	(Sekty & As’ari, 2023; Li & Niyomsilp, 2024; Kamimura et al., 2023; Sarkar et al., 2024; Nutbeam & Milat, 2025)
Figure 1: Parallel sections for methods vs. utility.
Emphasizing the difference in scope
Methodological contributions should be framed as field-level advances (usable by other researchers, across contexts) (Bergh et al., 2022; Hiver et al., 2021; Kolaski et al., 2023; Fish et al., 2025), whereas applied utility should be bounded to the studied setting and population, with explicit context (sector, geography, data, constraints) (Sekty & As’ari, 2023; Li & Niyomsilp, 2024; Cabitza & Campagner, 2021; Kamimura et al., 2023; Sarkar et al., 2024; Nutbeam & Milat, 2025).
Conclusion
A publication-ready conclusion clearly signals: “Here is what we contributed to methods, in general,” and separately, “Here is what this means for practice, in this domain,” using headings, parallel phrasing, and explicit statements about generalizability versus context-specific utility.

•	What language is most effective for summarizing the contribution of a physics-guided, proxy-based harmonic-risk framework?
Use dual, contribution oriented language that highlights physics guidance, proxy practicality, and system level impact.
For a physics guided, proxy based harmonic risk framework, conclusions in power-systems journals are most effective when they:
1. Separate the two core contributions
•	Methodological / physics-guided:
“Methodologically, we introduce a physics guided harmonic risk framework that embeds network and device dynamics as explicit constraints in the risk metric, improving stability and physical plausibility of predictions under noisy or sparse data.”
This mirrors language in physics informed and physics integrated models that stress “physical constraints” and “fundamental laws” guiding learning or regression (Zhang et al., 2022; Lu et al., 2025; Keren et al., 2022).
•	Applied / proxy-based utility:
“Practically, we provide a proxy based risk index that can be computed from routinely available measurements, enabling earlier and more reliable screening of harmonic stress at system scale.”
Proxy language is strongest when tied to observability (“limited cases,” “sparse data”) and decision support (“prioritization,” “screening,” “planning”) as in physics-informed risk and Bayesian risk factor models (Meng et al., 2022; Lu et al., 2025).
Example phrasing patterns
Role of language	Recommended pattern	Citations
Physics guidance	“physics informed / physics guided constraints ensure consistency with governing equations and improve robustness to noise and extrapolation”	(Zhang et al., 2022; Lu et al., 2025; Keren et al., 2022)
Proxy design	“proxy metric built from operationally measurable quantities while approximating non measurable harmonic stresses”	(Meng et al., 2022; Lu et al., 2025)
Risk framing	“integrated risk index combining probability and impact for prioritization of critical components or locations”	(Meng et al., 2022; Lu et al., 2025)
Figure 1: Phrasing axes for method vs. utility.
Conclusion
The most publication appropriate summary language clearly distinguishes: (i) a physics guided methodological advance (embedding grid physics into the risk formulation), and (ii) a proxy, system level risk index that is computable in practice and improves prioritization or early warning.
References
Zhang, P., Yin, Z., & Sheil, B. (2022). A physics-informed data-driven approach for consolidation analysis. Géotechnique. https://doi.org/10.1680/jgeot.22.00046
Meng, H., An, X., & Xing, J. (2022). A data-driven Bayesian network model integrating physical knowledge for prioritization of risk influencing factors. Process Safety and Environmental Protection. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.psep.2022.02.010
Lu, T., Kuang, G., Xu, D., Xu, S., Luo, Y., Wang, Q., Shang, S., Zhou, Q., & Nie, B. (2025). A physics-informed attention model for integrated driving risk assessment.. Accident; analysis and prevention, 223, 108266. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.aap.2025.108266
Keren, L., Liberzon, A., & Lazebnik, T. (2022). A computational framework for physics-informed symbolic regression with straightforward integration of domain knowledge. Scientific Reports, 13. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-023-28328-2
•	How do Q1 papers typically conclude with claims that are strong but not overstated?
Strong Q1 conclusions use precise, hedged language that matches the evidence and design, avoiding causal or absolute claims the data cannot support.
Core Features of “Strong but Not Overstated” Conclusions
1. Align claims with design and statistics
•	Observational studies should avoid unqualified causal verbs (“causes,” “leads to”) and instead use association language (“is associated with,” “was linked to”) unless assumptions for causality are justified (Parra et al., 2021).
•	Reviews show many papers drift from associative language in aims to causal language in conclusions, or recommend action as if causality were proven; this inconsistency is a red flag Q1 journals try to avoid (Parra et al., 2021).
2. Explicitly acknowledge uncertainty
•	High impact RCTs often overstate “no effect” when results are simply non significant; better practice is wording like “results are consistent with no effect to a small benefit/harm,” which reflects confidence intervals and sample size (Hemming et al., 2022).
•	Concise phrases that recognize imprecision (e.g., “do not rule out a clinically important effect”) convey caution without weakening the contribution (Hemming et al., 2022).
3. Use hedging / cautious language, not absolutism
•	Analyses of Q1 articles show frequent use of hedging verbs and modifiers (“may,” “could,” “appears to,” “generally,” “seems”) especially in conclusions and limitations (Jumanto et al., 2024).
•	Hedging is framed as a mark of rigor and as part of expert academic writing, not as weakness (Jumanto et al., 2024).
Typical Effective Patterns in Q1 Conclusions
Goal	Effective pattern	Citations
Strong claim	“Our findings provide evidence that X is associated with Y …”	(Lim & Koay, 2024; Susanto & Masli, 2025; Parra et al., 2021)
Guard scope	“… in the context of Z / this sample / this setting.”	(Lim & Koay, 2024; Paré et al., 2023; Susanto & Masli, 2025)
Add uncertainty	“… and should be interpreted in light of [limitations/CI/possible confounding].”	(Hemming et al., 2022; Jumanto et al., 2024; Parra et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Common linguistic patterns in strong, cautious conclusions.
Conclusion
Q1 papers typically conclude with firm, positive wording about what the study shows, but they: (i) match causal strength to design, (ii) signal uncertainty and limitations, and (iii) rely on calibrated hedging rather than absolute, universal claims.
References
Hemming, K., Javid, I., & Taljaard, M. (2022). A review of high impact journals found that misinterpretation of non-statistically significant results from randomised trials was common.. Journal of clinical epidemiology. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jclinepi.2022.01.014
Lim, W., & Koay, K. (2024). So you want to publish in a premier journal? An illustrative guide on how to develop and write a quantitative research paper for premier journals. Global Business and Organizational Excellence. https://doi.org/10.1002/joe.22252
Jumanto, J., Waluyo, D., Purwatiningsih, A., Andono, P., Nugroho, R., Ramayanti, I., & Minghat, A. (2024). IMRAD, synthesis, and hedging within expert academic writing to encourage a world discussion platform. Studies in English Language and Education. https://doi.org/10.24815/siele.v11i3.34776
Paré, G., Wagner, G., & Prester, J. (2023). How to develop and frame impactful review articles: key recommendations. Journal of Decision Systems, 33, 566 - 582. https://doi.org/10.1080/12460125.2023.2197701
Susanto, D., & Masli, A. (2025). FROM IDEA TO PUBLICATION: CONSTRUCTING A QUALITY RESEARCH PAPER. Jurnal Akuntansi dan Manajemen. https://doi.org/10.53916/jam.v36i1.156
Parra, O., Bertizzolo, L., Schroter, S., Dechartres, A., & Goetghebeur, E. (2021). Consistency of causal claims in observational studies: a review of papers published in a general medical journal. BMJ Open, 11. https://doi.org/10.1136/bmjopen-2020-043339

6.3 Concluding Recommendations
•	What recommendations are appropriate for utilities, researchers, and policymakers based on a harmonic-risk screening study?
Harmonic risk screening studies generally support coordinated, risk based actions by utilities, researchers, and policymakers to keep distortion within standards while enabling modern loads and DER.
Recommendations for Utilities
•	Institutionalize monitoring and screening
o	Deploy Class A PQ analyzers at key LV/MV nodes and major nonlinear/DER customers; track THD, individual orders, unbalance, sags, and resonance indicators (Adesina et al., 2025; Wanjekeche et al., 2025; Michalec et al., 2021; Verovkin, 2025).
o	Benchmark against IEEE 519 and IEC 61000 4 7 limits and set internal alarm thresholds below formal limits (Adesina et al., 2025; Abbas et al., 2021; Verovkin, 2025).
•	Targeted mitigation and planning
o	Prioritize feeders/substations with high THD, repeated sags, or known resonance risk for filters (single tuned, blocking filters, UPQC), network reconfiguration, or volt/VAR scheme adjustments (Wanjekeche et al., 2025; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Aldosary et al., 2025; Abbas et al., 2021; Al-Sharif et al., 2022; Gutiérrez & Téllez, 2025; Park et al., 2021).
o	Include harmonic losses and thermal impacts in transformer/feeder sizing and asset management models (Tavakoli et al., 2024).
o	Use contracts and tariffs (harmonic limits, penalties, PF incentives) to control large harmonic producers (Wanjekeche et al., 2025; Verovkin, 2025).
Typical Action Priorities from Screening
Stakeholder	Priority actions derived from screening	Citations
Utilities	Monitor, rank high risk nodes, apply filters/controls, revise tariffs	(Adesina et al., 2025; Wanjekeche et al., 2025; Lowczowski et al., 2024; Abbas et al., 2021; Verovkin, 2025; Tavakoli et al., 2024)
Researchers	Improve models, AI based detection, realistic test systems	(Taghvaie et al., 2023; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Kaur & Bath, 2025; Dehaghani et al., 2025; Assaffat et al., 2025)
Policymakers	Update standards, require reporting, support pilots	(Abbas et al., 2021; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Kaur & Bath, 2025; Razmi et al., 2023; Tavakoli et al., 2024)
Figure 1: Roles in responding to screened harmonic risk.
Recommendations for Researchers
•	Develop validated harmonic risk metrics and THD indices that distinguish harmonics, interharmonics, and supra harmonics for modern inverter rich grids (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021).
•	Move beyond pure simulation: combine field data, harmonic load flow, and hardware in the loop/on site validation of filters, control schemes, and AI based detectors (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Aldosary et al., 2025; Kaur & Bath, 2025; Dehaghani et al., 2025; Kotsonias et al., 2025; Assaffat et al., 2025).
•	Integrate harmonics into hosting capacity, DER placement, and BESS/PV planning models, not just energy or voltage criteria (Aldosary et al., 2025; Abbas et al., 2021; Owosuhi et al., 2023; Kotsonias et al., 2025).
Recommendations for Policymakers and Regulators
•	Update or regionalize harmonic and interharmonic standards (IEEE 519, IEC 61000 series) for high RES, EV, and electronics penetration, including supra harmonic ranges and PCC based limits (Taghvaie et al., 2023; Abbas et al., 2021; Arranz-Gimon et al., 2021; Kaur & Bath, 2025; Razmi et al., 2023).
•	Mandate routine harmonic reporting for large customers, DER plants, and DSOs; incentivize deployment of filters and advanced inverters able to provide harmonic mitigation services (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Aldosary et al., 2025; Owosuhi et al., 2023; Kotsonias et al., 2025).
•	Support demonstration projects and data sharing to validate new mitigation and AI based monitoring methods in real networks (Lowczowski et al., 2024; Kaur & Bath, 2025; Dehaghani et al., 2025; Assaffat et al., 2025).
Conclusion
Harmonic risk screening should lead to systematic monitoring and risk ranking by utilities, methodologically stronger modeling and validation by researchers, and updated standards, reporting rules, and incentives from policymakers, so that growing nonlinear and inverter based loads can be accommodated without breaching power quality limits.

•	How should concluding recommendations reflect both implementation feasibility and acknowledged study limitations?
Concluding recommendations should be clearly graded by how feasible they are and explicitly constrained by the study’s limitations, often using cautious, conditional language.
1. Make limitations shape, not follow, your recommendations
High quality guidance stresses that limitations should not just be listed, but linked to what can and cannot be recommended (Shaheen et al., 2023; Clarke et al., 2024; Kolaski et al., 2023).
Conclusions should therefore:
•	Identify how specific threats to validity (construct, internal, external, statistical) restrict generalizability or strength of claims (Shaheen et al., 2023; Clarke et al., 2024; Valentim et al., 2024).
•	Distinguish what seems usable now vs. what needs confirmation, labeling uncertain or inconclusive results so they stimulate further research rather than firm action (Clarke et al., 2024; Sarstedt et al., 2024).
2. Calibrate language and strength to evidence and feasibility
Guideline and GRADE style work recommends matching wording to evidence certainty, expected benefits/harms, and implementation practicality (Pezzullo et al., 2025; Teede et al., 2023; Kolaski et al., 2023):
Situation	How to frame recommendations	Citations
Strong evidence, high feasibility	“X should be implemented in settings with A, B, C…”	(Pezzullo et al., 2025; Teede et al., 2023)
Limited/indirect evidence, uncertain feasibility	“X could be considered in pilot or phased implementations…”	(Wilson et al., 2024; Pezzullo et al., 2025; Teede et al., 2023)
Evidence weak or biased, major gaps	“Current evidence does not support routine use; priority is rigorous trials / replication…”	(Shaheen et al., 2023; Clarke et al., 2024; Valentim et al., 2024; Kolaski et al., 2023)
Figure 1: Linking wording strength to evidence and feasibility.
Frameworks like APEASE (Affordability, Practicability, Effectiveness, Acceptability, Safety, Equity) are suggested to systematically test feasibility and equity impact of recommended strategies before endorsing them broadly (Wilson et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2023; Mash et al., 2025).
3. Pair practice advice with explicit research agendas
Scoping and systematic reviews emphasize that prescriptive recommendations should be grounded in available empirical evidence and accompanied by concrete research needs when evidence or applicability is limited (Negt & Haunschild, 2024; Valentim et al., 2024; Wang et al., 2023; Rojon et al., 2021; Kolaski et al., 2023).
Conclusion
Well crafted concluding recommendations are explicit about what is actionable now, what is conditional or pilot worthy, and what remains primarily a research priority—always tying these distinctions back to documented methodological and contextual limitations.

•	What types of recommendation statements are most persuasive and publication-appropriate in power-systems journals?
In power-systems journals, the most persuasive recommendations are precise, evidence graded, and clearly scoped to realistic next steps rather than sweeping claims.
1. Style of Recommendation Statements
a) Evidence linked and graded (strong vs. tentative)
Review articles in power and energy commonly conclude with tiered recommendations:
•	Strong: “Utilities should integrate X into Y under conditions A–C, where benefits were consistently observed…”
•	Conditional: “X could be considered for feeders with high Z, but further validation is required…” (Strielkowski et al., 2023)Such language mirrors best practice in technical reviews: clear direction where evidence is convergent, cautious phrasing (“could”, “may”, “promising”) where evidence or generalizability is limited (Strielkowski et al., 2023).
b) Explicit “practice vs. research” split
High impact reviews separate:
•	Implementation recommendations (e.g., adopt specific ML tools for defined tasks, under known constraints)
•	Research recommendations (e.g., need for better datasets, benchmarks, robustness studies) (Strielkowski et al., 2023)This dual structure is widely used in surveys of predictive analytics and recommender frameworks in energy contexts, and is well accepted by journals (Hasan et al., 2021; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Himeur et al., 2021).
2. Content Features That Increase Persuasiveness
Clear, operational next steps
Power systems and energy storage reviews that are influential recommend concrete, implementable actions (e.g., improving data quality pipelines, standardizing test conditions, harmonizing evaluation metrics), not just abstract calls for “more research” (Hasan et al., 2021; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Himeur et al., 2021).
Limitations aware but forward looking
Publication appropriate conclusions explicitly acknowledge scope limits (e.g., domain coverage, language, keyword bias) and then formulate how future work can systematically address them (expanded corpora, added descriptors, non English sources) (Roy & Dutta, 2022; Zhang et al., 2023).
Alignment with community priorities
Persuasive recommendations are framed around recognized challenges: data availability, safety, standardization, interoperability, explainability, and user acceptance in energy applications (Hasan et al., 2021; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Himeur et al., 2021).
Example structure for a concluding section in a power systems article
Element	Example phrasing	Citations
Strong practice	“System operators should prioritize X where …”	(Hasan et al., 2021; Strielkowski et al., 2023)
Conditional practice	“X may be suitable for … pending Y.”	(Strielkowski et al., 2023; Himeur et al., 2021)
Research gap	“Future work needs to address Z through …”	(Roy & Dutta, 2022; Zhang et al., 2023; Strielkowski et al., 2023; Himeur et al., 2021)
Figure 1: Common components of persuasive recommendations.
Conclusion
For power systems journals, the most persuasive and publishable recommendation statements are those that: (1) distinguish strong from tentative advice, (2) separate practical guidance from research agendas, (3) offer specific, operational next steps, and (4) are explicitly constrained by documented limitations and dominant field challenges.
